# 道德真经取善集

**The Daoist Classic *Taking the Good from the Dao De Jing* – A Compilation**

**Title:** *Taking the Good from the Dao De Jing*
**Compiled by:** Jin Li Lin (金李霖).
**Twelve volumes.**
Its preface states: *"Now I take the virtues of various schools, severing them by my own understanding of goodness,"* hence its name, *Taking the Good from the Dao De Jing*.
A preface by Liu You Sheng (刘尤升) precedes the volumes.
Source of the base text: *The Zhen Tong Daoist Canon* (《正统道藏》), in the **Tongshen (洞神) Section – Jade Precepts (玉诀类)**.

---

**Preface to *Taking the Good from the Dao De Jing***

When the Master Lao (老氏) was in the declining years of the Shang Dynasty, he pitied the corruption of the Way (道) and the moral decline of his age, attributing it to the excesses of culture. Thus, he sought to restore the simplicity of the ancient Dao, expounding the profound winds of mysticism to rouse the common folk—even to the point of scorn for virtue and morality, rejecting ritual and scholarship. Indeed, his words were so piercingly direct that they could not be bent by falsehood; hence, Confucius held him in reverence.

Upon seeing it, Confucius sighed, *"It is like a dragon!"*—only the sage knows the sage, and so he spoke.

Guan Yin (关尹) beheld the auspicious purple vapor and recognized the True Person (真人) crossing the pass. With devout sincerity, he knelt and pleaded for instruction, and only then did the Master descend his profound teachings.

Critics often say that the Five Classics (五经) are vast yet inferior to the two chapters of this text in brevity—truly, there is reason for this.

Zhuang Zhou (庄周) and Lie Yu (列御寇) expanded its teachings, as if stirring great waves in the boundless sea or raising towering peaks upon lofty mountains. Yet even this only approximates its edges without fully grasping its intent. Only when one forgets words does one truly recognize its guiding principles.

During the reigns of Emperor Wen (文) and Emperor Jing (景) of the Han Dynasty, governance favored simplicity and tranquility, and the empire flourished in peace. This was due to the guidance of Cao Can (曹参), who followed the teachings of Gao Gong (盖公), proving the efficacy of the Dao De’s virtue (德) in transforming the world—indeed, the Master’s lament was not in vain.

In the Jin Dynasty, however, the Dao degenerated into frivolous fancy. Wang Xuan (王衍) indulged in empty discourse, undermining the pure traditions. Ruan Ji (阮籍) raged recklessly, further destroying the foundations of moral order. To abandon the root and follow only the branches—how could one not grieve?

Fortunately, the Sui scholar Wang Zhongyan (王仲淹) deeply understood its nature, declaring that *"the Dao’s emptiness was prolonged, and the Jin Dynasty thus declined—not the fault of Laozi and Zhuangzi, but because their teachings were misapplied."*

In the Tang Dynasty, Han Yu (韩愈) still mocked its minimalism as *"petty virtue, like a frog in a well viewing the heavens."* Alas, Han Yu squandered his brilliance while ignorant of the Dao—he was deaf and blind in spirit, unable to perceive how the thunder of Tai Mountain (太山) could shake his ears and dazzle his sight.

To call this lack of wisdom, and to earn the sighs of the virtuous—how lamentable!

Devout adherents have never been lacking across generations, each interpreting the text according to their own understanding. Their commentaries number in the tens, making it difficult both to read and to compile.

Li Lin (李霖) of Rao Yang (饶阳), styled Zong Fu (宗傅), was by nature serene and unassuming, steadfast from youth to old age. He dedicated himself to studying the Five Thousand Characters (五千之文), embodying the resolve of *"knowing the lofty and noble as worthy of emulation, forgetting the labor of probing and analyzing."* He gathered the finest interpretations from various schools, blending them with his own insights, and produced six volumes.

Just as the Eight Tones (八音) differ yet harmonize to the ear, or the Five Flavors (五味) vary yet please the palate—so too does this work.

The purpose is to broaden understanding and enrich knowledge, thereby aiding fellow practitioners of the Way. Its contributions, though modest, are by no means insignificant.

Wang Bin was the Master’s old friend, and in admiration of his diligence and fulfillment of his aspirations, he commissioned artisans to engrave the text onto wooden blocks, sparing those who cherish the Way the labor of manual transcription. His thoughtful generosity can hardly be called anything but benevolent.

This was in the year *Dàdìng* *Rénchén* (大定壬辰), on the *Chóngwǔ* day (重午), prefaced by the River Interval Scholar Liu Yunsheng (河间刘允升).

All things derive from the Dao.
The Dao within us is virtue/power (*dé*).
The Dao is subtle and formless, its transformations unfathomable.
Virtue/power is manifest and substantial—yet all is virtue/power.
From its differences, it appears as two names.
From its unity, its essence remains singular.

The unlearned speak of the Dao yet often overlook virtue/power.
Those who speak of virtue/power invariably include the Dao.
Thus, the separation of Dao and virtue/power obscures their perfect unity.

Just as the Dragon, the Supreme Sage, lamented the decay of nature and life at the end of the Shang dynasty, and pitied the decline of Dao and virtue/power, he composed *Nine Times Nine* chapters to illuminate the most profound mysteries. His words did not exceed the *Five Thousand Characters*, yet their meaning permeated the Three Teachings. Internally, it teaches cultivation of the heart and nurturing of life; externally, it guides governance and the pacification of the people—being the foremost principle for all speech and the origin of all things. It encompasses the vast without omission, the minute without neglect. Its language is concise, its meaning profound—vast and boundless indeed.

Since the advent of written records, no text has surpassed this Scripture in its profundity. Though many have since commented upon it, few have grasped its entirety. Each interprets according to their own perspective, with inevitable gains and losses. Those who comprehend nature may attain the subtle mysteries of the Perfect Spirit, yet their understanding of life may remain incomplete. Those who master life may grasp the essential keys to longevity, yet their grasp of nature may be insufficient.

Yet truly, nature and life are inseparable—Dao and virtue/power are one.

From my youth to maturity, I recited the Mysterious Words in vain, awaiting the opportunity to answer inquiries. Now aged, I seek to delve into their deeper meanings to cultivate my own authenticity. Knowing my feeble understanding cannot fathom the Sage’s intent, I have gathered the finest interpretations of others and supplemented them with my own, not to enlighten later generations, but for the convenience of reference. I have titled it *The Gathering of the Virtuous* (*Qǔshànjí*), hoping readers will not scorn it.

**Prefatory Remarks to *The Gathering of the Virtuous* from the Rao Yang Recluse Li Lin**
*Dao and Virtue/Power Classic – The Gathering of the Virtuous, Volume One*
*Collected Works of the Song Dynasty Rao Yang Recluse Li Lin*

**Imperial Annotation:**
The Dao is the path all share.
Virtue/power is what the heart attains on its own.
The Dao endures through all ages without decay.
Virtue/power fills the nature and remains ever-present.

Laozi, in the declining years of the Zhou, saw the Dao descend while virtue/power waned, and thus composed *Nine Times Nine* chapters to clarify the eternal principles of Dao and virtue/power, calling it the *Classic*. Its words are simple, its meaning profound—students must ponder it in silence and strive deeply to master it.

**Chapter One: The Dao That Can Be Spoken Of**

The Dao that can be spoken of is not the Eternal Dao.
The name that can be named is not the Eternal Name.

Yán Zhūn remarked: *"The Dao that can be spoken of is the manifest virtue/power, not the natural and spontaneous."*

Today’s practitioners do not carry torches by day because the sun shines naturally.
The natural brightness of the sun is the Eternal Dao.
Carrying torches is the manifest Dao.

What can be inscribed on bamboo and silk, carved into metal and stone, and passed down to others—this is the Dao that can be spoken of.
Yet there is also the Dao that can be transmitted but not received, obtained but not seen—its origin and root precede Heaven and Earth, existing since antiquity. It is the eternal Dao that gives birth to gods, spirits, Heaven, and Earth.

The profundity of the *Five Thousand Characters* is unfolded in these few words—truly, the Mysterious beyond the Mysterious, the Divine beyond the Divine.

Wang Yuanzé said: *"Names arise from reality. Reality has form and measure; once form and measure are established, decay follows inevitably. How then can it be eternal? Only by grasping this impermanence can one attain true permanence."*

The opening phrase introduces *Dao*—the Great Dao.
The subsequent phrase, *"the Dao that can be spoken of,"* refers to the Dao of speech.

The Great Dao is empty and tranquil; its principles are profound and unfathomable. It cannot be spoken of, only grasped in silent understanding. Thus, the mind is perplexed and unable to comprehend, the tongue falters and cannot articulate—it must be intuitively realized within the spirit’s depths.

Even the word *Dao* itself cannot be spoken.
If left unspoken, the common folk follow it without knowing.
Thus, the Sage, unable to bear silence, named it *Dao*.
Once named, speech and explanation arise, waxing and waning in succession—this is not the Eternal Dao.

What can be spoken of is all the Dao, yet it is not the Eternal Dao.
Only what cannot be spoken of can be eternal.

Now, Confucian virtues like *rén* (仁, benevolence), *yì* (义, righteousness), *lǐ* (礼, propriety), and *zhì* (智, wisdom) are the Dao that can be spoken of yet cannot be eternal.
Only when they cannot be spoken of—when *rén* is *rén*, *yì* is *yì*, *lǐ* is *lǐ*, and *zhì* is *zhì*—do they remain unchanging while the Dao endures. Thus, the eternal Dao that cannot be spoken of is the natural and spontaneous, responding to stimuli and transforming without end, interacting with all things exhaustively. It cannot be transmitted through words or sought through intellect—only by embodying the mysterious workings of nature, containing light and concealing radiance, doing nothing yet accomplishing all, silently attaining its utmost.

The nameless is the beginning of Heaven and Earth.
The named is the mother of all things.

Wén Gōng remarked: *"Heaven and Earth are the greatest of forms. Their origin must derive from the nameless, hence the beginning of Heaven and Earth is called *nameless*. All things are born through form, their birth depending on the named, hence the mother of all things is called *named*."*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"The nameless is the Dao, from which Heaven and Earth originate. Thus, it is called the *nameless*, the beginning of Heaven and Earth.*

*Before the beginning, there was the nameless and the unnamed.*
*The unnamed gave rise to the named.*

*Without the unnamed, even Oneness cannot be attained.*
*The named is the origin of Oneness.*

*With Oneness yet unformed, this is the beginning of Heaven and Earth."*

---

Once it is called **Oneness**, can one still attain the **nameless**?

This thing, obtained and thus born, is called **virtue/power**. Hence, what is called the **named** is the mother of all things.
And all things obtain **Oneness** to be born—this is the case.

The **nameless** is called **Dao/Way**. The **Dao/Way** is ever the **nameless**, giving rise to Heaven and Earth,
and thus is the beginning of Heaven and Earth—this is the **Dao/Way**’s marvelous essence.

The **named** is called Heaven and Earth. Heaven envelops, Earth sustains,
and only then do all things stand—hence it is the mother of all things,
this being the **Dao/Way**’s broad manifestation.

Heaven and Earth possess form and position—this is the **named**.
The mother of all things, Heaven and Earth containing **qi**,
nurtures and matures all things, as a mother rears her child.

To dwell in the **nameless** is to seek its marvelous essence.
To dwell in the **named** is to seek its broad manifestation.

Sū Zǐyóu remarked:
*"From the **nameless**, form arises as Heaven and Earth.
When Heaven and Earth are positioned, the **named** begins to stand.
From the **named**, dispersion gives birth to all things.
When all things are nurtured, the **named** is beyond measure.
Thus, the **nameless** is the **Dao/Way**’s substance,
while the **named** is the **Dao/Way**’s function."*

The Sage embodies the **Dao/Way** for the world’s use,
entering all things yet ever **nameless**,
to behold its marvelous essence.

The Sage embodies its utmost **namelessness** yet ever **named**,
to behold its broad manifestation.

If one acts only in its broad manifestation while ignorant of its marvelous essence,
then it is coarse and not divine.

If one lingers only in its marvelous essence while ignorant of its broad manifestation,
then it is refined yet not practical.

The text states:
*"When moved by things, this is the stirrings of nature.*
Nature’s stirrings are the **Dao/Way**’s movement away from **stillness**,
relying on ears and eyes for sight and sound,
wearing out the heart and mind through ceaseless thought and planning.

Without desire, one may dwell in **stillness** to observe the beginning of Heaven and Earth—
this is the marvelous essence.
Hence it is said:
*'To ever lack desire is to call it **small**.*
The marvelous essence is refined yet **small**.

With desire, one moves to observe the mother of all things—
this is the broad manifestation.
Hence it is said:
*'All things return to it yet none know its master—this is the **great**.*
The broad manifestation is expansive yet **great**.*

The person without desire may perceive the **Dao/Way**’s refined marvel.
The person with desire sees only its coarse broadness.

These two arise from the same source yet bear different names,
both called **Xuan/Mysterious**.
Wén Gōng remarked:
*"The **Mysterious** is neither **having** nor **non-having**,
the ultimate of refined subtlety.
The two refer to **having desire** and **lacking desire**.
They arise from the same source—
both spring from the human heart.
Yet they bear different names,
for their names vary.
Though their names differ, their substance never truly does.
Since their substance never truly differs,
then both **having desire** and **lacking desire** are called **Mysterious**.*

The **Mysterious** as color is black,
yet shares the same origin as red.
Heaven’s hue is **Mysterious**,
for yin and yang share the same origin.

Both are called **Mysterious**,
for the **Mysterious** can embrace both yin and yang.
The **Mysterious** beyond the **Mysterious**,
is the gateway to all marvels.

The *Commentary* states:
*"The *Suwen* says: The **Mysterious** gives birth to the divine.*
*The *Yijing* calls the marvelous yet **small** the **Mysterious**.*
The **Mysterious** is Heaven’s color,
yet the colors that arise from it never reveal the **Mysterious** itself.
The **Mysterious** beyond the **Mysterious** is what is meant by 'colors that arise from it.'
The **Mysterious**’s refined principle permeates all things,
Heaven’s movement, Earth’s resting,
the human spirit’s vitality, and the flourishing of all creatures—
all are marvels,
yet all emerge from the **Mysterious**.
Thus it is called the **gateway to all marvels**.*

*The *Yijing* says:*
*'The interplay of one yin and one yang is called the **Dao/Way**,
and yin and yang both originate from Oneness.*
Oneness is what the **Dao/Way** gives rise to.
The **Mysterious** is where yin and yang share the same origin,
and the **Mysterious** is also the **Dao/Way** itself.
All marvels are the marvels of all things,
and all things possess marvelous principles,
yet all emerge from the **Dao/Way**—
thus it is called the **gateway to all marvels**.*

This chapter speaks of the **Dao/Way** of **True Constancy**.
Those who awaken shall find it themselves,
ineffable and beyond naming.
To harmonize the observation of broadness and marvel,
one may then construct the **Dao/Way** of **True Constancy**.

The **Supreme Sovereign** begins with this chapter,
summarizing the entire scripture’s intent,
illuminating the **Dao/Way**’s foundation—
this is the **Mysterious** beyond the **Mysterious**.

---

**Chapter Two: "All Under Heaven Know"**

All under Heaven know beauty as beauty—then ugliness arises.
All know goodness as goodness—then non-goodness arises.

The *Commentary* states:
*"The **Dao/Way** has no varied appearances—who then is beauty or ugliness?
Nature is inherently unified—who then is good or not?
There is beauty, and ugliness is its counterpart.
Hence it is said:
*'All under Heaven know beauty as beauty—then ugliness arises.'*
There is goodness, and non-goodness is its counterpart.
Hence it is said:
*'All know goodness as goodness—then non-goodness arises.'*

What the world esteems as beauty is the divine and strange.
What it abhors is the foul and rotten.
The divine and strange transform into the foul and rotten,
the foul and rotten transform into the divine and strange—
then what is the distinction between beauty and ugliness?

What was once affirmed may now be denied,
what was once rejected may later be employed."*

Then what is the distinction between virtue/power and non-virtue/non-power?

The Sage embodies the unchanging truth while always manifesting,
and transcends wondrous application while remaining non-attached—
beauty, ugliness, virtue/power, non-virtue/non-power,
all are but simplified illusions, beyond comprehension.
Why then should non-virtue/non-power be a burden?

Master Wang Yuanze said:
*"Non-virtue/non-power and non-goodness are the companions of virtue/power and goodness.
When virtue/power and goodness are in play,
non-virtue/non-power and non-goodness are already present.
To await their emergence and then realize it—
that is but late enlightenment.
Beauty and ugliness arise from deluded emotions,
virtue/power and non-virtue/non-power balance in the loss of true nature.
The beautiful is what human emotions cherish.
If one knows beauty as beauty,
then the mind has something it cherishes as beautiful.
When the mind cherishes beauty,
it brings affliction to itself—then ugliness arises.
If the River Spirit (河伯) rejoices exultantly,
boasting that all the beauty of the world lies within him,
he cannot escape sighing at the vastness before him,
almost falling into error.
The virtuous is what humans desire.
If one knows virtue/power as virtue/power,
then true nature has something it desires.
When true nature desires,
it strays from the Dao/Way to cling to virtue/power—then non-virtue/non-power arises.
If Master Bi (伯夷) saw the name of virtue/power as desirable,
starving beneath Chief Yang (首阳),
it was still a loss of true nature—almost falling into error.
For the Dao/Way’s beauty is the supreme beauty.
Supreme beauty transcends beauty, serene beyond all taste.
Zhuangzi said:
*'Faintly discerned, without limit—
all beauties arise from it.*
The Dao/Way’s virtue/power is the highest virtue/power.
Highest virtue/power forgets virtue/power,
and all virtues/powers are complete.
He also said:
*'Abandon virtue/power, and virtue/power itself is fulfilled.'*
This chapter unifies the Dao/Way as one,
fearing that people will cling to beauty and virtue/power,
and not awaken to the true and constant.
Thus it follows with this passage,
resembling *Zhuangzi’s齐物论* ["Discourse on Equalizing Things"].

The interplay of presence and absence,
difficulty and ease,
length and shortness,
height and depth,
sound and resonance,
before and after—
these six pairs arise from the interplay of transformation (韦变),
from myriad contemplations,
from divergent paths,
and are the means by which the world’s people
are ensnared and cannot extricate themselves.
Absence generates presence without movement,
presence returns to absence—
hence it is said: *presence and absence intergenerate.*
When presence encounters difficulty,
one knows ease in action—
hence it is said: *difficulty and ease intercomplete.*
Length and shortness define each other,
as with the ruler’s measurements.
Height and depth incline toward one another,
as with mountains and valleys.
A sound ascends and its echo responds—
hence it is said: *sound and resonance harmonize.*
A form moves and its shadow follows—
hence it is said: *before and after interfollow.*
The circulation of yin and yang,
the progression of the four seasons,
the principles of all things—
suddenly formed, then suddenly vanished.
Their difficulty is like the labor of contrivance (有为) in governing the world,
their ease is like the effortlessness of non-action (无为) in aligning with oneself.
What is long in nature is not to be severed,
what is short in nature is not to be prolonged.
Heaven’s own height, Earth’s own depth,
striking the gong of the palace (宫) and the palace resounds,
striking the gong of the ram’s horn (角) and the ram’s horn answers.
Spring precedes and summer follows,
the long precedes and the young follow—
though all these paired realms dwell within the Dao/Way,
they are far from its essence.
Master Wen (温公) said:
*'Whatever has form and trace
must not be equalized.
Unequalized, it leads to contention;
contention, to chaos;
chaos, to exhaustion—
thus the Sage does not value it.'*
Therefore, the Sage dwells in non-action (无为) in his deeds,
and practices the teaching of non-speech (不言).
Master Gu (顾欢) said:
*'The Sage follows Heaven and entrusts to things,
creating nothing.
His mind ever steadfast,
quiet before all.
When beauty and virtue/power are at rest,
there is no contention—
thus he is not swayed by the six realms.
Action brings gain and loss,
speech brings right and wrong—
one cannot escape burdens.
Therefore, the Sage in his affairs
acts through non-action and teaches through non-speech,
so that his heart remains serene,
never burdened by words,
and the world returns to its constant,
recovering its simplicity.
Only when there are no burdens,
does he, though dwelling between yin and yang,
transcend the power of creation,
and those six pairs—
how could they disturb him?*

The *Classic* says:
*"The teaching of non-speech,
the benefit of non-action—
few under Heaven attain to them."*
All things act without refusal—
the *Commentary* states:
*"All things move and respond,
sensing while reacting.
Like a mirror reflecting forms,
fair and foul appear without hesitation.
Like a valley echoing sounds,
beauty and ugliness all respond—
nothing is refused.
Now, though the count of things
does not stop at ten thousand,
it is called ‘all things’ to signify their multitude.
‘Act’ means to move and respond.
All things move and respond,
the Sage lets each fulfill its nature,
never refusing or obstructing,
for my heart is empty,
neither taking nor discarding.
If one harbors thoughts of taking or discarding,
then the myriad forms of things—
beauty, ugliness, and more—
how could one not refuse them?"*

To give birth without possession,
to act without reliance,
to complete the work without claiming it—
Master Zuan Wei (纂微) said:
*"All things generate themselves,
each standing alone in transformation,
not possessed by me.
The myriad qualities strive in their own doing,
each aligning with its own nature,
not relying on my virtue/power.
When deeds are completed and affairs fulfilled,
the Dao/Way harmonizes with things,
the heart wanders at Mount Gu Shou (姑射),
not dwelling over the ten thousand people—
this is the Sage’s complete virtue/power.
All things generate themselves,
each reaching their utmost height and grandeur.
All things act of their own accord,
each rectifying their own life and fate.
The Sage attributes function to things,
not burdening himself with the three affairs—
hence it is said:
*'To complete the work without claiming it—
if there is a self, then it claims.
Claiming, it strays.'*
Emperor Yao (帝尧) completed his work,
yet saw himself as deficient.
Only when one does not claim,
does one not stray.
Master Su Ziyou (苏子由) said:
*"The Sage dwells in poverty and humble station
yet feels no poverty or humble station’s distress,
dwells in wealth and honor
yet feels no wealth or honor’s burden—
this is called ‘not claiming.’
If I do not claim,
how could others follow to claim?*
This is the ultimate claiming."*

《鸿烈》 says: When the Chu general **Zi Fa** (子发) attacked the state of **Cai** (蔡) and crossed its borders, King **Xuan of Chu** (宣王) went to the outskirts to welcome him, granting him a hundred *mu* (亩) of land and the **jue** (圭) of a noble rank. Zi Fa declined the honor, saying:

*"To govern a state and establish policies—
this is the virtue/power of a sovereign.
To issue commands and have armies united—
this is the prestige of a general.
To fight battles and conquer the enemy—
this is the strength of commoners.
If I take credit for the people’s labor
and accept their rewards and honors,
this would not be the Dao/Way of benevolence and righteousness.
Therefore, I do not accept."*

Hence it is said:
*'To complete the work without claiming it—
if there is a self, then it claims.
Claiming, it strays.'*
Only when one does not claim,
does one not stray.

This chapter seeks to embody the **Zhenchang Dao** (真常之道),
forgetting distinctions of good and evil,
equating virtue and vice,
and not being swayed by the six pairs of opposites.
Only the Sage understands this truth.

Thus, in conducting affairs, he acts with **wuwei** (无为),
in teaching, he does not speak explicitly,
and he attributes function to things without claiming it—
the Dao/Way abides within me yet does not stray.

**Chapter Three: Not Exalting the Virtuous**

*"Not exalting the virtuous*
makes the people not contend."

**Master Wen** (温公) said:
*"That the virtuous cannot be neglected—
all people know this.
But when its excesses become corrupt,
it leads to constant strife over reputation.
Thus, **Laozi** (老子) corrected this,
urging people to value substance, not name.
The virtuous are those
who stand out among the crowd.
When rulers distinguish and exalt them,
elevating them with noble ranks
and honoring them with fine carriages and robes,
the people then vie for external glory,
aspire to emulation,
and pursue fame without end—
how could contention cease?"*

The Sage has never truly failed to use the virtuous.
He simply assigns them to their proper roles
and entrusts them with their duties—
only he does not exalt them.

**Zhuangzi** (庄子) said:
*"To exalt the virtuous
makes the people jostle one another.
To prize rare goods
makes the people not steal."*

**御注** explains:
*"Exalting the virtuous leads to widespread knowledge,
until the world is in uproar—
Confucianism and Mohism arise.
Prize rare goods, and the people’s desires multiply,
until daylight theft becomes rampant—
as seen in the saying, 'Midday burglary at the Lu holes.' (中穴陆)"*

**御注** further states:
*"The people’s desires,
when unchecked, override their natural inclinations,
leading to plunder, deception, and shamelessness—
there is no end to their excesses.
When **Yi** (夷) sees the allure of fame,
he starves beneath Shouyang’s slopes.
When **Zhi** (跖) sees the lure of profit,
he dies violently at Dongling’s heights.
Their passions burn like wildfire,
their coldness freezes like ice—
thus, their hearts are filled with fury, disorder, and arrogance,
and cannot be bound to the Dao/Way.
But the Sage
does not pursue profit,
does not shun harm,
does not rejoice in longevity,
does not lament fate,
does not glory in fame,
does not despise poverty—
then what is there to desire?
If desires do not arise,
my heart is clear and still,
responding only to what is felt—
when motion ceases, there is no obstruction,
when action begins, there is no compulsion—
who could disturb it?"*

The **junzi** (君子) desires only the virtuous.
The **xiaoren** (小人) is drawn to wealth.
Since there are no traces of exalting the virtuous
and no pursuit of rare goods,
there are no visible desires—
thus, the people’s hearts remain undisturbed.

**Fu Yi** (傅奕) in his *Yinyi* (音义) says:
*"The ancient text reads: 'makes the people’s hearts not be disturbed.' (民心不乱)"*

Therefore, the Sage’s governance
**He Shang Gong** (河上公) says:
*"The Sage’s rule over a state
is the same as his cultivation of the self."*

—empties the heart, fills the belly,
**Cao Daochong** (曹道冲) says:
*"An empty heart, white and clear,
retains the spirit and preserves the Dao/Way.
A filled belly,
ensures full vital essence and long life."*

—selects nothing, empties the heart so there is no virtue to exalt,
—contains all, fills the belly so there is no desire to covet.

—weakens the will, strengthens the bones.
**御注** explains:
*"A strong will leads some to pursue fame without rest,
others to chase wealth without satisfaction,
others to boast of their achievements,
or to pride themselves on their abilities—
the farther they stray from the Dao/Way.
The Sage’s will
ever humbles itself, yet others hold it high.
Ever lags behind, yet others place it first.
Knowing its strength, he guards its weakness;
knowing its glory, he guards its shame—
this is called 'weakening the will.'
As for 'strengthening the bones,'
if one does not harm them,
as seen in **Guangchengzi** (广成子) living a thousand two hundred years
without ever showing signs of decay—
this is called 'strengthening the bones.'"*

**Liu Zhongping** (刘仲平) says:
*"An empty heart and weakened will
nourish the spirit.
A filled belly and strong bones
preserve vital essence.
The will is where the heart directs—
guarding the Dao/Way weakens the will.
The bones are the repository of marrow—
when marrow is full, the bones are strong.
Another interpretation:
An empty heart leads to a weakened will,
a filled belly leads to strong bones.
Or:
An empty heart leads to a weakened will,
a filled belly leads to strong bones—
these are both plausible readings."*

The Sage’s governance
always makes the people **without knowledge and without desire**.

**御注** explains:
*"Zhuangzi said:
*'In harmony with the unknowing,
its virtue/power does not depart.
In harmony with the undesiring,
this is called "primordial simplicity." (素朴)
Primordial simplicity—
and the people’s nature is preserved.'*
The Sage’s governance
strives only to let the people realize their nature.
Excessive knowledge disrupts their natural roles,
excessive desire drowns their natural inclinations—
though it may be called 'governance,'
what chaos could be greater?"*

Thus, the Sage’s governance
always makes the people **without knowledge and without desire**.

**Wang Yuanzai** (王元泽) says:
*"Knowledge leads to false perceptions,
desire leads to external seeking—
when these two are removed,
nature and emotion are settled.
From not exalting the virtuous,
he empties the heart and weakens the will to transform them,
making the people lack contentious knowledge of the virtuous.
From not prizing rare goods,
he fills the belly and strengthens the bones to transform them,
making the people lack acquisitive desire for wealth."*

「Thus, the Sage’s governance ensures that the wise dare not act.
**Lǚ Jìfǔ** (吕吉甫) says:
*"The wise know that the virtuous are not prized by the Sage,
and that wealth is not valued by the Sage,
yet to act upon such knowledge is not beneficial—
hence, they dare not act."*

**To act without acting** (為無為),
then there is nothing left untamed.
**Shū Wáng** (舒王) says:
*"To act without purpose is to act without restraint;
to act without restraint is to act without ceasing.
The Sage acts without acting,
and thus there is nothing left untamed."*

This chapter speaks of the Sage embodying the **Dao/Way** of **wuwei** (無為) to govern.
**Not prizing the virtuous, not valuing wealth,**
emptying the heart and filling the belly,
weakening the will and strengthening the bones—
this is the Sage embodying the **Dao/Way** to govern the body **without acting**.

**To make the people not contend** and align with **wu zhi** (無知),
**to make the people not steal** and align with **wu yu** (無欲),
then there is nothing left untamed.

The *Classic* (《經》) says:
**"The Dao/Way is ever without acting yet nothing remains unacted upon.**
If lords and kings could guard it,
all things would naturally transform themselves.
The Sage who governs **without acting** is how **Yú Shùn** (虞舜) became Emperor.
The Sage who governs with **垂拱** (垂拱) is how **Zhōu Wǔ** (周武) became King.
Thus it is said:
**Emperors and Kings govern without acting, and the realm is perfected.**

**Chapter Four: The Dao/Way is Boundless** (道沖)
The Dao/Way is boundless,
yet its use may appear as though it were not full.

**御注** (Commentary):
*"The Dao/Way has sentiment and trust,
hence it has use.
Without acting and without form,
hence it is not full.
Boundless (沖) means central (中).*
The Dao/Way’s use,
though it suppresses the high and elevates the low,
reduces excess and supplements deficiency,
has no fixed course yet finds its center.
The Dao/Way’s essence,
empty yet not full,
overflowing yet without limit,
nourishes all things yet never possesses—
hence it is said to appear as though it were not full.

**Abyssal (渊)**, it seems like the origin of all things.
**Wēn Gōng** (温公) says:
*"Deep and unfathomable,
ever the master of all things.
Abyssal (渊) and flowing (反流) are one.*
The *Shuō Wén* (《说文》) says:
*"Returning water (回水).*
When water returns, it is deep and still;
when it returns and becomes an abyss (渊),
it ceases to flow—
thus, its unity is complete."

**兮** (Ah) is a rhythmic aid in singing.
**Zhuāngzǐ** (庄子) says:
*"The stillness of water is its abyss (渊).*
The abyss is deep and still,
unmixed with things—
the essence of the Dao/Way.
Only its depth—
no thing can fathom it.
Only its stillness—
no thing can move it.
The Dao/Way’s essence is abyssal (渊), deep and still,
appearing as the origin of all things.
Yet the Dao/Way itself has no inherent connection to things;
it is the origin of itself alone—
hence it is said to appear so.*

As for those who say "appears" (似),
it is because they dare not speak directly of it.

**Humans are originally sufficient within this Dao/Way;**
those who seek to embody it
must not seek it elsewhere.
They should blunt their sharpness,
resolve their entanglements,
harmonize their brilliance,
and align with the dust—
then they will naturally preserve themselves.

**Blunt their sharpness, resolve their entanglements,**
harmonize their brilliance,
and align with the dust.
**Wēn Gōng** (温公) says:
*"The sharpness of edges and the fierceness of exposure—
these are what the Dao/Way abhors.
Troublesome and chaotic affairs—
these are what the Dao/Way despises.
Dazzling radiance and ostentatious display—
these are the Dao/Way’s ruin.
Humility and obscurity—
these are what the Dao/Way values.*

When the heart strays outward and mingles with things,
that is sharpness—
blunt it and do not act.
When things arrive and the heart becomes entangled,
that is entanglement—
resolve it and do not be disordered.
To blunt sharpness and resolve entanglements,
the nature and emotions settle,
and naturally become full and radiant.

Once radiance is present,
it is not fundamentally different from things—
harmonized and aligned yet not displayed,
this is called **light yet not blinding** (光而不耀).
Within, it does not lose its truth;
without, it does not differ from the common—
aligned with the dust yet unstained,
this is called **committing with things and aligning with their waves** (與物委蛇，而同其波).

**Serene (湛)**, it seems as though it might be preserved.
**Guō Xiàng** (郭象) says:
*"Preserved (存) means 'to exist.'
The Dao/Way is serene and tranquil,
unchanging from ancient to present,
constant from beginning to end—
hence it is said to exist.
Yet it exists without things—
hence it is said to appear so.*

If humans can follow these four principles,
then they will be serene and ever still—
preserved.
To preserve yet be fixed is not the Dao/Way.
The saying "appears as though it might be preserved" (似或者不可定有之謂)
refers to this.

I do not know whose child it is.
**Wáng Yuànzǎi** (王元泽) says:
*"It is the embodiment of the Dao/Way as spoken of today.
Though it has an origin, it is vast and without form—
hence it is said: I do not know whose child it is.*
The phrase "whose child" (誰之子)
asks from whom this Dao/Way originates.

**The Form of the Emperor precedes the Dao/Way.**
**御注** (Commentary):
*"Form (象) is the initial appearance of things.
The Emperor (帝) is the manifestation of the divine responding to things.*
Things arise and then form appears;
the Emperor emerges and then wondrous things are manifest.
The Form of the Emperor (象帝),
though it marks the beginning of things,
is preceded by the Dao/Way itself.
This is what **Zhuāngzǐ** (庄子) meant by
**'the divine and the ghostly, the divine and the Emperor,**
**generating heaven and earth.'**
The Form of the Emperor (象帝) portends the beginning of things,
and the Emperor (帝) is the sovereign of all things.
The Form of the Emperor (象帝) is most profound,
yet the Dao/Way surpasses it even further.
This chapter speaks of the Dao/Way using centrality (中) as its function,
its essence empty yet not full,
the master of all existence,
preceding the Form of the Emperor (象帝).

**Chapter Five: Heaven and Earth**
Heaven and Earth are not **virtuous** (仁),
treating all things as **straw dogs** (刍狗).
The Sage is not **virtuous** (仁),
treating the people as **straw dogs** (刍狗).

**御注** (Commentary):
*"Kindness arises from harm,
harm arises from kindness.
If kindness is taken as kindness,
harm will inevitably follow.
Heaven and Earth toward all things,
the Sage toward the people,
aid their natural tendencies,
without a heart of love or benefit.
Kindness cannot be named with certainty."*

**Straw dogs** (刍狗) are made by bundling straw,
used in sacrifices to **Qí** (杞).
When the ritual is appropriate, they are used;
when it is over, they are discarded.
As for the spontaneous generation of all things,
the self-governance of the people—
how could they be contained by the heart?»

「爱人利物之谓仁。不仁者，谓无情於弁爱，非薄恶之谓也。
*The Way of loving people and benefiting all things is called **virtue** (仁).
The unvirtuous (不仁) is said to lack emotional attachment in aiding others—
not the meaning of being indifferent or cruel.*

天地生化万物，任其自然，无爱利之心焉。如刍狗当祭祀之用也。
Heaven and Earth give birth and transform all things,
letting them follow their natural course,
without a heart of love or benefit.
Like **straw dogs** (刍狗) used in ritual sacrifices.

盛以箧衍，巾以文绣，尸祝斋戒，然后用之。及祭之后，行者践其首脊，樵者焚其支体，
They are adorned with embroidered cases and silken cloth,
and the officiating priest, after fasting and purification,
then employs them.
After the sacrifice, travelers trample their heads and backs,
woodcutters burn their limbs—
the things are discarded not out of hatred or affection,
but simply because their use has passed.

且天地之於万物，圣人之於百姓，亦然。
Moreover, Heaven and Earth’s relation to all things,
and the Sage’s relation to the people,
is the same.

故天地无恩而大恩生，圣人不仁而大仁成。
Thus, Heaven and Earth, though without **virtue** (恩),
give rise to great **virtue** (恩).
The Sage, though not **virtue** (仁),
accomplishes great **virtue** (仁).

天地之间其犹弃钥乎。
Between Heaven and Earth, it is like the blowing of a **bellows** (橐) or the playing of a **flute** (钥).

唐明皇曰：橐者，韛也。钥者，笛也。
Emperor Xuanzong of the Tang said:
*"The **bellows** (橐) is the **pouch** (韛).
The **flute** (钥) is the **pipe** (笛).*

橐之鼓风，钥之运吹，皆以虚而无心，故能动而有应。
The bellows’ wind-stirring, the flute’s breath-playing,
both rely on emptiness and lack a heart—
thus they move and have response.

王元泽曰：橐钥虚以应物，物感则应，应而不藏。
Wang Yuanze said:
*"The bellows and flute are empty yet responsive to things;
when things are moved, they respond—
but they do not store the response."*

天地之於万物，圣人之於百姓，应其适然，而不系累於当时，不留情於既往，
Heaven and Earth respond to all things in their due measure,
without entangling themselves in the present moment,
without lingering emotionally in the past—

故比橐钥之无穷也。
thus likened to the boundless **bellows** (橐) and **flute** (钥).

道无方体，以冲和之气鼓动於覆载之问，而生养万物。
The Dao/Way has no fixed form—
with its **unbounded and harmonious qi** (冲和之气),
it stirs between the heavens and the earth,
giving birth and nurturing all things.

如橐以气化形，钥以气出声，
Like the bellows transforming qi into form,
or the flute letting qi issue forth as sound—
qi is empty yet awaits things,
and all that has form and sound
arises from this.
Thus likened to the **bellows** (橐) and **flute** (钥).

以喻天地圣人，亦以虚而无心故也。
This is to illustrate Heaven, Earth, and the Sage—
all because of their emptiness and lack of a fixed heart.

虚而不屈，动而愈出。
Empty yet never rigid,
moving yet ever more issuing forth.

王弼曰：橐钥之中空洞，无情无为，故虚而不能穷屈，动而不可竭尽也。
Wang Bi said:
*"The bellows and flute are hollow within,
without emotions or actions,
thus empty yet never exhausted—
moving yet never spent."*

王元泽曰：虚其体也，动其用也。多言数穷，不如守中。
Wang Yuanze said:
*"Empty in essence, active in function.
Excessive speech leads to depletion—
better to hold to the center."*

御注：慎汝内，闭汝外，收视反听，复以见天地之心，此之谓守中。
**Commentary**:
*"Be cautious within, close off without,
retract your gaze and reverse your hearing—
thus you may perceive the heart of Heaven and Earth.
This is called holding to the center."*

王弼曰：若不法天地之虚静，同橐钥之无心，动不从感，言不会机，
Wang Bi said:
*"If you do not follow Heaven and Earth’s **emptiness and tranquility** (虚静),
or align with the **bellows** (橐) and **flute** (钥)’s lack of a heart,
your movements do not arise from response,
your words do not accord with the Dao—

动与事乖，故曰数穷。
moving and affairs are mismatched—
thus it is said that depletion comes from excessive speech.

不如内怀道德，抱一不移，故曰守中。
Better to inwardly cherish **Dao/Way and virtue** (道德),
embracing the One without deviation—
thus it is called holding to the center."*

此章戒人多言，不如守内。
This passage warns against excessive speech—
better to hold within.

始言天地不仁，中以橐钥为况，终之以多言数穷者，
It begins by saying Heaven and Earth are not **virtue** (仁),
uses the **bellows** (橐) and **flute** (钥) as analogy in the middle,
and concludes with the warning that excessive speech leads to depletion—

欲人之出言，法天地之无心，如橐钥之虚中，
wishing that when people speak,
they follow Heaven and Earth’s lack of a fixed heart,
like the **bellows** (橐) and **flute** (钥)’s empty center—

因感而应，则无多言之失。
responding naturally to stimuli,
then there will be no fault of excessive speech.

与其言多而政祸，不如默默而守中。
Better to speak little and avoid misgovernance
than to speak much and invite calamity.

谷神章第六
谷神不死，

**Chapter Six: The Valley Spirit (谷神)**
The Valley Spirit does not die.

御注：有形则有盛衰，有数则有成败，形数具而生死分，物之理也。
**Commentary**:
*"Where there is form, there is flourishing and decline.
Where there is number, there is success and failure.
When form and number are complete, life and death are distinguished—
this is the nature of things."*

谷应群动而常虚，神妙万物而常寂，
The Valley responds to all movements yet remains ever empty,
the Spirit subtly transforms all things yet remains ever still—

真常之中，与道为一，不丽於形，不堕於数，
Within the **true and enduring** (真常),
it aligns with the Dao/Way as One,
not clinging to form,
not entangled in number—

生生而不穷。
endlessly giving birth.

如日月焉，终古不息。如维斗焉，终古不忒。
Like the sun and moon,
they never cease through all ages.
Like the **Pole Star** (维斗),
they never deviate through all ages.

故云不死。
Thus it is said that the Valley Spirit does not die.

河上公曰：谷，养也。人能养神则不死。
River Master Hu said:
*"The Valley (谷) is nurturing.
If a person can nurture the Spirit (神), then they do not die."*

神谓五脏之神，肝藏魂，肺藏魄，心藏神，肾藏精，脾藏志。
The Spirit (神) refers to the **vital essences of the Five Zang Organs** (五脏之神):
the liver stores the **hun** (魂),
the lungs store the **po** (魄),
the heart stores the **shen** (神),
the kidneys store the **jing** (精),
the spleen stores the **zhi** (志).

五脏尽伤，五神去矣。
When the Five Zang Organs are all injured,
the Five Spirits depart.

温公曰：中虚故曰谷。不测故曰神。
Duke Wen said:
*"Empty within, hence it is called the Valley (谷).
Unfathomable, hence it is called the Spirit (神).*

天地有穷而道无穷，故曰不死。
Heaven and Earth have limits,
but the Dao/Way is boundless—
thus it is said that the Valley Spirit does not die."*

王元泽曰：谷应而不穷，神化而不测，
Wang Yuanze said:
*"The Valley responds yet never exhausts,
the Spirit transforms yet remains unfathomable—
all things receive their mandate from it,
yet it has never been born or died.
This is the Valley Spirit.*

言神则极矣，而加谷者，
To speak of the Spirit is already to reach the utmost—
yet the Valley is added—

且言能虚能盈，而又能容以应也。
because it also speaks of the ability to be empty and full,
and to contain and respond.

以其活而不敌，故但称不死。
Since it is alive yet never opposed,
it is simply called the Way of not dying.

是谓玄牝。
This is called the **Dark Mother** (玄牝)."

御注：万物受命於无，而成形於有谷之用。
**Commentary**:
*"All things receive their mandate from the **None** (无),
and take form through the **Valley’s** (谷) nurturing function.*

无相，神之体；无方，万物所受命也。
The **None** (无) has no attributes—
this is the essence of the Spirit.
It has no direction—
this is where all things receive their mandate.

玄者天之色。牝者地之类。
The **Dark** (玄) is the hue of Heaven.
The **Mother** (牝) is the category of Earth.

万物所成形也，
All things take form from this—

谷神以况至道之常，玄牝以明造物之妙。
the Valley Spirit illustrates the Dao’s enduring nature,
while the Dark Mother reveals the marvel of creation."

王元泽曰：谷神受命而玄牝赋形，
Wang Yuanze said:
*"The Valley Spirit receives the mandate and the Dark Mother bestows form—
they themselves constitute **yin** (阴) and **yang** (阳),
thus completing Heaven and Earth.
Yet they are originally one thing.*

由其受命，故曰谷神。
Because of their receiving the mandate,
they are called the Valley Spirit.

由其赋形，故曰玄牝。
Because of their bestowing form,
they are called the Dark Mother."

曹道冲曰：玄者杳冥而藏神。牝者冲和而藏气。
Cao Daichong said:
*"The **Dark** (玄) is profound and mysterious yet stores the Spirit.
The **Mother** (牝) is **unbounded and harmonious** (冲和) yet stores the qi.
This is the origin of **yin** (阴) and **yang** (阳),
the ancestor of Heaven and Earth."*

谷，养也。谷虚而应，应而不着。谷虚而受，受而不积。
The Valley is nurturing.
Empty yet responsive,
responding yet not clinging.
Empty yet receptive,
receiving yet not accumulating.

言养神在於虚而已，
To nurture the Spirit is only to remain empty—

是谓不死之道，在於玄牝也。
this is called the Way of not dying,
found in the Dark Mother.

玄牝之门，是谓天地根。
The **gate of the Dark Mother** (玄牝之门) is called the **root of Heaven and Earth** (天地根).

御注：庄子曰：万物有乎生而莫见其根，有乎出而莫见其门。
**Commentary**:
*"Zhuangzi said:
‘All things have a place of birth yet no one sees their root;
they have a place of emergence yet no one sees their gate.
Only the Sage can see them.’*

故於此明言玄牝之门，是谓天地根。
Thus here it is made clear:
the **gate of the Dark Mother** (玄牝之门)
is the **root of Heaven and Earth** (天地根).

天地者，万物之上下也。物与天地，本无先后，
Heaven and Earth are the **up** and **down** of all things.
Things and Heaven and Earth originally have no precedence—

明大道之序，则有天地，然后有万物。
but when the Dao’s order is illuminated,
there are Heaven and Earth,
and then all things.

然天地之所从出者，玄牝是已。
Yet the origin from which Heaven and Earth emerge
is the Dark Mother.

彼先天地生者，孰得而见之。
Who can see the one that gave birth before Heaven and Earth?

河上公曰：根，元也。言鼻。
River Master Hu said:
*"The **root** (根) is the **primordial** (元)—
it refers to the **nostril** (鼻).*

之门，乃是通天地之元，气所从往来也."
The **gate** (之门) is where the primordial essence of Heaven and Earth passes—
the path through which the qi travels back and forth."*

---

Zhuǎn Wēi said:
*"Above, it speaks of the **immortal elixir of the Valley Spirit** (谷神不死),
urging people to nurture the spirit.
Here, it speaks of the **gate of the Dark Mother** (玄牝之门),
demonstrating the **art of refining the form** (炼形之术) to people.
Thus, when spirit and form are both perfected,
only then does one align with the Dao."*

Cáo Dàochōng said:
*"The **Dark** (玄) is the **substance of Heaven** (天之体).
The **Mother** (牝) is the **qi of Earth** (地之气).
The Dark Heaven’s **originative essence** (精光) gives rise to life—
the Dark Mother’s **nourishment** (育) brings forth all things as **form and vessel** (形器).
Though Heaven and Earth are vast,
they fundamentally stem from **virtue and power** (道德),
and manifest through **yin and yang** (阴阳),
hence the saying: **root of Heaven and Earth** (天地根).*

*"Endless as if it exists,
River Master Hu said: **the nostril** (鼻).
Inhaling and exhaling, breathing and sighing—
constantly subtle and endless.
If one can preserve it,
it is as if it has no form.
As if it does not exist.
Wang Yuanzé said:
*'**Endless** (绵绵) means drawing without ceasing.
The **Dark Mother** (神牝) endlessly gives birth—
yet it is as if it has or does not have,
defying fixed existence.
Using it without exhaustion—
The **Imperial Commentary** (御注) says:
*'From its own root and origin,
fixed since antiquity,
the transmission of the **Fire** (火) knows no end.
This is what is meant by **endless as if it exists** (绵绵若存).*
It is **vastly natural** (茫然天造),
letting the **single breath** (一气) move freely.
Swiftly, **Earth’s transformation** (地化) unfolds,
entrusting myriad forms to their own proliferation.

**Yin** (乾) is easily understood,
**Yang** (坤) is simple in its efficacy—
not achieved by force,
so what need for exhaustion?
River Master Hu said:
*'In using the qi,
one must be **expansive and relaxed** (宽舒),
not **hasty and laborious** (急疾勤劳).*
Sū Zǐyóu said:
*'**Endless** (绵绵) means subtle yet unceasing.
**As if it exists** (若存) means existing yet unseen.
If one can achieve this,
even using it all day long will not weary.
When the mind is **settled** (定) and breathing **subtle** (息微),
entrusting to **spontaneity** (自然而无使气之强),
what need for exhaustion?
This passage’s essence lies in **cultivating the spirit with emptiness of heart** (虚心养神),
so that **non-death** (不死) resides in the Dark Mother.
The Dark Mother is the **source of Heaven and Earth** (天地之宗),
the **ancestor of yin and yang** (阴阳之祖),
concealing the spirit and harboring the qi,
upon which all things depend to form and grow.
The principle of **formation and growth** (生成) comes endless,
unceasing and unexhausted—
using it brings no weariness,
yet there is **acquisition and fruition** (有得有成).
All other schools expound externally,
but only River Master Hu’s **inner teachings** (内说) align with true principle.
To uphold it internally is to uphold it externally—
both meanings are valid,
and both are recorded here."*

**Chapter Seven: Heaven Long, Earth Enduring** (天长地久)
*"Heaven and Earth, though vast,
remain bound to **form and number** (形数),
so their longevity is measured.
Sū Zǐyóu said:
*'Though Heaven and Earth are vast,
they are not free from form and number,
so their **long duration** (长久) is quantified.'*
Zhāng Jūnxiàng said:
*'**Yin** (乾) is firm and broad,
covering all ages without tipping.
**Yang** (坤) is yielding yet thickly supportive,
enduring through all transformations, steadfast and unyielding.
The **Supreme One’s** (太上) words state:
**Heaven and Earth’s** (天地) **longevity** (长久) **extends to their utmost** (极),
yet this is merely what people perceive.
If one speaks of **ultimate longevity** (长久之至),
then the **origin of Heaven and Earth** (所谓天地始) is what is meant.
Heaven and Earth endure long because they **do not generate themselves** (不自生),
hence they can **long generate** (长生).*
Wang Yuanzé said:
*'To generate oneself means to have a **self-generated** (有其生) origin—
if one has such an origin, then **generation is already lost** (生既丧矣).
Only when there is no **pattern of generation** (无以生为则) does generation never occur—
if it never occurs, then the **hosted form** (所寓之形) though generated
bears no **burden of generation** (无生之累),
thus it should be **long and enduring** (宜其长且久也).
Heaven and Earth’s **non-self-generation** (不自生) is not a **benefit** (利乎),
yet it results in **long duration** (长久),
for the Dao is thus.'*
Zhāng Jūnxiàng said:
*'All things **self-cultivate their own generation** (自营其生),
but Heaven and Earth merely know to **generate and nurture** (生育) all things,
not self-cultivating their own generation.
To **bestow generation without self-generation** (施生不自生),
thus they attain **long duration** (得长久).
Hence the **Sage** (圣人) **lays aside the self** (后其身) yet **the self comes first** (而身先),
**discards the self** (外其身) yet **the self endures** (而身存).*
The **Imperial Commentary** (御注) says:
*'All take the **first** (先),
yet the Sage alone takes the **last** (后)—
this is called **receiving the filth of Heaven and Earth** (受天下之垢),
hence **laying aside the self** (后其身).
By laying aside the self,
one does not contend with things,
yet none can contend against them.
Hence it says: **lay aside the self yet the self comes first** (后其身而身先).
In **muddy paths**, one does not compete for **ease and danger** (涂不争险易之利),
in **winter and summer**, one does not compete for **harmony of yin and yang** (阴阳之和),
**discarding death and life**, **abandoning calamity and fortune** (外死生遗祸福),
yet the spirit is **never constrained** (神未尝有所困).
This is called **discarding the self yet the self endures** (外其身而身存).'*
Mǎ Jùjì said:
*'The Sage aligns with Heaven and Earth,
so **laying aside the self** (后身) and **discarding the self** (外身)
is like **not self-generating** (不自生).
To **self-generate yet endure** (自先身存) is like **long generation** (长生).
**Laying aside the self** (后身) means **yielding to others** (屈己),
**discarding the self** (外身) means **forgetting the self** (忘我)—
yielding to others means one is **beneath all** (人下之),
hence the self comes first.
Forgetting the self means **discarding the self**,
hence the self endures.
Is it not because of **no selfishness** (无私)?
Thus one can **fulfill their selfishness** (成其私).
The **Imperial Commentary** (御注) says:
*'The **greatest virtue of Heaven and Earth** (大德) is **generation** (生).
The **greatest treasure of the Sage** (大宝) is **position** (位).
The **Dao** (道) **makes it universal** (为之公).
Heaven and Earth embody the Dao,
so they have no selfishness—
no selfishness, hence **long duration** (长久).
The Sage embodies the Dao,
so they have no selfishness—
no selfishness, hence **constant endurance** (长存).
To **self-cultivate is selfishness** (自营为私),
yet none have succeeded in fulfilling their selfishness.'*
Lǚ Jìfǔ said:
*'The **self** (身) is my **selfishness** (私).
To **lay aside the self** (后其身) and **discard the self** (外其身)
is to **make it universal** (公而无私).
No selfishness,
thus one can **fulfill their selfishness** (成其私).'*
Mǎ Jùjì said:
*'Heaven and Earth do not self-generate,
just as the Sage **lays aside and discards the self**—
both are **no selfishness** (无私).
Yet Heaven and Earth **long generate** (长生) through this,
while the Sage **long endures** (长存) through this—
both **fulfill their selfishness** (成其私).
Yet **not selfishness for selfishness is great** (不私之为私大矣).*
Zhuāngzi said:
*'No selfishness,
yet it is **selfishness** (乃私也).'*
This passage states:
*'The Dao **makes it universal** (为之公).
Heaven and Earth embody the Dao,
so they have no selfishness—
no selfishness, hence **long duration** (长久).
The Sage embodies the Dao,
so they have no selfishness—
no selfishness, hence **constant endurance** (长存).
To **self-cultivate is selfishness** (自营为私),
yet none have succeeded in fulfilling their selfishness.
Scholars who embody the Dao’s **universality** (体道之公),
not selfish toward **one’s own self** (不私一己),
also attain **long duration** (得其长久)."*

**The True Scripture of the Dao and Its Power: Compilation of the Good, Volume Two** (道德真经取善集卷之二)

**The True Scripture of the Dao and Its Power: Compilation of the Good, Volume Two**
**Chapter Eight: The Supreme Good Is Like Water**

**Master Li Lin of Song’s Residence in Rao Yang** collected:

The supreme good is like water.
Master Su Shi said:
*"Heaven first generates water—
the Dao’s movement manifests as the good,
just as qi moves to generate water.
Thus it is said: the supreme good is like water."*

Water embodies three virtues close to the Dao,
and seven benefits for all things.
The person of supreme good is like this—
the Dao’s true good,
not what all under Heaven recognize as good.

The good is the Dao’s continuation;
water leads the Five Phases,
yet remains close to the Dao—
for when one departs from the Dao, the name "good" is established.

The supreme good is like water—
this is the natural principle of things.
Water benefits all things yet does not contend,
dwelling where others abhor,
thus approaching the Dao.

**Zhuǎn Wēi says:**
This is the three virtues close to the Dao.

Water’s nature is nourishing,
benefiting all things—
this is the first virtue.
It takes any shape, filling vessels,
flooding or draining as needed—
this is the second virtue.
Others abhor lowliness,
yet water flows downward—
this is the third virtue.

When water benefits things, its virtue is vast and kind;
when it does not contend, its power is humble and bright;
when it dwells in what others abhor, its capacity endures filth.
The three virtues of water—
only the Perfect One embodies them as one.
Such virtue and conduct, so close to the Dao,
thus it is said: *near.*

**Residing in the good place,**
Master Ma Jùjì said:
The good resides in humility,
water flows downward—
in the good, one finds water’s place.
Thus it is said: *residing in the good place.*
Where water resides, the good is the land’s benefit.
Where the good person resides, transformation extends to the neighborhood.

**The good heart is deep,**
**御 Commentary:**
The deeper one probes, the deeper it becomes;
the farther one seeks, the more distant it grows.
Master Ma Jùjì said:
The good rests in tranquility,
water in depth and stillness—
in the good, one finds water’s depth.
Thus it is said: *the good heart is deep.*
Master Cáo Dàochōng said:
Depth is where water rests.
Though winds and waves arise,
none can disturb its stillness.
The Perfect One’s heart is likewise.

Master Wáng Yuānzé said:
Deep and still, yet level—
inner clarity, outer obscurity.
With a heart deep and still, the flow returns to unity.

**Giving is kind,**
**御 Commentary:**
Compassionate love without selfishness,
giving without discrimination.
Master Su Shi said:
Benefiting all things,
giving without seeking—
this is kind giving.
Master Ma Jùjì said:
The good aids others—this is giving;
water benefits all—this is kindness.
In the good, one finds water’s kindness.
Thus it is said: *giving is kind.*

Where water flows,
nothing fails to nourish and benefit all things,
bringing them to completion.
The person of supreme good,
like water’s giving,
broadly benefits the people,
extends kindness to all beings,
aids all sentient life,
and seeks no return.
Thus it is said: *giving is kind.*

**Words are trustworthy,**
**御 Commentary:**
Avoiding obstacles yet reaching the seas,
traversing dangers without losing sincerity.
Master Lù Tìng said:
Treading a thousand dangers without losing sincerity,
encountering ten thousand reversals yet never losing direction—
this is called *words are trustworthy.*

**Governance is skillful,**
**御 Commentary:**
It purifies the impure,
smooths the treacherous,
follows the natural order of things,
and harbors no scheming heart.
Thus all is governed.
Emperor Xuánzōng of Tang said:
Skillful governance is like water’s cleansing—
making things pure and clear.
Master Wáng Bì said:
The good in governance is without filth or bias,
like water’s governance—
most pure, most level.
Master Liú Zhòngpíng said:
Yet water itself has no speech of governance,
so why say: *words are trustworthy, governance is skillful?*
Because this passage discusses the good person as water,
thus extending to *words* and *governance* as well.

**Actions are capable,**
**御 Commentary:**
Following the land’s contours to form curves and straightness,
adapting to vessels to become round or square,
changing with circumstances yet ever level—
none but the capable could do this.
Master Su Shi said:
Encountering things and taking their form without clinging—
this is capability.
Water’s actions change without constancy,
adapting to where it flows,
round or square as the vessel demands,
never opposing things—
thus nothing is beyond its capability.
The Perfect One is likewise,
following people and things,
conforming to the world without resistance.
Thus it is said: *actions are capable.*

**Movement is timely,**
**御 Commentary:**
The sun releases it, and it flows;
the cold congeals it, and it freezes.
If directed east, it flows east;
if directed west, it flows west—
moving without constraint,
proper only to follow the times.
**Zhuǎn Wēi says:**
The Perfect One’s dwelling is in humility,
naturally transforming,
extending to the neighborhood—
like water on the land,
dwelling in lowliness,
nourishing all things.
Thus it is said: *residing in the good place*—this is the first good.

The Perfect One’s heart preserves stillness,
penetrates the subtle,
deep and clear—
like water’s depth, serene,
its currents shifting ninefold,
never losing clarity.
Thus it is said: *the good heart is deep*—this is the second good.

The Perfect One’s giving is kind,
extending mercy to all under Heaven,
without favoritism—
like water’s nourishment,
rising and falling without exclusion,
benefiting all it touches.
Thus it is said: *giving is kind*—this is the third good.

The Perfect One’s words are trustworthy,
guarding sincerity without expectation,
naturally harmonizing—
like water’s reflection,
beautiful or ugly without distinction,
filling and flowing without loss,
traversing dangers without failure.
Thus it is said: *words are trustworthy*—this is the fourth good.

The Perfect One’s governance is skillful,
bringing order to the people,
upright and perceptive—
things naturally comply.
Like water’s purity and levelness,
settling high and low,
cleansing all things,
leaving no filth.
Thus it is said: *governance is skillful*—this is the fifth good.

The Perfect One’s actions are capable,
adapting to things,
assigning roles as needed,
never failing.
Like water’s yielding nature,
accommodating round or square,
forming to the vessel—
nothing it attempts fails.
Thus it is said: *actions are capable*—this is the sixth good.

The Perfect One’s motion and stillness,
skilled in observing the times,
emerging and withdrawing in response to opportunity,
able to preserve the Dao—
like water’s movement,
skilled in adapting to change,
congealing in winter, melting in summer,
never deviating from its course.
Thus it is said: *motion, skilled in timing*—this is the seventh good.

To act with foresight and move at the right time,
to move only when the moment is ripe—
this is like water’s spring thaw and winter freeze.

Only the non-contending
thus has no blame.
Su Ziyou said:
One who does good yet cannot escape human censure
does so because of contention.
Water, by not contending,
possesses all seven goods without blame.

This chapter speaks of water’s nature—
benefiting all things,
gentle and weak, yet not contending,
ever dwelling in lowliness—
thus encompassing the three abilities and the seven goods without blame.
Who, if not the Perfect One, could emulate this?

**Chapter Nine: Holding and Filling**
To hold and fill,
is not as good as stopping early.
Lu Jifu said:
To forcefully hold and fill
does not necessarily make one rich.
Holding is meant to prevent overflow,
yet filling leads only to greater overflow.
If one wishes to avoid overflow,
one should avoid filling entirely.
*Holding* means to grasp and retain;
*filling* means to overflow;
*stopping* means to cease.
To grasp and retain while also filling
inevitably leads to collapse—
thus, it is better to stop early.

To probe and sharpen,
cannot be long preserved.
Wen Gong said:
To probe and understand others’ intentions,
then aggressively pursue them,
is bound to fail.
Lu Jifu said:
To measure and then act is to seek what is precious.
Probing is meant to avoid loss,
yet sharpening leads only to greater loss.
If one wishes to avoid loss,
one should avoid sharpening entirely.
*Probing* is skillful in measuring intentions;
*sharpening* is sharp in seizing opportunities.
Probing and sharpening,
pursuing honor and fame—
though one may gain them,
one is certain to lose them,
thus they cannot be long preserved.

Gold and jade filling the hall,
none can guard them.
Shu Wang said:
*A hall* is empty and receives things—
when filled with gold and jade,
it is overflowing.
Thus it cannot be guarded.
Ma Jiuji said:
A hall’s depths are meant to store gold and jade,
yet its harm lies in being filled.
If not by heavenly calamity,
then by human disaster.
This shows that overflow is difficult to long preserve.

To be full yet not overflow,
is how one long guards wealth.
To hold and fill,
then gold and jade fill the hall,
none can guard them.

Riches and nobility bring arrogance,
inviting one’s own ruin.
*Commentary*:
Gold, jade, riches, and nobility
are not the essence of life.
They are external things one cannot rely upon.
To treasure gold and jade
is to be burdened by things.
Can one burdened by things avoid loss?
Thus none can guard them.
Riches and nobility bring arrogance,
harming one’s virtue.
Can one who harms virtue escape misfortune?
Thus one invites ruin upon oneself.

The Sage does not cling to worldly gain
as personal possession,
nor does He rule the world
for His own display.
What need has He for gold and jade as treasures,
or riches and nobility as burdens?
Thus the most wealthy nation’s treasures are shared,
the most noble nation’s honors are shared—
its nobility is unmatched,
its wealth unparalleled,
yet the Dao remains unchanged.
He Shang Gong said:
Wealth should relieve poverty,
nobility should pity the lowly—
yet if one instead grows arrogant,
calamity will follow.
Shu Wang said:
Riches and nobility do not invite arrogance,
yet arrogance comes naturally—
thus one invites ruin and misfortune.
This shows that sharpening cannot be probed.

To be high yet not perilous,
is how one long guards nobility.
To probe and sharpen,
then riches and nobility breed arrogance,
bringing ruin upon oneself.

When achievement is complete and fame is fulfilled,
withdrawal is the Way of Heaven.
Su Ziyou said:
The sun moves at noon,
the moon wanes when full—
the cycles of the four seasons,
those who achieve must depart.
Heaven and Earth do so—
how much more so for humanity.
*Achievement* is the benefit to others;
*fame* is the reputation applied to oneself.
When achievement benefits others and is fulfilled,
when fame is applied to oneself and is complete,
to withdraw and avoid position,
to forever avoid calamity—
this is the constant Way of Heaven.
This chapter warns:
Wealth should not be filled to overflowing,
nobility should not be indulged.
To hold and fill is not as good as stopping early;
to probe and sharpen only leads to defeat.
Gold and jade, when filled, are hard to guard;
riches and nobility, when indulged, invite ruin;
achievement complete, yet not held—
fame fulfilled, yet not clung to—
to know that loss is gain,
is to align with the Way of Heaven.

**Chapter Ten: Nurturing Vital Essence and Spirit**
*Nurturing vital essence and spirit*—
Wang Yuanzhe said:
*The spirit* is the essence of yin’s form.
The nature of spirit is vast and unbound,
yet it does not realize itself—
spirit always depends on vital essence,
thus the vast becomes constrained,
the unbound becomes obstructed.
Those who seek this Way
must first expand their will and qi,
not burdening themselves with form,
so that spirit rides vital essence
without being ridden by it—
then one may embrace unity and embody the spirit.

Ma Jiuji said:
Of a person’s life,
the yang aspects form the *hun* (spirit-soul),
the yin aspects form the *po* (vital essence-corpsesoul),
stored between the liver and lungs.
Precision and spirit interdependently generate life,
while vital essence governs their inflow and outflow.
The *po* follows spirit in its movements,
the *hun* follows precision in its transformations.
This passage speaks of *nurturing vital essence*—
*vital essence* is the same as *hun*.
*The Inner Observation Classic* says:
Movement that nurtures the body is called *hun*.
The *hun* governs all bodily functions,
driving the body’s operations—
as *hun*, it governs the inflow and outflow of precision,
mastering transformation and change.
Now, the hundred bones and nine orifices,
all that composes our form,
are the domain of vital essence.
Without the *hun* to nurture them,
how would they differ from a walking corpse?
Vital essence cannot exist without *hun*,
just as the moon cannot exist without the sun.
Vital essence depends on *hun* to function,
the moon depends on the sun to shine—
this is why *hun* is called *nurturing*,
and vital essence is called *vital essence*.
*Nurturing* means to embody in form.
Past interpretations all say *nurturing* is *hun*,
yet Wang Yuanzhe’s commentary uniquely defines *nurturing* as *po*.

One interpretation says *zài* means the form carries *hun* and *po*.
Another says the spirit carries *po*.
How can one embrace unity without separation?

King Shū of Chu replied: *"One"* refers to *jīng* (vital essence).
When *hun* and *po* are complete, *jīng* arises;
when *jīng* arises, the spirit follows.

Wang Yuanzhe said: *"One"* is the measure of *jīng*, but instead of saying *jīng*, it speaks of *"one"*—for guarding *"one"* prevents *jīng* from dispersing.

Mǎ Jùjì said: *Yíng* and *po* are distinct matters, differing only in embracing *"one."*
The Dao generates *"one,"*
*"one"* generates water,
water generates *jīng*—
for *jīng* is the substance of *"one."*
Embrace *"one,"* and it unites with *jīng*;
abandon *"one,"* and it separates from *jīng*.
When *jīng* is complete, *qi* is whole;
when *qi* is whole, the spirit is whole—
and with the spirit whole, one masters *hun* and *po* as a superior adept.

*Jīng* is the source by which heaven, earth, and all things are generated.
*Yíng* means "to stop"—
*po* is yin.
The form’s master abides within the form and has a place to rest,
so when speaking of *yíng-po* carrying, it means the spirit carries *po*.
If there is no spirit to carry it,
then it stagnates in dark yin,
the form scatters, the spirit departs,
and it decays with all things below.
But if the spirit constantly carries *po* without being carried by it,
then the refined yang-spirit consumes the yin-*po*,
and the body transforms into the Dao.

The matter lies in embracing *"one"* without leaving *"one"*—that is *jīng*.
Embrace *"one,"* and *jīng* unites with the spirit without separation,
then gather the spirit with *jīng*,
direct the form with the spirit,
preserve the spirit with the form—
when the three merge into one,
the Dao is complete.
Those who seek this Way must take *jīng* as the foundation.
Zhuangzi said:
*"Not departing from jīng"* is called the *shénrén* (spirit-person)—
this teaches nurturing *jīng*.

Can you concentrate *qi* and achieve softness like an infant?
Zhuǎn Wēi commented:
*"Speaking of those who practice concentrating qi and achieving softness,
they should be as pure and harmonious as an infant."*
If one can be as pure and harmonious as an infant,
then they have attained the art of concentrating *qi* and achieving softness.

When *jīng* is complete, the spirit reigns;
when the spirit reigns, it can command *qi*.
With the spirit concentrated over *qi*,
joy, anger, sorrow, and pleasure do not control it—
this is how softness is achieved.
*"Concentrated"* means possessing and wielding authority;
*"soft"* means harmonious without violence.
When *qi* achieves softness,
it should be like the heart of an infant.
Desires unarisen, intent focused, mind unified—
throughout the day, one breathes deeply without strain,
until the throat relaxes in perfect harmony—
this teaches nurturing *qi*.

Can you purify and refine without blemish?
Mǎ Jùjì said:
This chapter establishes nurturing *jīng*, *qi*, and spirit as the root of Daoist practice,
so it speaks of *"no separation"* for nurturing *jīng*,
*"like an infant"* for nurturing *qi*,
and *"without blemish"* for nurturing the spirit.
*"Purify and refine"* refers to the heart.
*"Purify"* means washing the heart;
*"refine"* means carving it.
Wash it, and it becomes utterly still;
carve it, and it becomes utterly empty.
When the heart is empty and still,
no blemish remains.
Zhuangzi said:
*"Pure and unmixed, still and unchanging"*—
this teaches nurturing the spirit.

Can you govern the people and rule the state without action?
Hé Shànggōng said:
*"To govern the body, love qi and the body will be whole.
To govern the state, love the people and the state will be secure.
To govern the body, inhale and exhale *jīng-qi* without letting it be heard.
To govern the state, bestow virtue and kindness without letting the people know."*
This further explains the principle of embracing *"one."*

The *Inner Alchemy Classic* states:
*"The sage takes the body as the state,
the heart as the sovereign,
and *jīng-qi* as the people.
When the people are content, the state is mighty."*
*Jīng* is the foundation of the body;
cherishing and conserving *jīng-qi* brings order to the body.
The way of cherishing *jīng* is rooted in embracing *"one,"*
which is the natural Way—
what more is there to do?
Thus it is said: *"Can you act without action?"*

Now, those who refine *jīng* may pursue miscellaneous techniques,
treating *bān-yùn* (circulation) as their achievement,
and engage in excessive actions—
hence the warning against *"non-action."*
Zhuangzi said:
*"Only through non-action can the few be preserved."*

All other commentaries focus externally,
but Hé Shànggōng explains both inner and outer meanings.
Referring back to the earlier text,
those who speak of the body align with the Classic.

Can the opening and closing of the *tiānmén* (celestial gate) achieve receptivity?
Wang Yuanzhe said:
*"The perfected one has no intention in cultivating spirit and breath;
their responses are natural, without artificial prompting."*
This further explains concentrating *qi*.

There is fear that practitioners of *qi*-refinement may force *qi* too strongly,
so it is also warned against *"guarding the feminine"*—
for *"feminine"* means achieving softness.

Can one be enlightened and all-pervading without knowledge?
The commentary states:
*"Wisdom and sagacity, guarded by simplicity."*
This further explains *"without blemish."*
Practitioners of spirit-refinement,
pure and unadulterated, unobstructed and clear,
should not rely on knowledge—
instead, they must guard simplicity,
hence the warning against *"without knowledge."*

Nurture and preserve—
generate without possession,
act without reliance,
nourish without domination—
this is called *xiāndé* (mysterious virtue).
The commentary states:
*"The sage preserves the spirit and knows transformation,
harmonizing with the Dao as one body,
thus matching the divine in nurturing all things—
nothing is impossible.
Nurture them to fulfill their nature,
preserve them to perfect their nourishment.
Without the heart of love or gain,
hence generate without possession.
Without arrogance or self-aggrandizement,
hence act without reliance.
Without artificial constraint,
hence nourish without domination.
Such virtue is profound and distant,
contrary to all things—
thus it is called *xiāndé*."*

This chapter establishes nurturing *jīng*, *qi*, and spirit as the root of Daoist practice—
when the three merge into two,
the Dao is complete.

---
**Chapter Eleven: The Thirty Spokes**

Thirty spokes converge at one hub;
when the hub is empty,
the utility of the carriage arises.
Clay is molded into vessels;
when the vessel is empty,
its utility arises.
Doors and windows are carved for a room;
when the room is empty,
its utility arises.

The commentary states:
*"The unity of having and not-having,
the utility of entry and exit—
this is called the utmost spirit.
Having and not-having are contrasting forms;
their essence lies in *yǒu* (having) as substance,
and *wú* (not-having) as function—
the operation of yin and yang,
the principle of all things."*

**Chapter Eleven: The Thirty Spokes**

Thirty spokes share one hub;
when the hub is void,
the carriage’s function arises.
Clay is shaped into vessels;
when the vessel is void,
its function arises.
Doors and windows are carved for a room;
when the room is void,
its function arises.

The commentary states:
*"The marvelous function emerges from the utmost void,
transformation concealed in unburdened stillness—
like a mirror without image,
yet reflecting all things when they appear.
The sage employs the heart,
ever resolving in this principle.
Thus, having is used for utility,
voidness for function.*

*Zhong Hui says: "The three matters above illustrate that having and voidness mutually depend and neither can be neglected.
Thus, having is used for utility—
utility lies in substance.
Voidness is used for function—
function lies in emptiness.
Therefore, substance is external,
utility relies on emptiness to achieve completion.
Emptiness is internal,
function borrows substance for its manifestation.
But utility and voidness depend on each other—
none can be discarded.
Voidness depends on having for utility,
having borrows voidness for function—
the two principles interrelate.
Che Hui’s commentary: *‘To cultivate the self,
one must use substance to nourish emptiness,
and emptiness to guide substance.
Substance and emptiness mutually support the heart,
preventing it from leaning toward either—
thus attaining the sage’s utility and function.’*

*This chapter elucidates the interdependence of having and voidness,
utility and function,
using three matters to reveal the Great Dao.
The wheels and hub form the carriage,
clay shapes the vessel,
doors and windows define the room—
this is having,
which people depend on for utility.
The hub’s void allows the wheels to turn,
the vessel’s void holds things,
the room’s void shelters people—
this is voidness,
which people depend on for function.
Having brings real utility,
voidness must be employed for function.
Voidness is subtle,
function requires substance as its basis.
Having and voidness mutually depend,
just as form and spirit rely on each other
and cannot be neglected in isolation.
Spirit dominates form,
form houses spirit—
when form and spirit harmonize,
they mutually generate and perfect each other.
The ignorant in this world:
Those who refine spirit are obscured by voidness,
those who nourish form are drowned in having—
both lose their way.
Yet they fail to understand
that this chapter uses three objects as metaphors
to illustrate how having and voidness generate each other.
If one seeks to learn the Dao,
and practices according to this,
then form and spirit both attain perfection,
and align with the true Dao."*

---

**Chapter Twelve: The Five Colors**

The five colors blind the eyes,
the five tones deaf the ears—
*The commentary states: "Even if one indulges the ears and eyes in sounds and colors,
though possessing the forms of ears and eyes,
they cannot see or hear themselves—
what difference is there from blindness and deafness?"*

The five flavors dull the palate—
*Wang Yuanze says: "Man is born in stillness,
yet moves with things.
The ears and eyes are inherently subtle and refined,
but when sounds and colors confront them,
true perception is lost to illusion and decay.
The palate’s relation to flavor is no different."*

Chasing and hunting drive the mind to madness—
*Lu Jifu says: "When nothing can perturb the mind,
stillness brings sanctity.
To pursue externals is to lose all thought,
and thus the mind descends into madness.
How much more so with reckless hunting and chasing?
The *Taikang* [of the *Yuejiu*] speaks of the Five Sons in blame,
and Ai Gong’s love of hunting is censured by the poets.
To indulge in external excess—
hunting without measure—
is to stray from true nature.
What is this but madness?"*

Rare treasures obstruct one’s path—
*The commentary states: "To covet rare treasures
is to reach for what endangers life itself.
Greed for wealth and honor—
how can one’s path remain steadfast,
when such things ensnare the heart?
