Thus, Zhonghuai praised Tang [of Shang] with: ‘He did not accumulate wealth and gain.’
And Confucius told Zigong: ‘You do not await orders yet engage in commerce.’
Wealth’s obstruction of the path is evident."*

The moon’s gem and night’s jade—
mere tools for harm.
*Mencius says: "Jewels and jade
are sure to bring misfortune to oneself."
Thus, the sage nourishes the belly, not the eyes—
*Zhong Hui says: "True qi is internally abundant,
hence ‘nourishes the belly.’
External desires are discarded,
hence ‘does not indulge the eyes.’*
The five tones deaf the ears,
so the sage nourishes the belly, not the eyes—
all analogies follow this rule.
To nourish the belly is to preserve spirit and qi,
to not indulge the eyes is to abandon the six passions
and let the eyes not gaze wantonly.
Thus, discard that and take this—
*River Above Master says: "Discard the eyes’ wanton gazing,
take the belly’s nourishment of spirit.
The eyes govern external vision,
the belly governs internal containment.
The sage fills the belly,
not letting ears and eyes enslave the heart—
thus discarding the external-seeing eyes,
and taking the receptive belly.
Though the sage purifies the six senses,
here he speaks only of ‘not indulging the eyes’—
for once the eyes are controlled,
the rest may be inferred."*

*This chapter speaks of the harm of the five defilements,
to forget things and preserve true nature—
the sage’s principle of discarding and taking is thus revealed."*

---

**Chapter Thirteen: Honor and Humiliation**

Honor and humiliation are like sudden shock—
*Shu Wang says: "Honor becomes humiliation
because it is like sudden shock."*
*Ma Jiuji says: "Honor is the root of humiliation,
for it binds the heart to gain and loss.
If one is seen as honored by others,
the greater the shock—
honor is but another form of humiliation."*

Great calamity is like being burdened by one’s own body—
*The commentary states: "Honor is lower, nobility is higher.
To dwell in honor and take it as glory
is to invite humiliation.
To hold nobility yet see it as a burden
is to court the greatest calamity.
Nobility means exalted rank.
When the heart clings to nobility,
its affliction is great indeed.
Like a body that moves and strains itself—
what is honor and humiliation?
Honor is the lesser."*

*The commentary states: "To crave fame or covet wealth,
knowing how to advance yet not retreat,
knowing how to gain yet not lose—
to receive honor from others
is to embrace the path of inferiority.
Why does honor become humiliation?
Because receiving honor from others
places the power of gain and loss in their hands.
If one’s heart is weighed down by such gains and losses,
how can it remain unclouded?"*

Ziwen served three times and resigned three times,
without joy or displeasure in his face—
*Was there any humiliation for him?*
To gain is like shock,
to lose is like shock—
this is called honor and humiliation as sudden shock.*

Wen Gong said:
*"For the scholar, virtue and the Dao take precedence,
while rank and emolument are secondary.
The former brings honor,
the latter brings humiliation.
The common crowd delights in their humiliation,
yet trembles when they grasp it,
and weeps when they lose it.
To possess it is as if it were already theirs,
to lose it is as if it never existed—
how could there be any shock?
If, at the moment of gain or loss,
one’s heart still trembles with shock,
then this is true humiliation.

Liu Xia Hui served as a magistrate,
thrice dismissed yet never departing,
while Zheng Kao Fu received three summons
yet fled along the walls—
these are different indeed.

What is meant by *‘treating great calamity as one’s own body’*?
*The commentary states:*
To wield power and authority,
to bestow rewards and punishments at will,
to create blessings and instill awe—
when the world fears you like a deity,
when they revere you like the Supreme—
this is true nobility.
Yet the Sage does not burden himself with such nobility,
and thus can long preserve it without exhaustion.
It is like a man who, when his limbs are severed,
when his senses are dulled,
when he is stripped of form and intelligence,
becomes one with the boundless—
then nothing enters that he does not find himself within.

The people of the world exchange their nature for things,
and thus are burdened by things yet cannot forget their power.
They bind their form to their heart,
and thus lose their heart yet cannot forget their form.
Their calamity is great indeed.

Wang Yuanze said:
*Nobility lies above things,
yet those who possess kingdoms and families cannot forget them—
this is a great calamity.
It is like a man who treasures his body yet is burdened by it—
then cold, heat, sickness, and pain all assail him—
is this not a great calamity?
My greatest calamity is that I have a body.*

He Shang Gong said:
*My greatest calamity is that I have a body.
Having a body means fearing its movement and labor,
pondering hunger and cold,
indulging emotions and desires—
then misfortune befalls.
The body is a calamity,
for I cling to it—
this is a great calamity.
But when I have no body,
what calamity remains?*

Cai Zi Huang said:
*‘No body’ means not regarding the body as a self.
In this, one forgets the interplay of creation and self,
and thus transcends calamity.
This is not the destruction of the body to achieve ‘no body’—
rather, though the body exists, one does not cling to it,
and thus, in having it, one is without it.
As with Confucius’ mother,
when she mourned her son Ziqi—
this is the case.

Thus, to hold nobility for the world
is to entrust the world to it.
To love the world with one’s body
is to commit the world to it.
*The commentary states:*
*The world is a sacred vessel—
without the Dao, it cannot function.
The world is a divine instrument—
without the Dao, it cannot be preserved.
The Sage embodies the Dao,
and thus can govern the world with ease,
while the world rejoices in its support without exhaustion.
The next best course is to know one’s own nobility
and not degrade oneself,
serving things instead—
this is how one may entrust the world to them.
To know one’s love for life
and not harm oneself,
suffering under things instead—
this is how one may commit the world to them.

Thus it is said:
*The true Dao governs the self,
its overflow extends to the state and the world.
The upright govern the world.*

Yet the vulgar gentlemen of the world
endanger their bodies and abandon their lives
to serve things—
is this not tragic?

Wang Bi said:
*Without things to exchange for one’s self,
this is nobility.
Without things to diminish one’s self,
this is love.
Only thus can one entrust the world to them.
If one does not use honor and humiliation,
calamity and joy,
to exchange or diminish one’s self,
then only then can the world be entrusted to them.

The noble do not humiliate their bodies,
those who love do not endanger their bodies—
only thus can the world be entrusted to them.
Men like Zizhou’s Zhi Fu and Wang Zi Sou are examples of this.
These two did not glory in their honor
nor burden themselves with nobility.

This chapter speaks of humiliation arising from honor,
forgetting honor means no humiliation.
The body is the root of exhaustion,
forgetting the body means no calamity.
Having forgotten the burdens of honor and nobility,
one severs the worries of shock and calamity,
and thus, in treasuring and loving one’s body,
one can entrust the world to it.*

*End of Volume Two of the Collected Virtues of the Dao and Virtue Classic.
Begin Volume Three of the Collected Virtues of the Dao and Virtue Classic.*

**Chapter Fourteen: The Unseen**

That which cannot be seen is called *Yi*.
*Commentary*: The eyes govern vision, and vision discerns objects. *Yi* is smooth and without distinction—beyond what vision can reach, hence called *Yi*.
The *Taiyi* was unseen in its primordial *qi*—thus it is.

Master Wang Yuanze said: The Dao extends to all things, equal and without high or low—beyond what the eyes can see. This illuminates the Dao.
*Yi* means "smooth and effortless."
The Dao is not a color, so it cannot be seen.
Though unseen, it manifests as dark and as yellow—it cannot be called without color, only *Yi*.

That which cannot be heard is called *Xi*.
*Commentary*: The ears govern hearing, and hearing discerns sounds. *Xi* is subtle yet audible—beyond what hearing can grasp, hence called *Xi*.
The Great Sound is subtle in its voice—thus it is.
Zhuangzi said: What can be heard has a name and a sound.
The Dao has no sound—beyond what the ears can hear, hence called *Xi*.
Though unheard, it resonates with *gong* and *shang*—it cannot be called without sound, only *Xi*.

That which cannot be grasped is called *Wei*.
*Commentary*: So subtle, so formless—who can grasp it?
The Great Image is without form—thus it is.
Formless is *Wei*.
*Bó* means "to hold or seize."
The Dao is without form, so it cannot be seized.
Though ungraspable, it manifests as yin and yang, soft and hard, short and long, round and square, hot and cold, light and heavy, bitter and sweet—within the formless, it forms. Hence it is called *Wei*.

These three cannot be summoned or questioned,
so they merge into one.
*Commentary*: The *Taiyi* was undifferentiated—no division of high or low.
The Great Sound is subtle in its voice—no distinction of clear or turbid.
The Great Image is without form—no measure of great or small.
The eyes have no use for their brightness,
the ears no need for their sharpness,
the body no exhaustion in its effort—
the Dao’s completeness lies here,
in its depth beyond exploration,
its mystery beyond attainment.
*Qi*, form, and substance coexist yet do not separate,
so they merge into one.

Though merged into one, can it not be spoken of?
The three refer to *Yi*, *Xi*, and *Wei*.
What cannot be summoned or questioned
refers to the formless, soundless, shapeless—
the mouth cannot speak it,
the script cannot transmit it.
Receive it in stillness,
seek it with spirit—
it cannot be questioned and attained.
*Hun* means "to merge."
The three names merge into one,
for they are originally one body,
yet people name them according to their approach—
sometimes seeing, sometimes hearing, sometimes grasping.

Its upper reaches are not bright,
its lower depths are not dark.
Master Wang Yuanze said: All things, having substance, possess yin and yang—
the upper bright, the lower dark—this is inevitable.
Only the Dao, without substance, merges and becomes whole.
This speaks of the Dao’s fixed nature.
Zhuangzi said: Those who lose the Dao
see light above and become earth below.
*Shéngshéng*—ineffable, returning to nothingness.
Master Lü Jipu said: *Shéngshéng*—straight and trustworthy.
Though trustworthy, it cannot be named,
so it returns to nothingness.
This is called the "form of no-form,
the image of no-image"—
this is called *hūhuǎng*.
Master Zuan Wei said: To return to nothingness is not to speak of emptiness or stillness.
Within the formless, it shapes all forms;
within the imageless, it transforms all images.
To say it is "having" yet see no form—
this is having yet not having.
To say it is "not having" yet things arise from it—
this is not having yet having.
*Huǎng* is the doubt of having in non-having,
*hū* is the doubt of non-having in having.
The Dao, as a thing, is neither having nor non-having—
its name cannot be fixed, hence *hūhuǎng*.

Approach it, and you see no head;
follow it, and you see no tail.
*Commentary*: Its beginning has no head,
its end has no tail—
so approaching or following,
there is what cannot be seen.
Master Su Shiru said: The Dao is everywhere—
no front or back to be seen.
Hold the ancient Dao
to govern the present.
Master Wang Bi said: Though ancient and present differ,
the Dao remains constant.
Only by holding it can one govern things.
Master Lü Jipu said: Without front or back,
it is neither ancient nor present.
Though neither ancient nor present,
it is never without ancient or present.
Thus I say: Hold the ancient Dao
to govern the present.
To know the ancient beginning
is called the "Dao’s measure."
Master Lü Jipu said: The "ancient" is not different from the "present"—
to know the origin of the ancient.
The "present" is not different from the "ancient"—
to know the source of the present.
If one truly knows the origin of the ancient,
one knows the source of the present.
The beginning has no origin,
the source has no coming—
this is called the "measure of no-end."
The measure of no-end is the Dao’s measure.
The Dao cannot be held.
Yet by attaining this, one can hold it
as *virtue*.
To hold *virtue* is called "measure."
Master Wang Yuanze said: Pushing upward to the origin of nothingness,
one knows things have no source.
The Dao’s essence is attained—
the Dao’s measure,
the unchanging essence of ancient and present—
this is what Zhuangzi called the "measure of no-end."
Master Li Tian said: This chapter speaks of the wondrous root’s application—
what is there that it does not encompass?
If sought through seeing or hearing,
it is utterly unattainable.
If examined for brightness or darkness,
it is utterly undivided.
Images cannot be named,
beginnings and ends cannot be seen—
its use is difficult to fathom,
yet it governs all things.

**Chapter Fifteen: The Ancient Sage-Scholar**

The ancient sage-scholars were subtle, profound, and penetrating,
deep beyond recognition.

**Shu Wang said:** The term "sage-scholar" (*shànwéi-sì*) denotes one who practices the Dao/Way. At its beginning, when one is still a sage-scholar, one has not yet departed from practicing the Dao/Way. At its end, when one becomes a sage, one is unified with the Dao/Way—so much so that the Dao/Way cannot be spoken of separately, for it is one with the Dao/Way. This is what is meant by "subtle, profound, and penetrating, deep beyond recognition."

**Ling Xian said:** The Dao/Way encompasses all names—this is what it means to be "good." The practice of the Dao/Way reaches all things—this is what it means to be a "sage-scholar." The previous chapter grasped antiquity to govern the present; this chapter governs the present while drawing upon antiquity. The previous chapter discussed the Dao/Way’s complete essence; this chapter speaks of the sage-scholar’s embodiment of the Dao/Way.

"Subtle" (*wēi*) is the opposite of "manifest"; "profound" (*miào*) is the opposite of "dangerous." "Profound" implies depths beyond measure; "penetrating" (*tōng*) implies endless transformation. The ancient sage-scholars who embodied the Dao/Way possessed this Way yet concealed it in mystery beyond measure—who could truly recognize them? Hence it is said, "deep beyond recognition."

Only because they are beyond recognition does one forcibly attribute form to them. **Commentary:** The heavens’ height cannot be hastily measured; the earth’s depth cannot be hastily fathomed. When it says, "round to cover, square to bear," it merely approximates their form. To forcibly attribute form to them—how could one truly grasp their ultimate essence? This refers to the following text: Only because the Dao/Way is vast and difficult to recognize does one forcibly attribute form to it.

Like crossing a river in winter,
or fearing one’s neighbors;
**Master Lü Ji said:** The butcher Ding dissects an ox with ease, yet remains cautious—his gaze halts, his movements deliberate, the knife’s strokes barely perceptible. This is what is meant by "like crossing a river in winter." Caution arises at the outset of action. Once the task is done, the pieces lie scattered like earth; he stands with his knife, gazes around, and sighs with satisfaction, then sheathes his blade. This is what is meant by "like fearing one’s neighbors." "Like" implies careful restraint at the conclusion of action. Caution is rooted in preemptive stillness; "like" is rooted in lingering doubt after completion. "Crossing" refers to movement above the knees; "river" refers to the convergence of many streams. To cross a river is to encounter difficulty. In winter, the cold is extreme—yet to wade through a great river alone is to demonstrate that the ancient sage-scholars disliked action, only undertaking it when necessity demanded. They were always pressed into action, yet ever cautious in their approach. Hence it is said, "like crossing a river in winter"—once the crossing is made, there is no further hesitation. Yet when it comes to restraint, they are like the ancient sage-scholars, who were ever still and deep, never leaving their true nature. They always acted as if fearing their neighbors, gathering themselves without excess, repelling evil while preserving sincerity. No external things could probe them, for their hearts never strayed—hence it is said, "like fearing one’s neighbors."

Like a guest’s solemnity;
**Master Wang Yuanze said:** They did not take action as their own responsibility, so their bearing was tranquil. **Zhuàn Wēi said:** A sage-scholar with the Dao/Way stands solemnly upright, his mind unscattered, as if a guest facing his host—how could he dare to be hasty? When the term "container" (*róng*) is used, it is like Dongguo Shunzi’s composed demeanor, which made others’ intentions fade. Hence, Tianzi Fang revered him as a teacher. When the term "guest" (*kè*) is used, it is like Li Contained-Light residing in a darkened chamber, as if facing his lord and father—hence, Sima Zimeng praised him so highly.

Like ice about to melt;
**Commentary:** Water congeals to become ice; ice melts to return to water—they are fundamentally one. Those who cling to one-sidedness are like water congealing. Those who align with the great harmony are like ice melting. The *Yì* says: "Melt" (*huàn*) is "separation"—separating from others without attachment, hence it is called "melt." The *Sayings* say: "Congealed yet dispersed is 'melt.'" Water has no inherent ice; when it encounters cold, it congeals. Nature has no inherent obstruction; when it encounters things, it binds. A sage-scholar with the Dao/Way suddenly awakens to great insight—all affairs vanish, like spring ice melting away.

Like the solid yet simple;
**Commentary:** "Solid" (*dūn*) means the utmost thickness. Nature is fundamentally thick, like uncarved wood, before it disperses into vessels. This describes nature’s completeness. The *Classic* says: "Return to the uncarved."

Like the vast yet hollow;
**Commentary:** "Vast" (*kuàng*) means the utmost breadth. Nature is originally boundless, like a valley’s emptiness, receptive yet responsive. This describes nature’s hollowness. The *Classic* says: "The highest *virtue/power* is like a valley."

Like the turbid yet undifferentiated;
**Master Wang Bi said:** Concealing essence, hiding radiance—externally, they do not differ from things. They blend with dust and waves, hence it is said, "like the turbid." **Master Yangzi said:** "Unified, they are turbid; separated, they disperse." To be turbid is to blend with the world without separation, hence it is said, "like the turbid." This is what Zhuangzi called "aligning with things and flowing with their waves," differing from those who rigidly adhere to principles and reject worldly customs.

Thus, the sage-scholars who embody the Dao/Way: in winter, they dislike action, yet fear their neighbors and guard against later dangers; like guests who do not act as hosts; like ice that melts without obstruction; solid yet complete, vast yet hollow, turbid yet undistinguished by worldly norms—this is what is meant by "deep beyond recognition," yet forcibly attributing form to them.

Who could make the turbid clear?
Stillness gradually purifies.
**Master Wang Yuanze said:** To clarify nature is like clarifying water. Overworking makes it murkier with motion; only by waiting in stillness does it gradually clear itself. A sage-scholar with the Dao/Way is never made turbid by things, for his stillness gradually purifies.

**Master Cao Daochong said:** Would one follow the flow and forget to return? One merely clarifies stillness to gradually purify.

Who could make the settled endure?
Motion gradually arises.
**Commentary:** A sage-scholar with the Dao/Way is both settled and in motion—when he acts, he does so with ease, yet remains rooted in his original nature. He is fixed yet able to respond to all things; he is empty yet fulfills all demands. Hence, stillness gradually purifies, and things cannot make him turbid. Motion gradually arises, yet things cannot unsettle him. The *Yì* says: "Coming gradually" (*lái xúxú*)—gradualness implies ease and self-adaptation. The ancient sage-scholars’ use of their hearts is not to stop with stillness as good, but to have stillness as their intention. Nor is it to generate motion as their achievement, but to have motion as their purpose. They follow their inherent nature, entrusting it to its own course, without urgency or haste.

Thus it is said: When stillness gradually purifies, ten thousand things cannot perturb his heart—who could make it turbid? When motion gradually arises, ten thousand things cannot ensnare his thoughts—who could unsettle him? If one seeks stillness with the intent to stop, he will be bound by things, and thus will stop. How then could he respond to things without harm? Those who preserve the Dao/Way do not desire fullness.

Master Ma Juji said: Those who preserve without desiring fullness do so because the Dao/Way gathers in emptiness. Fullness, when it exists, is easily discerned. Only the unfull can—

Master Lü Jifu said: Though it is not called emptiness but always fullness, it is to prevent people from being burdened by emptiness. If one is burdened by emptiness, then it is not emptiness. Hence it is said: *Some* do not desire fullness, yet do not desire fullness. Thus they avoid exhaustion while remaining unspoiled.

Master Wang Yuanzai said: Those who attain the Dao/Way never become full, thus those who perfect the Dao/Way never become new. The Dao/Way’s function endures through ten thousand ages without exhaustion, for it has no exhaustion, no renewal, no completion, no ruin. Exhaustion arises from renewal, ruin from completion. Though a sage-scholar may perfect the Dao/Way, if he remains ever renewed, then he will never know exhaustion or ruin. This chapter speaks of the sage-scholar’s embodiment of the Dao/Way, profound beyond recognition, concluding with the admonition: *Do not desire fullness*—for emptiness is its essence. Emptiness is void and boundless, without a single thing—how could the world ever comprehend it?

The ancients who preserved this Dao/Way—like Great Yu who did not boast of his achievements, or Confucius who did not dwell on his sainthood—sought only emptiness. Laozi embodied emptiness itself, while Yanzi embodied the reality of emptiness. Here, it is clear that the Dao/Way gathers in emptiness.

**Chapter Sixteen: The Chapter of Attaining Emptiness to Its Utmost**

Attain emptiness to its utmost, guard stillness with sincerity.

**Commentary:** Nothing is more precious than emptiness, nothing more virtuous than stillness. Emptiness and stillness are the root of all things. Emptiness suffices to receive all things; stillness suffices to respond to all motion. *Extreme* means gathering all things to reach a point; *sincere* means establishing conduct to reach a point. If one attains emptiness while seeking its utmost and guards stillness to reach sincerity, then though myriad forms may be diverse, my heart remains ever clear, and though myriad changes may differ, my heart remains ever tranquil—this is called Heaven’s joy. Only those who embody the Dao/Way can partake in this.

Master Zhong Hui said: *Attain* means *reach*. To attain emptiness is to reach the utmost of emptiness. To guard stillness is to reach the utmost of stillness. When emptiness is attained, like a valley receiving all things, without a speck of dust accumulating—this is called *extreme*. When stillness is guarded, like water responding to all motion, without a hair’s breadth of disturbance—this is called *sincere*. As all things arise together, I observe their return.

Master Yan Zun said: Heaven and Earth return and invert, thus enduring forever. Man returns to sleep, thus attaining clarity. Dragons and serpents return to their dens, thus their scales shine. Grasses and trees return to their roots, thus they remain verdant. When transformation returns, spirit and clarity attain their proper places, communicating with emptiness, the soul rests, the vital breath is tranquil, each finds its place of ease, the will is serene, the qi harmonious, the blood and vessels balanced—these are but temporary returns to stillness, yet still they preserve their spirit. How much more so for those who long endure in the utmost Dao/Way?

I am the *command* given to all things. With emptiness and stillness attained to their utmost, I observe the arising of all things, commanding them without being transformed by them—thus it is said: *I observe their return*.

All things, teeming and diverse, each return to their root.

Master Wen Gong said: Things arise from non-being, return to non-being.
Master Wang Yuanzai said: From nature arises use, returning to nature’s root—though movement and stillness differ, their principle converges.

Returning to the root is called stillness.

Master Su Ziyou said: If one cannot return to nature, even if he ceases motion and stills thought to seek stillness, it is not stillness. Only by returning to the root can one attain stillness.

Man is born in stillness—this is Heaven’s nature. To return to nature is to be still. Stillness is called returning to command.

Master Lu Tian said: Speaking of the root, one knows there is an origin; speaking of command, one knows there is nature. Thus, returning to the root is called stillness, and returning to command is nature. Zhuangzi said: From the root and origin, before Heaven and Earth existed, from ancient times it has endured. *From the root* is returning to the root, called stillness. *From the origin* is returning to command, called stillness. *From ancient times* is returning to command, called constancy. *From the origin* is without dependence on an origin. *From the root* is without emergence from a root. *From ancient times* is without passage through time. Though returning to the root is called stillness, returning to command is called constancy, and returning to constancy is called constancy, their essence is one.

The order of speech—first root, then origin; first nature, then command—when speaking of the root, it is called returning; when speaking of command, it is called returning. Returning is not yet attainment; returning is attainment. The root is not yet attainment; command is attainment. The depth of returning is profound. Returning to command is called constancy.

Master Wang Yuanzai said: To be born from the Dao/Way is called command. To return to command is to revert to one’s origin, uniting with the Dao/Way, thus enduring through eternity without change. To know constancy is called clarity.

Master Shū Wang said: Constancy is the designation of that which has been unchanging since before beginning. To know its constancy is called clarity. Outside the Dao/Way, all is unconstancy. Though the Dao/Way is true and constant, formless and nameless, without self-awareness of its clarity, few escape being obscured by things.

Not knowing constancy leads to reckless action and ruin.

**Commentary:** The sage-scholar knows the constancy of the Dao/Way, thus his actions align with principle and always meet with good fortune. Those who do not know constancy drift with things, encountering misfortune at every turn, and cannot even preserve their own lives—thus they are ruined. Master Sun Deng said: Unable to return to the root and guard stillness, one’s life is cut short and constancy lost—this is the source of ruin.

The multitude not knowing the true constancy of the Dao/Way,
take delusion for constancy,
thus joy and sorrow arise,
and action brings its loss—
life’s vitality then fades.
This is what is meant by reckless action bringing ruin.

To know constancy is to embrace tolerance.
Su Ziyou said:
*"When lost in delusion, one clings to self and rejects others,
seeing all things as enemies—
how then could one tolerate them?
But if one knows all is delusion,
even foes shall be pitied and spared—
what then cannot be embraced?"*

Tolerance is virtue/power.
He Shang Gong said:
*"To embrace all without exclusion,
virtue/power is just and without partiality—
no evil can withstand it."*

The *Records of Rites* states:
*"When the Great Dao prevails,
the world is held in common.
No man cherishes only his own kin,
nor loves only his own children."*

Virtue/power is sovereignty.
Shu Wang said:
*"To abandon private gain is to achieve virtue/power;
to exhaust one’s measure is to attain sovereignty.
Virtue/power is the root of virtue/power—
sovereignty is its manifestation.
To act from virtue/power is to remain hidden within,
to act from sovereignty is to shine outward.
Virtue/power and sovereignty unite—
the Dao of inner and outer harmony."*

Cheng Xuanying said:
*"Sovereignty is to go forth—
virtue/power makes all things return."*

Sovereignty is Heaven.
Wang Yuanzhe said:
*"Sovereignty is the utmost of human conduct.
To exhaust human conduct is to align with Heaven."*

Heaven is the Dao/Way.
*Commentary*:
*"He who harmonizes Heaven, Earth, and humanity,
and stands centered between them, is the Sovereign.
One and vast above all, encompassing without end—this is Heaven.
Heaven, Earth, and humanity all move through it—this is the Dao/Way.
To exhaust humanity is to align with Heaven;
to embody Heaven is to align with the Dao/Way."*

The Dao/Way is enduring.
Lu Yu said:
*"Enduring means lasting—
to measure against the boundless void,
neither coming nor going,
the mind plunged to its utmost,
neither arising nor perishing,
mysteriously unified by nature,
indivisible—
this, when attuned to its principle, is called enduring.
Thus, one’s life may fade without peril."*

The *New Sayings* state:
*"What Heaven follows is the Dao/Way.
Thus it is said: Heaven is the Dao/Way.
The Dao/Way has neither ancient nor modern—
thus it is said: The Dao/Way is enduring.
When the Dao/Way reaches this enduring state,
one’s life may fade without peril—
who could surpass this?"*

This chapter’s meaning lies in returning to the root and restoring vital command.
Thus it begins with emptiness and stillness,
and concludes with the Dao/Way’s enduring nature—
for the Dao/Way takes emptiness and stillness as its foundation.
To grasp this and enter the Dao/Way
is like abandoning the boat to wade across—
how ineffectual!"

**Chapter Seventeen: The Supreme Dao**
The Supreme Dao—
the lower knows its existence.

「Wang Yuanzé said: In the age of the Three Sovereigns (*Sān Huáng*), there was governance without action (*wúwéi*), allowing the people to each fulfill their nature (*gé suì*) without knowing it was so. How could one claim personal credit for this?

Má Jùjì said: To know of a ruler (*jūn*) but not know of one’s ruler—this is the age of the Three Sovereigns.

As Zhuangzi said: The Taiyi (*Tàishì*) moved with leisurely ease in sleep, and awoke with serene clarity. One moment he took himself as horse, the next as ox.

The *Supreme Dao* (*Tàishàng*) refers to the nameless ruler (*jūn*) of the most ancient times (*Tàigǔ*). *Tài* means "the utmost," *shàng* means "the highest." Governance without action, the utmost and highest—none of the later ages could surpass this, hence it is called *Supreme Dao*.

To say "the lower knows its existence" (*xià zhī yǒuzhī*) means knowing of a ruler but not serving as a subject.

The next stage is to be cherished by the people and praised by them.
Má Jùjì said: Benevolent love (*huì ài*), this is *virtue* (*rén*), hence the people are cherished. Rewards and punishments (*shǎng fá*), this is *righteousness* (*yì*), hence the ruler is praised. Yet even when cherished, the people grow distant; even when praised, the ruler’s power is undermined—this is because they still fall short of the *non-human* (*fēi rén*) ideal.

This is the age of the Two Emperors (*Èr Dì*).

As Zhuangzi said: The age of the Yu (*Yú*) did not reach that of the Taiyi.

Yáo’s benevolence was like Heaven’s, and those who approached him did so as if drawn by the sun.
Shùn reached the outskirts of Dengyu, and ten thousand families came to him—this was cherishing the people.
Kāng Qú praised Yáo, and the songs of praise (*ōng gē*) accompanied Shùn—this was praising the ruler.

The next stage is to be feared by the people and scorned by them.
Zhàn Wēi said: When *virtue* (*rén*) and *righteousness* (*yì*) are lost, legal punishments (*xíngfǎ*) are established, and the people fear them.

Má Jùjì said: This is the age of the Three Kings (*Sān Wáng*).
As Zhuangzi said: When *virtue* (*rén*) and *righteousness* (*yì*) extended to the Three Kings, the world was greatly alarmed. Those who loved wisdom exhausted the people, and then the age of cudgels and saws (*zhuī záo yāng yán*) came.

When wisdom reaches its extreme, great deceit (*dàwěi*) arises. The clever exploit those below them, and the foolish scorn those above, while punishments are insufficient to deter them—this is the age of the Five Hegemons (*Wǔ Bà*).

As Mencius said: The Five Hegemons relied on this.

Wang Yuanzé said: When *virtue/power* (*dé*) is lost and governance (*zhèng*) fails, the people scorn the ruler.
Trustworthiness is insufficient, and distrust arises.

The *Imperial Commentary* (*Yù zhù*) states: When bonds are not reinforced with glue or lacquer, and restraints are not secured with ropes or cords, this is the utmost trustworthiness. The Shang people made oaths (*shì*) and the people began to betray them; the Zhou people made assemblies (*huì*), and the people began to doubt them—this is because trustworthiness was insufficient.

When the *Supreme Dao* is known only as "the lower knows its existence," then one should not know it as trustworthy. In the next stage, where the people fear and scorn the ruler, one knows of deceit. When Jiāo Huá’s cunning and Jì’s scheming arise, distrust begins.

Emperor Xuānzōng of the Tang (*Táng Míng Huáng*) said: Those who fear and scorn the ruler are all due to the ruler’s insufficient trustworthiness, causing those below to have distrustful hearts.

Even so, the ruler’s words are still honored.
Emperor Xuānzōng said: When the ruler is cherished and praised, it is because the ruler has words of *virtue/power* (*dé jiào*), hence his words are honored and he is cherished and praised.

Má Jùjì said: When words are not inherently honored yet are still honored, this is why it is said that though it falls short of the *Supreme Dao*, the ruler’s words still transform without explicit action. From this point onward, emperors have *canons and admonitions* (*diǎnmó*), and kings have *oaths and proclamations* (*shì gāo*).

When achievements are complete and affairs are settled, the people say: "This is our natural state."
The *Imperial Commentary* states: The ruler’s achievements are the *Dao/Way’s* (*shèngrén*) remaining work. To make the people content with their food, pleased with their clothing, secure in their dwellings, and joyful in their occupations—this is the *Dao/Way’s* remaining work sufficient to complete the ruler’s achievements. Yet the people say only: "This is our natural state."

They say: "What has the ruler’s power to do with us?" This is called the governance of the *Supreme Dao*.

Emperor Xuānzōng said: When achievements are complete yet not seized, and affairs are settled yet without further action, the people say: "The ruler acts as if this were our natural state." What part does the ruler’s power play in it?

This chapter speaks of the distinct transformations of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors, how the people’s customs swayed, the *Dao/Way* descending into *virtue/power* (*dé*), *virtue/power* declining into legal punishments (*xíng*), and punishments failing to control—inevitably leading to deceit and scorn. This is because trustworthiness was insufficient in the ruler, and the people had their doubts.

**Chapter Eighteen: The Dao/Way is Abandoned**
The *Dao/Way* is abandoned, and *virtue* (*rén*) and *righteousness* (*yì*) arise.

The *Imperial Commentary* states: When the *Dao/Way* is lost, *virtue/power* (*dé*) follows; when *virtue/power* is lost, *virtue* (*rén*) follows; when *virtue* is lost, *righteousness* (*yì*) follows. *Virtue* establishes the people, *righteousness* establishes oneself—this is far from the *Dao/Way*.

Han Yu did not grasp the true intent of the Sage’s *virtue/power* (*shèngrén dé yì*), instead claiming that *seven* (*qī*) and *righteousness* (*yì*) were definitive names, while *Dao/Way* and *virtue/power* (*dé*) were empty titles. Laozi’s *small virtue* (*xiǎo rén*) and *small righteousness* (*xiǎo yì*) were but narrow views.

As Zhuangzi said: These are the *people obscured by delusion* (*bìméng zhī mín*).

The *Supreme Dao* is the same as the *Dao/Way* of the most ancient times without action (*wúwéi*).
When *virtue* and *righteousness* are not manifest, it is like the sun at its zenith while all stars lose their luster. *Abandoned* (*fèi*) is the same as *hidden* (*yǐn*).

After the Three Sovereigns, people’s hearts were not pure and sincere. The *Supreme Dao* was hidden and unseen, while *seven* (*qī*) and *righteousness* (*yì*) were established and the *Dao/Way* declined. Like fish leaving the ocean for stagnant pools, the people then valued *benevolent care* (*rúmò zhī ēn*) more.

Having lost the *Dao/Way’s* natural path, the people then valued *virtue* and *righteousness*’s blessings more.
As Zhuangzi said: The *Dao/Way* is hidden in minor achievements.

When wisdom arises, great deceit arises.
Emperor Xuānzōng said: Those who employ wisdom seek to establish laws. When laws are established, deceit arises, and then great deceit emerges.

Wisdom (*huì*) has wisdom (*zhì*) as its essence, and wisdom (*zhì*) has wisdom (*huì*) as its application. Wisdom has great and small distinctions.

As Zhuangzi said: Abandoning minor wisdom (*xiǎo zhì*), great wisdom (*dà zhì*) becomes clear.
As Confucius said: Those who delight in minor cleverness (*xiǎo huì*) are said to have wisdom—this shows that wisdom has great and small distinctions.

The *Supreme Dao* says: These are the ones who take cudgels (*záo*) as wisdom and keen scrutiny (*chá*) as insight, crafting clever laws and decrees—this is minor wisdom.

Once wisdom arises, the people’s deceit and trickery flourish.
If one instead observes both near and far with tranquil ease, doing nothing yet benefiting all, following their natural state without adding or detracting—when the true and pure customs prevail, how could there ever be great deceit?

When the six bonds (*liù qīn*) are not harmonious, filial piety (*xiào*) and tender care (*cí*) arise.
Zhōng Huì said: If all nine kin (*jiǔ zú*) are in accord, then love and respect are without limit. When the six bonds are not harmonious, then filial piety and tender care are emphasized.

The blind and the deaf were considered foolish, yet Shùn was called a great filial son.
Zēng Xǐ was stern, yet his brother Zǐshēn was praised for nurturing him.
When the state is in chaos and confusion, loyal ministers arise.

Shū Wáng said: The *Dao/Way* is hidden in the formless, and names arise from insufficiency.»

The *Dao/Way* hides in the formless, then there is no distinction between great and small.
Names arise from insufficiency, then there are gradations of virtue, righteousness, wisdom, and sagacity.

The virtuous have that which they love.
The righteous have that which they distinguish.
Because of their love and distinction, this is how the Great *Dao/Way* is abandoned.

The wise know.
The sagacious discern.
Because of their knowing and discerning, this is how great deceit arises.

The filial honor their own kin, the tender care for their own children—this is how the six bonds become discordant.

The loyal are loyal to their own lord, and this is called loyalty; to another’s lord, it is called rebellion.
To be unclear is called benighted.
To be ungoverned is called chaotic.
In a benighted and chaotic age, loyal ministers arise to rectify their lord.

The *Transmission* says: *"In turbulent times, true ministers appear."*
As when Longfang’s fame flourished under Xia Jie, and Bi Gan’s praise resounded under Yin Zhou.

This chapter speaks of the *Dao/Way* hidden in the small, and names arising from insufficiency.

---
**Chapter Nineteen: Abolish the Sage, Discard Wisdom**

Abolish the sage, discard wisdom, and the people benefit a hundredfold.
Zhōng Huì said: Abolish the sage who makes laws, discard the wisdom of scheming, and people will return to simplicity and purity—thus their benefit is a hundredfold.

The maker is called the sage; the creator is called the wise.
The sage and the wise establish laws and decrees, desiring to curb deceit and suppress violence—yet how do they know that laws breed deceit and edicts spawn trickery? The people lose their true nature, ever drifting toward competition and strife.

If laws and edicts are abolished, the people will revert to their natural state and simplicity—how could their benefit be merely a hundredfold?

Zhuangzi said: *"Sever the sage’s limbs, release the thief and bandit, and the world will begin to be governed."*

Abolish virtue, discard righteousness, and filial piety and tender care return.
Wang Yuanzhe said: In an age of perfect virtue, parent and child naturally suffice.
Now, if we further generate virtue and righteousness, then names and realities become entangled, gains and losses confused—chaos ensues among the people.
For what flourishes at the end must have decayed at its root—this is the *Dao/Way* of Heaven.
Filial piety and tender care are the root of virtue and righteousness.

Some say: The *Dao/Way* of Confucius and Mencius, of Yao and Shun, is centered on virtue and righteousness, while the *Dao* of Laozi is the true path. How can it be said that Xia valued birth as merit, while Zhou valued austerity as virtue—one makes them flourish and abandon their roots, the other withers them and returns to their essence?

The *Dao/Way* is the seasons; the sage adapts to the times.
To understand the *Dao/Way*, then Confucius and Laozi are the beginning and end of each other.

Abolish cleverness, discard profit, and thieves and bandits vanish.
Wang Yuanzhe said: When cleverness and profit prevail, people’s desires grow extravagant, and their foundational pursuits decline—inevitably leading to theft.
The three matters above all harm the root through their excess.

Cleverness is ingenuity.
The clever are those who excel in deceit and profit.
Seeing profit while forgetting righteousness—if ingenious scheming is abandoned, people will know shame; if the heart of desire is discarded, people will know righteousness—this is how thieves and bandits vanish.
If cleverness and profit are not abandoned, and only laws are multiplied to forbid them, this is how thieves and bandits proliferate.

Zhuangzi said: *"Sever the artisan Tu’s fingers, and the world will begin to have its own cleverness."*
And again: *"Break the jade, shatter the pearls, and petty thieves will not arise."*

These three matters are insufficient as ornamentation, hence their reliance on external attachments.
Ma Jiuji said: Substance is in excess, ornamentation is insufficient—these three have strayed far from their root, serving only as superficial adornment. This is their insufficiency.

The four matters below are assigned to their natural attributes.

**Seeing Simplicity, Embracing Purity.**
Shū Wáng said: It is not said "guard simplicity" but "see simplicity"—not "return to purity" but "embrace purity"—not "have no selfishness" but "have little selfishness"—not "have no desires" but "have few desires." For only by seeing simplicity can one guard it; only by embracing purity can one return to it; only by having little selfishness can one achieve none; only by having few desires does one reach the point where there is nothing to be seen.

Seeing simplicity reveals the true nature, untainted by external things.
Embracing purity preserves the whole essence, unharmed by external things.

**Having Little Selfishness, Few Desires.**
The *Commentary* says: Self-seeking is selfishness, and nothing nourishes the heart better than its absence.
Few desires and little selfishness—few desires clarify the distinction between inner and outer, true and false. Virtue is complete, and nature is restored.

Ma Jiuji said: "Seeing simplicity" belongs to the sage’s aversion.
That which is unmixed is called simplicity; to see simplicity is to see the *Dao/Way*, to see the *Dao/Way* is to take no action, and taking no action means there is no sage, no wisdom.

"Embracing purity" belongs to virtue and righteousness.
Whole material is called purity. To embrace purity is to embrace nature, to embrace nature is to be without artifice, and without artifice means there is no virtue, no righteousness.

"Little selfishness, few desires" belong to cleverness and profit.
Self-possession is selfishness; having things is desire, and cleverness and profit arise from them.
Little selfishness and few desires mean cleverness and profit decline.

The *Zhuangzi·Tú Kuāng* chapter precisely clarifies the harm of sages, wisdom, righteousness, cleverness, and profit—this aligns with the intent of this passage.

Selfishness is my body.
Little selfishness means not burdening oneself with cleverness and profit.
Desire is the movement of nature.
Few desires mean not disturbing one’s heart with cleverness and profit.

Li Tian said: This chapter speaks of cutting off growth by uprooting the root, and suppressing overflow by blocking the source.
If lawmaking does not flourish, then true simplicity appears.
If ornamentation of righteousness is not applied, then purity is preserved.
If cleverness and profit are not practiced, then selfishness and desire are extinguished.
Then Heaven’s harmony flows naturally, and daily life is effortless.

---
**Chapter Twenty: Abolish Learning, Have No Worry**

Abolish learning, have no worry.
The *Commentary* says: Learning is to exhaust principle. When one is diligent in learning to exhaust principle, deliberating over good and evil, comparing order and chaos—how could there be no worry?

Learning is to attain the *Dao/Way*; seeing the *Dao/Way* and then abandoning learning—reducing it, reducing it again, until one reaches non-action and yet nothing remains undone—then one follows the natural disposition of life, finding joy in whatever arises, and thus has no worry.

**Chapter Twenty: Abolish Learning, Have No Worry**

Wen Gong said: The reason learning cannot be abandoned is to seek the *Dao/Way*.
If one abandons the root and pursues the branches, then laboring without result is inevitable—better not to learn and have no worry at all.

The *Absolute Learning* speaks of embodying the *Dao/Way*.
To embody the *Dao/Way* is to find joy in poverty and in prosperity, treating poverty and prosperity as the changing seasons—neither rejoicing in life nor dreading death, for life and death are but the natural cycles of night and day. When Heaven and Earth rejoice, all affairs dissolve into nothingness—what worry remains?

Moreover, *Absolute Learning* does not mean to utterly discard learning.
Laozi feared that men would drown in learning, letting words extinguish essence, drowning in knowledge, unable to embody the *Dao/Way*—thus he spoke of *Absolute Learning*.
If one does not learn, how then can one enter the *Dao/Way*?
Zixia said: The virtuous man learns to attain his *Dao/Way*.
To attain the *Dao/Way* is to forget the self entirely—this is why learning must be *Absolute*.

As for those who do not learn, they are empty and void—what need have they of *Absolute Learning*?
Yet Yangzi said: "If a man does not learn, though he may have no worry, he is like a beast—how does he know the *Dao/Way* of the Sage's *Absolute Learning*?"
The distance between *yes* and *no* is slight.
The difference between *good* and *evil* is minimal.

*The Commentary* says: Though *yes* and *no* differ in sound, they arise from the same voice. Though *good* and *evil* differ in nature, they both depart from the *Dao/Way*.
To illustrate, the learned man is like *yes* or *good*, while the unlearned is like *no* or *evil*.
Learning leads to wisdom, while not learning leads to folly—
the wise may err, the foolish may fall short,
yet both equally stray from the *Dao/Way*.

What men fear, they must fear.
*The Commentary* says: The *Dao/Way* moves all things yet does not share the Sage’s worry—
calamity and fortune are shared with the people, but affairs are not.
To embody the *Dao/Way* is to have no worry;
to engage with affairs is to know fear.
If one fears what men fear yet does not take it as a warning,
few will escape exhaustion.
Thus the virtuous man uses fear to reflect and correct himself.

*The Odes* say: Fear the might of Heaven.
The Tang Emperor Ming Huang said: What common men fear is arrogance and wickedness.
What the virtuous fear is worldly learning and striving—
both must be abandoned, hence the need for fear.
The masses labor in learning yet fail to grasp its truth;
when all affairs are summoned, they only disturb the mind—
this is what men fear, and it too must be feared.
If one does not *Absolute Learning*,
there is no way to embody the *Dao/Way*—
thus it is.

Oh, the wildness of it!
How endless it shall be!

River Master Yang said: The world is in chaos, and men rush to study, seeking words—
there is no end to it.
*Wildness* is the state of neglect.
If one does not *Absolute Learning*,
then one’s true nature is abandoned and neglected—
the worry of neglect has no end.

The masses bustle and rejoice,
as if feasting on the grandest sacrifice,
as if ascending the terrace in spring.

Wang Yuanzhe said: The transformations of nature are boundless; affairs are called *life*.
Only the Sage can unify past and present without change,
while the masses follow transformations in vain,
seeing beauty and evil, joy and gain in their *life*,
and deeming it all beautiful.
*Spring* is when all things flourish and depart from stillness,
when they move and abandon the root for the branches.
*The terrace* is where one gazes far upon all scenes.
Without understanding ultimate principle, all things generate emotions—
their state is thus.

All things flourish with *yang* and congeal with *yin*.
*Yang* governs motion, *yin* governs stillness.
The bustling state is the symbol of an active nature, delighting in joy.
*Grandest sacrifice* refers to the cow—its flavor is the richest.
*Spring* is the peak of the season’s transformations.
*The terrace* is where one gazes far upon all scenes.

「Thus, I liken the multitude to those who, through study, cultivate falsehood,
who, chasing fleeting scenes, lose their true nature—
their state is as such.

The Dao is indeed insipid, without embellishment,
substantial yet not ostentatious—
the delight of those who abandon learning.

To pursue learning is to lose the Dao,
to stray from the natural stillness of one’s essence,
to roam externally in vain—
their hearts are restless, yet they savor profound truths
as if relishing the richest flavors to nourish their appetites,
and broaden their perceptions
as if beholding lofty splendor to gratify their minds.
Those ensnared by pleasure,
ever more distant from the Dao.

I alone am *pure*—
before any signs appear.

Like an infant yet to mature—
*pure* in its simplicity.

*御注* says: The *Classic* states—
*"Return to the infant state."*
The infant’s thoughts are still unformed,
treating near and far alike—
*pure* in stillness, harmonious and gentle within,
its brilliance not yet manifesting outward—
thus like the infant yet to mature.

*Pure* means
stillness without flow.
In stillness, one attains the flavor of the insipid,
restoring the original unity of nature—
how different is this from the infant?

*Buoyant* yet without attachment—
as though belonging nowhere.

*御注* says: The Sage rides the Six Dragons in accordance with the seasons to govern the heavens.

*Buoyant* means
to follow the natural course of things without leaning toward one extreme—
thus like belonging nowhere.

*Buoyant* is the appearance of movement.
The Perfect One in stillness aligns with the infant,
in motion rides the transformations of all things—
adapting to change without fixation,
hence called *buoyant*.

The masses all have excess—
*马巨济* says: Nature lacks excess or deficiency;
what is called excess is merely external adornment.

Feasting on the *Grandest Sacrifice* and ascending the *Spring Terrace*,
all they gain is external adornment.

Thus, I speak of excess.
The masses pursue learning, increasing their knowledge—
hence the excess.

But I alone seem abandoned—
*马巨济* says: To seem abandoned is not true abandonment,
merely a reluctance born of insufficiency.

True abandonment is for those who do not learn.
The Perfect One does not abandon learning—
rather, they sever it to preserve the Dao,
diminishing their perceptions,
as the saying goes: *"For the Dao, one must daily diminish."*

My heart is that of a foolish man,
*pure*—
*马巨济* says: This belongs to the meaning of *"I alone seem abandoned."*
The masses pursue learning to acquire wisdom,
but I alone abandon it—
thus I am a foolish man.
Yet *pure* means untainted—
though foolish, I am not foolish.

*Zhuangzi* says: *Pure* means one’s spirit remains undefiled.
Foolishness is lack of wisdom.
*Pure* means unadulterated.
Learning never ceases to multiply wisdom,
but abandoning learning discards wisdom
and seems like foolishness—
*"Great wisdom resembles foolishness."*

The common folk are sharp and discerning—
I alone seem dim.

The common folk are keen and strict—
I alone am *obscured*—
*纂微* says: *Keen* means rigorous clarity.
*Obscured* means spacious ease.

The worldly, through learning, govern with laws—
rigid institutions, harsh decrees,
priding themselves on their actions—
thus the people barely survive.

Hence, the Perfect One follows Heaven’s natural course,
conforms to the Dao’s patterns,
and in yielding to the world,
embraces spacious ease—
*obscured*—
so the people may preserve their true nature.

*Zhuangzi* says: At the Dao’s utmost extreme,
*obscured* and silent—
this is what is meant.

Minor clarity is called *sharp*,
lack of clarity is called *obscured*.

The common folk are keen and strict,
perceiving minutiae, calculating hidden truths—
they deem this *sharp* clarity.

*Sharp* is not true greatness.
Those who abandon learning embody the Dao,
discard wisdom, and merge with all things—
without distinction—
thus they seem *obscured*.
*Obscured* is not innate dullness,
but a state of suchness.

If one governs by *sharp* clarity,
rigor and strictness prevail—
*"A man too keen has no followers."*
If one governs by *obscured* ease,
spaciousness and breadth emerge—
*"Constantly yielding to things,
never diminished by others."*

Sudden as if eclipsed,
*tranquil*—
as though no end in sight.

*御注* says: The depths of stillness
and the settled nature—
the complete embodiment of the Dao.
Change without loss—
the Dao’s practical application.

The masses all have purpose—
*马巨济* says: I alone seem *coarse*—
like a rustic.

*御注* says: The osmanthus can be eaten,
so it is pruned.
The lacquer can be used,
so it is cut.

All know the use of useful things,
but none grasp the use of the *useless*.

The masses’ purpose is the use of useful things—
I alone seem *coarse*,
like a rustic—
the use of the *useless*.

*The Classic* says: *"The heart that does not conform to the patterns of virtue and righteousness is called *coarse*."*
Anciently, it was said:
*"To call the capital *beautiful* is to be *coarse*."*
*"To call the wilderness *rustic* is to be *coarse*."*
*Coarse* means not embellishing wisdom,
*rustic* means not perceiving beauty—
thus the Perfect One remains *useless*.

*马巨济* says: *Use* means application.
The masses’ learning seeks worldly application—
what is called the use of the useful.
Yet how would they know
that abandoning learning is truly the most useful?

*Coarse* and *rustic*—
this is so.
For *sharp* and *keen* mean
distinct clarity between root and branch—
not the *useless*.

Thus, I alone differ from others,
and value nourishment from the *Mother*.

*马巨济* says: This concludes the meaning of *"the infant yet to mature."*
The infant takes the *Mother* as its foundation,
before maturity feeds on its root—
after maturity, on its branches.

The Dao is the root,
learning is the branch.
Others feed on branches,
I feed on the root—
this is how I differ.

Yet the Sage does not prize difference,
while I alone do—
this chapter’s theme is to extol abandoning learning,
for only by differing from the world
can one save the world.

This section emphasizes abandoning learning.
Every sentence within carries the intent of severing learning.
If one does not abandon learning,
the more one hears and sees,
the more one drowns the heart—
the Dao becomes obscured.
Thus, the chapter ends by valuing nourishment from the *Mother*,
for the *Mother* is the Dao.
Only by abandoning learning can one cultivate the Dao—
this is how those with the Dao
differ from the common folk.»

「The *Dao De Jing* ["Classic of the Way and Virtue"]—*Taking the Good* in its Third Volume—*Taking the Good* in its Fourth Volume—*Li Lin of Song’s* ["宋饶阳居士李霖集"] Collection of *Dao De Jing* ["Classic of the Way and Virtue"].

**Chapter 21: The Appearance of Virtue** ["孔德之容"]

The appearance of virtue follows only the Dao.

**Commentary of the Master** ["御注"]:
The interplay of yin and yang is called the Dao; by this interplay, all things are born and thus possess virtue.

The Dao is ever nameless,
how can it be described in words?—
thus its virtue is made divine.

Virtue has form and substance,
and all things share in it—
thus the Dao is revealed.

**Zhuangzi’s Annotation** ["纂微曰"]:
The Dao is ever nameless,
only virtue reveals it.

Virtue without root,
formed by following the Dao.

Speaking of those with great virtue,
their appearance transcends all forms—
how can they be seen as crooked?

Only the appearance of one who follows the Dao
barely reveals their true state.

In their movements and demeanor, they observe propriety—
this is the peak of virtue.

Those with peak virtue appear foolish,
how can they be seen as crooked?
Only those who follow the Dao
can be glimpsed as such.

Thus, when the eyes see, ears hear, nose smells, mouth speaks,
hands hold, and feet walk—
all are but the Dao.

Thus, the appearance of virtue follows only the Dao.

The Dao, as a thing,
is but vague and indistinct.

**Commentary of the Master** ["御注"]:
The Dao’s essence is absolute nothingness,
yet its manifestations are wondrous—
this is why it is called a thing.

Yet all things are but the Dao.
Vague—where there is form to be likened,
indistinct—where there are numbers to be traced.
But what is called "having" is but doubt of "nothingness."
Thus it is said: The Dao, as a thing.

**Gu Xuan’s Explanation** ["顾欢曰"]:
To speak of its definite existence,
yet it has no color, no sound.
To say it is definite nothingness,
yet it has trust and precision.
Since its essence cannot be defined,
it is called but vague and indistinct.

Thus, observing it as such
is called following the Dao—
this is how one attains it.

**Ma Jujie’s Explanation** ["马巨济曰"]:
When nothingness appears as having—
this is vague.
When having appears as nothingness—
this is indistinct.
This is the Dao’s thing.

The Dao, in its vague and indistinct nature,
returns to nothingness—
this is what cannot be contained.

**Wang Yuanzhe’s Explanation** ["王元泽曰"]:
The Dao encompasses yin and yang.
The subtleties of yin and yang,
when they appear as neither having nor nothingness,
are called vague and indistinct.

Indistinct yet vague—
within it, forms arise.
Vague yet indistinct—
within it, things manifest.

**Commentary of the Master** ["御注"]:
What is seen is called form,
what is shaped is called thing.
Within vague and indistinct,
forms and things are complete—
like vast space transforming into thunder and wind,
like great mass becoming water and fire,
to bring forth change,
to move the numinous and divine—
this is the Dao’s wondrous work.

Faint and dark—
within it, essence resides.

**Commentary of the Master** ["御注"]:
Faint—the extreme of seclusion,
dark—the concealment of illumination.
Within faint and dark,
the origin of supreme yin,
where Heaven’s One manifests—
essence truly arises.

Its essence is exceedingly true,
within it, trust resides.

**Wang Yuanzhe’s Explanation** ["王元泽曰"]:
Essence—the beginning of form’s birth.
Essence is never false,
yet it is further called "exceedingly true"—
because things may lose their proper order,
and essence, likened to human artifice, may err.

The essence within faint and dark,
gives rise to all things,
and the things born,
each follow their natural form and measure,
their laws and patterns—
once a standard is set,
the future can be known without deviation,
this is called trust.

**Zhuangzi’s Words** ["庄子曰"]:
Before form, there is division.
Essence—the peak of Heaven’s virtue.
True, then it is not false;
trustworthy, then it does not err.
From ancient times to today,
its name does not fade.

**He Shang Gong’s Words** ["河上公曰"]:
*Self*—to follow.
From ancient times to today,
the Dao remains unceasing.

**Wang Yuanzhe’s Explanation** ["王元泽曰"]:
The Dao is constant in name,
never changing or transforming.

**Ma Jujie’s Explanation** ["马巨济曰"]:
As all things transform and change,
their names shift accordingly.
What was once called "right"
is now called "wrong."
Yet what remains steadfast through ages past and present—
the Dao alone.

Thus, it oversees all beginnings.

**Wang Bi’s Explanation** ["王弼曰"]:
All beginnings—
the origins of things.
To oversee with the nameless
the origins of all things—
this is to oversee.

To oversee—
as when one examines many texts.
How do I know all beginnings are thus?
By this.

**Su Shi’s Explanation** ["苏子由曰"]:
The Sage knows how all things are one
through essence.
*Pattern*—law.
*One*—the source of all multiplicity.
The Sage attains the world with the few,
yet is troubled by the many.
The Sage embraces *One* without departing from essence,
the world abandons multiplicity and returns to *One*—
thus the Sage becomes the world’s law and pattern.

Not self-revealing, hence clear;
**Commentary of the Master** ["御注"]:
Not obscured by one’s own limited perspective,
then nothing remains unseen—
hence clear.

Yu Shun’s four eyes widened his vision,
this can be called not self-revealing.
To perceive all things,
to discern human relations—
this is not clear?

Cheng Wang’s open and unconstrained mind,
unblocked within himself—
this can be called not self-revealing.
To illuminate all and be illuminated in return,
this is not clear?

Not self-affirming, hence manifest;
**Commentary of the Master** ["御注"]:
Not partial to one’s own rightness,
yet following only what is right—
then great is their achievement,
bright their fame,
and the world submits—
hence manifest.

**Lu Jifu’s Explanation** ["吕吉甫曰"]:
To follow what the world affirms as right,
yet I do not self-affirm—
then what is right cannot be exhausted.
Thus it is said: Not self-affirming, hence manifest.

Not self-praising, hence meritorious;
**Commentary of the Master** ["御注"]:
*The Book of Documents* ["《书》"] says: "You alone do not praise yourself,
then the world cannot contend with you for merit."

**Shu Wang’s Explanation** ["舒王曰"]:
Not self-praising, then goodness is not lost—
hence meritorious.

Not self-boasting, hence enduring;
**Commentary of the Master** ["御注"]:
*The Book of Documents* ["《书》"] says: "You alone do not boast,
then the world cannot contend with you for ability."

**Lu Jifu’s Explanation** ["吕吉甫曰"]:
To entrust all things with ability,
yet I do not self-boast—
hence enduring.

Thus, only by not contending,
the world cannot contend with them.
**Commentary of the Master** ["御注"]:
Others take the lead,
I alone remain behind—
this is to bear the world’s filth.

「若是者，常处於不争之地，孰能与之争乎。
Thus, if one remains steadfast in the Dao of non-contention,
who could contend with them?

苏子由曰：忘我则不争。
**Su Ziyou** ["苏子由曰"] says:
When one forgets the self, contention ceases.

不自是，不自见，不自伐，不自矜，
Not clinging to one’s own correctness,
not self-displaying,
not self-praising,
not self-boasting—
all are remnants of non-contention.

故以不争终之。
Hence, they attain the ultimate through non-contention.

古之所谓曲则全者，岂虚言哉。
The ancients spoke of *yielding to preserve wholeness*—
was this not sincere?

河上公曰：古者曲从则全身，此言非虚。
**He Shang Gong** ["河上公"] says:
In ancient times, yielding and compliance preserved the body—
this saying was not in vain.

诚全而归之。
Indeed, wholeness returns to its source.

御注：圣人其动若水，以交物而不亏。
**Commentary of the Master** ["御注"]:
The sage’s movement is like water,
interacting with all things without depletion.

其全其应若绳，以顺理而不失其直。
Their wholeness and responsiveness are like a measuring line,
following the natural order yet never losing their straightness.

知洼之为盈，无亢满之累。
They know that *hollowing* ["洼"] is true abundance,
and avoid the burden of arrogant fullness.

知弊之为新，无夸耀之迹。
They know that *imperfection* ["弊"] is genuine renewal,
and leave no trace of vain display.

若性之自为而不知为之者，致曲而已。
If one acts according to nature’s spontaneous self-so,
yet does not know who does so—
they merely achieve yielding.

故全而归之，可以保身，可以尽年，而不知其尽也。
Thus, when wholeness returns to its source,
one can preserve the body,
fulfill their lifespan,
yet remain unaware of its completion.

是谓全德之人，岂虚言哉。
This is called the person of *complete virtue*—
was this not sincere?

锺会曰：诚能守曲，全必归之。
**Zhong Hui** ["钟会"] says:
If one truly upholds yielding,
wholeness will inevitably return.

此章之义，养生之旨也。
The meaning of this chapter is the principle of *nourishing life*.

其要在乎忘我。
Its essence lies in forgetting the self.

惟忘我，故委曲以应变，而不自恃一己之见。
Only by forgetting the self
can one bend and yield to respond to changes,
without relying on their own limited perceptions.

枉己而伸人，而不私一己之道。
They sacrifice themselves to benefit others,
without hoarding their own Dao.

知洼之为盈，无自伐之心。
They know that *hollowing* is true abundance,
and thus have no pride in self-praise.

知弊之为新，无自矜之行。
They know that *imperfection* is genuine renewal,
and thus avoid self-boasting conduct.

若性之自为而不知为之者，致曲以全，其形生而已。
If one acts according to nature’s spontaneous self-so,
yet does not know who does so—
they merely achieve yielding for wholeness,
and life’s form is all.

故终始以曲，则全言之。
Thus, from beginning to end, if one persists in yielding,
wholeness is fully achieved.

希言自然章第二十三 希言自然。
**Chapter Twenty-Three: Sparse Speech in Accord with Nature**

希言自然。
Sparse speech in accord with nature.

顾欢曰：希，少也。 人能爱气少言，则行合自然。
**Gu Huan** ["顾欢"] says:
*Sparse* ["希"] means *few* ["少"].
If a person cherishes qi and speaks little,
their actions align with nature.

纂微曰：夫至人有问，即应接物，即言动静以时，故合於自然。
**Zuan Wei** ["纂微"] says:
The *perfected one* ["至人"] responds to inquiries,
interacts with things,
and speaks of movement and stillness in due measure—
thus harmonizing with nature.

陆佃曰：夫物莫能使之然，亦莫能使之不然者，谓之自然。
**Lu Dian** ["陆佃"] says:
That which cannot be made to be so,
nor prevented from being so—
this is called *nature* ["自然"].

飘风不终朝，骤雨不终日。
Gale-force winds do not last a full morning,
torrent rains do not last a full day.

王元泽曰：风雨者，阴阳交感所为。 飘骤者，交感之过，所以不能久也。
**Wang Yuanzhe** ["王元泽"] says:
Winds and rains arise from the *interaction of yin and yang* ["阴阳交感"].
Gale-force and torrent are excesses of this interaction—
thus they cannot endure.

飘风，疾风也。 骤雨，暴雨也。
*Gale-force winds* ["飘风"] are swift winds,
*torrent rains* ["骤雨"] are sudden downpours.

从旦至哺为终朝，自早及暮为终日。
From dawn to midday is a full morning,
from early to evening is a full day.

飘风骤雨，气之暴戾，非出於常然也。
Gale-force winds and torrent rains are violent and untamed qi,
not arising from the usual order of things.

故虽天地为之尚，不能有终朝终日之久。
Thus, even though the heavens and earth honor them,
they cannot last a full morning or a full day.

人之言不出於自然，则多言数穷宜矣。
When a person’s speech does not arise from nature,
excessive words will inevitably exhaust them—
this is fitting.

孰为此者，天地。
Who does this?
The heavens and earth.

天地尚不能久，而况於人乎。
The heavens and earth themselves cannot endure—
how much more so for humans?

御注：天地之造万物，风以散之委众形之自化，而雨以润之任万物之自滋，
**Commentary of the Master** ["御注"]:
The heavens and earth create ten thousand things:
the wind scatters them, entrusting their forms to spontaneous transformation,
while the rain nourishes them, allowing all things to grow naturally.

故不益生，不劝成，而万物自遂于天地之间。
Thus, they neither *aid life* ["益生"] nor *urge completion* ["劝成"],
yet all things flourish naturally between heaven and earth.

所以长且久也。
This is how they endure long and last.

飘骤则阴阳有缪戾之患，必或使之，而物被其害，故不能久。
Gale-force and torrent rains bring the calamity of *errant yin and yang* ["缪戾"],
compelling their use—
when things suffer their harm,
they cannot endure.

风雨者，阴阳交感所为。 飘骤者，交感之过。
Winds and rains arise from the interaction of yin and yang;
gale-force and torrent rains are excesses of this interaction.

天地之大犹不能久，况人处天地之间，眇乎小哉。
Even the vast heavens and earth cannot endure—
how much more so for humans, who dwell within them,
so small in comparison?

为於多言速灭可知。
It is clear that excessive speech leads to swift annihilation.

故从事於道者。
Thus, those who follow the Dao—

王弼曰：从事谓举动从事於道者也。
**Wang Bi** ["王弼"] says:
*Following the Dao* ["从事"] means *acting in accordance with the Dao*.

道以无形无为，成济万物。
The Dao, without form or action,
accomplishes and nurtures all things.

从事於道者，以无为为君，不言为教，
**Those who follow the Dao** treat *non-action* ["无为"] as their sovereign,
and *non-speech* ["不言"] as their teaching.

绵绵若存，而物得其真，
They persist like unbroken threads,
and thus all things attain their genuine nature.

与道同体，故曰同於道。
They share the same substance as the Dao—
thus it is said: *They are in accord with the Dao*.

道者，同於道。
*Following the Dao* ["道者"] means *being in accord with the Dao*.

河上公曰：道者，谓好道之人。
**He Shang Gong** ["河上公"] says:
*Following the Dao* ["道者"] refers to those who love the Dao.

同於道，所谓与道同。
*Being in accord with the Dao* ["同於道"] is what is called *aligning with the Dao* ["与道同"].

德者，同於德。
*Virtue* ["德者"] means *being in accord with virtue*.

河上公日：德者，谓有德之人。
**He Shang Gong** ["河上公"] says:
*Virtue* ["德者"] refers to those who possess virtue.

同於德，所谓与德同。
*Being in accord with virtue* ["同於德"] is what is called *aligning with virtue* ["与德同"].

失者，同於失。
*Losing* ["失者"] means *being in accord with loss*.

蔡子晃曰：有为躁竞，执教生迷，名为失。
**Cai Zihuang** ["蔡子晃"] says:
*Action* ["有为"] and *restlessness* ["躁竞"],
clinging to teachings and thus generating confusion—
this is called *loss*.

既为同失，不能虚心冥会，而言道失者，独失道也。
If one aligns with *loss* ["同失"],
yet cannot maintain an empty heart to intuitively grasp the Dao,
and still speaks of *losing the Dao* ["道失"]—
they have lost the Dao alone.

自然之谓道。
What is called *nature* ["自然"] is the Dao.

从事於道者，悟道忘言，所谓与道同。
Those who follow the Dao awaken to the Dao and forget speech—
this is what is called *aligning with the Dao* ["与道同"].

得道之谓德。
Attaining the Dao is called *virtue*.

德者希言，所谓与德同。
*Virtue* ["德者"] is marked by sparse speech—
this is what is called *aligning with virtue* ["与德同"].

失道之谓失。
Losing the Dao is called *loss*.

失者多言，所谓与失同。
*Loss* ["失者"] is marked by excessive speech—
this is what is called *aligning with loss* ["与失同"].

同於道者，道亦得之。
Those who align with the Dao,
the Dao also attains them.

同於德者，德亦得之。
Those who align with virtue,
virtue also attains them.

同於失者，失亦得之。
Those who align with loss,
loss also attains them.

河上公曰：与道同者，道亦乐得之。
**He Shang Gong** ["河上公"] says:
Those who align with the Dao,
the Dao also finds joy in attaining them.

与德同者，德亦乐得之。
Those who align with virtue,
virtue also finds joy in attaining them.

与失同者，失亦乐得之。
Those who align with loss,
loss also finds joy in attaining them.

信不足，有不信。
When trust is insufficient,
there is distrust.

锺会曰：我信不足於道，道亦不信应我，所以两相失。
**Zhong Hui** ["钟会"] says:
If my trust in the Dao is insufficient,
the Dao also does not trust in responding to me—
thus we mutually lose each other.

只是同於道者，道得之。
Only those who align with the Dao
are attained by the Dao.

信於道者，道信之。
Those who trust in the Dao,
the Dao trusts them.

同於失者，道失之。
Those who align with loss,
the Dao abandons them.

信不足，有不信也。
When trust is insufficient,
there is distrust.

此章以希言为主。
This chapter takes *sparse speech* ["希言"] as its central theme.

希言则以道而言也。
*Sparse speech* ["希言"] means speaking in accord with the Dao.

道偶而应，故合自然。
The Dao responds only when fitting—
thus it aligns with nature.

故下文云同於道者，道亦得之。
Thus, the following text states:
Those who align with the Dao,
the Dao also attains them.

同於失者，失亦得之。
Those who align with loss,
loss also attains them.

观其失得之本，皆言之希与多尔。
Observing the root of attaining or losing,
it is all about the sparseness or excess of speech.

多言之人，外则招愆，内则耗气，
Those who speak excessively
invite external mistakes,
and internally deplete their qi.

人欲长久，希言内守。
If one seeks to endure long,
*sparse speech* ["希言"] must be inwardly upheld.

跂者不立章第二十四 跂者不立，
**Chapter Twenty-Four: The Unestablished Do Not Stand**

The *unestablished* ["跂者"] do not stand.

跨者不行。
The *overreaching* ["跨者"] do not walk.

「To take *virtue/power* ["德"] as one’s guide,
then all who have sufficiency shall arrive.

The *unestablished* ["跂者"] are hasty to stand,
the *overreaching* ["跨者"] are hasty to walk—
both deviate from the natural way of standing and walking,
thus they cannot endure.
Hence, though they stand, they do not stand,
though they walk, they do not walk.
Those who establish their person and practice the Dao
must not seek swiftness—
to follow its natural course,
then their person stands firm and the Dao is practiced.

The *self-perceiving* ["自见者"] lack clarity—
Shu Wang says: The *self-perceiving* ["自见者"] lack clarity.
Thus, those previously called *not self-perceiving* ["不自见者"]
are the ones who see all things.

The *self-righteous* ["自是者"] are not manifest—
He Shang Gong says: Those who insist on their own correctness
and reject others’ views
are obscured by the multitude,
thus they fail to shine.

The *self-praising* ["自伐者"] achieve no merit—
Gu Xuan says: When one undertakes deeds and aids beings
yet claims the credit for oneself,
once the name is taken,
the merit lies not with the deeds.

The *self-conceited* ["自矜者"] do not grow.
Guo Xiang says: Boasting and arrogance,
not forgetting one’s own merits,
earn the disapproval of others,
thus they do not flourish.

As for the Dao,
it is said: *Excessive consumption, superfluous action* ["余食赘行"].

*Excessive consumption* ["余食"] brings illness—
*Superfluous action* ["赘行"] brings encumbrance.
Things may hate it,
thus those who embody the Dao do not dwell in it.

*Excessive consumption* ["余食"] is when one’s nature overflows—
Heaven’s deficiency, Earth’s transformation,
and the hatred of all beings.
Thus those who embody the Dao do not dwell in it.

**Chapter Twenty-Five: The Undivided Unity of Things**

Things are undivided in unity ["有物混成"],
born before Heaven and Earth.

*Excessive consumption* ["余食"] is when one’s nature overflows—
*Superfluous action* ["赘行"] is when one’s deeds exceed their purpose.
The Dao’s true essence stands alone,
unpaired with things,
enduring through all ages without flaw,
permanent from ancient to modern times.

It moves in cycles without depletion ["周行而不殆"],
for its use is boundless in entering and exiting,
pervading all six voids ["六虚"].
The Dao’s wondrous function
is everywhere without end,
never exhausted.

As the *Yijing* says:
*"In constant motion, it does not dwell;
flowing through the six voids,
it can be the mother of all under Heaven."*

I know not its name,
so I call it Dao.
He Shang Gong says: I do not see the form of the Dao,
so I know not how to name it—
seeing all things arise from the Dao,
thus I call it Dao.

I forcefully name it *Great* ["大"].
Tang Ming Huang says: I see things born in unity ["有物生成"],
hidden without a proper name,
so I use *pervasive birth* ["逝"] to mark its virtue,
calling it Dao—
to encompass all without limit.
Its essence is forceful,
thus it is named *Great* ["大"].

Lu Ji Fu says: The Dao as a thing,
when applied fills the boundless void ["太虚"],
yet when abandoned is nowhere to be found—
how could *Great* suffice to name it?
It is merely a forced name.

*Great* ["大"] is called *Passing* ["逝"]—
*Passing* ["逝"] means *going* ["往"].
Though all things are great,
greatness has limits and a resting place.
This Dao’s greatness passes without bounds,
its origin is beyond what can be fully grasped.

Shu Wang says: The great
though beyond the six directions ["六合"],
cannot escape its influence.
The smallest speck
cannot evade its subtlety.
Thus *Great* is called *Passing* ["逝"].

*Passing* ["逝"] is called *Far* ["远"]—
*Far* ["远"] is called *Return* ["反"].

Shu Wang says: When *Far* reaches its extreme,
it returns to the *Uncarved* ["朴"].
Thus *Far* is called *Return* ["反"].
*Return* ["反"] means returning to the origin.
When harmony is complete and fills the six voids ["六虚"],
it is called *Far* ["远"].
When near, it does not depart from oneself,
thus it is called *Return* ["反"].

*Far* ["远"] is beyond the *Unbounded* ["无极"],
endless in its extent.
*Near* ["近"] is within oneself—
humans do not perceive it.

The Dao’s essence is strong,
thus it is named *Great* ["大"].」

Thus the Dao is Great, the Heaven is Great, the Earth is Great, and the King is also Great.

*Commentary*: The Dao encompasses and sustains Heaven and Earth. Heaven covers all without limit, Earth bears all without measure, and the King stands between them, harmonizing with Heaven and Earth. Thus, the King is also Great.

The Dao encompasses all things. It wraps Heaven and Earth, vast beyond bounds, hence it is said that the Dao is Great. Heaven and Earth are the Great in form. Heaven is boundlessly broad, beyond measure, and Earth is boundlessly vast, beyond calculation. Hence, it is said that Heaven is Great and Earth is Great.

Under all Heaven, there is nothing not the King’s soil. At the edges of all lands, there is nothing not the King’s subjects. The King’s virtue aligns with Heaven and Earth, his Dao flows through the Three Realms ["三才"], and none surpasses the Emperor and King in greatness. Thus, the King is also Great.

Within the realm ["域"], there are four Great things, and the King occupies one of them.

*Commentary*: From the Dao descending, there arises form and substance, hence it is said that within the realm, stillness is the Dao’s, while action is the Sage’s. The King can offer tribute to the Three Realms and harmonize them, making human conduct supreme. Thus, he shares substance with the Dao and function with Heaven and Earth, and is likewise called Great. The King is the culmination of human conduct.

The Dao was never confined by boundaries, yet this passage speaks of the realm ["域"], meaning though it is confined, it wraps all things without limit.

Humans model themselves after Earth, Earth models itself after Heaven, Heaven models itself after the Dao, and the Dao models itself after Nature ["自然"].

Shu Wang says: Humans model themselves after Earth—this is the King’s greatness. Earth models itself after Heaven—this is Earth’s greatness. Heaven models itself after the Dao—this is Heaven’s greatness. The Dao models itself after Nature—this is the Dao’s greatness. For Nature ["自然"] is as if it transcends causes and conditions ["因缘"]. Neither cause nor condition, nor yet Nature. Yet the Dao’s Nature, when viewed by the self-cultivator ["自学者"], is called *Mysterious* ["妙"]. When observed by Laozi, it still retains traces of causes and conditions. Yet Laozi does not fail to grasp the Dao’s profound essence, nor does he exhaust the law within the law. Hence it is said: the Dao models itself after Nature.
