Humans speak of the King. Humans model themselves after Earth’s steadfast stillness, thus without action ["无为"], all under Heaven is accomplished. Earth models itself after Heaven’s non-action, thus without growth ["不长"], all things are nourished. Heaven models itself after the Dao’s Nature, thus without giving birth ["不产"], all things transform. The Dao is self-originating, self-rooted; before Heaven and Earth existed, it endured from antiquity, having nothing to model itself after. That which has no model is simply Nature itself. Hence it is said: the Dao models itself after Nature.

This chapter speaks of the *Mixed and Complete* Dao ["混成之道"], which precedes Heaven and Earth in birth. Its substance is lofty and independent, its function flows through the six voids ["六虚"], beyond description. Though the name *Great* is applied, it is still said.

Though the Two Principles ["二仪"] are lofty and profound, and the King’s authority is supreme, all defer to the Dao.

The Dao is self-originating, self-rooted, without causes or conditions, yet naturally so.

**Chapter Twenty-Six: The Heavy as the Root of the Light**

The heavy is the root of the light,
the still is the master of the restless.

River Upper Master ["河上公"] says: If the sovereign does not hold weight ["重"], he will not be revered. If governing the body does not hold weight, the spirit will be lost. Grass and trees, leaves and flowers are light, hence they wither and fall. The root is heavy, hence it endures long.

If the sovereign is not still, his authority will be lost; if governing the body is not still, the body will be in peril. The dragon is still, hence it can transform and vary. The tiger is restless, hence it is cut down prematurely.

Lü Jifu ["吕吉甫"] says: The light is first to respond ["先感"], the heavy is last to react ["后应"]. That which reacts is born from what responds, hence the heavy is the root of the light.

The still governs things ["役物"], the restless is governed by things ["役於物"]. The restless is often governed by the still, hence the still is the master of the restless.

Liu Zhongping ["刘仲平"] says: The light arises from the heavy, hence the heavy is the root of the light. The restless arises from stillness, hence the still is the master of the restless.

Thus the virtuous person ["君子"] walks all day without straying from his burden ["辎重"]. Though he has splendid views ["荣观"], his repose ["燕处"] is serene and detached.

*Commentary*: Splendid views are in things ["物"], repose is in the self ["身"]. Only when the self is at ease can things bring joy. Walking is swift with lightness, yet one cannot walk without a burden. Observing is glorious with restlessness, yet one cannot observe without repose. Having the burden of the burdened, yet not hindered in the Dao, then one can walk. Having the repose of repose, yet serene and self-sufficient, then one can observe. Hence it is said: the heavy is the root of the light, the still is the master of the restless.

How can the ruler of ten thousand chariots ["万乘之主"] bear a light body ["身轻"] while weighing the world ["天下"]?

Lü Jifu ["吕吉甫"] says: Even the virtuous person’s daily walk and his splendid views are thus, how much more the ruler of ten thousand chariots, bearing heavy burdens ["任重"] and traveling distant roads ["道远"], surveying all under Heaven ["观天下"]—can he not be still and heavy?

Forced to move, then he reacts ["动"].
Sensate and then he responds ["感"].
Unable to help himself, then he rises ["起"]—
this is the heavy.

Without action ["无为"], then he is still ["静"].
If his movement is always at the point of compulsion ["得已"], and he cannot be without action, then he bears a light body while weighing the world, yet is not heavy. Not heavy, then he is restless, and not still.

Lu Tian ["陆佃"] says: All under Heaven ["天下"] is the sovereign’s burden and also his repose—what cannot be separated.

Thus it is said: How can the ruler of ten thousand chariots bear a light body while weighing the world? Lightness will lose his subjects ["臣"], restlessness will lose his authority ["君"].

*Commentary*: Not heavy, then he lacks authority ["威"], hence he loses his subjects. If he does not lead yet seeks harmony ["不唱而和"], he violates proper order ["犯分"], hence he loses his authority.

Lu Tian ["陆佃"] says: The heavy is the sovereign’s virtue ["德"], the still is the subject’s conduct ["道"].

Song Lingxian ["松灵仙"] says: If the heart is in turmoil ["动乱"], it will harm the *Mysterious Spirit* ["妙神"]. The spirit is the sovereign ["神即君也"]. This chapter warns the sovereign to take stillness and weight as his foundation.

All under Heaven ["天下"] is the sovereign’s stillness and weight. How can it be lightened with one’s own body?

Those who cultivate the True ["修真之士"] also take stillness and weight as their foundation: they weigh their body yet do not burden it with things ["不以物累形"], they still their heart yet do not let things disturb their spirit ["不以物乱神"]. Body intact, spirit settled—this is the Dao’s completeness ["全"].

**Chapter Twenty-Seven: The Good Way**

The good way leaves no tracks ["无辙迹"].

Shu Wang ["舒王"] says: The good way does not hasten ["不疾"], yet is swift ["速"]. The bad way does not move ["不行"], yet arrives ["至"]. Thus it is said.

「Thus, there are no tracks.

Lu Yu says: To act in accordance with the Dao, one’s traces do not differ from things, hence there are no tracks.

Zhong Hui says: The practitioner of the good way has no visible achievements in virtue and name.

Su Ziyou says: To ride upon principle and act leaves no traces. A carriage moves and leaves tracks, a lone traveler leaves footprints—so action indeed cannot lack traces. Yet the practitioner of the good way seeks it within oneself without stepping beyond one’s courtyard; their action emerges from non-action, hence there are no tracks—this is what is meant by "non-action yet arrival."

The good speech has no flaws or censure;

Du Guangting says: The practitioner of good speech attains the meaning and forgets the words.
Thus, there is no disease of blemishes or offense of reproach.

Lü Jifu says: The wise do not speak, and those who speak lack wisdom.
So speech indeed cannot lack flaws or censure.
To know why one speaks, speech arises from non-speech—hence it is said: The good speech has no flaws or censure.

The good plan requires no calculations or reckoning;
The Imperial Commentary says: To understand unity, all affairs are completed—how much more so for what is not mere numbers! Thus, without using calculations or reckoning, one adapts to myriad changes as though counting one and two.

The good closure needs no bolts or bars yet cannot be opened;
Shu Wang says: The practitioner of good closure allows no thing to enter through its gate, hence there are no bolts or bars yet it cannot be opened.
Wen Gong says: A well-governed state does not rely on the natural barriers of mountains and rivers.
The nine orifices are the gates of spirit and vitality.
The practitioner of good closure guards their spirit internally, preventing it from dissipating outward.
Though internally there may be no bolts or bars, how could it be opened?

The good binding needs no cords or constraints yet cannot be undone;
Gu Xuan says: To bind with the Dao, though lacking cords, it is self-secured. To bind with things, though having constraints, it is not firm.
Thus, to constrain others with coercive force, even three armies may desert.
To bind with the Dao, even a single man’s resolve is hard to seize.
Wen Gong says: To confine the people, one does not rely on the boundaries of sealed borders.
Du Guangting says: The practitioner of good conduct forgets all concerns, with no second thought; their stillness aligns with the Dao, merging with the True.
Not even myriad green things can encroach, nor all temptations seduce—this is called good binding, how could it be undone?

Therefore, the Sage always practices good rescue, hence there are none abandoned.
Cai Zihuang says: To benefit others is good.
Hong Ji says: To rescue all beings in the ten directions, one must first establish their own land.
The flow of good is ever enveloped in mystery, its blessings evenly spread, nurturing all life, returning to the Dao’s source.
The vulgar and deceitful, Emperor Shun taught them yet did not abandon.
The empty and foolish, Confucius exhausted all explanations.
Always practicing good rescue, there are none abandoned.

The Imperial Commentary says: Good is the Dao’s continuation in the unseen.
The Dao itself cannot be named as good.
When good is named and established, the Dao departs and good becomes the world.
The Sage embodies the Dao to benefit All-Under-Heaven, hence these five goods.
Yet, in the case of people and things, none are abandoned.

However, the Sage’s way of loving people and benefiting things, letting them fulfill their nature while humans find joy in theirs, is not arbitrary.
To invert unity is to lose the Dao; to cling to the constant yet neglect the Dao is to abandon its path.
All-Under-Heaven has lost its tranquil, still, and ever-present nature, daily sinking into the realm of sorrow and peril—who but the Sage could rescue them?

The *New Sayings* state: All things have their natural principles; indeed, there are those who defy and reverse them.
All things have their inherent nature; indeed, there are those who violate and shackle it.
All things have their proper fate; indeed, there are those who oppose and sever it.
The Sage, moved with compassion, thus employs their treasured virtue to rescue them.
To those who defy principle, they open what is blocked and make clear.
To those who violate nature, they remove what is harmful and bring ease.
To those who oppose fate, they restore what is severed and revive it.
This is called "inheriting clarity."

The Imperial Commentary says: To inherit is to reveal without display.
To inherit clarity is to shine without blinding.

Lü Jifu says: These five aspects—principle, nature, fate, and their shared essence—are what the Sage understands through constant wisdom, rescuing at the moment of shared truth.
Though they act, speak, plan, close, or bind, none know how it is done—
inherited clarity cannot be seen directly.
Hence it is said: This is called inheriting clarity.

Therefore, the good person is the teacher of the not-good.
The not-good person is the resource of the good.
The Imperial Commentary says: Resource refers to what is useful; there are not-good people.
Only then does one know good as beneficial.

Shu Wang says: The good person teaches the not-good.
Hence, the good person is the teacher of the not-good.
Without the not-good, one would not know good as good.
Hence, the not-good person is the resource of the good.
To not honor one’s teacher is to not cherish one’s resource.

Su Ziyou says: The Sage has no personal intent in teaching, hence does not cherish their resource.
All-Under-Heaven has no intent in learning, hence does not honor their teacher.
The Sage does not merely forget All-Under-Heaven; they also make All-Under-Heaven forget them.

Liezi, taught by Laozi, befriended Boyi, and attained the Way of the Immortal Wind.
After nine years, he also did not recognize his master as his teacher nor his friend as his friend—
his inner and outer progress was complete.
This is called not honoring one’s teacher.
Confucius, upon meeting Lord Shang of Yin, then declined further association and dismissed his disciples—
this is called not cherishing one’s resource.
Though knowing the great confusion, it is called the ultimate mystery.

The Imperial Commentary says: The Dao’s ultimate mystery lies in not perceiving the myriad goods,
having no need for cleverness or calculation.
The Seven Sages were all in confusion, yet none questioned their path—
this meaning converges here.

Li Zhan says: This chapter speaks of the Sage’s subtle and hidden use of virtue,
whose manifestations are imperceptible.
Though it is said they always practice good,
the mystery lies in their simultaneous forgetting—
thus they can teach and speak.
What they do not explain through intricate reasoning
has no means of reckoning or tracing.
This is the good teacher and resource as a path for gradual cultivation,
cutting off all attachment to enter the gate of sudden enlightenment,
aligning with inherited clarity to rescue all things.»

**The Daoist Canon: *Taking the Good* (Volume 5) – Collected Commentaries of Song Resident Scholar Li Lin**

**Chapter 28: Knowing the Heroic, Holding the Feminine**

Know its heroic, hold its feminine.

Du Guangting says: In the Upper Clarity Dao, there is the method of the One Feminine, and also the Three Chases and Five Feminines—all softening the will, harmonizing the spirit, to attain longevity.

It becomes the valley of all under Heaven.

Wang Yuanzhe says: A valley receives and channels all flowing waters. To hold the feminine is to remain in emptiness and stillness, receiving all things without obstruction, hence it is the valley of all under Heaven.

The heroic moves, the feminine is still. The Perfect One knows its movement yet does not follow it, always holding stillness without abandoning the True. Thus, it is said to be the valley below.

The Dao comes and returns to me like water flowing into a deep valley, hence it is the valley of all under Heaven.

It becomes the valley of all under Heaven, its constant virtue never departing.

Lü Jifu says: A valley receives from the ravine and delivers to the rivers and seas—receiving without resistance, delivering without accumulation. It is the thing that flows through without obstruction.

To flow through is to have constant virtue never departing.

Wang Yuanzhe says: Constant virtue is fixed and unchanging within me. "Not departing" means it remains within its nature, internal and not external.

It returns to the infant.

Wang Yuanzhe says: The infant holds harmony and unity, with no desires or thoughts yet formed. Its nature is pure and undispersed, its virtue profound—thus it aligns with the original.

Such is worthy of the name Great.

Mencius said: The Great Person does not lose the heart of the infant.

The feminine governs gentleness and stillness.

The infant’s bones are weak, its sinews soft; its *qi* is not violent, its desires and thoughts not yet formed. Its virtue is unchanging—this is the depth of contained virtue, like the infant’s purity.

Know its white, hold its black.

Wang Yuanzhe says: Black is the color of the North, where stillness cannot be fully described. The Sage establishes all things; without holding black, there is no foundation. This is verified by the emergence of spring and summer from the dark winter.

It becomes the model of all under Heaven.

Lü Jifu says: White, in color, is receptive; in things, it is brightness; in action, it is metal; in numbers, it is four. Black, in color, is untainted; in things, it is obscurity; in action, it is water; in numbers, it is one.

To know white and hold black is to receive no taint from all things. Like obscurity, like water, it ultimately embraces the One. Embracing the One, it can bend and yield, sink and be depleted—thus it can be the model of all under Heaven.

White is clarity, untouched by things. Black is silent stillness, in numbers the One.

The Perfect One knows its clarity yet holds silent stillness in the One. All under Heaven follows and emulates this, hence it is the model of all under Heaven.

It becomes the model of all under Heaven, its constant virtue never erring.

Lü Jifu says: To be the model of all under Heaven is to be One in all things, thus its constant virtue never errs.

"Not departing" means it does not stray from its original place, yet this does not guarantee it will not err. To not err is to not deviate.

It returns to the Boundless.

Lü Jifu says: The infant is a thing that focuses *qi* and softens, preserving its unity without loss.

To return to the Boundless, the infant is insufficient to describe it.

Luóshī says: *Tè* means to fail or lose. If one abandons wisdom and holds foolishness, acts in accord with spontaneity, and virtue aligns with action, becoming the model for all things—manifesting in action yet returning to the Dao—since the Dao is inexhaustible, it attains the Boundless.

One is the Boundless of wisdom, two is the Boundless of life.

Know its glory, hold its humiliation.

Du Guangting says: Glory is exalted honor. Humiliation is lowly shame. A ruler possesses the eight extremes, dominating the nine boundaries—this is exalted honor. To call oneself "lonely," "widowed," or "not-grain" is lowly shame.

It becomes the valley of all under Heaven.

Shū Wáng says: To know glory and hold humiliation is to guard with humility and respond well, hence it is the valley of all under Heaven.

It becomes the valley of all under Heaven, its constant virtue now complete.

Liu Zhongping says: A valley can be empty and responsive, contain and receive—thus its constant virtue is complete.

It returns to the Uncarved.

Su Shiru says: Not knowing yet not acting is inferior to knowing yet not acting to the utmost.

To know the heroic and hold the feminine is to know nature. To know white and hold black is to see nature. To know glory and hold humiliation is to restore nature.

When all delusions are exhausted, dwelling in humiliation without regret, vast like the emptiness of a valley—things come and it responds—virtue is complete here, pure nature without mixture.

Thus it is said: It returns to the Uncarved.

Sōnglíng Xiān says: *Zú* means sufficiency. *Fù* is return. *Pǔ* is the true origin.

At first, knowing the heroic ends with holding humiliation.

The three practices of holding humiliation are complete as the essence of the Dao. Like an empty valley with nothing to contain, this is how constant virtue attains supreme virtue, thus it returns to true emptiness, merging with the Dao. Hence it is said: It returns to the Uncarved.

Grasses and trees flourish as glory, wither as humiliation. What makes humans glory or humiliation is no different.

The Perfect One knows glory yet does not dwell in it, discards ornamentation and returns to the root, always holding humiliation—dwelling in what others despise, unburdened, for it is empty. Hence it is the valley of all under Heaven.

The valley is empty and receives, responds yet does not hoard. Virtue reaches this peak—exhausted, beyond addition.

Thus it is said: Its constant virtue is complete.

The Uncarved is the complete body of the Dao. To return to the Uncarved is to fully embody the Dao.

The titles "lonely," "widowed," "not-grain" are used by kings and dukes, hence embracing the Uncarved, all under Heaven returns. To return to the Uncarved is beyond the Boundless—this is the nameless Uncarved.

The Uncarved, though small, none under Heaven can subdue it.
Thus, to guard the female, guard the dark, guard humiliation is sufficient.
Why bother knowing the male, the white, or the glory?

To guard is to hold the Dao as mother;
To know is to grasp it as child.
To guard is to uphold the constant;
To know is to embrace the change.

The Uncarved disperses into vessels.
**Commentary:** That which transcends form is called the Dao; that which falls under form is called vessels.
Vessels are the dispersion of the Dao.
They have shape and name, they have division and governance—
Use them according to their purpose.

**Zhuangzi’s Disciple Zhong Hui said:**
The Uncarved is the Dao.
To guard it is the Dao of sincerity;
To disperse it is the vessel nurturing humanity.

The Sage employs it,
And thus becomes the ruler and the official.
**Commentary:** The fullness of the Dao,
The Sage uses to govern himself.
Its dispersion,
The Sage uses to benefit all under Heaven.

With form to be named,
With division to be governed—
Thus, he assigns roles and orders attributes,
And all under Heaven is harmonized—this is called the ruler and the official.

*The Classic of Changes* says:
Know the subtle, know the manifest,
Know the soft, know the hard.
The expectation of ten thousand men,
Is of the same meaning.

**Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said:**
When virtue is contained and internally harmonized, it returns to the Uncarved.
When constant virtue is applied, it disperses into vessels.
Once involved in form and vessels,
There must be refinement and coarseness—
Thus, the Sage employs them,
And becomes the ruler of the common folk.

Hence, the great governance does not sever.

**Su Shi said:**
The Sage, having returned to the Uncarved,
Then disperses the Uncarved into vessels,
To respond to all things—
Like a sovereign dividing authority,
To establish rulers and officials,
Also following the natural course of affairs,
Though governed, there is no severing or fragmentation.

**Cao Daochong said:**
The greatest of institutions
Lacks any trace of severing or fragmentation.
From this chapter’s meaning,
Constant virtue is the root—
If one guards its constancy,
Then knowing it must be its change.

Thus, knowing the male yet guarding the female,
Its virtue does not stray;
Knowing the white yet guarding the dark,
Its virtue does not falter;
Knowing the glory yet guarding the humiliation,
Its virtue is complete—
To this point, it is not false,
But simply returns to the state of infancy.

With no end, it shares the same body as the Dao.

The Dao endures through antiquity and the present, always existing.
Virtue aligns with the Dao—
This is what is called the constant.

This is the true Dao for governing oneself;
The rest, for benefiting all under Heaven,
Each follows its own capacity in use.

Hence it is said: The great governance does not sever.

**Chapter Twenty-Nine: On the Will to Govern**

When one is about to take all under Heaven and act upon it,
I see it as something he cannot avoid.

**Zang Xuanjing said:**
"Will" is the act of willing;
"To govern" is the act of forcing—
When one is about to force and willing,
He takes all under Heaven by might,
And governs it with contrivance.

**Wang Yuanzhe said:**
"Take" is to take things as if they were his own.
"Govern" is to contrive and act as if he were the maker.
Those who have themselves and contrive,
Are still caught between worry and peril—
How can they have leisure to govern others?

**Su Shi said:**
The Sage’s possession of all under Heaven
Is not a taking.
All things return to him,
And he receives them as unavoidable.
His governance of all under Heaven
Is not a contriving.
He follows the natural course of all things,
And only removes their harm.

To take and govern is impossible.
The world is a divine vessel—
**Commentary:** It is constrained by form and number,
Bound by square and round,
And confined within the expanse of the heavens and earth.
It stands in the unfathomable,
Acts without fixed direction.
To govern it leads to ruin,
To grasp it leads to loss—
Thus it is called the divine vessel.

To rule all things,
To command all movement,
There must be those who are not vessels—
Only then can all under Heaven be governed.
Hence it is said:
The ruler must act without contrivance,
Yet employ all under Heaven.

**Zhuangzi said:**
The Dao of Heaven wandered in the Yin Yang,
Reached the upper reaches of the Leaving Waters,
And encountered a nameless man,
Whom he questioned, saying:
"May I ask about governing all under Heaven?"
The nameless man said:
"Go! Why should a vulgar man like you ask about that which is not fitting?
To govern is to ruin,
To grasp is to lose."

**Wen Gong said:**
To govern is to harm the natural;
To grasp is to obstruct the flow of change.
The world is a divine and luminous vessel—
It cannot be forced by might,
Nor can it be rigidly grasped.
All things have nature as their essence,
Thus it can be followed yet not governed,
It can be guided yet not rigidly grasped.
To contrive against their constant nature,
Thus it must lead to ruin.
To grasp their coming and going,
Thus it must lead to loss.

Hence, things sometimes move forward,
And sometimes follow behind—
Like form and shadow never parting.
Sometimes warm to bring heat,
And sometimes cold to bring chill—
Like the seasons generating each other.
Knowing strength alone,
Then weakness and infirmity must come at times.
Knowing flourishing alone,
Then decline and destruction must follow at hand.

These are all the great emotions of creation,
The daily states of morning and evening,
The interplay of events and circumstances—
It cannot be otherwise.

Then how can one govern and grasp it?

Thus, the Sage avoids excess,
Avoids extravagance,
Avoids arrogance.

**He Shang Gong said:**
Excess refers to indulging in food, sound, and color;
Extravagance refers to clothing, adornment, and drink;
Arrogance refers to palaces, halls, and pavilions.

Go beyond these three [excesses], dwell in moderation and act without contriving—
then the world will transform itself naturally.

**Lu Tian** said:
The Sage, regarding the world,
follows its course without forcing,
trusts its unfolding without grasping—
thus he avoids excess through kindness,
avoids extravagance through frugality,
avoids arrogance by never presuming to lead the world.
These three are how one attains the world.

This chapter speaks of how the world cannot be seized by force,
cannot be taken by contrivance.
If one were to forcefully seize it,
how could one know the Sage acts only when compelled?
If one governs by contriving,
how could one know the Sage’s way of ruling without contriving?

Thus, those who act contrive will fail,
those who grasp will lose.
Therefore, the Sage understands the reversals of the Eight Laws,
avoids the excesses of the Three Matters,
and thus the nation’s fate extends long,
and self-cultivation brings enduring life and vision.

**Chapter Thirty: Assisting the Sovereign with the Dao**

To assist the sovereign with the Dao means
not to coerce the world with arms—
for such deeds return ill.

**Su Ziyou** said:
The Sage uses arms only when compelled,
not to force victory over the world.
Though victory may be won,
the calamity will return to haunt them.

The kings of Chu, Qi, Qin, and Han’s Emperor Wu—
some perished themselves,
some brought ruin to their descendants.
What men abhor,
what ghosts detest—
none escaped.

Great ministers serve the ruler with the Dao.
If they coerce the world with arms,
they assist the ruler with what is not his way.

To assist the sovereign with the Dao is already to refrain
from coercing the world with arms—
how much more so if the ruler himself embodies the Dao?
The reason is that such deeds invite retribution.

To subdue the world with the Dao,
then the world dares not disobey.
But if one raises arms against them,
those who kill their fathers will have their fathers killed,
those who kill their brothers will have their brothers killed—
as the saying goes: *"What you send forth returns to you."*

Where armies march, thorns and brambles grow.
After great armies, there are years of famine.
**Commentary:**
The lower classes are drained of strength—
thus thorns and brambles grow.
The upper classes defy the natural order—
thus there are years of famine.
*The Odes* say:
*"Soothing the myriad states brings abundant harvests."*
Soothing the myriad states brings harmony among people,
and harmony among people invites the harmony of Heaven and Earth.
When farming is neglected and fields left untended,
thorns and brambles grow.
When slaughter is excessive and *he qi* is injured,
there must be calamities from Heaven.
Neglect of farming precedes disaster,
and disaster follows—
there will surely be years of famine.
Thus, the virtuous achieve victory without force,
daring not to seize through strength.

**Lü Jifu** said:
*"Victory"* refers to defeating the enemy.
When the enemy is defeated,
the attackers lament their own ruin—
as in *"Zhòu attacks, and I lament from Bó"*—
this is called victory.
It arises from compulsion, not from seeking force.
*"Victory"* is the word for triumph.
Tang conquered Xia yet spoke of shameful virtue,
returned to Bó yet feared the precipice.
Victory without pride,
victory without boasting,
victory without arrogance,
victory born of compulsion—
this is victory without force.

**Chang Jifu** said:
Victory without pride in one’s ability,
victory without boasting of one’s deeds,
victory without arrogance in one’s power—
victory that always arises from compulsion—
this is the way of victory without force.
If one prides in ability,
boasts of deeds,
arrogates power,
then this is victory through force,
not victory born of compulsion.

**Things flourish then grow old,**
**Wang Yuanzhe** said:
The extreme leads to decline—
this is the inevitable nature of things.
In antiquity, those who met this fate were the Qin.

Things grow strong through reliance on arms.
Things flourish then decline,
arms grow strong then fail.
This is called *not-Dao*.
**Commentary:**
The Dao has no fixed beginning or end,
it does not align with the transformations of things.

**Wang Yuanzhe** said:
Those who embody the Dao
encompass all changes without dwelling in any single thing—
thus they have no thought of flourishing or decline.
*Not-Dao* perishes early.

**Lü Jifu** said:
All things wax then wane—
this is their nature.
The Dao values endurance,
softness as strength—
this is how one persists without peril.
If one relies on arms to coerce the world,
it is to abandon softness for strength.
Strength must decline,
and thus it is merely things—
how could this be the Dao’s way of transforming things?

Thus it is said:
*"Things flourish then grow old—
this is called not-Dao,
not-Dao perishes early."*
The Dao endures above antiquity without aging.
Those who attain the Dao can repel aging and preserve their form—
aging is *not-Dao*.
Just as arms grow strong then fail—
how could this align with the Dao?
Since it does not align with the Dao,
how could it endure?
Thus it is said: *"Perishes early."*

This chapter warns ministers:
to assist the ruler with the Dao,
one must not coerce the world with arms.
The Dao values soft persuasion,
not coercion through arms.
If one coerces the world to victory,
it is like things flourishing then declining—
how could this be the Dao?

**Chapter Thirty-One: Fine Arms**

Fine arms are an inauspicious instrument.
**Wen Gong** said:
The finer the arms, the greater the harm done.

**Zuan Wei** said:
Those who adorn spears, armor, and helmets as ornaments
revere what is not a benevolent instrument.
*"Fine"* means adorned,
*"auspicious"* means good.
Fine arms are strong armor and sharp blades.
Arms are a cruel instrument—
thus they should not be adorned.
Things may abhor them,
so those who follow the Dao do not dwell upon them.

**Commentary:**
Auspicious matters bring good fortune.
Arms are a cruel instrument—
thus they are called inauspicious.
When arms are stored yet mobilized in season,
those who follow the Dao display virtue without showing arms,
thus they do not dwell upon them.

**Shu Wang** said:
Fine arms are strong armor and sharp blades—
arms are a cruel instrument,
thus they are called an inauspicious instrument.
The previous chapter has already explained this in detail:
no thing escapes their cruel harm,
thus they are abhorred.

Those who follow the Dao take compassion as their heart, thus they do not dwell upon arms.

Arms are a cruel instrument.
When mobilized, all things abhor them—
thus those who follow the Dao must never dwell upon them.

The virtuous in peace honor the left,
in war honor the right.

**Gu Huān** said:
The left is Yang.
Yang is the Way of life, thus in peace it is honored.
The right is Yin.
Yin is the Way of killing, thus in war it is honored.

Arms are an inauspicious instrument,
not the instrument of the virtuous.

**御注:**
The left is Yang and governs life,
the right is Yin and governs killing.
Yang is virtue, Yin is punishment—
the virtuous honor virtue and fear punishment,
thus it is said: not the instrument of the virtuous.

**Wang Yuanzé** said:
To rule the realm is to act without action,
to govern the people is with compassion and benevolence—
thus arms are not revered.
When forced to use them,

**Zhuān Wēi** said:
One does not dare to be the aggressor but only the defender,
one does not dare to advance an inch but retreats a foot—
all are acts of necessity.

The skilled in arms respond only when provoked,
act only when pressed—
always arising from necessity.

Calm and still is the highest virtue.
To win without delighting in it,

**御注:**
To suppress tyranny and rescue chaos,
to act only when forced—
thus it is done out of necessity.
With no desire to conquer others,
it is said that calm and still is the highest virtue.
With no desire to conquer others,
arms are not a source of joy—
thus they are not delightful.

**Chéng Xuányīng** said:
Calm and still is non-action.
The virtuous heart cherishes non-action,
thus though arms are used,
they are not considered delightful.

**Zhang Jūnxiàng** said:
Calm is without disorder.
Still is a tranquil heart.
Calm and still is the Dao.
To hold the Dao as highest—
how could one delight in arms?

Those who delight in them
are those who take pleasure in killing.

**Wang Yuanzé** said:
Arms are meant for slaughter and destruction,
yet to adorn them excessively,
to make them beautiful and pleasing to behold—
this is to take pleasure in killing.
If one delights in arms,
then one delights in sending people to their deaths.

Those who take pleasure in killing
cannot achieve their desires in the world.

**Zhuān Wēi** said:
The cruel and violent who delight in killing
cannot be allowed to achieve their desires in the world.
If they do, they defy Heaven’s virtue,
unleashing slaughter at will,
treating the people as grass and chaff—
how could Heaven aid them?
If Heaven does not aid them,
how could they achieve their desires?

Those who take pleasure in killing
must not be allowed to achieve their desires in the world.
As rulers, one must not arbitrarily execute.
This is what Mencius meant by:
*"Only those who do not delight in killing can unite the people."*

Auspicious matters honor the left,
inauspicious matters honor the right.

**Chéng Xuányīng** said:
Auspicious refers to morning rites,
inauspicious refers to funeral rites.

**Hé Shànggōng** said:
The left is Yang, governing life,
the right is Yin, governing killing.
The General of the Left Wing is stationed on the left,
the General of the Right Wing on the right.

**Hé Shànggōng** said:
The General of the Left Wing is lowly yet occupies the Yang position,
for he does not wield killing alone.
The General of the Right Wing is exalted yet occupies the Yin position,
for he alone commands killing.
This is treated as funeral rites.

The momentum of war
places the right in the superior position—
thus it is treated as funeral rites.
When many are slain,
one mourns them with tears.

**Cáo Dàochōng** said:
All under Heaven are the ruler’s people—
by their obedience or defiance,
some are killed in great numbers—
thus one weeps and grieves for them.

**Yánggù** said:
It is made clear that this is not a delight.

When victorious in battle,
one treats it as funeral rites.

**御注:**
The *Yì* calls the army a poison to the world—
though victorious in battle,
there will always be those harmed by its poison,
thus the superior position is given to mourning,
as if the victor were in funeral rites.

**Hé Shànggōng** said:
In ancient times, when victorious in battle,
the general occupied the position of the funeral master,
wearing plain mourning garments and weeping.

The virtuous honor virtue and despise arms,
using them only when forced to punish inauspiciousness—
their hearts grieve as if in mourning.

**Lǚ Jífǔ** said:
To treat victory as funeral rites
makes it clear that arms are inauspicious
and not delightful.
To mourn with tears
makes it clear that one does not delight in killing.

**Lǐ Tián** said:
This chapter states that the virtuous should take morality as their village instrument,
not war and conquest as their achievements.
If one delights in arms and strength,
then killing will follow.
If not for funeral rites,
there would be no way to treat it.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Two: The Dao Ever Nameless**

The Dao ever nameless.

**Wang Bì** said:
The Dao has no form,
thus it cannot be named.
To be ever nameless is its constant—
thus it is said: the Dao ever nameless.

**Liú Zhòngpíng** said:
"Constant" refers to an eternal, unchanging designation.
The height of Heaven,
the depth of Earth,
the brilliance of the sun and moon—
though great in nurturing life,
they endure through the ages,
yet are not ultimately unchangeable.
Their forms appear above—
thus I may call them *Qián*.
Their patterns manifest below—
thus I may call them *Kūn*.
Thus, the luminous symbols descending—
I may call them sun and moon.
These three are not beyond what I name,
thus they are not nameless.

But the Dao—
its ebb and flow, its fullness and emptiness,
faint and insubstantial,
yet not in communion with anything.
Ancient and modern succeed one another in the boundless,
as if naturally self-aware.
Neither Yin nor Yang,
neither soft nor hard,
neither small nor great,
neither round nor square,
neither white nor black,
neither *gōng* nor *shāng*—
seen, yet unseen,
heard, yet unheard,
grasped, yet unattainable—
this is called the ever nameless constant.

Though crude and seemingly small,
the world does not dare to enslave it.

**Hé Shànggōng** said:
Though the Dao is crude and small,
subtle and formless,
the world does not dare to enslave those who follow it.
Crude is the complete essence of the Dao,
before anything began.
Though small, it suffices as sovereign over all things.
If kings and lords could guard it,
all things would willingly submit.

「Lǚ Jífǔ said:
Why does the Dao/Way endow all beings with their beginning,
and why do all things rely on it to be born?
Then, who in the world would dare to enslave their own origin and their own source of life?

This is called the **true sovereign**.
All things possess a true sovereign—
this is what it means.

If kings and lords could guard it,
then they would rule all things with the true sovereign,
and who would there be among all things
that obtained the true sovereign yet did not submit?

The Dao/Way is the master of all things.
If kings and lords uphold it,
then without resorting to coercive force or incentives of reward,
things would not know how, yet they would willingly submit.

The heavens and earth unite,
and sweet dew descends.
No one commands it,
yet all is balanced.

**Imperial Annotation**:
The pure and undifferentiated Dao/Way,
guarded without loss,
not only ensures that all things submit,
but also that it reaches the heavens above
and coils beneath the earth below,
flowing in harmony with the heavens and earth—
thus, all things are interconnected and unified,
and all are nourished by its grace.

Sweet dew is the harmonious *qi* of the heavens and earth.
*The Classic of Changes* says:
The virtue/power of emperors and kings,
ascends to the **Tàiqīng** (Supreme Clarity),
descends to the **Tàiníng** (Supreme Tranquility),
and permeates all living beings—
then sweet dew descends.

**Wang Yuanzé** said:
If one guards the nameless essence of the Dao/Way
as the foundation of governance,
then the rising and falling of Yin and Yang
will proceed according to their natural order,
and the harmonious *qi* will respond accordingly.

Sweet dew is the product of Yin and Yang’s mutual harmony,
naturally and evenly bestowed
without any need for command—
it is the Dao/Way’s influence,
reaching everywhere without fail.

**Mencius** said:
To flow in harmony with the heavens and earth above and below—
is this not the way of the Dao/Way?
This is what it means.

These two interpretations explain
how kings and lords, if they guard the Dao/Way,
will see the heavens descend sweet dew as an auspicious sign.

**Wang Bì** said:
When the heavens and earth unite,
sweet dew descends without being sought.
If I guard its true nature and take no action,
then the people will not be commanded yet will be balanced.

**Wén Gōng** said:
Kings and lords who guard the Dao/Way
will see things submit,
*qi* harmonize,
and the people transform.

**Lǚ Jífǔ** said:
The utmost Yin is solemn and still,
the utmost Yang is radiant and vast—
the solemn arises from the heavens,
the radiant emerges from the earth.
When these two interact,
harmony is formed and things are born.

It is called the **measure** (*jì*), yet no one sees its form.
When the heavens and earth unite to descend sweet dew,
this is the peak of harmony.
Kings and lords wield the Dao/Way,
and all things submit to it—
that is all there is to it.
Who can truly see its form?

Thus, no one commands it,
yet all is balanced.

**Sū Zǐyóu** said:
The undifferentiated *qi* ascends and descends,
uniting to form one—
and sweet dew descends,
wetting all things uniformly without exception.

The sage embodies the utmost Dao/Way
to respond to all existence,
just as sweet dew reaches everywhere—
this is how they can govern all things.

These four interpretations explain
how the sage embodies the Dao/Way,
and all things submit to it,
just as sweet dew reaches everywhere.

At first, the Dao/Way was nameless.
Once names were established,
then one would also know where to stop.

To know where to stop
is the means of avoiding peril.

**Cài Zǐhuǎng** said:
If one knows where to stop,
returning from the end of named things
to the root of the nameless,
then one neither dies nor lives—
this is the way of avoiding peril.

Named things are the scattered manifestations of the Dao/Way.
When names were first established,
one should immediately know where to stop,
and return to the nameless essence of the Dao/Way—
then one will not follow things’ transformations,
calmly self-sufficient,
and free from all peril.

It is like the Dao/Way’s presence in the world,
comparable to streams and valleys converging with rivers and seas.

**Imperial Annotation**:
The world is of one nature.
The Dao/Way’s presence in the world
unites through nature,
just as streams and valleys gather with rivers and seas
through water.
Those of the same nature are drawn to it,
those of the same kind respond to it—
what need has the sage of governing?
They simply follow nature.

**Lù Yù** said:
Streams and valleys return to the sea,
yet the sea does not summon them.
The people return to the Dao/Way,
yet the Dao/Way does not contrive to gather them.

**Wang Yuanzé** said:
Rivers and seas do not seek water to return to them,
because they are the **reservoir** (*zhōng*).
When water accumulates in abundance,
those with the same nature go to it.
The Dao/Way is the nature of the people.
If the sage gathers its purity and completeness,
then all living beings will submit to it,
for this is their nature.

**Sū Zǐyóu** said:
Rivers and seas are the reservoir of water.
Streams and valleys are its divisions.
The Dao/Way is the **origin** (*zōng*) of all things.
All things are its branches.
All are water,
so streams and valleys return to their reservoir.
All are the Dao/Way,
so all things submit to their origin.

This chapter speaks of kings and lords
guarding the Dao/Way,
and all things submitting to them,
just as the heavens descend sweet dew,
and all is balanced without command.

The Dao/Way is what all people share,
the same nature they all possess.
Kings and lords first grasp
what all people share in nature—
then the world will not submit to them,
yet they will go to it,
just as water returns to the sea,
naturally and without effort.

**Chapter Thirty-Three on Knowing People**:
To know people is wisdom—」

**Chapter Thirty-Three on Knowing People** (continued):

「The **Commentary** says: Wisdom is like the eye—
able to see a hundred paces distant yet unable to perceive its own lashes.
To discern the crooked and straight in others,
as clear as distinguishing white from black,
is the work of wisdom—
to know people alone.
To know oneself is **enlightenment** (*míng*).

The **Commentary** says: *"Returning to know oneself"* (*fù yǐ zì zhī*).
The **Commentary** says: *"Inner vision"* (*nèi shì*) is called enlightenment.

Wisdom in knowing others
leads to conflict with those you meet,
daily battling with the heart.
But returning to know oneself
is to remain still and reflect upon the **root** (*běn*),
to perceive oneself alone.
The **balance** (*yíng*) of Heaven and Earth,
the **mirror** (*jìng*) of all things.

Duke Wen of Song said: *"Self-knowledge and self-mastery are especially difficult."*

Master He Shang said: *"When a person can know their own worth—whether virtuous or otherwise—
this is called *receptive listening without sound* (*fǎn tīng wú shēng*), *inner vision without form* (*nèi shì wú xíng*).
Thus, they attain enlightenment."*

Chéng Xuán Yīng said: *"To illuminate and discern the realms before one,
to mirror others—
whether their nature is vast or shallow,
whether their understanding is deep or shallow—
this is wisdom.
To know one’s own destiny,
the retribution of good and evil across the three ages (*sān shì*),
without any obscurity—
this is enlightenment.
The **Scripture** speaks of *seeing the subtle as enlightenment* (*jiàn xiǎo yuē míng*).
The subtle refers to the **Dao/Way’s** subtle essence.
Again, *to know constancy is enlightenment* (*zhī cháng yuē míng*).
Constancy refers to the **Dao/Way’s** true nature.
When a person knows their own nature and destiny,
returns to the **root** (*gēn*), and aligns with the **Dao/Way’s** origin,
unveiled by no external thing—
then they may truly be called enlightened.
People merely know Heaven, Earth, and all things,
yet fail to know their own source of life—
this is profound ignorance.
To conquer others is to have **strength** (*lì*).

Wang Yuán Zé said: *"Strength can conquer others,
yet it cannot conquer oneself."*

The saying goes: *"Yì excelled in archery, and Gōu in steering boats—
both conquered others through strength."*
The character *victory* (*shèng*) is derived from *strength* (*lì*), yet placed below it.
If strength is insufficient, victory cannot stand.
To conquer oneself is to be **strong** (*qiáng*)?

Wang Yuán Zé said: *"To conquer oneself is to restrain oneself and follow the Dao/Way,
to be able to focus one’s **qi** (*qì*)."*

Confucius said: *"The **root** (*gēn*) of a person desires this,
yet they cannot attain firmness—
this is the conqueror of themselves."*

Yang Zhu said: *"To conquer one’s own selfish desires is called *restraint* (*kè*)."*
When a person can restrain themselves,
overcome their own selfish desires and ambitions—
this is how they become truly **strong** (*qiáng*).
Not the strength of *forceful aggression* (*qiáng liáng*), but the strength of *upholding gentleness* (*shǒu róu*).

To know contentment is to be **wealthy** (*fù*).

Emperor Xuānzōng of the Tang said: *"To know when to stop seeking is to have no greed—
this is true wealth."*

Wang Yuán Zé said: *"Within the bounds of one’s nature,
all things are sufficient.
The humble may dwell in seclusion without loss,
the noble may act with great virtue without excess—
yet fools may cling to extreme nobility,
only to perish in their own corruption.
Thus, to know the wealth of the ten thousand things (*wàn zhōu*),
one may lightly regard the world and not be swayed.
This is true wealth."*

Mencius said: *"All things are already complete within me—
how could I not be wealthy?"*
This is the higher saying: *to know the limits of contentment is wealth.*
The next saying: *to take sufficiency from one’s nature alone is wealth.*

To act with **unyielding resolve** (*qiáng xíng*) is to have **will** (*zhì*).

The Duke of Shū said: *"The superior man hears the Dao/Way,
and acts with unyielding resolve—
thus, to act with resolve is to have will."*

Some will for high renown,
others for great gain—
this is not what is meant by *will*.
Only to act with resolve upon the Dao/Way
may one truly be called a man of will.

Not to lose one’s place is to **endure** (*jiǔ*).

Master He Shang said: *"When a person can regulate themselves,
not losing the **qi** they receive from Heaven—
then they may endure."*

Wang Yuán Zé said: *"When nature is not swayed by external things,
then they endure."
This is to exhaust one’s nature.

Master He Shang spoke of *nurturing the vital essence* (*yǎng jīng*).
Wang Yuán Zé spoke of *nurturing the spirit* (*yǎng shén*).
The two sayings combined in one are wondrous.

The *I Ching* says: *"The enduring is firm and long-lasting"* (*yì dá jiǔ yě*).
The virtuous person establishes a steadfast **Dao/Way** (*lì fāng*).
Not to change one’s **Dao/Way** (*fāng*) is to not lose one’s place—
thus they may endure.

To die yet not perish is **longevity** (*shòu*).

Wang Bí said: *"Even in death, if the **Dao/Way of life** (*shēng zhī dào*) is not lost,
then one attains complete longevity.
Though the body perishes, the **Dao/Way** remains—
how much more so when the body lives yet the **Dao/Way** is not fully realized?"*

Lù Xī Shēng said: *"When the body dies yet the **Dao/Way** does not perish,
then it may be called longevity."*

Wang Yuán Zé said: *"The death of a virtuous person is called *ghost* (*guǐ*),
for they have fulfilled their **Dao/Way** and returned to their true nature.
The death of a sage is called *god* (*shén*),
for they have never truly died nor truly lived.
The death of a fool is called *thing* (*wù*),
for though they live, they are already dead.
Those who exhaust the **Dao/Way** and nurture the spirit (*shén*),
though their form may change ten thousand times,
their true nature remains undiminished,
without end—
this may be called longevity.
This reaches the **Dao/Way’s** ultimate decree."*

Lù Diàn said: *"The *Lüzi*’s transformation,
Zhuāngzi’s non-death,
and the Buddhist teachings of non-extinction—
these share the same meaning.
Thus, the sage’s life
and death are spoken of together—
this is called *god* (*shén*).
The sage’s death,
and life are spoken of together—
this is called *longevity* (*shòu*).
This is because their life and death
have never truly differed.
For the sage, life and death share the same form,
and all things are held within the same **treasure-house** (*fǔ*).
Thus, the body is like the discarded husk of a cicada,
or the shed skin of a serpent—
mere temporary dwellings.
Though the cicada’s husk is dead,
its true nature has never perished.
Though the serpent’s shed skin is rotted,
its true nature has never been lost—
why so?
Because the true essence, though it dies, is not extinguished.

It is said: *"The perfect person is not burned by fire,
nor drowned by water.
Tigers cannot seize them,
and rhinoceroses cannot gore them.
They ride the void yet do not fall,
and strike rocks yet are unobstructed—
yet they have never truly died.*
Then again it is said: *"To die yet not perish—what does this mean?
The sage, in accordance with the times,
merely follows them.
When the times demand action,
the sage does not forcibly avoid.
When the times demand rest,
the sage does not forcibly act—
they simply observe Heaven’s will.
Thus, they have the ability yet do not exert it—
this is why they have life yet no death,
death yet no perishing."*

Yán Zūn said: *"To establish oneself in the world,
to promote benefit and remove harm,
to interact with others and adapt to changes—
nothing surpasses the wisdom of knowing people.
To gather intelligence and cultivate enlightenment,
to found a state upon its people,
to grasp the intent of the Dao/Way,
and the mind of Heaven—
nothing surpasses self-knowledge.
To govern with policies and rites,
to establish laws and customs,
to implement transformations and decrees,
to rectify the seas within—
to serve as a minister to the lords of the states—
nothing is more noble than conquering others.
To uphold **Dao/Way** and **virtue** (*dào dé*),
to align with Heaven’s clarity (*shén míng*),
to receive from Heaven and Earth,
to harmonize yin and yang,
to move the household and guard the state,
to bring the people joy and ease,
to be reverent, respectful, and cautious,
to be kind, filial, and law-abiding—
nothing is more exalted than knowing contentment."*

The mind wandering through the luminous clarity of **Dao/Way** and **virtue** (*shén míng*),
serene and tranquil, honored and flourishing—
nothing surpasses attaining one’s aspirations.

Serving in office and fulfilling duties,
attending to superiors and overseeing inferiors,
completing others’ undertakings,
continuing their legacies,
extending benefits to the myriad people—
nothing is more urgent than enduring longevity.

What Heaven and Earth hold most precious,
what all living beings rely upon,
to dwell within without satiety,
to rejoice in without cessation,
with ten thousand blessings arising together,
and none contending for favor—
nothing is more beautiful than longevity.

When functions and uses are fully realized,
yet no claim is made as one’s own.

Su Shi said:
*"There are beings born without claiming them,
and thus they are named and taken as one’s own.
There are beings avoided without possessing them,
and thus they are rejected and do not take form.
To be born without claiming,
to achieve without possessing—
only **Dao/Way** does this."*

Nurturing the myriad things yet not ruling over them.
*(One variant reads: "clothing and covering like garments.")*

He Shangong said:
*"Though **Dao/Way** nurtures the myriad things,
it does not, like a sovereign, take anything in."*

Cai Zihuang said:
*"To ‘clothe and cover’ means to shelter and nourish.
Though it shelters and nourishes the myriad things,
it does not rule over them—
it seems ever so small.
To always lack desire,
one may be called ‘small.’"*

Lǚ Jifu said:
*"All things great cannot be called ‘small,’
and small things cannot be called ‘great’—
this is **Dao/Way**.
Because it can move left and right with ease.
Thus, the myriad things rely upon it to live,
yet it does not claim them;
its deeds accomplished, it does not dwell upon them;
it clothes and covers the myriad things yet does not rule over them.
Only because it does not dwell,
only because it does not rule,
does it always lack desire.
To always lack desire—
this is the utmost subtlety.
Thus, it may be called ‘small.’"*

Wang Yuanzhe said:
*"This ‘small’ is truly ‘great.’
For those who embody **Dao/Way**,
like thoughts not yet arising,
serene and unified within—
is this not ‘small’?
The *Classic of Changes* says:
‘Returning to the small, yet distinguishing among things.’
The myriad things return to it without its ruling—
thus it may be called ‘great.’"*

Wang Yuanzhe said:
*"If one has intent to rule,
it opposes the things themselves.
Only because it rules over the myriad things
without ever intending to,
is it thus all-pervasive, with none able to separate.
**Dao/Way** returns to the utmost subtlety—then it is small,
yet distinguishes among things;
it manifests in the utmost transformation—then it is great,
yet interacts with things.
Distinguishing among things,
it covers the myriad things without displaying its sovereign function,
and does not rule—
thus it always lacks desire and may be called ‘small.’
This is what is meant by:
‘Returning to the small, yet distinguishing among things.’
Interacting with things,
it encompasses the myriad things without revealing their return,
and is unknown as ruler—
thus it may be called ‘great.’
**Dao/Way** is not something that can be named as ‘small’ or ‘great.’
When it is said ‘may be called,’
it refers to **Dao/Way**’s effect upon things.
Thus, the Sage never claims to be ‘great,’
and so is able to achieve true greatness."*

Cheng Xuan said:
*"This clarifies embodying **Dao/Way**.
The Sage forgets self to preserve things,
retiring in stillness and humility,
never claiming to be ‘great.’
Only because it prioritizes things over self,
forgetting the greatness of deeds,
does it become the leader of all Sages,
solely holding the highest honor of the Three Realms,
and thus achieves true greatness."*

Du Guangting said:
*"The Sage loves the people and cares for all things,
extending kindness to great and small alike—
this may be called ‘small.’
Yet it lets things follow their nature and return to Heaven—
this may also be called ‘great.’
The **Dao/Way**’s methods and transformations,
spreading **virtue** to the people,
stirring the pure and harmonious winds,
covering them with tranquil governance,
forgetting deeds and not claiming them,
not exalting itself—
thus its grand achievements may be great,
and its Sage **virtue** may endure.
Because it does not claim to be ‘great,’
it is able to achieve this exalted greatness."*

Those who cultivate their character
float without attachment,
like clouds without intention,
water conforming to its vessel—
able to turn left or right,
adapting to square or round,
not fixed in constancy,
responding as things come,
broadening with each reflection,
without measuring its applications,
benefiting things though many,
not boasting of its comprehensive aid,
its kindness reaching even the lowly,
never acting for private gain,
all virtues converging without its ruling—
thus it displays the **Dao/Way**’s non-small, non-great **virtue**,
without self-assertion or self-praise,
and may align with the complete and true **Dao/Way**.

Though crude and seemingly small,
none under Heaven can subdue it.
The Sage embraces simplicity and always lacks desire—
thus it may be called ‘small,’
meaning it never claims to be ‘great.’
Yet none under Heaven can subdue it,
and it alone achieves its exalted greatness—
thus it is able to achieve true greatness.

This chapter speaks of **Dao/Way**’s boundless use,
the foundation of all that is born and formed,
distinguishing among things as ‘small,’
interacting with things as ‘great.’
Thus, the Sage follows **Dao/Way**’s simplicity as ‘small,’
and achieves supreme honor as ‘great.’

---
**Chapter Thirty-Five: Grasping the Great Symbol**

Grasp the Great Symbol, and all under Heaven will follow.

Lǚ Jifu said:
*"**Dao/Way** in the world is like rivers and valleys to the seas—
the myriad things return to it without knowing its rule.
This is formlessness.
What formless thing could preserve me and not have me follow?
Thus it is said:
‘Grasp the Great Symbol, and all under Heaven will follow.’
‘Grasp’ means ‘to hold fast.’
The Great Symbol is **Dao/Way**.
The Great Symbol is formless—
**Dao/Way** in its entirety.
The Sage holds fast to it,
to govern the world’s norms.
All under Heaven and the myriad people
turn their hearts and follow.
Emperor Shun came to Denghu,
and ten thousand families came to him.
King Tai resided at Qishan,
and those who followed were like those going to market.
To follow without harm,
to be serene, peaceful, and at ease."*

Lu Yu said:
*"To receive people with emptiness—
what harm could there be?"*
To cause no harm to things,
one is serene, peaceful, and at ease.

Lǚ Jifu said:
*"If one loses **Dao/Way** and all under Heaven follows,
they will depart—
and their following will inevitably cause harm.
If one holds **Dao/Way** and all under Heaven follows,
even if they forget **Dao/Way**’s methods,
they will never truly separate—
thus their following causes no harm."*

Che Huibi said:
*"If one follows with burdens of life and death,
with worries and sorrow—
this is harm.
If one follows the Great **Dao/Way** of effortless ease—
this is no harm.
To speak of ‘serene, peaceful, and at ease’—
it is because one is not swayed by life and death,
and this is called ‘ease.’"*

「All **dharma** are undivided—
this is called *peace*.
Effortless ease—
this is called *tranquility*.

Once one has gone forth upon the **Dao/Way**,
then the realm is secure and the people flourish,
rejoicing in the Great Peace—
what harm could there be?
This is the meaning of *peaceful, tranquil, and at ease*.

*Secure* means no fear of peril or ruin,
*peaceful* means no treacherous or deceitful troubles,
*tranquility* means harmony and governance.

*Music* and *bait*—
passing travelers pause.
Emperor Xuanzong of the Tang said:
*"Music gathers through sound,
bait gathers through flavor—
passing travelers take delight and stay a little,
but not long."*

Lǚ Jifu said:
*"If there is music to be enjoyed,
if there is bait to be savored,
then those who pause are merely passing travelers.
The **Dao/Way** when spoken of—
its flavor is faint, without taste.
To gaze upon it—
it is not clearly seen.
To listen to it—
it is not clearly heard.
To employ it—
it cannot be exhausted.

*御注*: *"What is tasted is tasted;
yet those who taste it never truly perceive its flavor—
thus its flavor is faint, without taste.
What is colored is manifest;
yet those who see it never truly perceive its color—
thus it is not clearly seen.
What is sounded is heard;
yet those who hear it never truly perceive its sound—
thus it is not clearly heard.
If this is so,
then it can be bitter, can be sweet,
can be dark, can be bright,
can be *gōng* [1], can be *shāng* [2]—
without knowing yet without not knowing,
without ability yet without inability.
Thus it cannot be exhausted when employed."*

This passage speaks of the Sage guarding the **Dao/Way** to govern the world,
all under Heaven turning toward it without worry.
The **Dao/Way** is faint without flavor,
not like bait to be savored.
When listened to, it has no sound,
not like music to be enjoyed.
When employed, it is inexhaustible—
how could it be like passing travelers pausing briefly?

*"The chapter on **‘clasping and dispersing’** is the thirty-sixth.
**‘Clasping and dispersing’**—
when one would **‘clasping’** [3], they must first **‘dispersing’** [4].
When one would **‘weakening’** [5], they must first **‘strengthening’** [6].
When one would **‘abolishing’** [7], they must first **‘exalting’** [8].
When one would **‘taking away’** [9], they must first **‘bestowing’** [10].
This is called *subtle clarity* [11].

*御注*: *"Yin and Yang mutually illuminate and cover each other,
the four seasons mutually replace, give birth, and destroy each other.
The principles of all things,
the transmission of human relationships—
their gathering and scattering,
their flourishing and decline,
their rising and falling,
their deficiency and excess—
these are often revealed in the most subtle moments,
yet cannot be seen in their forms.
Only by studying these subtle shifts can one discern them.

Yin reaches its peak in summer while Yang arises at noon,
Yang congeals in winter while Yin arises at midnight.
King Goujian of Yue wished to weaken Wu, so he urged them to attack Qi.
Zhi Bo of Zhongshan wished to ambush Comrade You, so he gave him a wide carriage.
This is how the Sage treads upon frost and knows the approach of solid ice—
the ebb and flow of excess and deficiency are not fixed in their appearance.
Thus the brave cannot weaken,
and the wise cannot take away.

Yán Zūn said:
*"The **Dao/Way** and **virtue/power** [12] are the path,
the divine and the bright are the record.
The transformations of Heaven and Earth,
the regulations of Yin and Yang—
the real becomes the virtual,
the bright becomes the obscure,
the flourishing becomes the declining,
the firm becomes the slack,
the present becomes the absent,
the living becomes the dead.
This is the nature of things,
the principle of the natural world.
Thus the advantage of reversals,
the benefit of bending and extending—
the **Dao/Way** governs Heaven,
Heaven governs the sovereign,
the sovereign governs the ministers,
the ministers govern the people.
All beings that contain **qi**,
employ it to sustain their lives.
Tigers and leopards, when they wish to seize,
first conceal their claws.
Jackals and wolves, when they wish to eat,
do not show their teeth.
The Sage removes their intentions,
to comply with the **Dao/Way**.
The wise adapt to the world,
to comply with the people.
Faithful words are harsh to the ear,
yet they contain truth and falsehood.
When ministers rise to act,
they must strive to comply with their sovereign.
To know this and employ it,
then between Heaven and Earth,
within the six directions [13],
all is blessed.
To not know this and employ it,
then within the household gates,
among kin,
all are thieves.
Thus sons and brothers,
at times become jackals and wolves;
enemies and grudges,
at times become fathers and elder brothers.
Yet within *this* there is *negation*,
and within *negation* there is *this*—
one negation, one *this*—
sometimes they perish, sometimes they endure.
Thus, even among the close, those not truly faithful cannot be trusted,
and even among the near, those not truly good cannot be loved.
This is the lament of the worthy,
the concern of the Sage and the wise."*

Lù Diàn said:
*"This is the ultimate **‘adaptation’** [14] of Heaven and Earth,
not merely the domain of the Sage.
For **‘adaptation’** is hidden in the profound and distant,
yet enacted through compliant and yielding means.
The depths are where the profound and distant reside.
The *Yì* says: **‘Compliance governs adaptation’** [15].
It also says: **‘Compliance’s virtue lies in concealment’** [16].
Compliance is the term for *gentle and weak*.
Concealment is the term for *subtle clarity*.
The *Lǎozǐ* says: **‘This is called *‘veiling clarity’*** [17].
It also says: **‘This is called *‘subtle clarity’*** [18].
*Veiling* means using clarity secretly,
*subtle* means concealing clarity deeply.
When clarity is veiled,
the divine and the bright align in meaning."*

Lù Xīshēng said:
*"The profound depths of the Sage’s teachings
are most wondrous in the interplay of **‘adaptation’** and reality.
Reality is rooted in compliance with the constant,
**‘adaptation’** is enacted by reversing the norm.
**‘Adaptation’** serves to fulfill reality,
reality serves to enact **‘adaptation’**.
Though they differ,
their ultimate aim is one.
The *Lǎozǐ* first guides through reality,
establishing the teaching of knowing the constant,
then employs **‘adaptation’** to aid things,
revealing the principle of reversal.
This is what the *Yì* means by:
**‘Curving to form all things without omission,
encompassing Heaven and Earth without excess.’** [19]

The *Hóng Liè* says:
*"King Huán of Qi loved flavors,
and Yí Yá cooked his own son as bait.
The Lord of Yu loved treasures,
and Duke Xiàn of Jìn used jade and horses to lure him.
King Hú of Chu loved music,
and Duke Mù of Qín enticed him with female performers.
These are all cases where people were governed by their own desires.

*Gentleness and weakness overcome firmness and strength.*
*御注*: The *Jīng* says:
**‘Nothing under Heaven is as gentle and weak as water,
yet in attacking firmness and strength,
nothing can surpass it.’** [20]
The *Zhuāngzǐ* (Outer Chapters) discusses how the *qíu* snake and the *fēngmù* bird [21] take pity on each other:
**‘If they point at me, they will overpower me;
if they blow upon me, they will overpower me—
yet when it comes to breaking great trees
and toppling great halls,
only I can do it.’** [22]"

---
[1] *Gōng* (宫) – The fifth tone of the pentatonic scale, associated with earth and stability.
[2] *Shāng* (商) – The second tone of the pentatonic scale, associated with metal and sharpness.
[3] *‘Clasping’* (歙) – Here, referring to contraction or restraint.
[4] *‘Dispersing’* (张) – Here, referring to expansion or release.
[5] *‘Weakening’* (弱) – To diminish or reduce.
[6] *‘Strengthening’* (强) – To enhance or fortify.
[7] *‘Abolishing’* (废) – To discard or annul.
[8] *‘Exalting’* (兴) – To elevate or promote.
[9] *‘Taking away’* (夺) – To seize or withdraw.
[10] *‘Bestowing’* (与) – To give or grant.
[11] *Subtle clarity* (微明) – The interplay of hidden and revealed, often through indirect means.
[12] *Virtue/power* (德) – Here, aligned with *Dao/Way* as transformative force.
[13] *Six directions* (六合) – The six cardinal directions: up, down, east, west, south, north.
[14] *Adaptation* (权) – Strategic maneuvering, often through indirect or paradoxical means.
[15] *Compliance governs adaptation* (巽以行权) – From the *Yì* (I Ching), indicating adaptability through yielding.
[16] *Compliance’s virtue lies in concealment* (巽德而隐) – From the *Yì* (I Ching), suggesting subtlety in action.
[17] *‘Veiling clarity’* (袭明) – A layered or concealed manifestation of clarity.
[18] *‘Subtle clarity’* (微明) – The hidden yet profound nature of the **Dao/Way**.
[19] *Curving to form all things without omission, encompassing Heaven and Earth without excess* (曲成万物而不遗，范围天地而不过) – From the *Yì* (I Ching), describing the **Dao/Way**’s adaptability.
[20] *Jīng* (经) – Here, referring to classical texts like the *Yì* (I Ching) or *Lǎozǐ*.
[21] *Qíu* snake and *fēngmù* bird – Mythical creatures symbolizing strength and fragility, respectively.
[22] *‘Point at me, blow upon me’* – Likely referencing the *Zhuāngzǐ*’s metaphor of wind and water overcoming strength.

「Zhōng Huì said: To subdue the rigid and strong, one must appear soft and yielding—first expanding, then contracting—then victory and defeat become apparent. The Tang Emperor Xuānzōng said: Compliance and submission enable the exercise of authority; when authority is wielded, one can govern all things. Thus, it is known that the soft and yielding must prevail over the rigid and strong.

Taking examples from oneself: Teeth, though firm, wear out first, while the tongue, though soft, endures. Taking examples from nature: Mountains, though high, lose their strength, while marshes, though low, grow fertile. Fish cannot be removed from the depths, nor can a state’s most potent tools be revealed to others.

*御注*: The depths are where fish conceal their bodies. Potent tools are what a state uses to subdue others. A fish as large as a boat, if stranded and loses its water, even ants can torment it—thus it cannot be removed from the depths. When a ruler is seen granting rewards, ministers exploit his authority. When a ruler is seen imposing punishments, ministers exploit his power. Rewards and punishments are instruments of governance and must not be displayed—how much more so the principles of governance itself?

The Sage wields potent tools yet does not reveal them—not because he relies on brute force, but because he possesses a subtle **Dao/Way**. He can exhaust the land’s resources without earning a reputation for cunning, dominate all things without displaying valor, and conquer without being defeated, attaining the **Way** of perpetual victory.

Yielding opens, concealing closes—endless transformations, guiding ministers and ruling the world without being fathomed. Thus, he governs others without being governed himself, for his power originates from above and rests with the sovereign, and the world is thereby governed.

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: Just as a fish cannot be separated from the depths, so too must a person not abandon softness and yielding—otherwise, they are doomed to death.

The ultimate softness traverses the ultimate rigidity; without entering any gap, it is the sharpest of tools. Wielding such tools to govern the world, a state’s strategies lie in the intangible—how can one reveal their origins?

Thus it is said: *A state’s most potent tools must not be revealed to others.*

Wáng Yuànzé said: Fish submit to softness and yielding, hiding in the depths to preserve themselves. The Sage retreats into seclusion, yet wields supreme authority alone, guiding all things beyond comprehension. Thus, he turns the heavens and earth effortlessly, yet none perceive his strength; he commands all under heaven, yet none attribute it to force. Would he expose his divine essence and let the masses debate it?

I have pondered this: What distinguishes the Sage from others is his foresight (*knowing the few*). If one meets things with rigidity and strength, those things’ rigidity and strength become insurmountable. All under heaven rely on rigidity and strength to conquer. I alone dwell in softness and non-contention, yet when I act, who can resist?

Observe the **Dao/Way** of Heaven: Autumn and winter give way to spring and summer—another verification. The Sage hides in the depths, yet presents softness and yielding as his exterior, guiding all things within their natural unfolding, while his essence remains unknown to all. This is what is called *secret action, solitary transformation*.

The *Yì* (I Ching) says: *Compliance enables the exercise of authority.*
The *Zhuāngzǐ* says: *Fish are trapped by calculation*—their meaning aligns here.

This passage speaks of compliance enabling authority, of softness and yielding prevailing over rigidity and strength. First expanding, then contracting—softness and yielding prevailing over rigidity and strength is evident. A person cannot abandon softness and yielding, just as a fish cannot be removed from the depths. The Sage wields potent tools yet does not reveal them—not because he relies on rigidity and strength, but because he embodies softness and yielding. Thus, he attains the **Way** of perpetual victory.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Seven: The Dao/Way Constantly Does Not-Act, Yet Does Not Fail to Act**

Shū Wáng said: Earlier, it was said that the **Dao/Way** constantly has no name—speaking of the **Dao/Way**’s essence. This chapter speaks of the **Dao/Way** constantly not-acting—speaking of the **Dao/Way**’s transformations.

Líng Qì said: The **Dao/Way** as a formless form stands independent and unchanging, yet its form constantly resides in the utmost emptiness. The **Dao/Way** as a non-use that uses moves in cycles without beginning, yet its use encompasses all things. Because it is independent, it remains motionless and still, its essence complete and whole. Because it moves in cycles, it responds and communicates, its use encompassing all things thoroughly.

The **Dao/Way** exists as form yet is not form; it exists as use yet is not use. Though it does not-act, when it responds and communicates, it does not cease. Though it does not fail to act, it remains motionless and still alongside it. From its pairing and response, it is seen that it does not fail to act exhaustively, yet its ultimate return is to constant not-acting.

Because the **Dao/Way** constantly does not-act, it can do all things. Not-acting is motionless and still—the true essence of the **Dao/Way**, the so-called formless form. Not failing to act responds and communicates—the true use of the **Dao/Way**, the so-called non-use that uses. Thus it is said: *The **Dao/Way** constantly does not-act, yet does not fail to act.*

If lords and kings could uphold this,

Wáng Yuànzé said: A ruler embodying the **Dao/Way** to govern follows the times and aligns with principle without deliberate action, so though he does not-act, the world’s affairs are not neglected. Though the world’s affairs are not neglected, I have truly never acted. What does Heaven say?

The four seasons proceed, all things flourish—lords and kings’ **Dao/Way** exhausts Heaven’s purpose. All things will transform of their own accord.

*御注*: When lords and kings uphold the **Dao/Way**, they govern the world from a realm of not-acting, and their actions emerge from not-acting, lending transformation to all things. All things transform as if by their own nature, unaware of the one who guides them—thus, it is said they transform of their own accord.

Shū Wáng said: Speaking of the **Dao/Way**’s essence, it is said: *All things will submit to it as guests.* Speaking of the **Dao/Way**’s transformations, it is said: *All things will transform of their own accord.* When lords and kings uphold the **Dao/Way**, they do not-act. All things transform of their own accord through the **Dao/Way**, hence it does not fail to act.

Zhuangzi said: *Without acting, all things transform.* When transformation is about to manifest, Shū Wáng said: *To shape transformation is called change.* Speaking of transformation about to manifest, manifesting is merely movement.

Wáng Yuānzé said: When transformation progresses daily, it is like an infant’s growth—sure to reach a point where wisdom and deliberation arise, and the harmony of Heaven gradually wanes. Observing the end of the Three Dynasties, where customs and morals became refined and exhausted, one can understand this.

I shall govern with the nameless simplicity of the Uncarved Block.

Zhuān Wēi said: The **virtue/power** of the Sage’s transformation always rescues people. If there are those who do not submit to his transformation, yet cultivate sincerity in themselves and extend this cultivation universally to the world, then if lords and kings know and uphold it, will not the world’s cultivation be universal?

He who does not hoard the **Dao/Way** yet seeks to share its benevolence with the world—this is the **Dao/Way** of the lords and kings. To seek to share it with the world, one must first uphold it as lords and kings. Yet scholars, seeing only his words, speak of abandoning benevolence and righteousness, saying: *"Laozi undermines and belittles my benevolence and righteousness; I will not accept this."* Alas, they do not see the intent behind the abandonment, so it is fitting they reject it.

Lù Xīshēng said: The first chapter establishes the **Dao/Way** as its substance, common names as its function, and culminates in the *Yet More Mysterious*. This chapter takes *not-acting* as its substance, *without-not-acting* as its function, and unifies them under *comprehensive forgetting*. Beginning and end are interconnected, exhausting its substance and function.

Humans are born in stillness—this is the nature of Heaven. When stirred by things, they move—this is the nature of desire. Even the nameless simplicity of the Uncarved Block, if it does not desire, then nature remains still and corrects itself first. Thus, the world does not await correction but corrects itself naturally.

Lǐ Tián said: The Great **Dao/Way** takes emptiness and stillness as its true constancy, and responsive application as its subtlety. If lords and kings uphold its true constancy—remaining motionless in tranquility—and model its subtle applications, responding and thus harmonizing, then all things will transform in purity and the world will be rectified.

This chapter begins by speaking of the **Dao/Way**’s constancy in *not-acting* yet *without-not-acting*, and concludes with the *nameless simplicity of the Uncarved Block* without desire. If one harbors the desire for simplicity, it is not true *not-acting*. The *nameless simplicity of the Uncarved Block* in *comprehensive forgetting*—this is the **Dao/Way**’s constancy in *not-acting*. The world will naturally rectify itself—this is *without-not-acting*.

*The Compendium of Good Selections from the Dao De Jing*, Volume Five concludes. *The Compendium of Good Selections from the Dao De Jing*, Volume Six begins.

The *Collected Writings of Master Li Lin of Rao Mountain, Song Dynasty*
*Imperial Commentary*:

The **Dao/Way** has no fixed form, yet virtue has completeness and deficiency.
When aligned with the **Dao/Way**, there is no virtue to name;
when distinguished from virtue, there is a name to discern.
Ren, yi, li, and zhi—
received according to measure, applied according to time—
this is virtue alone.
Those who embody the **Dao/Way** differ from this,
and thus are placed in the lower scriptures.

Master Wang Yuanze said: *Virtue* is *obtaining*.
Things arise from the **Dao/Way**, each obtaining from it,
and thus it is called *nature*.
To obtain one’s nature without losing it—
this is the completeness of virtue.
Virtue has never differed from the **Dao/Way** yet possessed its own virtue,
but it has reached the point of self-indulgence and lost the **Dao/Way**.

As for the *True Person*, they do not cling to the reality of nature, life, virtue, and the **Dao/Way**,
but merge them into one.
Though the four names respond to the world with varied titles,
I know neither their difference nor their unity—
this is the profound aspect of virtue.

Still, virtue is *obtaining*—
how could one avoid losing it?
Only by taking *non-obtaining* as virtue,
and thus virtue becomes non-virtue—
this may be called the utmost.

This is the embodiment of the **Dao/Way**,
not one who possesses virtue.
