**Chapter Thirty-Eight: The Supreme Virtue Does Not Cling to Virtue**

The supreme virtue does not cling to virtue,
and thus has virtue.
*Imperial Commentary*:
Things obtain life and are called virtue.
All obtain in harmony, silently uniting with the **Dao/Way**,
passing without regret, achieving without self-possession—
this is called *not clinging to virtue*.

Confucius did not dwell on his sainthood,
but acted as a sage when the time demanded—
thus he truly had virtue.

Master Wang Bi said:
The person of supreme virtue uses only the **Dao/Way**.
Not clinging to virtue, without attachment or action,
thus they truly have virtue.

Master Gu Shenzi said:
The supreme virtue does not cling to virtue—
it aligns with the **Dao/Way**.
Virtue is *obtaining*.
To obtain the **Dao/Way** is to say this.
The person of supreme virtue aligns with the **Dao/Way** without a *heart of obtaining*,
thus their virtue is pure and tranquil.

The *Classic* says:
Though named as obtaining the **Dao/Way**,
in reality, there is nothing obtained.
The utmost ritual does not enter the self,
the utmost righteousness does not objectify,
the utmost wisdom does not scheme,
the utmost benevolence has no partiality—
this is called *not entering, not objectifying, not scheming, without partiality*—
this is *not clinging to virtue*.
Not clinging to virtue—
this is the utmost.
Thus it is called the supreme virtue.

The *Classic* says:
The supreme virtue is like a valley.

The lower virtue does not lose virtue,
and thus has no virtue.
*Imperial Commentary*:
Recognizing and holding onto it,
self-indulgent and losing the **Dao/Way**—
what virtue is there?

Master Cheng Xuanying said:
Not yet reaching the utmost,
thus called the lower.
Clinging to virtue without forgetting,
thus called *not losing*.

Master Su Shi said:
The sage follows their heart’s desire without overstepping the measure.
Not intentionally seeking virtue,
yet virtue is naturally complete.
Those of lesser understanding value virtue highly,
striving to avoid losing it—
merely preserving themselves,
what virtue is there?

Master Gu Shenzi said:
The lower virtue does not lose virtue—
virtue is obtained.
The lower virtue scatters the **Dao/Way** as virtue.
Thus, limping is called benevolence,
stumbling is called righteousness,
fastidiousness is called ritual,
and contrivance is called wisdom.
Though named as not losing,
virtue is far from the **Dao/Way**,
thus it has no virtue.

The supreme virtue acts without doing,
yet does not act.
*Imperial Commentary*:
Obtaining without thought,
achieving without effort,
arriving without action—
this is the supreme virtue.

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said:
To know *non-acting* and thus *not act*—
this is not the utmost.
To *not act* without knowing *non-acting*—
this is the utmost.

Master Wang Yuanze said:
The supreme virtue is *non-acting*,
yet also does not *do* anything—
virtue is naturally complete.

Master Cao Daochong said:
The highest virtue is originally *non-acting*,
not deliberately cultivating the thought of *non-acting*.

Master Lü Jipu said:
The supreme virtue is *non-acting*,
not intentionally so,
but naturally obtaining it,
and truly doing nothing.

Master Gu Xuan said:
Speaking of the supreme virtue’s transformation,
handling *non-acting* affairs,
practicing speechless teaching—
its traces are not evident.
Thus it is said:
*Non-acting* has no traces,
the heart has no desires—
thus it is said *without doing*.
Thus the supreme virtue’s *non-acting*
is not conforming to the beauty of *non-acting*,
but contains and nurtures pure simplicity,
naturally *non-acting*—
thus it is said *without doing*—
this is the heart and traces both *non-acting*.

The lower virtue acts,
and thus does something.

Master Lü Jipu said:
The lower virtue does not know this and acts,
thus cannot avoid *doing*.

Virtue, without *doing*, is sufficient.
Yet if there is benevolence in it,
then it is *doing*.

The person of lower virtue
does not obtain without thought,
does not arrive without action—
thus they neither reach *non-acting*
nor are compelled to *do*.

The supreme benevolence acts,
yet does not *do*.
*Imperial Commentary*:
Emperor Yao and Shun’s nature covered the world,
yet they did not do so for personal gain—
thus they did not *do*.

Master He Shanggong said:
Comprehensive aid without bias,
their benevolence is supreme—
thus it is called *upper canopy*.
*Doing* refers to the seven graces.
Accomplishing without claiming,
completing without controlling—
no attachment to action.

King Shuzong of Shu said:
The seven graces are
having those to love and those to cherish.
Only because they have those to love and cherish
can they not *do* without action.
The lower ones are known accordingly.

Master Wang Yuanze said:
Transformation is the longest of goodness,
another name for virtue.
Since it is distinguished from virtue,
this is *doing*.
Yet the sage’s transformation
fulfills nature and is sufficient,
requiring no artificial effort—
thus there is no *doing*.

The supreme righteousness acts,
and thus does something.
*Imperial Commentary*:
Arranging enemies and measuring appropriateness—
this is righteousness.
To establish the self,
to regulate affairs—
how could one *not do*?

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said:
Righteousness is to judge what is wrong—
to say *judging what is wrong* is righteousness,
thus it is said *acts*.
With something to judge what is wrong and cut through,
ordering what is appropriate—
thus it is said *and thus does something*.
This is both heart and traces *doing*.

The *Miscellaneous Sayings* say:
The supreme virtue *does not act* yet *does not do*—
this is the Yellow Emperor.

The supreme virtue of *Ren* acts yet does nothing—
this is Yao and Shun.
The supreme righteousness acts and thus does something—
this is Tang and Wu.

Supreme righteousness, inferior virtue.

Someone said: Tang and Wu were great sages—
to call their virtue inferior, is that acceptable?
It was said: What sages share is the heart.
The reason virtue has superior and inferior is time.
Great sages in different times act accordingly.

When supreme ritual acts and none respond,
they roll up their sleeves and press forward.

Yan Junping said:
Empty and effortless, opening the Dao for all things—
this is the *Dao*-person.
Serene and responsive, doing nothing yet nothing is left undone—
this is the *virtue*-person.
Loving all things broadly and giving without end—
this is the *Ren*-person.
Ordering names and rectifying affairs according to what is fitting—
this is the *righteousness*-person.
Humility, withdrawal, yielding words, and reverent harmony—
this is the *ritual*-person.
These five are all acceptable—
this is the Dao.
But they are traces, not the ultimate.

The ultimate transcends all five—
how could it encompass them all?

Wu Jun said:
Ritual and wisdom are the great barriers against chaos.
Virtue and the Dao are the vast principles to soothe it.
Thus, the Dao is the root of ritual,
and ritual is the branch of the Dao.
Holding the root is easy and firm,
grasping the branch is difficult and perilous.
Therefore, the sovereign takes the Dao as heart,
virtue as substance,
*Ren* and righteousness as carriage and attire,
ritual and wisdom as crown and cap—
then, with hands resting, the world transforms itself.
But if one values ritual and wisdom while forgetting virtue and the Dao,
it is like having fine adornment without essence—
though diligent day and night, the world will decay.

The *Ren*-person gives without measure.
Righteousness chooses what to give fittingly,
without demanding return.
Ritual demands reciprocity.
To give without receiving in return is not ritual;
to receive without giving in return is also not ritual.
The principle of giving and receiving—
when ritual is practiced upon another and they do not respond,
one rolls up their sleeves and, angry, presses forward in pursuit.
Rolling up one’s sleeves means baring the arm by pushing aside sleeves.
In the Spring and Autumn era,
a single unavenged word, a single improper bow,
could lead two states to violent conflict—
yet they would still roll up their sleeves and press forward,
focusing on the lesser offense.
Thus, one loses the Dao and then virtue,
loses virtue and then *Ren*,
loses *Ren* and then righteousness,
loses righteousness and then ritual.
Ritual is the thinnest layer of loyalty and trust,
yet the first cause of chaos.

*Imperial Commentary*:
The Dao cannot be attained,
so one loses the Dao and then virtue.
Virtue cannot be fully realized,
so one loses virtue and then *Ren*.
*Ren* can be practiced,
and when practiced, approaches righteousness—
so one loses *Ren* and then righteousness.
Righteousness can be diminished,
and when diminished, is adorned with ritual—
so one loses righteousness and then ritual.
When it comes to ritual, one drifts further from the Dao,
and the losses multiply.

All things cannot flourish simultaneously—
this is yin and yang.
They take from each other—
this is power and virtue.
Substance is thick where appearance is thin—
this is the ritual of father and son.
Thus observed, the more elaborate the ritual,
the more surely substance decays.
When substance decays, deceit follows,
and strife and chaos arise.
Therefore it is said:
Ritual is the thinnest layer of loyalty and trust,
yet the first cause of chaos.

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said:
To lose the Dao is to lose supreme virtue.
Supreme virtue aligns with the Dao, hence "losing the Dao."
The seven stages of virtue and righteousness
are names for the times—simple or perilous.
Thus, when the Dao declines, virtue appears;
when virtue is lost, *Ren* remains;
when *Ren* vanishes, righteousness arises;
when righteousness is destroyed, ritual intervenes—
these are all adaptations to the times.
To discuss ritual in an age of purity is madness or heresy;
to forget ritual in a corrupt age is folly or deceit.
If one can dissolve and transform,
they should retreat from ritual and practice righteousness,
retreat from righteousness and practice *Ren*,
retreat from *Ren* and practice virtue,
forget virtue and merge with the Dao.
When people return to purity,
supreme virtue’s non-action prevails.

Zuan Wei said:
To neglect virtue, *Ren*, righteousness, and wisdom,
and rely solely on ritual teaching—
is this not the thinnest layer of loyalty and trust,
the first cause of chaos?
But if one honors virtue, *Ren*, righteousness, and wisdom
while also employing ritual and wisdom,
this is ritual elevated.
Then where would disorder arise?

Loyalty means no deceit;
trust means no falsehood.
Zhuangzi said:
In the age of supreme virtue,
substance exists without knowing it as loyalty,
appropriateness exists without knowing it as trust—
though loyalty and trust exist, they are unknown.
When ritual is applied,
jade and silk are exchanged in swift succession,
giving and receiving follow in turn,
bowing and kneeling, clasping hands—
all are mere outward decorum.
The heart may not truly align.
Yet substance is pure and returns to natural joy,
trust is genuine and conforms to ritual propriety.
When ritual and music spread everywhere,
the world descends into chaos.
The *Taishang* speaks of ritual as a deficient text—
dwell in its depth, not its shallowness.
Reside in its substance, not its ornament.
Thus, discard the latter and take the former.

Lu Jifu said:
Aspire to *Ren* and righteousness,
abandon ritual and wisdom,
and seek the fullness of virtue and the Dao—
this is discarding the superficial for the substantial.
Virtue and the Dao are thick and real;
ritual and wisdom are superficial and thin.
Thus, the true man discards the superficial ritual and wisdom,
taking instead the substantial virtue and Dao—
this is how the true man fulfills the Dao and preserves virtue.
This chapter speaks of the root of the Dao,
while *Ren*, righteousness, ritual, and wisdom are its losses.
Supreme virtue aligns with the Dao.
Thus, one does not retreat to virtue
but instead transforms righteousness and ritual,
and seeks the substantial virtue and Dao—
only a true man can attain this.

Formerly, in *Chapter Thirty-Nine: The Ancient Who Attained Oneness*—

The *Er Lu Tian* says:
To enter the One Dao is to gain it,
to exit the One Dao is to lose it.
The One is the boundary between existence and nonexistence.

*Liezi* says:
The One is the beginning of form’s transformation.

"Formerly" (*xī*) means speaking of antiquity as if it were recent.
"Formerly" refers to the time after Heaven and Earth.

The *Classic* says:
The Dao gives birth to the One; in things, it becomes *jing* (精).

Those who attained the One in antiquity—Heaven, Earth, the divine, and all things—
though they differ in high and low, dark and bright,
the One was never unequal.
Thus, it is said: *Its attainment is the One.*

The One is the root.
Heaven attains the One and becomes clear.
*Gu Huān* says: Heaven is pure *yang* qi.
Attaining the One, it is thus light and clear above.

Earth attains the One and becomes tranquil.
*Gu Huān* says: Earth is pure *yin* substance.
Attaining the One, it is thus still and settled below.

The divine attains the One and becomes numinous.
*He Shang Gong* says: When the divine attains the One, it can transform without form.
*Wang Yuanze* says: "Divine" refers to the spirit of ghosts and gods.
*Numinous* is the dispersion of the divine.

Valleys attain the One and become full.
*Guo Xiang* says: Valleys are the hollows of rivers.
Valleys and rivers, attaining the One, thus flow with springs and streams,
filling ravines and gullies.
*Wang Yuanze* says: The One, as One, pervades without limit,
so valleys, though empty, can respond—
the One abides within them.
*Lu Tian* says: The empty is the body of valleys; the full is their function.

All things attain the One and thus come to life.
*Zhang Junxiang* says: Sentient and insentient alike—
all things share the One Dao and thus take form.

Lords attain the One and become the rectification of the world.
*Its attainment is the One.*

*Commentary* says: *Zhuangzi* says: "To harmonize with the One is to complete all things."
Attaining the One means no duality;
embracing the One means no separation;
guarding the One means no deviation;
knowing the One means knowing all;
not knowing the One means knowing nothing.

Those who attained the One in antiquity!
They embodied the utmost *jing* of the world—
no thing could pair with them.
Thus, they stood firm above, pure and unmixed;
they settled below, still and unchanging.
The most hidden and formless—this is the divine.
Attaining the One, it does not grow dim.
The most empty and responsive—this is the valley.
Attaining the One, it does not run dry.
All things transform *jing* into form, thus attaining the One to live.
Lords govern the many through singularity, thus attaining the One to rectify the world.

From Heaven and Earth down to lords,
though high and low differ in rank,
dark and bright in dispersion,
Heaven’s clarity, Earth’s tranquility,
and the lord’s rectification of the world—
none seek this externally.
The One alone governs it.

Thus it is said: *Its attainment is the One.*
*Wang Yuanze* says: The One is undivided—
present in all things, yet its essence remains singular.
No other can achieve the One as the One itself can.

The meaning of the One—
the utmost *jing* of the world.
Only because it is *jing* can it become divine;
once divine, it is complete.
Yet the divine’s virtue ever abides in the One.

Lords govern the many through singularity,
thus becoming the rectification of the world.

The *Classic of Change* says:
"The movement of the world is rectified by the One and stops."

Heaven without clarity would fear splitting;
Earth without tranquility would fear erupting;
The divine without numinosity would fear ceasing;
Valleys without fullness would fear exhaustion;
All things without the One would fear annihilation;
Lords without singularity would fear toppling.

*Commentary* says:
Heaven’s duty is to cover; Earth’s is to bear.
If split, it cannot cover; if erupted, it cannot bear.
*Erupted* means to overflow.
The divine depends on humans to act—
if it ceases, it has nothing to show.
Valleys receive but do not store—
if exhausted, none would respond.
When gathered, *jing* qi becomes things—
this is attaining the One to live.
When scattered, wandering spirits become transformations—
this is losing the One to perish.
Perishing is the cessation of life.
Only rectitude can govern all transformations
and stand alone.

Above all things, there is none to rectify them,
yet lords, though noble and high, cannot preserve themselves—
how could they avoid toppling?
*Toppling* means the fall of the exalted.

*Wang Yuanze* says:
The One, as One, is everywhere—
to speak of its principle exhausts language.

Even in human form:
When a person is born, *jing* is the root;
the divine gathers from *jing*, and the body takes shape.
When *jing* is lost, the form dies and the divine departs.
Whether virtue or form, the principle is the same.

Thus, nobility depends on humility as its root,
and height depends on lowliness as its foundation.

*Commentary* says:
The humble is what the noble relies upon for stability;
the low is what the high arises from.

Common people see only the end,
but the sage probes the root.
Common people see only completion,
but the sage examines the subtle.

Thus, they ever attain the One.

*Sun Deng* says:
A nine-tiered tower begins with piled earth;
a hundred *rèn*-high height starts with a single basket.
This illustrates how nobility depends on lowliness as its foundation.

*Wang Yuanze* says:
Water, of the Five Phases, is numbered One,
yet it flows downward without contention—
this is the nature of *yin* and *yang*.

Those who know this Dao,
though seated in nobility and height,
do not forget their roots.
Thus, they are secure in their positions,
like preserving the divine while storing *jing*—
the divine ever remains.

Therefore, lords call themselves *gū* (孤), *guǎ* (寡), *bùgǔ* (不谷)—
this is their grounding in humility, is it not?

*Commentary* says:
*Gū*, *guǎ*, and *bùgǔ* are names of lowliness,
yet lords use them as titles—
they know their roots.
This is how lords, though noble and high, do not topple.

*Gu Huān* says:
*Gū* is the name for the fatherless.
*Guǎ* is the name for the bereaved.

「Not谷 (*bùgǔ*) is the term for the unvirtuous.
It refers to those who are not the central focus of all things and thus cannot unify the multitude’s praise.

All three of these names—*gū*, *guǎ*, and *bùgǔ*—
are what people hold in disdain,
yet lords and kings take them as their own titles—
is this not the very meaning of grounding in lowliness?

The term *bùgǔ* is one of careful inquiry,
stating that lords and kings label themselves *gū*, *guǎ*, and *bùgǔ*
to make clear their foundation in lowliness.

*Wang Yuanze* says:
One, though numerically the least,
is the root of all things.
Thus, knowing that the foundation lies in lowliness,
one understands that true nobility arises from embracing lowliness.

Therefore, he attains *shù* (数) and *yù* (舆),
yet finds no *yù* (舆).
*Commentary* says:
Those who exalt themselves to dominate others,
who claim nobility while degrading others,
yet fail to uphold their foundation in gentleness—
white without knowing to guard with black—
if they seek praise through such excess,
then hollow praise gathers violently,
while baseless slander follows swiftly,
and thus they attain no true praise.

*Tang Ming Huang* notes:
The character *shù* (数) is pronounced with an ascending tone,
while *yù* (誉) is pronounced as *yùzi* (舆子).

*Say* explains:
When *shù* (数) and *yù* (舆) are attained,
there is no *yù* (舆) left—
the axle is the foundation of the wheel.
When *shù* (数) and nobility are attained,
there is no true nobility—
lowliness is the root of nobility.
The axle is the foundation of the wheel,
so one must preserve the axle to stabilize the wheel.
Lowliness is the root of nobility,
so one must uphold lowliness to secure nobility.
Thus, this passage warns lords and kings to take lowliness as their foundation,
and so it speaks of attaining *shù* (数) and *yù* (舆).

*Wang Yuanze* says:
The character *shù* (数) is pronounced with an entering tone,
while *yù* (舆) is the character for *yù* (誉, praise).

*Say* explains:
Those who grasp the One
use lowliness as their foundation,
yet internally conceal the utmost nobility—
the world cannot attain them,
and they do not seek to be attained by the world.
If lowliness is truly their own,
and they govern with praise and reputation,
is this truly praise?

They do not wish to be polished like jade,
nor rugged like stone.
*Commentary* says:
Jade is noble, while stone is lowly,
yet both are fixed and unchanging.
The Sage adapts to the times and follows the natural order,
unhindered in all transformations.
Though the myriad changes are without constancy,
my heart remains ever One—
this is the true grasp of the One.
Thus, one cannot be attained in terms of nobility or lowliness.

*Mencius* said:
What I dislike about those who cling to the One
is that they cling to the One while abandoning the hundred.
They do not wish to be polished like jade,
nor rugged like stone—
this is not merely knowing the transformations;
even the Sage cannot surpass it.
This is what is called *shàngdé* (上德, highest virtue).

*Héshàng Gōng* (河上公) says:
*Lìnglìng* (琭琭) is a metaphor for scarcity,
*luòluò* (落落) is a metaphor for abundance.
Jade is scarce, hence it appears noble.
Stone is abundant, hence it appears lowly.
The saying does not wish to be like jade,
to be held in others’ esteem,
nor like stone,
to be held in others’ contempt.
One must dwell in the balance between them.

*Wang Yuanze* says:
Jade and stone are firm and unyielding,
fixed and unable to bend or change—
this is not the One.
But if one embraces the myriad transformations while remaining ever One,
then this is the true One.
Thus, jade is polished and noble,
yet cannot be lowly.
Stone is rugged and lowly,
yet cannot be noble.
*Lǎo Shì* (老氏, Laozi) having illuminated the One’s meaning,
feared that some might grasp the One as unchanging,
rigid like jade or stone,
and thus lose the true essence of the One.

Only those who embody the One
are both noble and lowly—
their virtue is like water,
adapting to square, round, crooked, or straight,
endlessly responding to all things,
yet never departing from the One.
Thus, they cannot be attained in terms of nobility or lowliness,
for the One transcends all nobility and lowliness.
This passage’s meaning is the most profound and difficult to express;
now I merely outline its core principles.

The Dao gives birth to the One,
and the One is the completeness of virtue.
When embodied in things,
it approaches the Dao.
This chapter takes the One as its central theme.

The One is the utmost refinement of the world.
Heaven, Earth, gods, and things—
noble or lowly, moving or still, growing or decaying—
all derive from the One to achieve their forms.
The *Tàishàng* (太上) fears that people will cling to the One
without understanding transformation,
and so concludes with *bùyù* (不欲, "do not wish"),
for those who are rigid like jade or stone
lose the true meaning of the One.

I humbly believe that the *Tàishàng*’s Dao
begins with refining *jīng* (精, essence) as its foundation,
then perfecting the *shén* (神, spirit) as its wondrous Way.
If the foundation is not established,
then the Dao has no means to arise,
so the One is taken as the foundation,
just as essence is refined to perfect the spirit.

It concludes with jade and stone,
fearing that people will cling to refining essence
without nurturing the spirit.
Even if their lifespan equals that of tortoise and crane,
they will never hope to attain the Dao of immortality.
Only when both essence and spirit are refined
and harmonized with the Dao
is this the true attainment.

*End of Volume Six of the Collected Virtues of the Dao/De True Classic*
*End of Volume Seven of the Collected Virtues of the Dao/De True Classic*

---

**Volume Eight of the Collected Virtues of the Dao/De True Classic**

**Chapter Forty: The Dao’s Movement Lies in Reversal**

Reversal is the Dao’s movement.
Wang Yuanze said: *"To return to the origin is to be still; only stillness can give rise to movement—*
*just as autumn and winter give rise to spring and summer."*

Su Ziyou said: *"Reversal is restoration; to restore one’s nature is to be still.*
*Yet though it remains motionless and tranquil,*
*it responds and thus communicates with all things under Heaven—*
*this is how movement originates from stillness.*

*Gazing upward at Heaven,*
*the four seasons unfold,*
*withdrawing and storing in winter,*
*and flourishing and renewing in spring.*
*Looking downward at Earth,*
*the Five Phases have water,*
*which reverses its flow to complete the One,*
*and thus moves beneficially with the times.*
*The Dao of Heaven and Earth lies in reversal,*
*so they endure eternally.*
*Once a person receives their formed body,*
*they abandon its nature,*
*like a vessel among myriad things,*
*never reversing throughout their life—*
*alas, the sorrow of it.*

**Chapter Forty-One: The Weakness of the Dao**

The weak are the Dao’s instrument.
Shu Wang said: *"The Dao’s instrument lies in weakness,*
*for it is empty.*
*Consider Heaven—*
*even when it points to me, it also overcomes me,*
*and when it overcomes me, it also points to me.*
*Thus, the wind moving through the boundless void*
*may be called weak.*
*Yet nothing fails to be stirred by it,*
*no form fails to be swept by it—*
*so the wind’s power lies in its weakness.*
*Consider Earth—*
*when guided to the east, it flows east,*
*when guided to the west, it flows west.*
*Thus, water entrusted to the depths of the void*
*may be called weak.*
*Yet it dwells where all others abhor,*
*and assaults the firm and strong,*
*none can surpass it—*
*so water’s power lies in its weakness."*

He further said: *"Reversal is not the means by which movement arises,*
*yet there is what is called movement—*
*movement arises from reversal.*
*Weakness is not the means by which strength arises,*
*yet there is what is called strength—*
*for weakness can give rise to strength.*
*Though it speaks of reversal without mentioning stillness,*
*speaks of weakness without mentioning strength,*
*when it speaks of movement, one knows reversal is stillness,*
*and when it speaks of weakness, one knows its use is strength.*
*All things under Heaven are born from existence,*
*existence from non-existence—*
*and it is just so with this.*

*Weakness overcoming strength,*
*the Dao’s wondrous use—*
*as water, the weakest,*
*can assault the firm and strong.*
*All things under Heaven are born from existence,*
*existence from non-existence.*
*He Shang Gong said:*
*‘All things arise from Heaven and Earth,*
*Heaven and Earth have form and position,*
*thus they are said to be born from existence.’*
*‘The divine luminosity of Heaven and Earth all arise from the Dao,*
*the Dao has no form,*
*thus it is said to be born from non-existence.’*
*Lu Jipu said:*
*‘Only existence can give birth to all things under Heaven,*
*yet non-existence can also give birth to all things of existence—*
*thus the Dao’s movement lies in reversal,*
*and its use in weakness is known.’*
*So then, to seek reversal and weakness is no other thing,*
*but to perfect the One and reach the utmost of non-existence.*

*The meaning of this chapter:*
*To return to the origin and be still,*
*then movement will not be lost.*
*As Zhuangzi said:*
*‘Stillness gives rise to movement,*
*movement attains its purpose.’*
*The Dao’s use is never not beneficial,*
*so without contention, it can overcome strength—*
*as the Classic says:*
*‘The weak overcome the strong.’*
*So then, to seek reversal and weakness is no other thing,*
*but to perfect the One and reach the utmost of non-existence.*

**Chapter Forty-One: The Superior Person Hears the Dao**

The superior person hears the Dao
and diligently practices it.
*[Commentary:] A person of virtue who aspires to the Dao.*

The superior person hears the Dao,
with true virtue accumulating strength over time,
and utmost sincerity ceasing not.
Li Rong said: *"The more one believes in the Dao,*
*the more resolute one’s practice becomes.*
*Changing seasons do not alter their resolve,*
*melting gold and stone cannot move their conduct.*
*Firm from beginning to end,*
*steadfast without artifice—*
*this is the superior person’s movement in practice."*

The term *‘superior’* (上) refers to the highest rank.
The term *‘person’* (士) refers to one who takes the Dao as their calling.
The superior person, having attained enlightenment,
hears this wondrous Dao.
The more they believe in the Dao,
the more resolute their practice becomes.
Practice and reality correspond,
whether appearing or withdrawing,
whether acting or dwelling,
always relying on reality without separation.
True enlightenment grows with every thought,
the cultivation of the heart never falters—
thus it is called *‘movement in practice.’*

*A question arises:*
*The Dao cannot be heard,*
*yet if one hears it, it is false—*
*how then does the superior person hear it?*
*An answer is given:*
*‘To hear with the ears and practice with the heart,*
*this is true hearing.’*
*Confucius said:*
*‘To hear the Dao in the morning,*
*and die in the evening,*
*that is enough.’*

The middle person hears the Dao
as if it exists and yet does not exist.
Shu Wang said: *"The middle person knows the Dao as beautiful,*
*yet does not know what constitutes the Dao.*
*Knowing the Dao as beautiful,*
*they hear it like the great sound*
*that does not enter the vulgar ear."*

Li Rong said: *"Unspun silk does not resist change,*
*yet follows the shifting hues of black and yellow.*
*The middle person is undetermined,*
*their heart shifts with their likes and dislikes.*
*If they hear the true Dao for preserving life and stabilizing the realm,*
*they cherish the Dao and forget the world.*
*If they see wealth, beauty, or sensual pleasures,*
*they delight in their nature to gratify their desires—*
*then they cherish the world and forget the Dao.*
*The middle person can ascend or descend,*
*yet they harbor doubt in their heart.*
*Doubt arises, and their resolve is divided,*
*so though they hear the Dao,*
*they govern their body to prolong life,*
*and govern the realm to achieve great peace,*
*they rejoice and yet cling to it.*
*When they retreat and see wealth, beauty, or honor,*
*they may again forget it in their emotions and desires—*
*then they cannot move and practice it.*
*One moment they emerge,*
*one moment they withdraw—*
*like Zi Xia, who emerged to see the bustling splendor,*
*the dazzling beauty, and delighted in it,*
*yet hearing the Master’s Dao,*
*he took joy in that instead."*

The inferior person hears the Dao
and laughs loudly at it.
Wang Yuanze said: *"The Dao is vast, resembling what is not like,*
*unrecognized by shallow vision—*
*thus they laugh.*
*Indeed, as the following text says:*
*‘How could the vulgar ever perceive it?’*
Zhi Cong said: *"The inferior person hears of this wondrous Dao,*
*formless, nameless,*
*neither coming nor going,*
*neither born nor extinguished—*
*not believing in it,*
*they instead rebel against it,*
*so they clap their hands and laugh loudly,*
*calling it empty and false.*
*The inferior person is born with lowly nature,*
*indulging their emotions and many desires,*
*their wisdom insufficient to discern,*
*their understanding insufficient to know.*
*They admire beauty with their eyes,*
*desire to hear sounds with their ears,*
*crave to taste flavors with their mouths,*
*and their spirit longs to be fulfilled—*
*if they do not obtain it,*
*they fall into great anxiety and fear."*

The Dao/Way is without sound, color, taste, or flavor that can be obtained,
then its distance from what the ears, eyes, nose, and mouth crave is far indeed.

Hearing the Dao of tranquil stillness, desolate emptiness, and effortless non-action,
they laugh and reject it.
Not to laugh is not enough to be the Dao/Way.

Yang Fu said:
The lowly and ignorant are muddled,
valuing the superficial while despising substance.
The Dao/Way is profound and mysterious,
empty and distant.
If it is not scorned by shallow understanding,
it is not worthy of the utmost profundity.

Su Ziyou said:
The Dao/Way cannot be seen without form,
nor heard without sound.
If one does not first know the futility of all things,
then, unobstructed and insightful,
they may still be prone to disbelief.
Thus, the inferior person hears of the Dao/Way
and calls it absurd and extravagant, laughing at it.

The middle person hears of the Dao/Way,
and though they associate with its rise and fall,
they doubt it.

Only those who clearly perceive it
then act and embrace it without hesitation.

Confucius said:
*"Speak to them of the Dao/Way, and they do not grow weary—*
*that is my disciple Zilu."*

This is what is called the superior person.

To live with the common while opposing the Dao/Way—
*the "Classic" says:*
*"Few know me, and thus I am precious."*

**Commentary on the passage:**
*Jiàn* means "to establish."
*Here, establishing words of the Dao/Way—*
*this refers to the following text.*

Shu Wang said:
Confucius once remarked:
*"Lost in confusion, yet not awakening."*
I liken this to our Old Master [Laozi].
Indeed, the Old Master praised the ancient speakers of the Dao/Way,
for the ancients had these three sayings,
so he merely spoke of their confusion.

**Clarifying the Dao/Way as if in darkness,**
*the Imperial Commentary says:*
*"Like the sun’s light illuminating the lowlands—*
*that is clarity.*
*Abundant wisdom yet not displayed,*
*borrowing its radiance without flashing,*
*thus it appears as darkness."*

Wang Yuanzhe said:
Great clarity seems obscure.

Zhi Cong said:
Inner wisdom is clarity,
outer non-display is darkness.

**Advancing the Dao/Way seems like retreat,**
Li Rong said:
Hearing the Dao/Way and diligently practicing it—
this is advancing toward great achievement,
yet lacking is retreat.

Lü Jifu said:
To cultivate the Dao/Way is called "subtracting" (损).
Subtracting again and again,
until one reaches effortless non-action—
this is what is called advancing the Dao/Way as retreat.

**Leveling the Dao/Way as if uneven,**
Li Rong said:
To smooth and equal all things—
this is leveling the Dao/Way.
To blend with the world like dust and light—
this is being uneven.

Emperor Ming Huang of the Tang said:
*Leveling (夷)* means "smoothing."
*Uneven (颣)* refers to the irregular threads of silk.
Indeed, one who knows the mind’s clarity and stillness,
where desires and dust do not arise,
is naturally smooth and easy,
boundless with all things,
yet outwardly appears like silk with uneven threads.

Du Guangting said:
The enlightened person cultivates themselves—
internally, they are smooth and level,
externally, they blend with the world.
Enduring hardship while meeting joy,
adapting to the times—
like silk with uneven threads.

The Dao/Way is singularly unified,
yet things are infinitely diverse.
Those who embody the Dao/Way,
internally are smooth and level,
fixed and unchanging,
externally adapt to all things,
shifting with their transformations—
thus, they appear uneven.

**Superior virtue is like a valley,**
*the Imperial Commentary says:*
*"A valley is empty yet receptive,*
*receptive yet does not accumulate.*
*Empty yet responsive,*
*responsive yet inexhaustible."*

Lu Tian said:
The utmost emptiness supplies all demands,
the utmost void responds to all feelings—
thus, it is called superior virtue like a valley.

**Great whiteness seems like disgrace,**
Li Rong said:
Pure yet not tainted—
this is great whiteness.
Mingling with filth—
this is disgrace.

Lu Tian said:
Knowing whiteness yet guarding against blackness,
knowing honor yet guarding against disgrace—
thus, it is called great whiteness as disgrace.

The truly pure and white,
internally harbor clarity and stillness,
clearly manifest and refined,
washing away all stains—
this is great whiteness.
Hiding their light, blending with the world,
dwelling where the crowd reviles—
this is disgrace.

**Ample virtue seems insufficient,**
Sun Deng said:
If virtue is deep and broad,
then the distant and shallow are seen as trifling,
and minor details are overlooked—
like wisdom and knowledge being insufficient.
Thus, a skilled merchant hides their wealth as if they had none.
A person of great virtue,
though their appearance seems foolish—
this is ample virtue appearing insufficient.

Zhi Cong said:
Indeed, superior virtue accomplishes the ten directions,
adorns all things,
its transformation boundless—
this is called "ample."
Though it is said to have virtue,
yet it appears as if without virtue—
this is truly without virtue,
hence, it is called insufficient.

**Establishing virtue seems like theft,**
Emperor Ming Huang of the Tang said:
*Jiàn* means "to establish."
*Theft (偷)* means "to steal."
Speaking of establishing hidden virtue,
secretly cultivating and acting—
like a thief, always fearing discovery,
thus it is called seeming like theft.

Che Huibì said:
The Sage establishes all virtues,
saves all beings,
wondrously employing hidden and flowing functions,
unnoticed by others—
thus, it is called seeming like theft.

These two interpretations speak of theft as *stealing*.

Su Ziyou said:
To follow things’ natural course without imposing—
externally, it seems like laziness and theft,
yet truly, it is establishing.

Wang Yuanzhe said:
*Theft (偷)* means "hasty and careless."
Small-minded, seeking quick results,
striving to establish something—
how could this suffice as virtue?
Only those who follow the seasons and act according to principle,
appearing as if lazy and careless,
truly establish the great virtue.

Zhuangzi said:
*"Acting only when there is no alternative."*

These two interpretations speak of theft as *laziness and carelessness*.

**True substance seems to change,**
Wang Yuanzhe said:
Embodying nature and holding onto the spirit,
wandering among the world’s affairs—
endlessly adapting to the world,
yet their Dao/Way remains constant,
so none know its true substance.
That ancient recluse of the Han dynasty,
Yin Zhang, steadfastly held to his true nature,
yet differed from the world,
startling Zigong’s disciples,
while the Sage saw him as a false cultivator of chaos—
could this be called true substance?

Su Ziyou said:
Embodying nature and holding onto the spirit,
adapting to things’ transformations,
yet never losing their true essence—
externally, it seems to change.
*Substance (质)* refers to inherent nature,
*true (真)* to pure unity,
*change (渝)* to shifting colors.
Speaking of the Dao/Way’s practitioner,
true in nature and pure without falsity,
yet appearing to change,
like waves blending with all things.

**The boundless has no corners,**
*the Imperial Commentary says:*
*"The boundless is the Dao/Way without boundaries—*
*boundless yet not dividing,*
*thus it has no corners."*

Li Rong said:
The universe is vast and has form—
thus, it has boundaries.

The Dao/Way’s ultimate reaches formlessness—
thus, it has no corners.

Lǚ Jìfǔ said:
The boundless form encompasses no south, no north,
yet unfolds in four directions,
plunging into the unfathomable.
No east, no west—
it originates in the Mysterious Void,
returns to the Great Communion—
this is the boundless’s lack of corners.

Wáng Yuānzé said:
The boundless is the Dao/Way’s essence.
If it had four corners,
its form would be confined to what is seen—
its smallness would be long past.

**Great vessels take time to mature.**
*Imperial Commentary says:*
The great vessel is the vessel that is not a vessel.
Neither aiding birth nor assisting growth,
thus it matures late.

Táo Hóngjìng said:
Accumulating virtue, the Dao/Way is fulfilled—
this is called a great vessel.
Not achieved in a single day,
hence, it matures late.

Lù Dìan said:
In conduct, its pace is slow yet unceasing—
thus, it is said: *Great vessels take time to mature.*
The great vessel is the vessel of the Dharma-body.
The Dharma-body’s great vessel is realized through accumulated merit,
not in a single morning or evening—
thus, it matures late.

Zhuāngzi said:
Perfection lies in duration.

**Great sound is rare in voice.**
*Imperial Commentary says:*
Its motion is boundless, yet response follows—
thus, it is rare in voice.

Wáng Bì said:
To hear without hearing is called rare—
a sound that cannot be heard.
If there is voice, there is division;
if there is division, it is neither *gōng* nor *shāng*.
Division cannot harmonize all things,
so one with voice is not great sound.

Zhì Cóng said:
Rare in voice is like having no voice.
The Sage’s single teaching pervades the ten directions,
opening the dense and enlightening the vulgar,
though teaching fills the ten directions—
this is called constant stillness.
Teaching is no teaching,
speaking is no speaking—
by this meaning, it is called rare in voice.

*Commentary says:*
The Dao/Way responds to all sounds—
this is great sound.
To listen without hearing—
this is rare in voice.

**Great images are formless.**
Zhōng Huì said:
Without images, it does not respond—
this is called great image.
Since it lacks substance and form,
how could it have shape or description?
The great image is the image of no image.
Since the image has no image,
how could it have form or shape?

The Dao/Way is hidden, nameless.
Zhuǎn Wēi said:
The Dao/Way originally has no name,
yet it is forcibly named *Dao*.
Now the Dao/Way is hidden within it—
what name could there be?
This is truly what is called *erasing traces and concealing origins*.

Lǚ Jìfǔ said:
All these are the Dao/Way.
Yet calling it clear yet obscure,
level yet uneven,
advancing yet retreating—
to the point of sound being rare in voice,
images being formless,
names and reality constantly opposing—
since the Dao/Way is hidden in the nameless,
and it is named with names,
it is constantly as if opposed.

Only the Dao/Way
is both generous and fulfilled.

Gù Huān said:
Giving before receiving is called generous;
things obtaining the Dao/Way is called fulfilled.
When fulfilled, it returns to the Dao/Way—
the Dao/Way obtains it.

Zhì Cóng said:
Now, the few who are born are born by the Dao/Way.
Generous, you should awaken—
returning to the Dao/Way, why cling to life as life
and not return to the root?

Shū Wáng said:
The truly generous is what all things rely upon without depletion.
Yet it returns to its origin—
thus, it is fulfilled.

This chapter speaks of the Dao/Way’s profound subtlety,
its hidden depth and difficulty to perceive.
From the *Illuminated Dao* to the *Great Image*, all are the Dao/Way.
The Dao/Way’s marvel cannot be sought by intellect,
cannot be pursued by form—
it can truly be called hidden.
The Dao/Way’s essence is hidden in the nameless,
yet its function is both generous and fulfilled.
The superior person awakens to this and diligently practices;
the inferior hears of it and thus laughs greatly—
truly, as the chapter says,
how could the common crowd recognize it?

**Chapter 42: The Dao/Way Generates One**
The Dao/Way generates One,
One generates Two,
One generates Three,
Three generates the ten thousand things.

Lù Dìan said:
The Dao/Way generates One—this is the Supreme Ultimate.
One generates Two—this is yin and yang.
Two generates Three—this is the *chōng* qi.
With yin and yang, and the *chōng* qi between them,
then the ten thousand things are born.
Thus, it is said: *Three generates the ten thousand things.*

Lǐ Róng said:
One generates Two—clear and turbid separate, yin and yang manifest.
Two generates Three—cycling the two qi, forming the Three Powers.
Three generates the ten thousand things—
the round heaven covers above,
the square earth bears below,
and the Sage rules between—
what thing does not generate?

Gǔshénzǐ said:
The Great Dao/Way is natural,
transforming to generate spirit,
spirit moves to form harmony,
harmony disperses to condense qi,
qi condenses to form shape—
thus, it is said:
*The Dao/Way generates One,
One generates Two,
Two generates Three,
Three generates the ten thousand things.*
One is the beginning of form’s transformation.
The light and clear become heaven,
the heavy and turbid become earth,
the harmonious *chōng* qi becomes humankind.
Thus, heaven and earth contain essence,
and the ten thousand things transform and generate.

The ten thousand things bear yin and embrace yang,
Héshànggōng said:
The ten thousand things without fail bear yin and turn toward yang,
retreating inward yet seeking the sun.
The *chōng* qi harmonizes them.

Lù Dìan said:
The Daoists call it *chōng* qi;
medical scholars call it stomach qi.
With yin and yang, then stomach qi arises within.
Bear means *back*;
embrace means *face*.
*Chōng* means *central*.
All that is dark and unfathomable is yin;
all that is bright and visible is yang.
All that lives must turn its back to the dark
and face the bright.
Yet the ten thousand things cannot generate alone with yang,
cannot form alone with yin—
they must have the central harmony of yin and yang,
then things generate.
As Zhuāngzi said:
Extreme yang is blazing,
extreme yin is solemn—
solemn arises from heaven,
blazing issues from earth.
The two intermingle to form harmony,
and thus things generate.

What people detest—
the terms *gū* (orphan), *guǎ* (widow), *bùgǔ* (barren)—
kings and dukes adopt as titles.

Zhuǎn Wēi said:
*Gū*, *guǎ*, and *bùgǔ* are terms of softness, weakness, and humility—
what the vulgar detest and avoid.
Yet it is the noble Sage and great person who thus refers to themselves,
and by this attains the nobility of kings and dukes.

This law is the harmony of *chōngqì* (penetrating qi), diminishing the overbearing strength of the will while seeking to augment virtue and Dao.

The terms *gū* (orphan), *guǎ* (widow), and *bùgǔ* (barren)—
these are the lesser evils.
Kings and dukes, occupying the highest stations,
adopt what people detest as their own titles,
dwelling in humility, conforming to softness and weakness,
to align with the harmony of yin and yang.

Thus it is said:
Things may be diminished to gain benefit,
or augmented to suffer loss.

Li Róng said:
To hold wealth and nobility yet call oneself *gū*, *guǎ*, or *bùgǔ*—this is diminution.
Renewing virtue with daily humility—this is augmentation.
To occupy high station without virtue is self-augmentation;
talent unmatched by rank invites ruin—this is diminution.

The *Commentary* states:
*"Gu"* is a continuation of the preceding argument.
When kings and dukes name themselves *gū*, *guǎ*, or *bùgǔ* in self-abnegation,
the people rejoice in honoring and serving them—this is augmentation through diminution.
Or, when augmentation leads to loss:
If kings and dukes, in their exalted station, grow arrogant,
the people scatter and withdraw—this is diminution through augmentation.

As the *Yijing* says:
*"Fullness invites loss; humility invites benefit."*
Thus it is spoken.

Shū Wáng said:
The Dao of Heaven diminishes excess and augments humility.
Only by augmenting humility can one truly diminish—
for what is diminished becomes the source of benefit.
Only by diminishing excess can what is augmented become the source of loss.
Thus, the titles kings and dukes adopt
are the means to attain benefit while dwelling in high station.

What people teach,
I also teach as my doctrine.

Hé Shànggōng said:
What the masses teach is to discard weakness for strength,
to discard softness for rigidity.
I teach the masses to discard strength for weakness,
to discard rigidity for softness.
What people teach others,
they should teach with my doctrine of softness and weakness.

The overbearing perish without proper death.
Hé Shànggōng said:
The overbearing are those who reject the Mysterious and Profound,
who defy virtue and Dao, who disregard teachings,
and rely on power and force.
To not die a proper death means to be cut off by Heaven,
slain by blades, executed by law—
not to die a natural death in full years.

Yán Xiān said:
Strong Qin perished through tyranny,
Great Han flourished through harmony.
The overbearing lose the Way,
the rigid lose the numinous.
Once the vital principle withdraws,
how can they endure?

The *Explanation of Characters* says:
The beams of a house rest on sturdy supports—
thus the name *liáng* (beam).
Of all things, none are stronger than beams.
The "overbearing" are like beams in strength.
The strong among men are but companions of early death.

Zǐlù loved valor yet died untimely.
Yì excelled in archery, yet drowned in a boat—
both perished without proper death.
Yet these were all failures of the doctrine of softness and weakness.
I shall take this as my teaching for fathers.

Li Róng said:
To disobey one’s parents and rulers,
to defy the teachings of the sages,
to hoard glory while clinging to victory,
to exalt oneself over others—
this is overbearing.
To violate laws, to meet untimely death in mid-life,
to not die a proper death—
this is the fate of the overbearing.

All things conform to the Dao;
the sage never fails to teach.
Human nature, straying from reason, is the origin of speech.
Through the splendor of benevolence and righteousness,
the reality of virtue and Dao is revealed.
From the nature of overbearing strength,
the method of softness and weakness is expounded.

This chapter speaks of how the Dao generates *yīqì* (unity-qi),
how *yīqì* generates yin and yang,
how yin and yang generate *chōngqì* (penetrating qi).
Things obtain *chōngqì* to achieve harmony.
The qi of penetrating harmony is the essence of softness and weakness.
Kings and dukes, conforming to softness and weakness,
adopt *gū*, *guǎ*, and *bùgǔ* as titles—
this is diminution yielding benefit.
The masses, favoring overbearing strength, do not die proper deaths—
this is augmentation yielding loss.
The great sage expounds this as his teaching for fathers.
Truly, it is so.

**Chapter 43: The Supreme Softness of the World**
The world’s supreme softness
gallops across the world’s supreme firmness.

**Li Rong said:**
The supreme softness among things with form—water.
The supreme softness without form—Dao.
Water, though supremely soft, can dissolve gold and pierce stone,
shatter the firm and the strong.
Dao, though supremely soft, can abandon all and forget self,
shatter this stubborn fixation.

If one reflects upon it like water,
embodies it like Dao,
one can penetrate the self and the other,
traverse hills and mountains—
subtle, mysterious, and boundless,
without obstruction or hindrance.
This is called galloping across the supreme firmness.

The supreme softness is water.
The supreme firmness is metal and stone.
Water can penetrate the firm and enter the hard,
passing through all without obstruction.
Water, being supremely soft, nearly attains Dao.
How much more so the formless!

The supreme softness of Dao
envelops heaven and earth,
penetrates all things—
this is how softness overcomes hardness.

"Galloping" means penetrating and flowing freely.

*The Classic* says:
Nothing is softer or weaker than water,
yet nothing surpasses it in attacking the firm and strong.
It enters without gap—
*Riverbank Master* said: "Without gap" refers to Dao.
Dao has no form or substance,
thus it enters and exits without obstruction,
communing with the divine and benefiting all beings.

*Yan Zun* said:
Formed things cannot pierce sharpness,
but the formless divine dwells within—
entering without openings, exiting without traces,
in the blink of an eye, traversing thousands of miles.
"Without gap" is the divine.

The divine’s work—
its utility in entering and exiting—
leaves no trace,
piercing metal, penetrating stone,
entering without gap.
The divine resides in the heart,
the heart harbors the divine.
Empty the heart to preserve the divine,
preserve the divine to seek the ultimate.
Straightforward, it pushes forward;
curved, it yields.
In all directions—up, down, left, right—
it rests where it will,
endless in coming and going,
circulating through the boundless void,
ascending to the heavens, descending to the earth,
sixfold passage, fourfold opening—
nowhere it enters without finding its own way.

Thus I know the benefit of *wuwei*.

*Imperial Commentary*:
Softness overcoming hardness,
the void embracing the having—
the marvelous function of Dao lies here.
Abandoning affairs, the form does not labor;
forgetting life, the essence does not diminish—
this is how it is beneficial.

*Wang Bi* said:
Softness and weakness, emptiness and void—
nothing they cannot penetrate.
The supremely soft cannot be bent,
the void cannot be exhausted—
thus, by extension, one knows the benefit of *wuwei*’s Dao.

*Li Rong* said:
Dao is formless, yet things obtain life;
the sage acts without doing, yet people are transformed—
this is the benefit of *wuwei*.

Softness is the root of Dao,
the void is its function—
the supremely soft and void is Dao.
Dao acts without doing, yet all things transform—
thus one knows the benefit of *wuwei*’s Dao.

The teaching of the unsaid,
the benefit of *wuwei*—
few in the world attain it.

*Imperial Commentary*:
The teaching of the unsaid is set by the divine,
the benefit of *wuwei* does not deplete the true.
The sage embraces simplicity,
and the world submits as a guest.
*Wuwei* yet all things transform—
thus those who attain it are few.

*Gu Huan* said:
Following Dao without words, yet customs shift—
thus it is called the teaching of the unsaid.
Following Dao without doing, yet people and things transform—
thus it is called the benefit of *wuwei*.
This chapter speaks of Dao’s softness, weakness, and void,
acting through the unsaid to teach,
*wuwei* to transform the people.
Those who attain this Dao in the world—
are they not few?

**Chapter 44: Name and Body**

Name and body—
which is more intimate?
Body and wealth—
which is more abundant?

*Imperial Commentary*:
If both arms weigh heavier than the world,
then name and body—
which is more intimate?
Life is not merely as precious as the pearl of Huai—
then body and wealth—
which is more abundant?
The most desired name is within me—
it is not intimate.
The most abundant wealth is within me—
it is not abundant.
Only those who fail to discern intimacy and distance,
abundance and scarcity,
ruin life and harm nature,
sacrificing the body for it—
like Boyi starving to death for name beneath Chief Yang’s hill,
or Zhi the robber dying for gain at Dongling’s peak—
is this not delusion?

*Li Rong* said:
The body is the foundation for cultivating Dao—
thus it is intimate.
Fame is a hollow convention—
thus it is distant.
People cannot sacrifice the body for name;
they only sacrifice the body for name.

*Wang Bi* said:
To prize name and seek loftiness—
one’s body will be distant.
To covet wealth without satisfaction—
one’s body will be diminished.

*Lu Jifu* said:
The resolve of the virtuous is for name—
yet to ruin life and harm nature,
they do not know the body’s intimacy to name.
Thus it is said: Name and body—
which is more intimate?
The resolve of the greedy is for wealth—
yet to ruin life and harm nature,
they do not know the body’s abundance over wealth.
Thus it is said: Body and wealth—
which is more abundant?
Name is empty fame.
Wealth is material goods.
The body is the foundation for cultivating Dao—
how could it not be more intimate than name,
more abundant than wealth?

Fame brings danger to the body—
Meng Zhang declined the Chu chancellor,
preferring to be a tortoise dragging its tail.
The Celestial Master refused the official post,
wishing only to learn the Dao of Huangdi.
Thus it is known: the body is intimate, name is distant.

Gain and loss—
which causes greater harm?

*Zuan Wei* said:
This elucidates the two sentences above.
Empty fame and fleeting gain—
obtaining them is as light as a feather.
Life, nature, form, and spirit—
losing them is as heavy as Tai Mountain.
The enlightened weigh them—
which causes greater harm?

*Li Rong* said:
Name is external praise.
Wealth is worldly goods.
The body, when loyal and filial,
or when cultivating the ultimate Dao—
if weakened by loss, it does not return;
if pursued by desire, it loses its true nature.
To sacrifice the body for name,
to harm oneself for wealth—
the deluded are many.
The sage inquires carefully:
Between gaining name and wealth,
and losing the body—
which causes greater harm?

*Lu Jifu* said:
If one’s resolve is for name,
the world calls them a *junzi*.
If one’s resolve is for wealth,
the world calls them a *xiaoren*.
Though their resolves differ,
losing what they cherish is the same.
Then to gain name and wealth
while losing what is essential—
they do not know that loss is worse than gain.

Thus it is said:
Which is more harmful—gain or loss?

Therefore, the more one loves, the greater the expenditure.
The more one hoards, the greater the loss.

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said:
To love name excessively is to exhaust one’s spirit—is this not great expenditure?
To hoard wealth excessively is to burden oneself—is this not great loss?

Liu Jinxi said:
Insatiable greed is called excessive love.
Excessive desire harms the body, hence it is called great expenditure.

Lu Jifu said:
To love name is to desire wealth and noble status for oneself.
Because of this excessive love, one loses even the finest virtues—this is great expenditure.
To hoard wealth is to desire riches for oneself.
Because of this excessive accumulation, one loses even the greatest riches—this is great loss.

Knowing contentment brings no disgrace.
Knowing when to stop brings no peril.
Thus one may endure and live long.

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said:
Disgrace is loss and burden.
Peril is danger and ruin.
To shun fame is to know contentment.
To refrain from amassing wealth is to know when to stop.
Knowing contentment, one’s name matches reality without excess burden.
Knowing when to stop, one does not hoard wealth without the harm of greed.
Without disgrace or peril, one may endure and live long.

Li Rong said:
Not seeking name through external means is to avoid disgrace.
Not pursuing wealth against reason is to avoid peril.
To distance oneself from name and profit is to distance oneself from peril.
Thus, governing a state may endure, and cultivating oneself may last long.

Lu Jifu said:
Only those with virtue know the true nobility within themselves and need no external name.
Thus, knowing contentment brings no disgrace.
Only those with true wealth know the abundance within themselves and need no external riches.
Thus, knowing when to stop brings no peril.
Without disgrace or peril, one may endure long.

This chapter’s intent is that those who seek longevity must first abandon profit and forget fame.
If name and profit are not discarded, body and mind are enslaved—
not only does this obstruct the Dao,
but in time, it will harm the body.
Thus, ancient masters of the Dao
did not seek fame,
did not amass wealth.
Though they held high office or vast emoluments,
they discarded them like worn-out shoes,
viewed them as passing clouds.
Some took joy in coarse rice and water,
others dwelled in humble huts.
Ministers of state could not recognize them,
emperors could not command them as subjects.
They lived in forests and caves,
ate pine shoots and drank from streams.
Would they harm the true essence of life
for the fleeting illusions of fame and wealth?
This is the utmost warning for Daoist practice,
the essential duty of self-cultivation.
Thus, ancient examples are cited—
those who seek the Dao must cast off these shackles.

**Chapter Forty-Five: Great Fullness as if Deficient**

Great fullness as if deficient—
its use is unexhausted.

*Official Commentary:*
Within the realm, there are four great principles, and the Dao occupies one.
To embody the Dao fully is why it may be called "great."
Without completion or deficiency, this is called "great fullness."
Not having its fullness, it is as if deficient.
Understanding transformation and adaptation,
yet not clinging to fixed forms,
its use is unexhausted.
This is how Confucius gathered the great teachings and became the Sage of his time.

River Master Hu said:
This refers to a ruler of great virtue and fullness,
as if deficient—
erasing his name, concealing his reputation,
like a broken vessel lacking completeness.
If one’s mind is thus, there is no time of exhaustion.

*Zuanwei* said:
Deficient means broken.
Exhausted means ruin and defeat.
Great fullness refers to a person of complete virtue.
One whose virtue fills within
can embrace chaos and contain filth.
Though their form may be fragmented,
like a vessel with cracks and stains,
rarely is their use seen—
thus they preserve their completeness
and avoid the ruin of exhaustion.

Those who achieve success are deficient in it.
Those who achieve success but do not dwell on it
thus do not abandon it.
Those who achieve fame gather it,
but if they do not cling to empty reputation,
their fame does not fade.
The Perfect One sees the forms of completion and decay,
thus abandons success and fame,
and returns to the common way.
Their Dao is great and full,
yet ever as if deficient.

Emperor Yao achieved success yet saw himself as deficient—
thus he became the greatest of the Five Emperors.
Confucius gathered the great teachings yet did not dwell on his sainthood—
thus he became the teacher of all ages.
His use grows ever newer, never exhausted—
how could it be worn out?

**Great Fullness as if Empty**

Its use is inexhaustible.

*Official Commentary:*
Filling all without boundary,
surpassing all things—this is great fullness.
Empty to respond to all things,
simple yet effective—
thus its application is boundless,
its use inexhaustible.

A wise merchant hides his goods deep,
yet appears empty and virtuous,
his demeanor as if foolish.

Lu Jifu said:
All things are measured from it yet it is never depleted—this is fullness.
Yet when added to, it does not increase—
thus it is as if empty.
Only because it is as if empty
is its use ever renewed and inexhaustible.

Luosha said:
Wisdom without accumulation is called fullness.
Empty yet upright—this is called "empty."
Speaking of those who are greatly full,
they forget their fullness,
though full, they appear empty—
empty, they are not depleted.
If their use is thus,
then it has no end.

The moon wanes when full,
the satisfied mind is diminished.
The Perfect One knows the cycles of fullness and emptiness,
yet though full, remains as if empty.

**Great Straightness as if Bent**

Wang Bi said:
To follow things in straightness—
straightness does not lie within oneself—
thus it appears bent.

Luosha said:
Upright in principle, without deviation—this is straightness.
To conform to things in bending—this is bent.
The body may be bent,
but the Dao cannot be bent.
Great straightness is bending the self to extend the Dao.

Goulu Hui was dismissed three times for his straight path yet did not leave.

**Great Skill as if Clumsy**

Su Shi said:
Skill without clumsiness
would labor in its skill—
to entrust to natural spontaneity,
though clumsy, is skillful.

Liu Jinxi said:
The artisan shapes ten thousand things,
the furnace refines all life—
though possessing great skill and function,
they forget their labor,
hide their traces like the common,
thus it is called clumsy.
Skill lies in what is not done,
letting nature form things.
Without artificial crafting, there is no skillful function—
to give form to things, square or round,
straight or curved—
their subtlety is unseen,
thus it appears clumsy.
To carve and refine all forms yet not claim skill—
is this not the proof?

**The Great Skill as if Clumsy**

There is the craftsmanship of Qi Qing’s chiseling the furnace, not like the Song artisan carving mulberry wood.

**Great Eloquence as if Tongue-Tied**

*Commentary:* Silent eloquence is called great eloquence.
Huizi’s many arguments were indeed small.

Luo Shi said:
To encompass all wisdom is great eloquence—
speaking only what is lawful, hence called tongue-tied.

Su Shi said:
Eloquence without tongue-tied speech
would surely exhaust itself.
Speaking in accordance with principle,
though tongue-tied, is eloquent.

**Hot and Cold, Stillness and Motion**

Hot overcomes cold,
stillness overcomes heat—
clearing and tranquil is the norm for all under Heaven.

Lü Jifu said:
Cold and heat are the workings of Heaven and Earth,
inevitable for all things with form.
Yet one moment of agitation can overcome cold,
one moment of stillness can overcome heat—
if a single instance of agitation or stillness
can prevail over Heaven and Earth’s operations,
how much more so the embodied stillness of non-action,
which establishes the norm for all under Heaven?
This chapter first speaks of the great virtues,
concluding with clearing and tranquil as the norm for all under Heaven.

The Old Master said:
Those who attain enlightenment in the Dao
are always clearing and tranquil.
Those who enlighten to the Dao
must be still, must be clear—
only in emptiness can one attain
the above great virtues.

**Chapter Forty-Six: When the World is Governed by the Dao**

When the world is governed by the Dao,
warhorses are driven back to manure fields.

*Commentary:* When rulers govern by the Dao,
the people take joy in their occupations and do not contend—
they merely manure their fields.

"Governed by the Dao" refers to a ruler who follows the Way.
"Driven back" means to withdraw.
"Warhorses" are steeds that gallop.
"Manure" means to fertilize fields.
It speaks of a ruler who governs the world with the Dao—
content with little, knowing sufficiency,
seeking nothing from beyond,
not employing arms or martial arts,
abandoning warfare and cultivating culture,
with no battles or pursuits—
only tending to the fundamental occupations,
thus withdrawing the galloping steeds
to till the fields.

For those cultivating the self,
the mind-horse does not gallop,
and the elixir field fills naturally.

When the world is without the Dao,
warhorses breed in the suburbs.

Wen Gong said:
Insatiable greed, internal strife, external contention.
A ruler without the Dao
indulges in desires, seizes without restraint,
wages war endlessly—
thus warhorses, bred in the suburbs,
remain there for long without return.

"Suburbs" refers to the outskirts of a city.
The restless and competitive
cannot know contentment and sufficiency,
thus they ride their steeds recklessly,
galloping through the suburbs of sensual pleasures.

**No greater crime than desiring what is desirable.**

*Commentary:* If one does not see what is desirable,
the mind remains undisturbed.
When people’s desires reach the point of endangering life and nature,
crime arises from this.

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said:
When the mind perceives what is desirable,
that is the greatest crime.

Zhang Junxiang said:
When the external scene is beautiful,
the mind calls it "desirable"—
thus it is said.
Yet if the scene can adapt to the mind,
it becomes the cause of crime.
If the cause persists without end,
great crime will surely follow.

**No greater calamity than not knowing sufficiency.**

*Commentary:* Moderation brings fortune,
excess brings calamity.
Knowing sufficiency brings no disgrace—
what calamity could there be?

Wang Yuanzhe said:
Endless external seeking,
losing one’s nature and inviting calamity.
Not recognizing the sufficiency of one’s natural endowment,
yet consuming external things—
this leads to suffering and humiliation,
wandering through life and death,
forever sinking in the sea of filth,
eternally losing the true Dao.
No greater calamity exists than this.

**No greater fault than desiring to obtain.**

Da Meng said:
Seeking what is already sufficient,
yet still desiring more—
the heart, having no bounds,
will inevitably invite great fault.

This speaks of those without the Dao,
indulging their nature and emotions,
thus inviting retribution.
Accumulating evil without end,
they burden both life and death,
their punishment confined to themselves—
a crime passed to their descendants,
a fault extending to their ancestors.

Zuan Wei said:
Fault is retribution.
All things that pass before the eyes
still have limits.
Yet those without the Dao,
seeing or unseen,
desire all things,
demanding they all belong to them without remainder—
this is the extreme of those without the Dao.
Not only does calamity befall oneself,
but retribution and fault extend to future generations.

Crime refers to violating prohibitions—
the first offense is called crime,
the Daoist practice of repentance.
The *Classic* says:
The first offense is crime, also called fault.
Fault means error.
Eighteen hundred offenses become calamity,
which is heavier than crime.
Two thousand one hundred sixty offenses become fault,
which is heavier than calamity.
These three—crime, calamity, fault—
are the deeds of those without the Dao.

All things, seen or unseen,
that one desires must be obtained—
desiring and obtaining is what invites fault.
Self-inflicted disaster is crime,
defying the divine brings calamity,
defying others brings fault.
From desiring what is desirable
to not knowing sufficiency,
not knowing sufficiency leads to desiring to obtain.
Crime leads to calamity,
calamity leads to fault,
and fault brings disaster from the multitude.

No greater crime than calamity,
no greater calamity than fault—
this is the natural order.

Thus, sufficiency in knowing sufficiency
is always sufficient.

*Commentary:* When people see what is desirable,
they do not know sufficiency;
not knowing sufficiency leads to desiring to obtain;
desiring to obtain stirs contention and brings chaos.
Thus, when the world is governed by the Dao,
warhorses are driven back to manure fields.
Yet if one knows sufficiency
and each finds contentment in their nature and life,
applying neither cleverness nor skill—
merely fulfilling daily needs in eating and drinking,
what contention or chaos could there be?

King Shū of Chu said:
Mòzǐ said:
"It is not that there is no place to reside,
but that my heart lacks sufficiency.
It is not that there is no wealth,
but that my heart lacks sufficiency.
All things naturally reach sufficiency,
yet there are those who say they lack—
because their hearts lack sufficiency."
Those who attain the Dao know their hearts and wealth are sufficient,
thus it is said:
"Sufficiency in knowing sufficiency
is always sufficient."

Wang Yuanzhe said:
Each fulfills their natural endowment—
what lack could there be?

This chapter speaks of governing the world with the Dao:
internally knowing sufficiency,
externally seeking nothing—
thus ending contention and war,
avoiding calamity and fault.

**玉诀类**

Cultivating the self means few desires and knowing sufficiency—
the mind’s wild steed unbridled no more,
the elixir field guarded within—
then one may be called a man of the Dao.

*Excerpt from the True Classic of Dao and Virtue, Volume Seven of the Collected Goodness of the Song Dynasty*
*Collected by Resident Master Li Lin of Raoyang*
*Chapter Forty-Seventh: Not Leaving the Door*

Not leaving the door, one knows the world.
Not peering through the window, one sees the Dao of Heaven.

Wang Huibian said:
The traveler, with an empty heart and inner stillness,
does not let the mind gallop through the world’s affairs,
nor does he labor in grasping at external things.
With true discernment of falsehood,
all matters become thoroughly known.

Lu Tian said:
All things are already complete within me.
There is the Dao of Heaven, the Dao of Earth, the Dao of Man.
If one turns inward to seek them without being deceived by the external,
then the Dao of Man can be known without leaving the door,
and the Dao of Heaven can be seen without peering through the window.

Though the world be vast, the sage knows it through the Dao.
Though the Dao of Heaven be distant, the sage sees it with the heart.
When heart and Dao align,
and one observes the world through the Dao,
there is nothing distant that cannot be discerned—
thus, no need to leave the door.
When one sees the Dao of Heaven with the heart,
there is no height it cannot reach—
thus, no need to peer through the window.

Yang Zi said:
How wondrous is the human heart!
When held, it exists; when released, it vanishes.
Who but the sage can constantly hold and preserve it?
The sage preserves the spirit to its utmost,
harmonizing with the juncture of Heaven and Man,
making them unbroken.
The farther one goes, the less one knows.

Cai Zi Huang said:
Those without the Dao cannot examine themselves or know others.
Their minds race after external phenomena—
though they leave the door and travel far,
or peer through windows to gaze at stars,
their knowledge and perception remain scant,
for they abandon themselves to seek without.

Lü Jifu said:
The world is vast, indeed boundless—
if one waits to leave before knowing it,
then the reach of one’s strength is limited,
and how much can one truly know?
The Dao of Heaven is distant, indeed unfathomable—
if one waits to peer before seeing it,
then the reach of one’s sight is limited,
and how much can one truly see?
Thus it is said:
*The farther one goes, the less one knows.*

Wang Yuanzhe said:
The boundless principle is exhausted in a single mote of dust.
No subtle thought arises, all entanglements manifest.
Is not this where the study of the Dao lies?
Yet some seek it afar—how blind they are!
The farther one goes, the less one knows—
is this not so?

Thus the sage acts without going, knows without seeing,
accomplishes without doing.

*Commentary:*
To know the world with one’s wisdom is called *knowing without going*.
To see the Dao of Heaven with one’s heart is called *seeing without peering*.
To know without going, to see without peering—
what need is there for action?
Yet lofty indeed is their accomplishment—
this is called *accomplishing without doing*.
Their knowledge is divine in discerning human affairs—
Emperor Yao and Shun knew the world could be known.
Then the vastness of Heaven and its unity—
Yao and Shun saw the Dao of Heaven could be known.
To know without going, to see without peering—
what need is there for action?
Only to rectify oneself and face south—
thus it is said: *accomplishing without doing*.
This chapter speaks of the Dao itself—
there is the Dao of Heaven, the Dao of Man.
Though Heaven and Man differ, their Dao communicates.
The sage aligns heart with Dao,
and though the world be vast,
it can be known without leaving the door.
Though the Dao of Heaven be distant,
it can be seen without peering through the window.
To know without going, to see without peering,
to accomplish without doing.

**Chapter Forty-Eighth: Daily Increase for Learning, Daily Decrease for the Dao**

*Commentary:*
Learning seeks to attain the Dao—
beginning as a scholar, ending as a sage.
Daily increase accumulates within oneself.
Confucius said to Yan Yuan:
*"I see his progress—
the seeker of the Dao discards limbs and senses,
abandons form and intelligence,
and all affairs dissolve into forgetfulness—
thus it is called *decrease*."*

Lian Boyu thus lived sixty years and transformed sixty times.
Those who cultivate the True begin as scholars seeking the Dao,
and end as sages who have attained it.
While striving in learning to exhaust principles,
and examining all things to seek harmony,
it is called *increase*.
For the Dao, one exhausts nature and reaches supreme emptiness—
all things are known to be false,
and though one does not intend to decrease,
all that was gradually fades away.

First speaking of the learner—
if one does not learn, one cannot clearly see goodness,
nor firmly believe in the Dao,
and thus cannot govern it.
Having attained the Dao, one must abandon learning—
if one does not abandon learning,
then the more one hears and sees, the more one drowns in excess,
and the heart cannot embody the Dao—
thus later speaking of *daily decrease for the Dao*.

Thus it is said:
*Beginning with learning to attain the Dao,*
*ending with attaining the Dao to abandon learning—*
*then one is a fully true man.*

Decrease again, and again decrease,
until reaching *wuwei* [non-action].

Cheng Xuanying said:
The learner clings to desires.
The man of the Dao lingers in *wuwei*.
Though shallow and deep differ,
both are forms of stagnation.
Now to dispel these two fixations,
there is the text of *double decrease*—
first to decrease the *having*,
then to decrease the *non-having*—
both extremes of learning and Dao are cast aside,
neither *having* nor *non-having*—
this is the single accord.

Shu Wang said:
To exhaust principles and fulfill nature,
one must return to the original command.
Thus *decrease again, and again decrease*,
until reaching *wuwei*—
this is returning to the command.
Yet the command is not hastily restored—
one must first exhaust and then restore,
only then reaching the command—
thus it is said:
*Decrease again, and again decrease*,
until reaching *wuwei*.

Lü Jifu said:
To decrease is still to engage in action.
To decrease even the act of decreasing,
only then reaching *wuwei*—
*wuwei* yet nothing remains undone.

Luó Shì said:
To decrease is to have no surplus to discard,
to discard until reaching forgetfulness of evil.

Then there is nothing so slight that it is not removed,
until the act of removal reaches the forgetting of good.

Evil is not; good is.
Having already diminished the "not," one must also diminish the "is,"
thus it is said: *Decrease again, and again decrease.*

When both "is" and "not" are forgotten,
and passions are severed,
virtue and Dao unite—
reaching *wuwei*.

Though one’s own actions are *wuwei*,
one lets all things act of their own accord—
thus nothing remains undone.

Wang Bi said:
To act is to lose something;
thus *wuwei* is to lose nothing.

Lü Jifu said:
The "non-having" is merely the absence of having;
the "not undone" is the means by which one transcends non-having—
this is called *Absolute Learning, No Worry*.

The scholar accumulates what they lack;
the Daoist diminishes what they possess.
To diminish is to diminish what one possesses,
yet even this act of diminishing retains a diminishing mind.
Only when one diminishes even this mind
does one reach *wuwei*.

Silent and unmoving,
yet nothing remains undone—
responding and thus harmonizing with the Way of all things.

The *Classic* says:
*The Dao acts without doing yet nothing remains undone.*

To govern the world,
one must act without action.
Liu Zhongping said:
Confucius praised Yao, saying:
*"Broad and boundless—people cannot name it."*
He praised Shun, saying:
*"He ruled without action;*
*his rule reached such heights*
*that the people said he acted naturally."*

If one does not act without action
to govern the world,
how could one attain this?

*Wuwei* is the true Dao.
The Sage embodies the true Dao.
The world returns to his embrace—
this is how *wuwei* governs the world.
When one acts with affairs,
it is insufficient to govern the world.

*Commentary*:
The world is a great thing.
One who possesses great things
cannot treat all things as things
without being treated as a thing—
thus they can treat all things as things.
Therefore, to govern the world,
one must act without action.

The world is a divine instrument—
it cannot be forced.
Those who force it ruin it;
those who grasp it lose it.
Thus, when one acts with affairs,
it is insufficient to govern the world.

The Sage embodies the Dao
and governs with its truth.
When the Emperor arises,
the King emerges,
responding in harmony—
the world will naturally submit.

The Duke of Zhou and Confucius
left their homelands
and established their states beneath the Qishan peaks.

Tang Minghuang said:
To act with affairs is to labor;
labor leads to decay—
thus it is insufficient to govern the world.

This chapter begins by saying:
The scholar seeks to attain the Dao.
To attain the Dao is to forget the mind,
thus *decrease again, and again decrease*,
until reaching *wuwei* and *wuwei*.

*Wuwei* is the constant Dao;
*wuwei* is the true Dao—
this is how one governs the world.

The *Western Ascension Classic* says:
*Wuwei and wuwei—*
*the state is prosperous, the people are rich.*

**Chapter 49: The Sage Has No Fixed Mind**
