The Sage has no fixed mind,
taking the people’s mind as their own.

*Commentary*:
The Sage’s mind is the illumination of all things.
Empty yet receptive,
still yet responsive—
like a mirror reflecting forms,
adapting to beauty and ugliness;
like a valley echoing sounds,
responding to loud and soft.
How could there be any fixed mind?

Observing all things reveals their nature;
following the people is all—
this is called taking the people’s mind as one’s own.

Zhuangzi said:
*"The people are lowly—*
*one cannot but follow them."*

Liu Jinxi said:
The people are the collective term for all.
Yet the Sage, having no mind,
responds when touched—
responses follow things as they are touched—
thus they take the people’s mind as their own.

Having no mind to respond,
yet nothing remains unresponded to.

The good, I treat as good;
the ungood, I also treat as good—
virtue is good.
The trustworthy, I trust;
the untrustworthy, I also trust—
virtue is trust.

*Commentary*:
Good and evil contradict each other;
truth and falsehood mock each other—
these are the common sentiments of the world,
self-made distinctions.
How could this be virtue?

Virtue as good means seeing all actions as good,
so even the ungood is treated as good.
Virtue as trust means seeing all emotions as trustworthy,
so even the untrustworthy is treated as trustworthy.

True and false are both forgotten,
right and wrong are unified—
this is called the person of perfect virtue.

Wang Yuanzhe said:
Good and evil arise from delusion;
delusion arises from self-interest.
If one is impartial to the Great Dao,
even when seeing good and evil with one’s eyes,
the mind holds no differing thoughts.
Thus the Sage, following the world’s sentiments,
artificially establishes praise and blame,
yet knows that good and evil are originally not opposed—
not out of pity or tolerance,
but because one unconsciously sees no difference.

To forget the reality of good and evil is true goodness.
Thus all phenomena, though differing,
are equal in their true nature.
Trust and distrust arise from self-interest.
Knowing all appearances are delusion,
one sees the ungood as good;
knowing all appearances are real,
one sees the untrustworthy as trustworthy.

When delusion is known as reality,
and reality is known as delusion—
this is how the Sage’s wisdom differs from the common people.

The Sage moves among the people,
ever vigilant,
harmonizing their heart with the world.

*Commentary*:
When in the world,
the Sage shares in the people’s joys and sorrows—
though having no fixed mind,
one cannot but be cautious.
Thus, for oneself, one is ever vigilant,
never at ease;
for the world, one unifies good and evil,
forgets truth and falsehood,
and closes the door to praise—
harmonized, and that is all.

Cai Zixiang said:
With teaching that has no distinctions,
one blends hearts that have distinctions.
Since people have good and ungood,
trust and distrust,
and make dual distinctions,
the Sage appears to all as good and trustworthy—
ultimately, all is unified in mystery,
thus it is said: *harmonized*.

Wang Yuanzhe said:
*Vigilant* means fearful.
The people fix their ears and eyes on the Sage;
the Sage treats them like children.

Wang Yuanzhe said:
Looking up to emulate them,
the ignorant people’s every move is illogical.
The Sage pities and guides them,
like parents to an infant—
gentle and accommodating to their nature,
without anger or resentment.
Who but one who embodies the Dao,
forgets things,
and is as vast as Heaven and Earth,
could do this?

This chapter speaks of the Sage embodying the Dao without thought,
thus forgetting good and evil,
equalizing trust and distrust,
and when in the world, vigilant yet never daring to stray.
As for governing the world,
the Sage simply blends all and that is all.

The people look up and follow them,
heeding only the Sage’s vision and hearing.
When the Sage meets them,
treats them with kindness and generosity,
even those who are not good or trustworthy
become so—
this is the way of nurturing them like children.

Out of ten people,
three are those who preserve life,
three are those who hasten death,
and three, though desiring life,
unwittingly rush toward death.
Those who preserve life
do so by indulging in life to excess,
while those who hasten death
do so by clinging to emptiness and neglecting the body.

The ten who rush toward death
are those who move in perilous ways—
the gallows loom before them,
prisons stare them in the face.
All these forget that the body is their greatest affliction,
that life is limited,
yet cling to it too heavily.
The ancient ones who attained the Dao
neither let wealth and honor harm their bodies
nor let poverty and humiliation burden their forms.
They do not rejoice in longevity,
nor lament in early death.
They awaken in solitude,
and thus can transcend time
and enter the realm of neither death nor life.

The *Classic* says:
People who lightly regard death
do so because their attachment to life is too great—
thus they lightly rush toward death.
Only those who do not cling to life
are wiser than those who value it.

For those who excel in preserving life,
River Master said:
*"Preserving"* means nurturing.
Zhuangzi did not speak of preserving life himself,
but said *"those who hear"*—
a humble phrase.
*"Preserving"* also means guarding.
Within guarding life, one grasps its subtle essence,
thus it is said: *those who excel in preserving life*.

They walk on land but do not meet rhinoceros or tigers,
enter armies but are not struck by weapons.
River Master said:
Walking on land yet not meeting rhinoceros or tigers,
naturally avoids harm, not engaging with it.
Entering armies yet not struck by weapons,
does not delight in killing others through war.

Chen Xuanjing said:
This shows the three poisons cannot harm them—
if one preserves life without clinging to life,
harm cannot harm.
Since they have no internal three poisons,
external three poisons cannot harm them,
thus they walk on land yet do not meet rhinoceros or tigers,
enter armies yet are not struck by weapons.

Why?
For tigers embody anger,
rhinoceroses embody delusion,
weapons embody greed.
The rhinoceros has no place to thrust its horn,
the tiger no place to set its claws,
the weapons no place to lodge their blades.

Shu Wang said:
Only those who excel in preserving life
can be without self.
Without self, they do no harm to others,
and others cannot harm them.

Zhuangzi said:
If a person can empty themselves and wander through the world,
who could harm them?
Why?
Because they have no deathly realm.

「Wang Yuanzé said:
Those who have no deathly realm do so because they have no realm of birth.
For those without birth, they are ever serene and alive,
yet do not cling to their own life—
thus they never die, nor do they ever truly live.
When the Dao reaches this state,
even though their form may have *meditation* (禅),
their spirit never alters—
how could death come to them?
This is the sacred and divine of the Central Realm,
and the Buddha of the Western Land.

If one were to change and fluctuate without constancy,
water and fire could not scorch or drench them,
axes and saws could not wound or sicken them.
They ride the void yet touch the solid,
and go where none can pass—
then desires of the world cannot grasp them,
how much more could they harm?

Someone said:
Then why call them *preserving life* (摄生)?
They replied:
Descend one degree from this,
and you enter the realm of birth-and-death.
All who preserve life must reach this state first,
then life remains ever constant.
This is akin to the profound virtue of those who contain *virtue/power* (德).
Wen Gong said:
The rhinoceros and tiger are metaphors for the strong and violent.
Those who dwell among the strong and violent yet suffer no harm
do so because they are gentle, weak, and do not contend.

Luo Shi said:
*Realm* (地) is akin to *birth* (生).
Because they preserve life without clinging to birth,
the three poisons cannot harm them.
This chapter speaks of the Dao of life-and-death,
expressed in ten words,
each containing three meanings within.

Only those who excel in preserving life
have the Dao unified in one,
transcending these three categories—
even rhinoceroses, tigers, swords, and armies
dare not harm them,
thus it is said they have no deathly realm.

**Chapter 51: The Dao Gives Birth (道生之)**
The Dao gives birth to them,
*Virtue/Power* (德) nurtures them,
things take form,
and circumstances complete them.

*Commentary*: Once form and substance are complete,
the body and its circumstances are established—
the relative shapes of long and short,
high and low, are thus determined.
Lu Jiufu said:
The birth of all things
is always rooted in the *nameless* (无名),
while their nurturing lies in the *one* yet unformed (一而未形),
at the moment when things begin to take shape.
The *nameless* is the Dao,
the *one* yet unformed, which allows things to be born, is *Virtue/Power* (德).
When they become things,
they are merely forms—
not the means by which they were born and nurtured.
Once forms are manifest,
the naked must remain naked,
those with scales, shells, feathers, or fur must remain so,
and the young, the mature, the old, and the dying
cannot escape their respective stages—
all these are the inevitable consequences of circumstances.
Thus it is said:
*The Dao gives birth to them,
Virtue/Power nurtures them,
things take form,
and circumstances complete them.*

The Dao gives birth to spirit,
Virtue/Power distributes *qi* (气),
and through its flowing motion, it gives rise to things—
when things are born and their nature established, that is *form* (形).
Thus all things reverence the Dao
and honor Virtue/Power.

Lu Jiufu said:
Circumstances arise from form,
form arises from Virtue/Power,
and Virtue/Power arises from the Dao.
The Dao and Virtue/Power are the root,
form and circumstances are the branches—
the root is revered while the branches are held low,
the root is honored while the branches are despised—
thus all things reverence the Dao
and honor Virtue/Power.

The Dao is the origin from which all things come,
descending as the pure essence that births and nurtures them.
Virtue/Power is what things receive,
abandoning harmony to cultivate their forms.
When the Dao gives birth to things and acts as their father,
the Dao is revered while things are held low.
Revered, it can overcome and diminish things.
This is what Zhuangzi called *True Sovereign* (真君).

When Virtue/Power nurtures things and acts as their mother,
Virtue/Power is honored while things are held low—
thus things cannot despise it.
This is what Zhuangzi called *True Ruler* (真宰).
The reverence of the Dao,
the honor of Virtue/Power—
none can bestow these titles,
yet they are ever naturally so.

Emperor Ming of Tang said:
Speaking of the reverence and honor of the Dao and Virtue/Power,
it is not conferred by external titles or commands,
but by the inherent function of giving birth and nurturing,
which is ever naturally revered and honored.

Wang Yuanzé said:
If one’s life is decreed by Heaven, they become the Son of Heaven;
if decreed by the Son of Heaven, they become a feudal lord—
wherever life is received from another,
the giver can hold them low.
Only the Dao precedes all things
and governs their life—
who could confer rank upon it?
Thus it is ever naturally revered.

Therefore, the Dao gives birth, nurtures, lengthens, and matures them,
completes and covers them.
This is profound!

When all things transform and change,
the Dao gives birth with them;
when all things gather and hide,
the Dao completes them.
It emerges from *Zhen* (震),
is established in *Gèn* (艮),
is nurtured in *Kūn* (坤),
and is covered in *Qián* (乾).
The interplay of the firm and the yielding
grinds through the eight *trigrams* (卦),
as though bound by a mechanism yet unable to resist—
the Dao truly transcends them.

*Zhuǎn Wēi* said:
The previous passage spoke of the Dao giving birth and Virtue/Power nurturing.
Here, Virtue/Power is not mentioned,
for the Dao’s single *qi* (气) permeates all without distinction,
and the text omits the elaboration of Virtue/Power.

To receive its essence is to be born,
to contain its *qi* is to be nurtured,
to fulfill its form is to grow,
to mark its *village* (村) is to mature—
*Commentary*: This likely refers to the stages of life, not a literal village.
To aid its function is to complete,
to reach its end is to be perfected,
to preserve its harmony is to be nourished,
to shield its harm is to be covered.
These eight aspects
are the primordial functions of the Great Dao.
How could even the smallest living creature
fail to revere and honor it?

To be born yet not possess its function,
to act yet not rely on its ability,
to grow yet not claim dominion—
this is called *Mysterious Virtue/Power* (玄德).

*Commentary*: When the Dao gives birth to all things,
if it were to show ownership,
it would limit its benevolence.
When it nurtures all things,
if it were to claim its actions,
it would dwell in its own works.
When it grows and matures all creatures,
completes and perfects all kinds,
and sustains both living and non-living—
if it were to boast its sovereignty,
it would occupy its own length.
Such withdrawal into hidden depths
can be called profound and distant *hidden virtue/power* (阴德).

Emperor Ming of Tang said:
This summarizes all eight meanings from above.
The Dao’s birth to all things,
if it were to reveal itself as having,
would be constrained by its own benevolence.
Its nurturing of all things,
if it were to rely on its own actions,
would be limited by its own functions.
Its growth and maturation of all creatures,
its completion and perfection of all kinds,
its sustenance of both living and non-living—
if it were to arrogate its rule,
it would be bound by its own length.
Thus, in possessing these qualities yet withdrawing into secrecy,
its virtue is profound indeed,
and its reach is distant.
As it was explained in the *Chapter on Nourishing the Vital Essence* (载营魄章).」

The chapter states that the person’s cultivation aligns with the Dao,
and this chapter speaks of the Dao’s application mirroring humanity.

This chapter expounds on how **virtue/power** (德) and **Dao/Way** (道) bring forth and nurture all things,
and how all things revere the **Dao’s virtue/power** (道德).
The Dao is supremely honored,
its virtue is supremely noble—
who could bestow rank upon it?
It remains ever naturally,
and that is all.

Yet when spoken of separately,
they exhibit distinct qualities.
When spoken of together,
they both arise from the Dao.
Thus, it concludes with *hidden virtue/power* (玄德).

Hence, one who understands the Dao’s **virtue/power** (德) and **Dao/Way** (道) as unified,
sees their profound mystery as one.

**Chapter on the Origin of All Things (天下有始章) – Fifty-Second**

All things have an origin,
and this is called the **origin of all things** (天下有始),
the **Mother of All Things** (天下母).
Lu Jiufu said:
*"All things have an origin,
and this is called the **Mother of All Things**—
then the *Classic* speaks of 'five'?
I do not know its name,
but this is the Dao."*

The nameless is the origin of Heaven and Earth.
The named is the **Mother of all things** (万物之母).
The Dao is always nameless,
yet it serves as the **Mother of All Things**—
why so?
For though the named may suffice as the **Mother of all things**,
it is not enough to be the **Mother of All Things** itself.
The nameless is the origin of Heaven and Earth—
from Heaven downward,
all things are born from the nameless.
Thus, it is said:
*"All things have an origin,
and this is called the **Mother of All Things**."*

The **origin** (始) is the Dao.
The Dao is the **Mother of all things** (万物母).
The **origin** and the **Mother** are both the Dao.
From the beginning of its *qi*,
it is called the **origin**.
From its eternal nurturing,
it is called the **Mother**.
Once there is an **origin**,
it can give rise to eternal nurturing.
Having obtained the **Mother**,
one knows its offspring.
**御注**: *"The Dao, as the **Mother of all things**,
names them—thus, things are its offspring.
Those who are attuned to the Dao
comprehend both the **Mother** and the offspring.
Thus, having known the **Mother**,
one knows its offspring."*

Wang Yuanzhe said:
*"All things emerge from the Dao,
the Dao is their **Mother**,
and things are their offspring.
Gaining the Dao,
one understands the principles of all things
without needing to discern them.
Having known the offspring,
one must then preserve the **Mother**—
He Shangong said:
*'Having understood the principles of all things under Heaven,
one must then preserve the Dao and return to non-action,
so that one’s life ends without peril.'*
Wang Yuanzhe said:
*'If, after seeing the principles,
one follows them without returning to the Dao,
then one acts recklessly and invites disaster—
far from the Dao indeed.
Thus, knowing the offspring while preserving the **Mother**,
one is ever free from peril.
Having known the offspring,
one must then preserve the Dao,
abandoning clever scheming
to return to ignorance
and safeguard oneself—
thus, one is free from all danger of ruin.
Close its **ui** (兑), shut its **door** (门),
and one’s life will never be weary.
He Shangong said:
*'**Ui** (兑) is the eyes—
let the eyes not gaze recklessly.
**Door** (门) is the mouth—
let the mouth not speak recklessly.'*
Lu Jiufu said:
*'This is the meaning of preserving the **Mother**.
When the heart stirs within,
and one indulges it,
this is called having **ui** (兑).
If one has **ui**,
the heart will stray outward,
and one will lose distinction between self and things—
thus, close its **ui** and keep it sealed,
and the heart will not stray outward.
When things draw one’s attention externally,
and one accepts them,
this is called having a **door** (门).
If one has a **door**,
things will enter and disturb the heart,
so shut its **door** and do not accept them—
and things will not enter.
The heart does not stray outward,
things do not enter—
though all things change,
and myriad transformations unfold before one,
each returns to its root,
yet one remains unaware.
How then could one be weary?'*

Open its **ui** (兑), further its affairs,
and one’s life will never be saved.
Shu Wang said:
*"The opposite of 'saved' (没) is 'to exist' (存).
The opposite of 'beginning' (终) is 'end' (始).
When paired with 'door' (门),
'further its affairs' (济其事) implies
that the **door** is not shut—
thus, affairs do not remain sealed.
If one further its affairs,
then the **door** is not shut—
this is clear."*

Pei Chu’en said:
*"Opening the **ui** of the five desires,
furthering the affairs of the six dusts,
wearies the spirit within,
fatigues the senses without—
this is lifelong labor and exhaustion."*

**Seeing the Small as Clarity (见小曰明)**
**纂微曰**: *"This means preventing issues before they arise,
and addressing them before they become chaotic.
If one knows the art of closing **ui** and shutting **door**,
then one sees the small and subtle—
seeing the small and subtle,
one’s affairs cannot grow dim;
this is called clarity.
For only those who preserve the **Mother**
ever see the stirrings of their hearts.
The small and subtle are the Dao’s subtleties—
seeing the Dao’s subtleties,
one knows oneself alone—
thus, there is no lack of clarity."*

**Preserving Softness as Strength (守柔曰强)**
Zhang Junxiang said:
*"Following the Dao without resistance is softness;
beginning and ending without loss is strength."*
Lu Jiufu said:
*"Preserving the formless utmost softness,
and no thing can overcome it."*

**Using Its Light, Returning to Its Clarity (用其光，复归其明)**
**御注**: *"Clarity (明) is the essence of light.
Light (光) is the manifestation of clarity.
When the Sage responds to the world,
clarity manifests as light—
thus, brilliance is light.
Gathering light back into clarity,
wisdom penetrates as clarity.
Displaying all virtues outwardly,
while concealing their applications—
like the sun and moon illuminating all things,
each thing is clear and bright,
yet nothing is diminished—
this is how the Sage’s **virtue/power** (德) is divine and profound."*

He Shangong said:
*"Using its light outwardly,
one must then return its clarity inwardly,
lest the spirit be depleted.
Avoiding harm to oneself,
this is called *clinging to constancy* (袭常)."*
**御注**: *"The transformations of things are without constancy.
Only those who return to their original nature
abandon things and separate from humanity,
and return to their clarity,
without transforming along with things—
their essence remains constant,
free from peril,
differing from those who merge with form and become light."*

Gu Huan said:
*"If one opens its **ui**,
furthering its affairs,
disaster and blame will surely come.
Yet this disaster is not external calamity—
it is self-inflicted harm.
Reflecting light inward,
observing the spirit within,
not allowing it to leak away,
then one’s life will never suffer disaster and blame—
this is called the *path of profound constancy and enduring duration* (密合常久之道)."*

This chapter centers on the principle of preserving the **Mother**.
The Dao is the **Mother of all things**,
and all things are the Dao’s offspring.
Closing **ui**, shutting **door**,
seeing the small, preserving softness—
even to the point of responding to things,
using light, and returning to clarity—
all are the principle of preserving the **Mother**.
Thus, its Dao remains ever constant,
free from disaster and blame—
this is the *path of profound constancy and enduring truth* (密用真常之道).

**Chapter on the Sage’s Isolation (使我介然章) – Fifty-Third**

*"The Sage stands alone, with knowledge,
walking the Great Dao—
only the **Way** (施) does he fear."*

「Lu Tian said: The Dao is preserved by having no desires,
and is practiced by having no knowledge,
thus it is said: *"The Sage stands alone, with knowledge,
walking the Great Dao—
only the **Way** (施) does he fear."*

Indeed, one with knowledge,
within cannot avoid pride,
without cannot avoid partiality,
thus when he acts, he has something to fear.
**Isolation** (介) is small yet discerning,
knowing little of things,
yet following the Dao with greatness.

The Dao, when practiced by one who knows,
is already cautious of **bestowal** (施设).
How much more so for one with great knowledge?
For the **Perfect Person’s Dao** (至人之道),
does not allow the superficial to harm the fundamental.

If **bestowal** (施) is excessive externally,
then the inner **roots** (根本) will be depleted.
Thus, those walking the Great Dao
do not nurture trifling concerns,
and all entanglements are abandoned;
there is no room for **isolated knowledge** (介然之知).

**Yin Que** asked **Wang Ni**,
four questions posed, four answers unknown.
The Great Dao is most **level** (夷),
yet people love **shortcuts** (径).

Lu Tian said: A **shortcut** (径) is not the path,
taking shortcuts while hastening brings affliction.
**Level** (夷) means plain and easy.
A **shortcut** (径) is crooked, not upright.
People are confused,
abandoning the Great Dao and not following it,
preferring crooked shortcuts to seek swiftness,
desiring speed to invite near-term success,
using knowledge to seek the Dao while drifting farther from it.

Moreover, how could they know
that the Great Dao is attained by **knowing without knowing** (以不知而知),
that is the most **level** (夷) of all?
People instead wish to seek it through knowledge,
loving shortcuts while unaware of their inner harm.

**Dan Tai Mie Ming** (澹台灭明),
walking without shortcuts,
is thus walking the Great Dao.

The harms of shortcuts are fully detailed below:

**Morning affairs are most neglected** (朝甚除),
Gu Xuan said: *除* (除) means to **overindulge** (修).
Those who love shortcuts
consider only the **overindulgence of courtly affairs** (修朝) as good.

The **palaces of Zhou**, the **Chang Hua of Chu**, the **Ai Fang of Qin**—
all are examples of this.

**Fields are most overgrown** (田甚芜),
He Shang Gong said: High towers and pavilions,
courtly affairs **overindulged** (修),
farm work abandoned,
not plowing and tilling in season.

Gu Xuan said: When grass grows tall, it is called *overgrown* (芜).
*Overgrown* (芜) means **neglect and ruin** (荒废).
If one indulges in **carved halls and painted walls** (峡宇雕墙),
exhausting wealth and beauty to the utmost,
then human effort is drained,
fields lie **neglected and ruined** (芜荒),
inner **clarity** (明) is **sacrificed for outward fame and gain** (徇名好利),
abandoning little for much,
**Dao practice and alchemical work** (道业) are not cultivated,
the **elixir field** (丹田) lies **neglected and ruined**.

Many fields lie **ruined** (荒),
this is why the *Book of Songs* (《诗》) censures **King You of the Shang** (幽王).
**Granaries are most empty** (仓甚虚),

Shu Wang said: *除* (除) means **governance** (治).
Though courtly affairs are **governed** (治),
fields instead become **ruined** (芜),
granaries instead become **empty** (虚).
Granaries are the **roots** (本).
Now they are **ruined and empty** (芜虚),
this is from people abandoning the Great Dao
and rushing toward shortcuts.

Wang Yuanze said: When fields are **governed** (治),
granaries are filled,
this is the **root** (本) of a state.
Now they instead **overindulge** (务除) in courtly affairs,
for the sake of short-term splendor,
while not heeding that the **roots have already been depleted** (根本之已竭),
how could this be the **path of enduring duration** (持久之道)?

Those who **sacrifice clarity for superficial gain** (以末伤本者),
are all like this.

**Wearing ornate attire** (服文彩),
He Shang Gong said: **Loving false adornment and outward splendor** (好饰伪，贵外华).
**Green and red** (青赤) are **patterns** (文),
**varied silks** (色丝) are **embroidery** (采).

**Carrying sharp swords** (带利剑),
Cheng Xuan Ying said: **Intricately embroidered robes** (黼黻文绣)
are worn to **adorn the body** (丽其身).
**Dryad’s and Mo Chai’s blades** (干将镆铘)
are carried to **guard one’s life** (卫其命).

**Sated with food and drink** (厌饮食),
yet **wealth and goods remain in excess** (财货有余).」

「Han Fei said: When one enters a state, its **governance and teachings** (政教) can be discerned.

If the **courtly halls** (朝阙) are **fastidiously adorned** (甚修除),
and the **palace walls** (墙宇) are **elaborately carved** (甚雕峻),
then one knows the **state’s ruler** (国君) **loves grand constructions** (好土木之功)
and **indulges in frivolous pleasures** (多游嬉之娱).

If the **fields and pastures** (田野) are **barren and neglected** (甚荒芜),
and **agricultural labor is forsaken** (农事失耕治),
then one knows the ruler **relishes forced labor** (好力役),
**seizing the people’s time** (夺民时).

If the **granaries** (仓廪) are **empty and void** (甚空虚),
and **farmers perish from hunger** (农夫多殍饿),
then one knows the ruler **prizes superficial pursuits** (好末作),
**abandoning foundational livelihoods** (废本业).

If **garments are rich in patterns and embroidery** (衣服多文采),
**coarse fabrics are discarded in favor of ornate ones** (质丧而贵华),
then one knows the ruler **delights in licentious craftsmanship** (好淫巧),
**exhausting female artisans** (蠹女工).

If **all who wear sashes carry sharp blades** (佩带皆利剑),
**rigid and fierce in their rivalry for combat** (刚强而竞斗),
then one knows the ruler **cherishes martial prowess** (好武勇),
**breeding state calamities** (生国患).

If **feasting is marked by gluttony** (饮食常厌饮),
**rich broths are savored and fresh meats struck** (烹肥而击鲜),
then one knows the ruler **indulges in drunkenness and satiety** (好醉饱),
**neglecting the people’s affairs** (忘民事).

If **wealth and goods are always in excess** (财货常有余),
and **treasures of rare and exotic things are hoarded** (务多藏珍异),
then one knows the ruler **relishes accumulation and extortion** (好聚敛),
**burdening the people’s resources** (困民财).

**Master Zhongni** (仲尼) said: *"If the common folk lack sustenance, how can the ruler be abundant?"* (百姓不足，君孰与足)
This is the very meaning.

**Emperor Tang Ming Huang** (唐明皇) said:
*"Rich broths and fresh meats are struck—
I weigh the flavors heavily,
and gluttony sates my appetite."* (烹肥击鲜，重滋味，厌饮足也)
*"Accumulated wealth and stored goods abound with rare treasures."* (聚敛积实饶珍异)

**Wang Yuanze** (王元泽) said:
*"Lavish expenditure on externals
to seek fleeting gratification,
while neglecting foundational livelihoods—
this is the clear case of one who **loves wasteful display** (好施) **to harm the roots** (以伤本)."* (此明好施以伤本者)
One text reads: *"**Loves wasteful display** (好施) **to harm the people’s wealth** (以伤货财)."* (一本作货财)

**Zhou** (纣) **filled the Lu Tai Treasury** (实鹿台之财)
with **abundant millet** (盈巨桥之栗).
This is called **"robbery and boast"** (盗夸),
but is it truly robbery? (非盗也哉)

**Luo Shi** (罗什) said:
*"Taking what is not one’s own is **theft** (盗),
boasting of one’s own abilities is **vanity** (夸).*
*The thief **plunders secretly** (阴取於人) **and fears detection** (畏人知).
Yet these seven matters—
all **pursue superficial gains while forgetting the roots** (徇末忘本),
**take what is not their own** (取非其有),
and **do not conceal their excesses** (仍不自隐密),
**flaunting them before others** (夸大於人)—
this is called **"robbery and boast"** (盗夸).

**If one boasts yet cannot endure** (矜),
**how can this persist?** (岂能持久)
Thus, it is said: *"This is not the **Dao/Way**."* (非道)

This chapter speaks of those who **walk in the Great **Dao/Way** (行於大道者),
yet **do not stand firmly** (不可介然).
If they **know a little** (有知) **and claim to know more** (若小有所知),
then their **actions flourish outwardly** (施为盛於外),
while their **roots wither inwardly** (根本虚於内).
This is to **abandon the Great **Dao/Way** in favor of perverse paths** (弃大道而趋邪径).

The **Great **Dao/Way** is the root** (大道者本也).
When **superficial pursuits flourish** (末盛), the **root decays** (则本衰).
All the following passages **illustrate how superficial excess harms the root** (皆以末伤本而施之过也).»

*(End of **Dao De Zheng Jing Qu Shan Ji** Volume 8 / Volume 9)*

「The **Chapter on the Unshaken Foundation of Virtue/Power** (善建者不拔章第五十四):

**A foundation of virtue/power that is not shaken** (善建者不拔),
**Commentary of the Imperial Edict:** *The foundation is unshaken because it encompasses both the upper and lower aspects* (建中以该上下故不拔).
**Explanation by Jie Jie:** *It speaks of those who embody the virtuous person (君子), establishing their body and mind with virtue/power and the Dao/Way. If they lack virtue/power to display, and no traces remain to be seen, then their roots are deep and their foundation firm, and they are not pulled away by the winds of right and wrong, desire or evil. If, however, those who establish virtue/power seek fame by cutting down virtue/power, flaunt their deeds to gain reward, and though their outward virtues are displayed, their hidden merit is not cultivated, they will ultimately be uprooted by the impermanent obstacles of karma. Thus, the **Huainanzi** says: "A foundation of virtue/power is seen in one's appearance. Gold is consumed by fire, fire is extinguished by water, water is blocked by earth, wood is felled by an axe. Only that which is founded upon the foundationless (無建者) cannot be uprooted."*

**Those who embrace virtue/power do not lose it** (善抱者不脱),
**Annotations by Zuan Wei:** *Those who embrace the Dao/Way to hold the unity of the primal essence (元), then their vital essence and spirit (精神) remain whole and do not slip away.*
**Commentary by Shū Wáng:** *To embrace (抱) is to be one (一). If one embraces unity and does not depart from it, then there is no loss.*
**Sacrifices by descendants never cease.**
**Commentary of the Imperial Edict:** *The foundation is centered within the Dao/Way and not external to it; embracing unity without separation from vital essence. If one is such, how could their influence be confined to a single state or generation? Rather, it will extend to the whole world and future ages, its transmission far indeed.*
**Commentary by Tang Emperor Xuanzong:** *It speaks of a ruler who establishes virtue/power and embraces the Dao/Way, whose merit extends to posterity, cherishing the sweet osmanthus trees (甘棠) for their descendants. For rulers, their ancestors have merit, and their lineage has virtue/power. Thus, the rise of the Zhou began with Hou Ji and was completed by King Wen and King Wu. The Zhou sacrifices were made to Hou Ji at the outer altars (郊祀), and to King Wen at the ancestral temples (宗祀). Though divination predicted a change after thirty generations, and a span of seven hundred years, the destruction of the temple's main deity (毁庙之主) and the scattering of its influence (流溢於外), the sacrifices to Hou Ji and King Wen at the outer altars and ancestral temples never ceased.*

**Cultivate it in oneself, and its virtue/power becomes true** (修之身，其德乃真);
**Commentary by Dù Biàn:** *Illustrate the near cultivation and distant establishment of the Dao/Way in oneself, so that it does not slip away, and its roots are firm. Cultivating oneself in this way, true virtue/power lies within oneself.*

**Cultivate it in the family, and its virtue/power overflows** (修之家，其德乃余);
**Commentary by Hé Shàng Gōng:** *In a family that cultivates the Dao/Way, the father is benevolent, the son is filial, the elder brother is friendly, the younger brother is obedient, the husband is trustworthy, and the wife is chaste. Such virtue/power overflows indeed.*
**Commentary by Wáng Bì:** *This extends from oneself to others.*

**Cultivate it in oneself, and its virtue/power overflows; cultivate it in the family, and its virtue/power becomes long-lasting.**
**Cultivate it in the community, and its virtue/power becomes abundant;**
**Cultivate it in the state, and its virtue/power becomes plentiful;**
**Cultivate it in the world, and its virtue/power becomes universal.**
**Commentary of the Imperial Edict:** *Cultivate it in oneself, and its virtue/power becomes true. This is the Dao/Way’s true virtue/power to govern oneself. Cultivate it in the family, and its virtue/power overflows. Cultivate it in the community, and its virtue/power becomes long-lasting. This is the surplus extending to govern others. Cultivate it in the state, and its virtue/power becomes abundant. Cultivate it in the world, and its virtue/power becomes universal. This is the straight path to govern the world and its states. The farther one cultivates, the broader its virtue/power becomes. Within oneself, it is all true. In others, it is merely the superficial aspect. Thus, overflowing leads to long-lasting, and abundant leads to universal—these are external to the Dao/Way.*

**Annotations by Zuan Wei:** *These five stages begin with near cultivation of oneself and extend far to the world. Thus, observe oneself to understand oneself; observe the family to understand the family...*

**Commentary by Dào Chōng:** *Examine the gains and losses, right and wrong of oneself, and one will know all people. Examine the harmony or discord, prosperity or decline of one’s family, and one will know other families.*

**Observe the community to understand the community; observe the state to understand the state; observe the world to understand the world.**
**Commentary of the Imperial Edict:** *All things are complete within me. Reflecting sincerely upon oneself, no greater joy exists. Thus, by observing oneself to understand oneself, one’s self is governed. Extending this analogy...*

**The world has its constants, yet by observing them, the world is governed.**
**Annotations by Zuan Wei:** *To observe is to scrutinize and reflect. Thus, by the Dao/Way of sage-rulers (先、圣) to govern oneself, reflect upon my own body and mind. If my body and mind can embody the Dao/Way, then its virtue/power is true. By the Dao/Way of sage-rulers to govern the family, reflect upon my own family. If my family can harmonize their relationships, then its virtue/power overflows. By the Dao/Way of sage-rulers to govern the community, reflect upon my own neighbors. If my neighbors can trust in their friends, then its virtue/power becomes long-lasting. By the Dao/Way of sage-rulers to govern the state, reflect upon my own citizens. If my citizens can fulfill their lives, then its virtue/power becomes abundant. By the Dao/Way of sage-rulers to govern the world, reflect upon all the people in the world. If they can be without desire and without knowledge, then its virtue/power becomes universal. How do I know the world is thus? By this.*

**Commentary by Hé Shàng Gōng:** *The **Laozi** says: "How do I know the world?" Those who cultivate the Dao/Way thrive, while those who abandon it perish. By these five stages, one observes and knows it.*

**Commentary by Wáng Bì:** *This is what was spoken of above. It says: "How do I know the world?" Observe oneself to know it, seeking no knowledge from outside. This is the saying: "Without leaving one’s door, one knows the world."*

**Commentary by Wáng Yuàn Zé:** *The sage-rulers uphold one Dao/Way, with no other divergent teachings. Thus, their method of being and their method of knowing are all derived from this Dao/Way. This chapter begins by speaking of those who establish virtue/power, embrace it, and thereby know the world—these are the fundamental principles of the world itself. Those who establish virtue/power and embrace it are the cultivation of oneself. Extending this to the world, one can say it is the foundation of governance and knowledge.*

---

**Chapter on the Depth of Embracing Virtue/Power** (含德之厚章第五十五):

**The depth of embracing virtue/power** (含德之厚)
**is like that of an infant.**
**Commentary of the Imperial Edict:** *Only the common people are born with thick virtue/power; as they grow, their nature changes. But those who deeply embrace virtue/power do not change with circumstances, thus their **qi** is concentrated and their will is unified.*
**Commentary by Mèngzǐ:** *The noble person does not lose their infantile heart.*
**Commentary by Tang Emperor Xuanzong:** *The perfected sage (至人) who deeply embraces the richness of virtue/power and the Dao/Way, their conduct is like that of an infant.*

**Chapter on the Depth of Embracing Virtue/Power** (含德之厚章第五十五)

Lǚ Jífǔ said:
*"When a person is first born, their virtue and nature are most profound and rich.*
*As they grow older, their ears and eyes engage with the external world,*
*and their mind and awareness receive impressions from within.*
*With the passage of days, their richness diminishes.*
*Those who cultivate the Dao/Way reduce what they have gained through growth,*
*refining their nature and returning to their original virtue.*
*Thus, it is said:*
*'The depth of embracing virtue/power is like that of an infant.'*
*含 (contain) is the same as 抱 (embrace).*
*The infant’s nature is pure yet undispersed,*
*their qi is harmonious and not violent.*
*Those who deeply embrace virtue/power*
*possess a nature inherently profound and rich,*
*unshaken by external influences,*
*thus likened to an infant.*
*But embracing virtue/power to its fullest*
*is not merely to have the appearance of an infant—*
*it is to possess the infant’s heart.*
*Venomous creatures do not sting them,*
*ferocious beasts do not attack them,*
*and raptors do not seize them.*

Zhāng Jūnxiàng said:
*"Venomous creatures—such as bees, wasps, and snakes—*
*strike by means of their qi.*
*Ferocious beasts—like tigers and rhinoceroses—*
*trample with their feet.*
*Raptors—such as hawks and eagles—*
*injure with their claws.*
*The infant has no intention of harm,*
*thus they are not harmed by such creatures.*
*Moreover,*
*venomous creatures symbolize anger,*
*ferocious beasts symbolize delusion,*
*and raptors symbolize greed.*
*The infant transcends these three evils,*
*thus they are not injured by the Three Poisons.*
*Those who deeply embrace virtue/power*
*practice enduring compassion and patience,*
*generating no anger within,*
*so it is said that venomous creatures do not sting them.*
*Having relinquished all attachment,*
*they have no greed or desire,*
*thus likened to raptors that do not seize.*
*They possess constant clarity and wisdom,*
*forever free from delusion,*
*so it is said that ferocious beasts do not attack them.*
*The infant, being in perfect harmony with the received qi,*
*has no intention of harm,*
*thus nothing can injure them.*
*How much more so for those whose qi is fully guarded and harmonized!*
*What creature, shaped by external forces yet in perfect accord with all things,*
*could possibly harm them?*
*Zhuāngzǐ said:*
*'When a person can empty themselves and wander through the world,*
*who could harm them?'*
*Their bones are weak, their sinews soft, yet their grip is firm.*
*Héshàng Gōng said:*
*'The infant’s sinews and bones are weak yet their grip is firm,*
*because their intention is concentrated and their mind unshaken.'*
*The infant’s intention is concentrated, their will unified,*
*their mind knows nothing, their hands do nothing—*
*thus their fists naturally clench with unyielding strength.*
*They do not yet know the union of male and female*
*yet their movements are spontaneous and pure,*
*their essence at its peak.*

The Imperial Edict commentary said:
*"Those whose virtue is complete possess a form that is complete,*
*thus their bones are weak and their sinews soft yet their grip is firm.*
*Those whose form is complete have a spirit that is complete,*
*thus they do not yet know the union of male and female*
*yet their movements are spontaneous and pure.*
*When their essence is at its peak,*
*they can enter the spirit and restore their vital command (神命)."*

Zhuāngzǐ said:
*"The sage values essence (精)."*

Lín Língsù said:
*"作 (movement) refers to the infant’s yin (obscure, receptive force).*
*牝牡之合 (union of male and female) is the interplay of yin and yang.*
*The infant, not yet knowing the principles of union,*
*yet moving spontaneously,*
*possesses essence that does not disperse.*
*Those who deeply embrace virtue/power,*
*though enlightened to all dharmas,*
*remain in a state of emptiness and stillness,*
*yet do not abandon movement.*
*They respond to things without attachment,*
*their actions in accord with virtue and the times—*
*this is the manifestation of tranquil bodily conduct."*

Wáng Yuànzé said:
*"If their movement arises from external craving,*
*then it is the infant’s essence being squandered,*
*and death follows.*
*How could such a state endure?*
*This is the greatest affliction of the world,*
*yet also the most critical warning for those who seek the Dao/Way."*

*The infant cries all day without ceasing,*
*yet their voice does not grow hoarse—*
*this is the harmony of essence at its utmost.*

The Imperial Edict commentary said:
*"To achieve unity is to attain essence (精).*
*Essence ensures virtue is complete and the spirit undiminished.*
*Harmony arises from the received qi (冲气).*
*Harmony ensures the qi is complete and the throat does not grow hoarse.*
*When a person is born,*
*their essence is received from the One (天一) as the source of wisdom,*
*and their harmony is obtained from the Five (天五) as the foundation of trust.*
*When this harmony reaches its peak,*
*they can enter the spirit and restore their vital command (神命).*
*But those who lose the infant’s heart*
*let their essence waver without restraint,*
*their qi erupt violently and impurely,*
*driving their form and nature to exhaustion,*
*and sinking them into the myriad things—*
*is this not tragic?"*

*When crying reaches its limit yet produces no sound, it is called 嗄 (hoarse).*
*The infant, from morning to evening,*
*cries without variation in tone,*
*because their mind is unmoved and their qi harmonious.*
*If the infant’s mind were troubled by worry or resentment,*
*their qi would clash and become unharmonious—*
*could it remain thus?*
*Those who deeply embrace virtue/power*
*utter profound speech without exhaustion,*
*and expound the law without depletion—*
*this too is the result of embracing the harmonious and received qi.*

*"To know harmony is to know constancy (常)."*
Shū Wáng said:
*"Harmony, in its application, is constant and unchanging,*
*thus it is said:*
*'To know harmony is to know constancy (常).'*
*When a person knows that their qi is harmonious yet weak,*
*they hold command over their life,*
*possessing constancy that does not vary.*
*Thus, at the peak of cultivating the Dao/Way,*
*they attain the restoration of vital command (复命).*
*To restore vital command is to know constancy (常).*
*Embracing virtue/power to its depth*
*leads one to know harmony,*
*and to know harmony is to know constancy (常).*
*Though virtue/power and the Dao/Way may seem separate,*
*when they converge in constancy (常),*
*they become One.*
*To know constancy is to be enlightened (明)."*

Sōng Língxiān said:
*"Harmony is the undivided middle.*
*The Dao/Way is One, without relative form,*
*unchanged by birth and death,*
*thus it is named constancy (常).*
*To know this constancy*
*brings daily enlightenment."*

Shū Wáng said:
*"Without enlightenment, one cannot know constancy (常)."*

*"To increase what is already born is called 祥 (auspicious)."*
Shū Wáng said:
*"Birth cannot be increased,*
*yet people always seek to augment what is born—*
*this brings disaster and inauspiciousness (凶祥)."*

Sūn Dēng said:
*"The richness of perpetual birth*
*is a perversion that brings auspicious yet ominous transformations.*
*Once their form is received,*
*their innate divisions are fixed,*
*their vital processes fully sufficient—*
*no lack, no excess.*
*If nourished directly,*
*they will reach their peak.*
*But if one forcibly increases it,*
*it becomes only an encumbrance,*
*not merely useless but harmful—*
*this is called disaster and inauspiciousness (凶祥)."*

*"The mind directing the qi is called 强 (forced)."*
The Imperial Edict commentary said:
*"When the body aligns with the mind,*
*and the mind aligns with the qi,*
*then the qi is harmonious and not violent.*
*Those who rush or stumble—*
*this is the qi.*
*If the mind truly directs it,*
*then it is forced (强).*
*To contend with things thus*
*is not the Dao/Way of self-mastery."*

Shū Wáng said:
*"The qi should be concentrated to achieve softness (柔).*
*Now it is instead directed by the mind,*
*unable to remain inwardly guarded,*
*thus it becomes violent."*

Thus it is said:
*"When the mind directs the qi, it is called 强 (forced)."*
*The Classic of Documents says:*
*"Do good deeds, and auspiciousness will descend upon you a hundredfold."*
*The previous chapter says:*
*"Guarding softness is called 强 (forced)."*
*Here, auspiciousness is not the auspiciousness of doing good deeds—*
*it is the inauspiciousness of calamitous omens.*
*Here, 强 (forced) is not the 强 (forced) of guarding softness—*
*it is the 强 (forced) of tyranny and aggression.*

*Wang Yuanze said:*
*"If the mind directs the qi, then the qi in turn directs the mind.*
*When mind and qi vie for control,*
*the harmony of Heaven is destroyed,*
*and the True Essence is diminished.*
*What a person receives cannot be artificially increased or diminished.*
*Thus, both forcing growth and depleting the qi*
*are violations of natural order.*
*Mencius warned against pulling up seedlings to hasten their growth,*
*and Laozi spoke against artificially augmenting life.*
*All that is required is to preserve one’s pure qi.*
*This 强 (forced) is not the 强 (forced) of self-mastery—*
*it is the 强 (forced) of tyranny and aggression.*

*Alas!*
*Those of vulgar pursuits, unable to emulate the innocence of an infant,*
*indulge in boundless desires,*
*exhausting the True Essence’s vitality.*
*Some take medicines, others employ petty techniques,*
*seeking to augment the True Essence,*
*yet only invite sickness.*
*Thus Laozi warned against artificially augmenting life.*

*Unable to emulate the infant’s harmonious qi,*
*some use intent to cultivate visualization,*
*others strain the mind to move the qi,*
*desiring the qi to flourish and the body to be full,*
*yet only exhausting the spirit and depleting the qi.*
*Thus Laozi spoke against forcing the qi.*

*When things grow robust, they age—*
*this is called *not-Dao/Way*,*
*and *not-Dao/Way* brings early decay.*
*Su Ziyou said:*
*"To artificially augment life and force the qi,*
*not allowing it to follow its natural course,*
*is to daily incline toward rigidity,*
*and thus aging follows.*
*This is not the nature of the infant.*

*Things cannot be made robust—*
*robustness brings aging.*
*The Dao/Way precedes Heaven and Earth yet does not grow robust,*
*endures beyond ancient times yet does not age.*
*If one does not grasp the True Constant of the Dao/Way,*
*and instead artificially augments life and forces the qi,*
*becoming a tyrant of aggression,*
*one is merely a thing—*
*how can this be the Dao/Way?*
*Thus it is said: early decay.*

*This chapter speaks of the infant’s lack of contrivance,*
*and those who embody profound virtue resemble this.*
*The infant harms nothing with its mind,*
*yet nothing harms it in return.*
*Thus its essence remains whole and undispersed,*
*its qi harmonious and unviolent.*
*To know this is to grasp the True Constant.*
*If one does not know this,*
*then artificially augmenting life and forcing the qi*
*brings the distinctions of robustness and decay,*
*losing the infant’s mind.*
*This is not one who embodies profound virtue.*

---
*The Wise Do Not Speak* (知者不言)

*The Master of the Yellow River said:*
*"The wise value action, not speech."*

*Cheng Xuanying said:*
*"Those who know the Dao penetrate its subtle principles.*
*Principles transcend speech,*
*thus they do not speak."*

*Gu Huan said:*
*"When the Dao is manifest, speech is forgotten.*
*When speech is preserved, the Dao is lost.*
*The Dao can be silently understood,*
*not articulated by the mouth.*
*Thus Zhuangzi said: ‘The Dao has no questions, questions have no answers.’*
*Those who speak do not know."*

*The Compilation of Subtleties said:*
*"The wise act with the mind, not with rhetoric.*
*They value action, not speech.*
*Those who discuss the Dao rely on rhetoric, not the mind,*
*losing the Dao yet preserving their words.*
*The Dao cannot be spoken,*
*and if spoken, it is false.*
*The wise do not speak,*
*thus the Sage Confucius desired silence,*
*and Mencius was not fond of debate.*

*There are those who awaken beings through proper bearing,*
*those who perceive the Dao through direct observation.*
*Four questions, four unknowns,*
*three questions, three unanswered—*
*these are all instances where the Dao cannot be spoken.*

*Moreover, those who recognize sounds cannot transmit them with the mouth,*
*and the wheelwright cannot speak of his craft.*
*Even those who excel in technique cannot articulate it—*
*how much more so the profound and subtle Dao?*
*Much speech leads to exhaustion,*
*and distance from the Dao.*

*Close the mouth’s aperture,*
*shut the door to speech.*

*The Imperial Edict commentary said:*
*"Retreat and conceal within the profound.*
*Withdraw to store oneself in secrecy."*

*The Compilation of Subtleties said:*
*"This passage shares the same text as Chapter Fifty-Two,*
*yet its meaning differs.*
*There, it emphasizes the Dao’s purity and stillness,*
*to block the roots of desires and joys.*
*Here, it upholds the Dao’s wordlessness,*
*thus invoking the saying of diminishing hearing and discarding sight.*

*The Dao is formless,*
*thus it cannot be seen with the eyes,*
*nor transmitted by the mouth.*
*The Perfect One closes the mouth’s aperture and shuts the door,*
*yet excels in gathering the eyes and contracting the ears,*
*clamping the mouth and sealing the tongue.*
*Thus it is said:*
*"Close the mouth’s aperture,*
*shut the door to speech."*

*Lu Tian said:*
*"The mouth’s aperture is what speaks to things.*
*The door is what communicates with things.*
*To close what delights,*
*to shut what connects—*
*only then can one blunt its sharpness.*

*To blunt its sharpness,*
*to resolve its confusion,*
*to harmonize its brilliance,*
*to blend with the dust—*

*Pei Chu’en said:*
*"To harmonize its brilliance is to make light not dazzle.*
*To blend with the dust is to make dust not stain.*
*To blunt sharpness so nothing escapes within,*
*to resolve confusion so nothing enters from without,*
*to harmonize light so it does not dazzle,*
*to blend with dust so it does not stain—*
*this is how the truly knowing conduct themselves.*
*This is called *playful unity* (玄同)."*

*Su Ziyou said:*
*"The Dao is not in speech,*
*yet it is not separate from speech.*
*But those who know may not speak,*
*and those who speak may not know.*
*Only by closing the mouth’s aperture and shutting the door,*
*blocking the external,*
*blunting sharpness, resolving confusion,*
*harmonizing brilliance, blending with dust,*
*to govern the internal—*
*remaining silent yet not speaking,*
*thus aligning with the Dao—*
*this is *playful ascent* (玄升),*
*entering the Dao.*
*When reaching the playful,*
*the small and the great distinguish themselves,*
*yet only by closing the mouth’s aperture and shutting the door,*
*blunting sharpness, resolving confusion,*
*harmonizing brilliance, blending with dust,*
*remaining silent yet not speaking,*
*can one align with the Dao.*

*Thus it cannot be approached with familiarity,*
*nor rejected with distance,*
*nor exploited for gain,*
*nor harmed by loss,*
*nor elevated in status,*
*nor degraded in rank—*
*thus it is the most noble under Heaven."*

*The Imperial Edict commentary said:*
*"The world’s people attack each other with love and hate,*
*creating distinctions of closeness and distance.*
*Emotions and pretense influence each other,*
*giving rise to perceptions of benefit and harm.*
*Choices and abandonments are weighed,*
*creating divisions of nobility and lowliness.*
*Alternating shifts without end—*
*how can this be the most noble under Heaven?*

*The wise forget speech,*
*and in forgetting speech,*
*they erase love and hate,*
*dissolve emotions and pretense,*
*transcend choices and abandonments,*
*and achieve *playful unity* within the single nature.*
*Thus their nobility is complete,*
*and the world forgets them in turn—*
*hence, they are the most noble under Heaven."*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said:

If this is so, then all things are stored within a single treasury,
and life and death share the same form.
There is nothing to be particularly close to,
nor anything to be particularly distant from—
thus, one cannot be drawn to closeness,
nor pushed toward distance.

One does not pursue gain,
nor shun harm—
thus, one cannot be said to benefit,
nor be said to suffer harm.

One does not glory in prominence,
nor wallow in obscurity—
thus, one cannot be exalted,
nor debased.

Those who can be drawn to closeness or distance,
benefit or harm,
nobility or lowliness—
their worth lies in things,
and all things hold them in contempt.

Those who cannot be drawn to closeness or distance,
benefit or harm,
nobility or lowliness—
their worth lies within themselves,
and no thing can debase them.
How could they not be the most noble under Heaven?

Those who cannot be drawn to closeness or distance—
like Liè Yùzōu dwelling in the gardens of Zhèng,
where no one recognizes him—
is the case.

Those who cannot be said to benefit or harm—
like Liè Zǐ declining Zhèng Zǐyáng’s grain—
is the case.

Those who cannot be said to be noble or lowly—
like Zēng Zǐ dwelling in Wèi—
is the case.

This chapter extols forgetting speech to embody the Dao;
the following passages all demonstrate the Way of silent completion.
Who, then, can be drawn to closeness or distance,
benefit or harm,
nobility or lowliness?

True nobility resides within oneself—
no thing can debase it,
thus they are the most noble under Heaven.

*—From the chapter "Govern with Dao to Rule the State" (Chapter Fifty-Seven)*

The embodiment of the Dao in its constancy is called *zhèng* (正).
*Zhèng* rests in oneness without change—this is the constancy of the Dao.
The Way of governing the state holds constancy as its highest virtue.
Mencius said: *"Rectify the ruler, and the state will be settled."*

In warfare, *"qí"* (奇) is employed—
Gu Huān remarked: *"Qí* is the art of deception and adaptation."
Facing peril, one must master change—warfare knows no surfeit of deceit.
The *Sānlüè Jiǔqí* (三略九奇) and *Jiǔgōng Bǎishèng* (九攻百胜)
are the achievements of the supreme general:
calming strife, ending rebellion, and securing martial virtue.

Liu Zhòngpíng said:
*"What is impure and mixed is the common heart of the world.
What flows without restraint is the common emotion of the world.
But the Way of *zhèng* differs entirely from this.
Thus, to be one without mixture—this is the essence of *zhèng*.
To be still without yielding—this is the application of *zhèng*.
To be one without mixture is to command what is impure.
To be still without yielding is to govern what flows unrestrained.
Therefore, the ruler of a state must not seek it elsewhere—
only by upholding *zhèng* can governance be secured.

But in warfare, it is otherwise.
Wielding deadly weapons, confronting peril, commanding ten thousand,
victory or defeat may hinge on a single moment.
If one can subdue the enemy, then seizing opportunity and adapting to change—
there is nothing that cannot be achieved.
Thus, its manner differs from governing the state,
and so *qí* is employed rather than *zhèng*.

To take the world with *wúshì* (無事).

*—Commentary:* *Zhèng* is the constancy of the Dao,
*qí* is its variation,
*wúshì* is its true essence.
A state is governed by *zhèng*,
warfare is won by *qí*,
and the true Dao admits no private favor.
When one follows nature effortlessly, the world is at peace.

*The Miscellaneous Sayings* state:
*"Zhèng* suffices to govern a single state,
*qí* suffices to wield the five armies.
Only *wúshì* can take the world.
Thus it is said: 'To take the world, one must first be *wúshì*.
When conflict arises, it is no longer sufficient to take the world.'

Yet, did not Táng and Wǔ launch their campaigns without *wúshì*?"
It is replied:
*"Táng and Wǔ aligned with Heaven and responded to humanity.
Their conquests were as effortless as dismissing a single man—
there was no 'conflict' in the true sense.
How do I know the world is thus?
By this."*

Emperor Tang Minghuang said: *"By this, the following text is understood.*

When the world abounds in taboos and prohibitions, the people grow ever poorer.
*Zhuǎn Wēi* explains: *"Taboos"* refers to decrees and edicts.

A ruler cannot refrain from governance, yet uses laws and teachings to rule the state. When prohibitions and nets of law are dense, the people fear transgression, unable to move hand or foot. They have no time but to avoid taboos, daring not to act—every movement disrupts their livelihood, and poverty follows daily.
*Wang Yuanzé* says: *"When actions are forbidden, the people are unsettled and lose their means of subsistence—thus, they become poor."* This is where disorder begins.

When the people possess many sharp tools, the state grows ever more corrupt.
*Wang Bi* says: *"Sharp tools are instruments for self-advantage."* When the people grow strong, the state weakens.
*Wang Yuanzé* says: *"Where sharp tools exist, there must be scheming intentions. When scheming arises, the lower ranks become untrustworthy, and thus the state grows corrupt."*

When the people excel in craft and artifice, strange things proliferate.
*The Imperial Commentary* states: *"When skill and artifice prevail, people turn to frivolous pursuits, and bizarre garments and strange tools emerge to disrupt customs."*
*Wang Bi* says: *"When the people are too clever, deceit and artifice arise. Where deceit and artifice arise, wicked deeds follow."*
*Zhuǎn Wēi* explains: *"Craft and artifice"* refer to skilled and ingenious workmanship.

When laws and edicts grow ever more pronounced, thieves and bandits multiply.
Emperor Tang Minghuang says: *"When *wúwéi* is abandoned, laws grow ever clearer. Those who exploit the law for deceit become thieves—how could they not multiply?"*
Prince Shū says: *"Laws are meant to prohibit wrongdoing in the world. Yet because they prohibit wrongdoing, they give rise to deceit. For where laws are made, deceit follows; where edicts are issued, trickery arises. Thus it is said: When laws and edicts grow ever more pronounced, thieves and bandits multiply."*

Therefore, the Sage says:
*River Above Master* [i.e., Laozi] says: *"This refers to the following text."*

The Sage says:

*"I practice wúwéi, and the people transform of themselves.*
**Commentary:** *Heaven acts with wúwéi and thus remains clear; Earth acts with wúwéi and thus remains tranquil. When these two wúwéi harmonize, all things transform. The Sage embodies Heaven and Earth, so the people daily advance in virtue without knowing who guides them.*

*Emperor Yao and Shun sat in silent repose within the Hall of Law, hands folded in repose, observing the flourishing of the people. The Duke of Zhou and Wu Wang governed without knowing or acting, yet all followed in harmony. The Emperor ruled with sleeves draped and hands folded, and the realm was governed—this is the Way of the Sage: wúwéi, yet the people transform.*

*Zhuangzi says: ‘With wúwéi, all things transform.’*

*I do nothing, and the people grow wealthy.*
**River Above Master says:** *I impose no corvée labor or conscription, so the people secure in their occupations, and all grow wealthy.*
**The *Lèi Jié* says:** *When the heart practices wúwéi and actions do nothing, the four classes take joy in their work, and the people prosper abundantly. This reverses the world’s excess of prohibitions, which only makes the people poorer.*

*When the ruler does nothing, forced labor does not arise, and the people’s agricultural seasons are not disrupted—thus they grow wealthy. This further explains the meaning of ‘the people grow poorer’ from above.*

*I love stillness, and the people become upright.*
**Wang Yuanzé says:** *To love stillness is to return to one’s nature. When the ruler returns to nature, the people also return to nature, and thus become upright.*
**This further explains the meaning of ‘thieves and bandits multiply’ from above.*
**Yang Fu says:** *When I act, the people become perverse; when I am still, the people become upright. This reverses the harm of excessive sharp tools, which bring confusion to the state.*

*I have no desires, and the people return to simplicity.*
**Emperor Tang Minghuang says:** *With wúwéi, there is clarity and stillness, so the people transform. With no affairs, there is no disturbance, so the people grow wealthy. With love of stillness, one attains nature, so the people become upright. With no desires, harmony is preserved, so the people return to simplicity. This is the Way of taking the world without action.*

*Simplicity is the preservation of nature. When my nature is whole and I have no desires, the people also have no desires and return to simplicity.*
**This further explains the meaning of ‘strange things’ from above.*
**This chapter advocates taking the world without action.*

*From ‘the world abounds in prohibitions’ to ‘thieves and bandits multiply’—this is the error of taking the world with action. From ‘I practice wúwéi and the people transform’ to ‘I have no desires and the people return to simplicity’—this is the proof of taking the world without action.*

---
**Chapter 58: The Governance of Murky Tranquility**

*When governance is murky and tranquil, the people are pure and thick.*
**River Above Master says:** *When governance is broad and tranquil, as if unclear, the people become pure and abundant, living in mutual harmony.*
**Emperor Tang Minghuang says:** *When governance is murky and tranquil, without action or strictness, the people respond with purity and simplicity.*
**Lǚ Jìfǔ says:** *‘Pure and thick’ means they are not thin or sparse.*

*When governance is sharp and scrutinizing, the people become depleted and exhausted.*
**Emperor Tang Minghuang says:** *When governance is sharp and scrutinizing, with action and urgency, the people respond with depletion and decay.*
**Prince Shū says:** *‘Murky and tranquil’ means no distinctions are made. Only when no distinctions are made can the people remain without knowledge or desire, and thus they are pure and thick. ‘Sharp and scrutinizing’ means distinctions are made. When distinctions are made, the people cannot remain without knowledge or desire, and thus they become depleted and exhausted.*
**Lǚ Jìfǔ says:** *‘Depleted and exhausted’ means they are not fully simple.*

*Disaster, yet fortune depends upon it,*
**River Above Master says:** *‘Depends upon’ means ‘arises from.’ Disaster arises from fortune. When one encounters disaster and reflects, blaming oneself and cultivating virtue, disaster departs and fortune arrives.*

*Fortune, yet disaster lurks within it.*
**River Above Master says:** *Disaster lurks hidden within fortune. When one obtains fortune and becomes arrogant, fortune departs and disaster arrives.*
**Emperor Tang Minghuang says:** *‘Depends upon’ means ‘arises from.’ ‘Lurks within’ means ‘is hidden in.’ Above, it is said that when governance is murky and tranquil, the people mistakenly believe there is no governance, yet they become pure and simple. This is disaster arising from fortune. When governance is sharp and scrutinizing, the people believe there is governance, yet they become depleted and exhausted—this is fortune hiding disaster.*

*Where disaster and fortune depend upon and lurk within the realm of auspiciousness and inauspiciousness, where shall one reside?*
*Indeed, when disaster comes and one is not afraid, reflecting and blaming oneself to avoid disaster—this is to dwell between disaster and fortune. If so, disaster will not come, nor will fortune arrive. Disaster and fortune will be nonexistent, and who would suffer calamity?*
*Zhuangzi says: ‘At peace with disaster and fortune.’*

*Who knows their limit?*
**River Above Master says:** *Disaster and fortune generate each other; who can know their end?*
**Sun Deng says:** *‘Who’ means ‘who.’ ‘Limit’ means ‘exhaustion.’ One person’s disaster and fortune interdepend, alternating without fixed pattern—disaster arises from fortune, fortune comes through disaster—who can know their exhaustion?*

*There is no fixed right or wrong.*
**Wang Yuanzé says:** *The great course does not linger in any age—this is to wish to establish the right, yet not knowing that the right cannot be constant. Then can this be called wisdom?*
*Although it is said there is no fixed right, this is to take ‘no fixed right’ as the right. ‘Wrong’ is a doubtful phrase, yet it is also not fixed in ‘no fixed right.’*
*The right becomes strange, the good becomes ominous, and the people’s confusion has long endured.*
**Commentary:** *All under Heaven is but one breath. What was right yesterday may be wrong today; what was contrary before may align later. Divine and strange, fragrant and putrid—these transform into each other, beginning and ending. Thus, the strange and the right generate each other, the ominous and the good alternate. This is the natural unfolding of one breath.*

*The world has existed for long—small errors change methods, great errors change nature. The customs of selfishness prevail and are not illuminated. Where disaster and fortune depend upon and lurk, and governance is sharp and scrutinizing, how can the people return to their true nature?*
**Lǚ Dì says:** *‘There is no fixed right or wrong’—this means there is right when there is right, and right is what is called right. Right is without fixed right. Only when there is no fixed right can one transcend the realm of auspiciousness and inauspiciousness, having neither disaster nor fortune, and know their limit.*

「If one cannot attain the state of *Wu Zheng* [無正, "without fixed right"], but instead wanders into the realm of auspiciousness and inauspiciousness, then disaster and fortune will follow in turn—like the revolving of a wheel, like the cycling of seasons—ultimately leading only to confusion.

Hence it is said: *Wu Zheng* becomes *Qi* [奇, "strange"], *Shan* [善, "good"] turns into *Yao* [妖, "ominous"]. The people’s confusion is indeed long-standing.

Thus, the Sage is square yet does not cut [方而不割],*
**御注:** *Square* refers to being steadfast in the Dao/Way while not rigidly fixed, flowing without obstruction, yet leaving no trace of discrimination.*

**Gu Xuan** says: *Square one’s conduct, and the strange and ominous will keep their distance; one does not sever with one’s steadfastness, yet they follow along.*

Frugal yet not stingy [廉而不刿],*
**御注:** *Frugal* means maintaining purity in oneself.*
**Wang Bi** says: *Frugal* is the same as *clean and at ease*. *Sath* [刿, "stingy"] is to harm. To guide the people with purity and ease, ordering them to abandon their evil, ordering them to cast off their impurities—this is not to harm them with purity and ease.*

Straightforward yet not domineering [直而不肆],*
**He Shang Gong** says: *Dominering* means to press one’s views upon others.*
The Sage, though straight, bends to follow others; never does he press his own views.*
**Wang Bi** says: *To guide with straightforwardness, ordering them to abandon their perversity, yet not to press one’s own straightforwardness upon them, provoking resistance—this is what is meant by "great straightness is like bending."*
**Wang Yuanze** says: *Straightforwardness that is domineering will have its own straightness. Great straightness aligns with principle, remaining constant; bending to follow principle. The *Book of Documents* says: "Straightforward yet gentle, gentle so as not to harm within."* Straightforward yet harsh externally, not harsh so as to domineer over others—this is what is meant by *not domineering*.
The *Analects* says: *"Of substance yet loving righteousness, bright yet not dazzling."*
**御注:** *Straightforwardness that is domineering will domineer over others, and the faults of such behavior will manifest; brightness that dazzles will invite the calamity of flaunting one’s deeds.*

To be straight within yet yielding without, to use one’s brightness yet return to one’s clarity—this is only the Sage.

When the people are confused, those who take *Wu Zheng* [方, "square"] as correct are like the *Zi Mo* [子莫] who clutches the disorder in his mouth. Those who take *Kang* [康, "at ease"] as correct are like the *Zhong Zi* [仲子] who holds his breath. Knowing how to stretch yet not knowing how to bend, knowing how to display yet not knowing how to conceal—with such boastful, worldly customs, they lose the Sage’s complete harmony. How can they rectify the world?

The Sage rectifies the world—how? Thus and no other way.

**Wang Yuanze** says: *Brightness that dazzles others is called "dazzling."* To harmonize one’s brightness and return to one’s clarity—how can this be dazzling? This passage speaks of disaster and fortune, *Wu Zheng* and *Qi*, *Shan* and *Yao* generating each other, yet the people cannot know their confusion. Indeed, it has been long-standing—how then can they be further misled by sharp and scrutinizing governance? How can the people preserve their original purity?

Thus, though the Sage is square, at ease, straightforward, and bright in his own conduct, he never displays his traces to show others. He takes this *mèn mèn* [闷闷, "mysterious and unassuming"], abandoning that *chácháchá* [察察, "sharp and scrutinizing"], and thus remains tranquil amid disaster and fortune.

---

**Chapter 59: Governing People and Serving Heaven [治人事天]**

Governing people and serving Heaven—nothing surpasses frugality [啬].
**御注:** *Frugality* is the preservation of one’s spirit and essence without external depletion or internal waste.*

The spirit and essence flow boundlessly in all directions, reaching their utmost without limit—above, they touch Heaven; below, they coil within Earth. Transforming and nurturing all things, their name is *Tong Di* [同帝, "in harmony with the Emperor"]. This is how Heaven becomes Heaven and humanity becomes humanity—only through *Yin* [音, "resonance/harmony"].

To cherish and nourish the spirit and essence, then to govern people below and serve Heaven above—hence it is said: *Governing people and serving Heaven—nothing surpasses frugality.*

Only frugality is called *early return* [早复].
**Lü Jifu** says: *Only frugality preserves the spirit and essence without expending them, thus it is "early return."* If one is not frugal and instead squanders, until the spirit is exhausted and the essence worn out, even if one wished to return to one’s original nature, there would be no way to enter it. Is this not too late?

Hence it is said: *Only frugality is called early return.*
**Wang Yuanze** says: *When movement reaches its limit and stillness begins, then the return is late. Only frugality does not indulge in waste, for its departure from the root is never distant—thus, the return is early.*

*Early return* is called *heavily accumulating virtue* [重积德].
**Lü Jifu** says: *Humanity’s life is inherently sufficient through virtue. If one truly harmonizes and returns early, virtue will daily increase and fill.*

Hence it is said: *Early return is called heavily accumulating virtue.*
**御注:** *To overcome is called "conquer."*

Heavily accumulating virtue, one conquers all—
**Lü Jifu** says: *Heavily accumulating virtue means virtue reaches its utmost. The utmost virtue cannot be scorched by fire, drowned by water, harmed by cold or heat, or preyed upon by beasts. How can it not conquer all?*

Hence it is said: *Heavily accumulating virtue, one conquers all.*
**御注:** *When virtue reaches its utmost, it is boundless in response—who can know its limit?*

Heavily accumulating virtue, one conquers all—
**Lü Jifu** says: *If there is something one cannot conquer, then the Dao/Way has its limits. If one conquers all, who can know its limit?*

Hence it is said: *Heavily accumulating virtue, one conquers all—then none can know its limit.*

None can know its limit—thus one can have a state [國].
**御注:** *When one’s body is boundless, there is ample room to govern the world—how much more so to have a state?*

**Lü Jifu** says: *One who holds land possesses a great thing. One who possesses a great thing cannot be constrained by things. To constrain yet not be constrained—thus one can constrain all things. None can know its limit, so one is not constrained yet can constrain all things. Even if one holds land, there will be no burden.*

Hence it is said: *None can know its limit—thus one can have a state.*
The mother of a lasting state—
**御注:** *The Dao/Way is the mother of all things.*

Those who follow the **Dao/Way** are unblemished through ten thousand generations. **Shu Wang** said: *"States all arise from the **Dao/Way**, thus the **Dao/Way** is called the mother. If one abides by it, one’s life will not perish."*

Hence it is said: *"Thus one can endure forever."*

This is called **deep-rooted and firmly anchored**—**Wang Yuanzhe** said: *"Humans take **nature/xing** as their root; all else beyond this is but branches and leaves. Those who lose their **nature/xing**** overdevelop their branches and leaves to harm their root. When the root is injured, vital **qi** and essence decline, and the anchor is not firm—this is why they perish early."*

Only through **parsimony** can one avoid external harm to the root. When the root is deep, the anchor is firm; when the anchor is firm, one’s form remains bound to life, and separation is delayed.

**Lu Tian** said: *"When nothing can measure it—this is called **deep**. When nothing can topple it—this is called **firm**."*

The **Dao/Way** of long life and enduring vision—
**御注:** The **Dao/Way** is the mother of all things. Things are its offspring. **Nature/xing** is the root of form; form is its trunk. Knowing its offspring and preserving its mother, one’s life will not perish, hence the enduring forever. When the root is deep, the trunk is firm; when **nature/xing** is preserved, the form remains whole. Aligning with Heaven and Earth as constants, one can thus achieve long life. With the sun and moon sharing their luminosity, one can thus endure vision forever. When humans and things transform yet the **Dao/Way** alone remains—this is called the **Dao/Way**."

**Lü Jifu** said: *"To practice **parsimony** as the **Dao/Way**—this is called **deep-rooted and firmly anchored**, the **Dao/Way** of long life and enduring vision. **Spirit/shen** is the root of life; if one hoards and preserves it, the root will be deep and life will endure. **Long life** is the anchor of vision; if one guards and protects it, the anchor will be firm and vision will last long. This chapter begins by saying that in governing people and harmonizing with Heaven, nothing surpasses **parsimony**, and concludes with the **Dao/Way** of long life and enduring vision. Indeed, the **Dao/Way** of enduring forever lies in conserving one’s **spirit/shen**. **Spirit/shen** is the root; form is the anchor. When the root is deep and the anchor is firm, life and vision endure forever."*

In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor asked about the **Dao/Way** of enduring forever, and Guangcheng instructed him: *"Embrace the **spirit/shen** and remain still; your form will naturally rectify itself. Do not disturb your vital **qi**—then you may achieve long life."* The *Inner Canon* states of the **True Person/zhenren**: *"Inhaling and exhaling vital **qi**, standing alone to guard the **spirit/shen**, thus one’s lifespan can compare to Heaven and Earth, without end."* Speaking of the **Perfect One/zhi** it says: *"Accumulate **jing/essence** and preserve **shen/spirit**."* Speaking of the **Sage/shengren** it says: *"The **spirit/shen** does not scatter."* Examining from ancient times to the present, no **True Sage/upper sage** has ever achieved perfection without conserving their **spirit/shen**.*

As for the people of today’s age, they exhaust their **jing/essence** with desire and dissipate their **True Self/zhen** through wastefulness. They do not know how to maintain fullness nor to govern the **spirit/shen** at the proper time. They lose their **jing/essence** and **ling/soul**, their form declines, and they perish early—how can this be the **Dao/Way**?"

Hence it is said: *"The **Dao/Way** is what the **Sage/shengren** practices; the foolish turn away from it."* **Daoist Canon/True Classic of Dao and Virtue**—*Collection of the Good, Volume Nine* concludes. *Collection of the Good, Volume Ten* concludes.

**Collection of the Master Li Lin of Song’s Residence in Rao: Chapter Sixty – Governing a Great Realm**

Governing a great realm is like cooking a small fresh fish.
*Commentary:* Stirring a small fresh fish too often causes it to break apart.
To govern a great realm with frequent changes in law brings confusion.

Thus, the **Dao/Way** of governance values tranquility, and the people settle themselves.
Wang Bi says: *"It means not to disturb."*

Agitation brings many harms; stillness preserves the **True Self/zhen**.
Hence, the greater the realm, the more tranquil its ruler must be—only then can he broadly win the hearts of the people.

Liu Zhongping says: *"For the greatest of realms, governance should be as simple as cooking a small fresh fish. Why? Because it avoids unnecessary disturbance.*
A small fish is easily ruined by overhandling.
A weak people are easily troubled by interference.
The cook of a small fresh fish lets it cook itself.
The ruler of a great realm lets the people govern themselves.

The **Classic** says: *"I do nothing, yet the people transform themselves; I love stillness, yet the people align themselves."*

To govern the world with the **Dao/Way**, its spirits are not numinous.
Lu Zhan says: *"Ghosts and spirits, when unsettled and restless, manifest as calamities.*
The ancients who governed the world with the **Dao/Way**—even birds, beasts, fish, and turtles, as well as mountains, rivers, and ghosts—all found peace, and thus there were no ominous portents."

Wang Yuanze says: *"When the people are undisturbed, they fulfill their nature.*
When the people fulfill their nature, the harmony of Heaven and Earth responds, and all things flourish without hindrance.
