Thus, even ghosts find peace in their place and cannot become numinous."

When humans and ghosts follow different **Dao/Way**s yet often clash, it is because the **qi** of yin and yang is disordered, and their proper balance is lost—thus, spirits exploit the opportunity.

Though the world is vast, true governance lies in the **Dao/Way**, which acts without action and trusts in spontaneity.
To govern a great realm is like cooking a small fresh fish—this is called governing the world with the **Dao/Way**.
Thus, the **qi** of yin and yang remains harmonious, and humans and ghosts each dwell in peace—hence, *"its spirits are not numinous."*

Not that its spirits are without numen—rather, they do not harm the people.
Emperor Xuanzong of Tang says: *"The saying ‘its spirits are not numinous’ does not mean ghosts vanish or lose their numen; it means their numen does not manifest as malevolence to harm people."*

Not that their numen does not harm the people—rather, the Sage does not harm the people.
Liu Jinxi says: *"Numen is the manifestation of spiritual efficacy.*
These ghosts lack such efficacy, but when a ruler governs with the **Dao/Way**, the spirits bless the people and cannot harm them.
To govern a great realm is like cooking a small fresh fish—this is called the Sage not harming the people.

Only the Sage does not harm the people, so Heaven and Earth’s harmony responds, and humans and ghosts each fulfill their nature without mutual harm.
When neither harms the other, **virtue/power** naturally returns.

*Commentary:* *"To govern the world with the **Dao/Way** is to act without action, letting things follow their nature, avoiding war and capital punishment—this is called not harming the people.*
At such times, spirits and people do not harm each other, and **virtue/power** returns.
The spirits do not manifest their numen—only blessings abound.
Thus it is said: ‘Its numen does not harm the people; the people have no need to exert their cleverness.’
Their daily lives are but eating and drinking—what harm could there be?
This chapter urges rulers to govern the world with the **Dao/Way**, acting without action and trusting in spontaneity, so that the subtle and manifest each dwell in their place, and humans and spirits do not interfere with one another, while **virtue/power** returns.

In the time of Emperor Yao, celestial communications ceased, and no spirits descended.
In the age of the Xia, mountains, rivers, and ghosts all found peace.
This was the **Sage** governing the world without action.

**Chapter Sixty-One – Great Realms Are Like the Lower Reaches**

Great realms are like the lower reaches [of a river].
He Shangong says: *"To govern a great realm, one must be like the river and sea dwelling in the lower reaches, not resisting the smallest currents."*

Wang Bi says: *"The river and sea are vast yet dwell low, so all streams flow to them.
A great realm is vast yet dwells low, so all under Heaven turns to it—thus, great realms are like the lower reaches."*

Lu Jifu says: *"If one knows to govern with the **Dao/Way**, one certainly does not harm the people.
But if one does not know how to win the world, then even if one does not harm the people, others may harm them.
Great realms are like the lower reaches—the gathering place of the world.
The world’s gathering place is receptive, like the female.
The female always overcomes the male through stillness, and stillness is humility—this is the way to win the world and preserve the people.

The world’s gathering place.
Wang Bi says: *"Where all under Heaven converges—an assembly."*

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang says: *"The lower reaches signify the **virtue/power** of humility.
A great realm, by dwelling low and opening itself, invites all under Heaven to gather.

The world’s gathering place is receptive, like the female.
Wang Bi says: *"Still and not seeking, yet all come of their own accord."*

Wang Yuanze says: *"One should receive all with feminine stillness.*
The female always overcomes the male through stillness.
He Shangong says: *"Yin overcomes yang by being tranquil and not seeking first."*

Stillness is humility.
Wang Bi says: *"Because of its stillness, it can be humble.*
The female is the receptive.
The male is restless, consumed by desire; the female is always still, thus overcoming the male.
Because of its stillness, it can also be humble, and thus all things turn to it."*

Duke Shū of Shu says: *"Gathering is the assembly of the people; if one can endure what the people dislike, then all movements of the world will turn to it.
Thus it is said: ‘The world’s gathering place is receptive’—for the world’s gathering is but the gathering of the receptive.
The receptive always overcomes the active through stillness, and stillness is humility—thus, stillness is the ruler, and always dwelling low, it is sufficient to overcome the active.
Thus it is said: ‘With stillness, it overcomes the active; with stillness, it is humble.’

Lu Jifu says: *"The world’s gathering place is receptive, and the receptive always overcomes the active through stillness—yet what allows it to overcome is its humility through stillness.
Thus, great realms, by dwelling low over small realms, take the small realms.
Wang Bi says: *"‘Great realms dwell low’—meaning even great realms dwell low over small realms, and the small realms attach to them."*

Gu Huan said: *"When great realms are humble with emptiness, then small realms rejoice and submit. Their submission is how one takes those small realms. When small realms submit to great realms, they take the great realms."*

Heshang Gong said: *"This means that regardless of a realm’s size, if it practices humility and nurtures others, it will have no faults."*

Wang Bi said: *"The great realm receives them."*

Lu Yu said: *"It takes them because they accommodate it."*

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said: *"The great takes the small to make them subjects and servants. The small takes the great to seek aid."*

Thus, sometimes one submits to take, and sometimes one submits while taking.

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said: *"‘With’ means the great takes the small, ‘while’ means the small takes the great."*

Lu Jifu said: *"Thus, when great realms submit to small realms, they take the small realms—this is what is meant by ‘sometimes submitting to take.’ Submitting to take means that though the great could dominate the small, it submits to take them. When small realms submit to great realms, they take the great realms—this is what is meant by ‘sometimes submitting while taking.’ Submitting while taking means that though the small cannot avoid submitting to the great, they do so to take them."*

King Xuan of Qi asked: *"Is there a Way to interact with neighboring realms?"*

Mencius replied: *"There is. Only the virtuous can serve the small with the great, hence Tang served Ge and King Wen served Kunyi. Only the wise can serve the great with the small, hence Taiwang served Xiányú and Goujian served Wu. Serving the small with the great is the act of one who rejoices in Heaven’s order. Serving the great with the small is the act of one who fears Heaven’s order. Those who rejoice in Heaven’s order preserve all under Heaven, while those who fear it preserve only their own realm."*

Great realms seek only to encompass and nurture others.

Heshang Gong said: *"If great realms do not lose their humility, they will encompass and nurture small realms."*

Cheng Xuan Ying said: *"The reason great realms practice humility is that they have no ulterior motives—they seek only to love and nurture small realms."*

Small realms seek only to submit and serve others.

Liu Jinxi said: *"When small realms use gentleness, they have no hidden intentions—they seek only to enter the great realm’s embrace, admiring its virtue and serving it."*

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said: *"When great realms hold to the virtue of humility and submit to small realms, they seek only to encompass and nurture small realms as subjects and servants. When small realms offer tribute and submit to great realms, they seek only to serve the great as allies. Both parties thus obtain what they desire, hence the great should submit."*

Liu Qihui said: *"Those who rely on greatness risk arrogance, so this is a special warning."*

Shu Wang said: *"It is easy for the small to serve the great, but difficult for the great to serve the small. Since this is what is difficult for people, Laozi says the great should submit."*

Lu Jifu said: *"Now, the great submits to the small, and the small submits to the great—each obtains what it desires. Then who would not take? ‘Taking’ means winning their hearts without losing them. Yet if the great should not submit, and only the great is said to submit, why? If the small cannot submit to the great, not only will it fail to take the great realm, but disaster will befall it. Thus, even if it seems unfit, it is said: ‘The great should submit.’ The great, encompassing the four seas and ruling all under Heaven, holds a lofty position and exalted rank. If it falls into excess and fullness, it is especially prone to arrogance. Thus, it must practice humility."*

Liuzi said: *"Heaven’s Way is to descend and illuminate, and seas and rivers rule by descending. Mountains are humble when rooted in the earth, and kings and lords consider solitude and poverty as detriments. This chapter speaks of the Way to interact with neighboring realms, taking humility as its foundation. Thus, regardless of size, all realms should practice humility. The great must especially fear arrogance, hence the special warning."*

---
**Chapter Sixty-Two: Dao as the Mysterious Origin of All Things**

Dao is the mysterious origin of all things.

Liu Zhongping said: *"Mysterious" refers to the deepest dwelling. "Border" refers to a narrow edge. When speaking of Dao’s subtlety, it is likened to the mysterious. When speaking of Dao’s coarseness, it is likened to the border. Speaking of subtlety reveals the border’s coarseness; speaking of the border reveals subtlety’s mystery.*

The treasure of the virtuous.

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said: *"The virtuous know that those who guard Dao prosper, so they always treasure and value it for use."*

Wang Yuanzhe said: *"The treasure of the virtuous is what the Sage embodies. Under Heaven, nothing is more valued than Dao. Those who know Dao’s value are the virtuous. ‘Treasure’ means precious and honored. Dao as treasure is the essence of vital breath and spirit. Vital essence is the foundation of the body—preserving it without dissipation ensures the body’s completeness. Concentrating qi without recklessness ensures the body’s fullness. Guarding the spirit without scattering ensures the body’s perfection. The three merge as one—this is why Dao is the virtuous’ treasure."*

The non-virtuous’ reliance.

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said: *"‘Reliance’ means ‘to depend upon.’ The non-virtuous cannot treasure Dao, but when facing peril, they seek to preserve themselves by relying on Dao to escape."*

Nothing is not Dao—
the virtuous seek it to attain virtue,
the non-virtuous rely on it to avoid ruin.

Sun Deng said: *"Fine words can elevate one’s reputation above all goods, and conduct can place one above others. Yet Dao, when spoken, is as insipid as water. The Sage acts without doing, teaches without speaking—how could he adorn speech to please the people’s ears or embellish conduct to dazzle all things?"*

Lu Tian said: *"‘Words’ refers to wind and waves; ‘conduct’ refers to ruin."*

Yet even fine words can command esteem, and virtuous conduct can elevate one above others—how much more so is Dao the profound essence of all things! This is why the ancients employed this Dao to awaken the people.

When even one person is enlightened by it, though one were to fill the Dharma-realm with the most precious treasures and distribute them all, no merit could surpass this.

Thus it is said:
*"Fine words can command esteem,
virtuous conduct can elevate one above others.
What reason would the unvirtuous have to abandon it?"*

Therefore, the Son of Heaven is established, and the Three Exalted Ministers are appointed.

Gu Huan said:
*"To establish the sovereign and set up ministers,
to discuss Dao and govern the state—
this is to lead the world by Dao,
to teach and transform the unvirtuous."*

Du Guangting said:
*"The vastness of the Four Seas,
the wealth of myriad things—
from the first emergence of the people,
there must be no ruler without authority.
Thus the Son of Heaven is established to shepherd them.
The Son of Heaven honors the Supreme Deity,
serves Heaven as a son,
serves Earth as a mother—
this is called the Son of Heaven.
One person cannot govern broadly,
so the chiefs of a hundred officials are appointed,
among whom are the Three Exalted Ministers.
The *Book of Documents* says:
‘These are the Three Exalted Ministers,
discussing Dao and governing the state.’
The Three Exalted Ministers are the Grand Teacher, Grand Tutor, and Grand Guardian.
Wen Gong said:
‘The sovereign is established to oversee and shepherd the people,
ministers are appointed to instruct and safeguard the sovereign.
Even with a jade tablet held before four horses,
it is not as good as advancing this Dao.
The Imperial Commentary says:
*The noble person’s cultivation of self brings peace to the world.*
When the Son of Heaven and the Three Exalted Ministers
use jade tablets and four-horse carriages to attract the worthy,
but do not strive to advance Dao and cultivate themselves,
they abandon self-governance for others,
losing the Way of self-regulation.
It is better to simply advance this Dao within oneself.
Where Dao resides, the Sage holds it in highest honor,
so the people follow it as they flock to market.
Shu Wang said:
‘Though one may hold a jade tablet the width of two hands
and lead four horses to welcome the worthy,
it is still not as good as simply advancing this Dao.’
Wang Yuanzhe said:
‘The Son of Heaven and the Three Exalted Ministers
take the transformation of the people as their duty.
If they possess Dao, the world will naturally submit.
Jade tablets and four-horse carriages are used to attract the worthy—
this is the greatest of governance.
Even if the worthy are obtained,
if Dao is not advanced, the people will still not submit.
Thus it is better not to seek externally but to grasp the essence of Dao.’

This is the threefold explanation of using jade tablets and four-horse carriages to attract the worthy.

Lü Jifu said:
‘Therefore, the Son of Heaven is established,
and the Three Exalted Ministers are appointed.
Even with a jade tablet held before four horses,
these are the rites of respect and the weight of offerings for the sovereign.
Yet it is still not as good as advancing this Dao while seated,
for Dao is the most honored in the world—
even if advanced while seated,
it surpasses respectful rites and heavy offerings.
The Son of Heaven and the Three Exalted Ministers
discuss Dao while seated—
this is all there is to it.’

This explains how jade tablets and four-horse carriages are used to honor the sovereign.

Why did the ancients hold this Dao in such high regard?
Gu Huan said:
‘Why did the ancients value this Dao so highly?
What was their reasoning?
Was it not because seeking brings attainment,
and guilt is thereby absolved?
Thus it is the most honored in the world.’

The Imperial Commentary says:
‘Seeking brings attainment—
this is seeking within oneself.
The ancients sought it in yin-yang and numerology
but did not attain it,
for they sought externally.
The wicked become virtuous,
the foolish become wise—
this is how guilt is absolved.
Such is the power of Dao to save,
and thus it is the most honored in the world.’

The *Transmission* says:
‘There is nothing in the world more honored than Dao.’

Lü Jifu said:
‘Seeking brings attainment—
this is the treasure of the virtuous.
Guilt is absolved—
this is the refuge of the unvirtuous.
Because of this,
Dao is the most honored in the world,
and thus the ancients could not help but honor it.’

This chapter speaks of all things arising from Dao,
and Dao being the most honored in the world.
Those who practice it virtuously are the virtuous.

Thus it is said:
‘Seeking brings attainment,
and those who preserve and entrust it are the common people.
Guilt is absolved—
for Dao is never without use,
and thus it is the most honored in the world.’

---
**Chapter 63: *For Non-Action***

To act without acting,
to manage without managing,
to savor without savoring.

The Imperial Commentary says:
‘The nature of Dao is without doing, hence non-action;
without form, hence no managing;
without desire, hence no savoring.
The Sage responds to things as they are,
while embodying the non-being of Dao—
here, the three are clearly seen.’

Liu Jinxi said:
‘To act without acting—
this is cultivating Dao’s work.
To manage without managing—
this is perceiving Dao’s form.
To savor without savoring—
this is penetrating Dao’s principles.
Non-action is Dao’s constant.
No managing is Dao’s true nature.
No savoring is Dao’s simplicity.
These three are all Dao itself.’

Great or small, many or few—
repaying enmity with virtue.

The Imperial Commentary says:
‘Great and small refer to form,
many and few refer to quantity—
things are boundless, beyond measure.
Great yet not excessive,
small yet not deficient—
then the repayment of enmity and gratitude,
who can distinguish them?
Thus the Sage unifies all things in the One,
and accomplishes the great.’

Wang Yuanzhe said:
‘To repay enmity with enmity is a matter of action.
To repay enmity with virtue is virtue itself.
Action shares in fortune and misfortune with the people,
thus all methods must be employed without omission.
But virtue is unseen—
how could enmity arise?
As for the three matters above,
these are the embodiment of Dao.
When embodying Dao,
it must be thus.
If one speaks of enmity yet repays with virtue,
then one knows virtue pervades all things.’

Cao Daochong said:
‘Repaying enmity with virtue extinguishes enmity.
Repaying enmity with enmity leads to endless retaliation.
Difficulties arise from what is easy,
the great from what is small.’

Zuan Wei said:
‘This is a grave warning.
Difficult and easy, great and small—
these are the uppermost categories of greatness and multitude.
Right and wrong, beauty and ugliness,
enmity and gratitude—
all arise from subtle beginnings,
none without first being easy,
then becoming difficult,
first small,
then great.’

Now if one plans for difficulties when they are still easy,
then at the end, there will be no hardship.
If one acts on small beginnings while they are still small,
then later there will be no greatness.

But if one waits until difficulties have already arisen to address them,
or undertakes great tasks when they are already great,
then resentment and anger will deepen while calamities accumulate—
and when one seeks to resolve difficulties and untangle chaos,
how could it not be difficult?

The seeds of disaster lie hidden in the subtle and minute,
manifesting where ordinary people fail to notice.
By the time they take root,
even the wise cannot remedy the consequences.

Thus, when resentment arises,
one must attend to it while it is still easy and small,
and then there will be no resentment.

The *Classic of Documents* says:
*"Resentment does not lie in what is obvious—
it is unseen when one fails to act early."*

Great undertakings in the world
must begin with what is easy.
Great affairs in the world
must begin with what is small.

**Commentary:**
*A thousand-zhang dike collapses from the burrow of an ant;
a hundred-foot hall burns from a crack’s smoke.*
White Gui sealed the dike’s holes when building it,
so there was no water disaster.
The aged craftsman sealed the gaps when tending the fire,
so there was no fire calamity.

All things under heaven
begin from the most minute origins,
yet by the time they reach their end,
they cannot be easily managed.

Thus, the Sage acts early.

**Commentary by Cui (疏):**
*"Action"* means *"to arise."* This elaborates on how calamities originate.
Difficult matters must arise from what is easy, so one must address them while they are still easy.
Great affairs must arise from what is minute, so one must eliminate them while they are still small.
Their instances are indeed numerous and cannot be exhaustively listed.
Hence, the summary is given as *"all under heaven."*

Du Guangting (杜光庭) said:
*"To prevent disaster before its signs appear,
to cut off calamity before its sprouting—
this is the utmost caution."*

Thus, the Sage never undertakes what is difficult and great,
and therefore is able to achieve what is great.

**Imperial Commentary (御注):**
*"If one acts while things are small,
then one can achieve what is great.*
If disorder has already formed and is then addressed,
is it not too late?"

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang (唐明皇) said:
*"The earlier rulers often undertook what was difficult and great,
so they planned and eliminated it.*
This shows that the Sage does not undertake what is difficult and great,
and thus is able to achieve what is great."*

**On light promises:**
Cheng Xuanying (成玄英) said:
*"This is an illustrative analogy.*
*'Promise'* means *'to assent.'*
*'Few'* means *'little.'*
Those who lightly assent to others
will necessarily have little sincerity in their words."*

Du Guangting said:
*"Yang Wu had no second thoughts in his commands,
Zilu had no lingering promises—
the ancients praised them thus."*

*The Records* say:
*"Better to have no promises to break
than to incur personal resentment."*

**On excessive ease:**
Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said:
*"Lightly promising others will result in few fulfilled words.
Frequent ease in action will inevitably lead to many difficulties."*

Cheng Xuanying said:
*"This is the resolution of the analogy.*
Those who lightly commit offenses
will necessarily invite grave retribution."*

Luoshi (罗什) said:
*"If one acts lightly without restraint,
the retribution will necessarily be severe."*

Thus, the Sage still finds difficulty in such matters,
and therefore never faces great difficulty.

**Imperial Commentary:**
*"Disaster is often hidden in what is minute,
yet erupts when people overlook it.*
The Sage’s response to the world
is always to be cautious of the minute and never negligent,
so from the start there are no light or easy actions,
and thus great, unmanageable calamities are ultimately avoided.*

All this is without cause.

Wang Bi (王弼) said:
*"Even the Sage finds difficulty in what is minute and easy.
How much more so for those not of the Sage’s caliber,
who would dare to neglect this?"*
Hence it is said:
*"The Sage still finds it difficult,"*
for it is because of the difficulty in what is minute and easy
that there are ultimately no great difficulties.

Liu Renhui (刘仁会) said:
*"To deliberate before acting
is to avoid regret and hesitation.*
The Sage acts thus—how much more so for others?"*

This chapter’s three matters embody the Dao.
Thus, repaying resentment with virtue
is to address it while it is still easy and small,
and therefore there are no great difficulties.
Without embodying the Dao, who could be like the Sage?

**Chapter Sixty-Four: On Ease and Firmness in Holding** *(其安易持)*

Du Guangting said:
*"Those born with a naturally tranquil nature,
whose desires have yet to sprout—
to approach and uphold them is easy to grasp.
What has not yet manifested is easy to plan."*

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said:
*"When a person’s true nature remains tranquil,
and one seeks to hold it steady,
preventing its scattering—
though the mind may stir,
its form and intent have not yet taken shape or measure,
so to suppress it is most effortless."*

Wang Yuanzhe said:
*"Discipline must come before the deed arises.
What is fragile is easily shattered;
what is subtle is easily dispersed."*

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said:
*"When wicked intent first takes root,
it is still frail and perilous—
those who sever it find it easily broken.
When calamity first arises,
its signs are still faint—
to forestall it, what is faint is easily dispersed."*

Lü Jifu said:
*"What is fragile is easily shattered—
so one must not let it harden.
What is subtle is easily dispersed—
so one must not let it take root.
All things are thus, but the mind is most so.
If this is applied to the mind,
then all under Heaven and the state will face no difficulty."*

Wang Yuanzhe said:
*"To act at the very beginning of what has already arisen—
this is called ‘ordering before chaos takes hold,
acting before it exists,
governing before disorder arises.’"*

Yan Zun said:
*"Before sickness strikes, the patient is easy to heal.
Before a state is in peril, its counsel is easy to devise.
When troubles are but sprouts, they are easy to address.
When misfortunes are still weak, they are easy to avert.
How may this be demonstrated?
A cart’s axle unbroken, a child can guide it.
But when it topples—
overturned and crashing into valleys—
a thousand men cannot right it.
Before the egg is cracked,
a finger’s touch may halt it.
But once it becomes a flying swan,
flapping wings toward the clouds,
no net or snare can ensnare it.
A tree thick enough to embrace
begins as a hair’s tip.
A nine-tiered tower
rises from a single clod of earth.
A thousand-li journey
commences beneath one’s feet."*

**Commentary:**
*"All things with form—
the great must grow from the small,
the high must be founded on the low,
the distant must begin with the near.
In their inception, they are simple;
when complete, they are vast.
The Sage perceives the beginning and contemplates,
discerns the end before the finger moves—
thus he does not act before fortune arrives,
nor does he begin before calamity takes hold.
He deliberates before acting,
and thus achieves success."*

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said:
*"This speaks of how evil arises from the subtle
and solidifies when it takes root.
Like a tree thick enough to embrace,
which begins as a hair’s tip—
this shows how it grows from its own nature.
A nine-tiered tower,
built from a single clod of earth—
this shows how habits accumulate.
A thousand-li journey,
starting with a single step—
this shows how distant paths are traversed without pause.
Thus, all under Heaven’s affairs
truly begin with the minute and subtle,
yet people often neglect them,
and so their roots become disasters.
Hence, three analogies are offered to confirm the foregoing."*

Du Guangting said:
*"People do not guard against what is nascent,
states do not heed what is subtle.
When disaster manifests, they seek to remove it;
when chaos is complete, they seek to govern it—
these are all failures of foresight.
From a hair’s tip to a tree thick enough to embrace,
it grows from small to great.
From a clod of earth to a nine-tiered tower,
it rises from low to high.
From a single step to a thousand-li journey,
it extends from near to far.
Yet men of the world,
seeing only the towering tree, the lofty tower, the distant path,
only then wish to wield the axe to fell it,
pile up the pickaxes to demolish it,
or gallop to chase it—
their labor is extreme, yet the calamity cannot be undone.
It is like leaning against the market gate and singing,
yet bringing ruin to the Jin dynasty.
Or like presenting the Sandalwood Deer,
nearly toppling the Han house.
Silent foresight and distant discernment—
these are what one must attend to with utmost care.
To act is to court ruin;
to grasp is to lose."*

「Emperor Tang Minghuang said: *"For"* means to labor and contrive. *"Execute"* means to cling and fixate.
He spoke of how people cannot act before things arise nor govern before chaos ensues,
yet instead contrive labor beyond their natural inclinations and fixate upon the dusty realms of the world,
thus inevitably inviting calamity and suffering loss.

 Lü Jifu said: To act before things arise is to not truly act in accordance with *for*.
 To govern before chaos ensues is to not truly govern in accordance with *treat*.
 Once things exist and one acts upon them, desiring their completion only to bring about their ruin.
 Once disorder has arisen and one governs it, clinging to it only to lose it in the end.

 Therefore, the Sage does nothing and thus meets no defeat,
 holds nothing and thus suffers no loss.

 *Imperial Commentary*: The Sage does not engage in worldly affairs,
 thus meets no defeat. He does not cling to anything as his own,
 thus suffers no loss. The more one deviates from the Dao/Way,
 the farther one strays from its rules. Can one truly avoid ruin and loss?

 Lü Jifu said: Thus, the Sage acts before things arise,
 yet does so through *non-action* (*wúwéi*), and so meets no defeat.
 Governs before chaos ensues,
 yet does so through *non-execution* (*wúzhí*), and so suffers no loss.
 The common folk, in their pursuits,
 often reach near completion only to meet ruin.

 *Imperial Commentary*: To halt midway, abandon halfway through, or begin with vigor only to end in slackness—
 these are the ways of the common folk, and none can escape them.
 Thus, their affairs often reach near completion only to fail.
 Lü Jifu said: The common folk, in their pursuits,
 act only after things have already arisen,
 thus reaching near completion only to meet ruin—
 for they do not know their fundamental nature.

 When the common folk reach near completion yet fail,
 it is because they do not heed their final steps.
 The *Book of Documents* says: *"To build a mountain nine *rèn* high,
 yet fail by one basket’s worth of earth."*
 To be as careful at the end as at the beginning,
 then there will be no ruined affairs.

 *Imperial Commentary*: Few can maintain their beginning to the end.
 To renew each moment as the start,
 applying this to affairs—
 what is there that cannot be accomplished?

 Emperor Tang Minghuang said: If one is vigilant in the final stages,
 ever preserving the mind of goodness from the start,
 then there will surely be no calamity or ruin.

 Lü Jifu said: If one knows that greatness arises from smallness,
 height from lowliness, and distance from proximity,
 and remains as careful at the end as at the beginning—
 how could there be any ruined affairs?

 Therefore, the Sage desires without desiring,
 does not prize goods that are hard to obtain,
 studies without studying,
 and corrects the errors of the common folk.

 *Imperial Commentary*: Those who seek profit exchange their nature for material things,
 and those who study diligently drown their hearts in trivial pursuits.
 How could this ever approach *non-action*?

 The Sage does not burden his form with profit,
 desiring only in *non-desire* (*bùyù*).
 Nurturing both self and others is complete when stopped,
 thus he does not prize goods that are hard to obtain.
 He does not extinguish the heavenly in himself for worldly gain,
 studying only in *non-study* (*bùxué*—
 cultivating brightness and harmony without contrivance),
 thus correcting the errors of the common folk.

 When the Dao/Way is not illuminated,
 even the worthy err in it—
 how much more so the common folk?
 To correct their errors and return them to their true nature—
 this is how scholars without worries find joy.

 Lü Zhi said: To discard gold as though it were mountain soil,
 to cast pearls into the abyss,
 to abandon the treasures of *extreme authenticity* (*jízhēn*),
 to sever the tools of exhausting skill (*qióngqiǎo*),
 this is what is meant by *desiring without desiring*.
 To forget about benevolence and righteousness,
 to abandon ritual and music,
 to cast aside the stale dregs of old teachings,
 to throw away the rotten husks of false doctrine—
 this is what is meant by *studying without studying*.

 Wang Yuanzé said: *Desiring without desiring* is not the absence of desire,
 but desire lies in *non-desire*—
 thus he does not prize goods that are hard to obtain.
 When the people have food to eat and warm clothing,
 these are necessities of their nature—
 to desire no more than this is not to have desire,
 yet after satisfying these needs, to further covet hard-to-obtain goods—
 this is the delusion of the foolish masses,
 straying far from their fundamental nature.

 Therefore, the Sage desires without desiring,
 to rescue them from their delusion and return them to their true nature.
 *Studying without studying* is not the absence of learning,
 but what is learned lies in *non-study*—
 to correct the errors of the common folk.

 The common folk chase trivial ends and engage in endless affairs,
 while the Sage, through *non-study* that corrects their errors,
 returns them to the Dao/Way.
 To assist the natural unfolding of all things,
 yet dare not to act.

 Lu Yu said: *Assist* means *aid*.
 Man is born in stillness—
 this is the nature of things.
 With *non-desire* and *non-study*,
 aiding the natural unfolding of all things,
 letting each fulfill its nature,
 yet daring not to act—
 what is there to do?
 It does not disrupt their inherent order,
 nor go against their natural inclinations.
 To aid their arising when they arise,
 to aid their form when they take shape—
 this is how the officials of *You* (有虞) managed affairs,
 and how the virtuous power of *Xia* (有夏) brought harmony to birds, beasts, fish, and tortoises.

 This passage speaks of how the Sage addresses difficulty in its ease.
 Thus, he holds fast in times of tranquility—
 this is what is meant by *acting before things arise*.
 He establishes order in moments of fragility—
 this is what is meant by *governing before chaos ensues*.」

«Take three matters as analogies. If one seeks to forestall future hardship by acting early, one must act without interference and without grasping—then all will be well. The Sage does not wish to still the people with *non-action* (无为) or to cut off learning with *non-study* (无忧), but rather to assist all things in their natural unfolding, thus embodying the Dao of *non-action*. Only then can one maintain ease in tranquility and avoid the burden of future difficulty.

The ancient masters who excelled in the Way did not seek to enlighten the people, but rather to keep them simple. *Commentary*: The people can be guided, but cannot be made to know.

The ancient masters who excelled in the Way merely led them.

To reverse their natural inclinations, the Way can be carried out, yet when one acts with them, no cleverness or artifice is needed.

Thus it is said: To keep the Three Dynasties and those below in simplicity, to release the unadorned *non-action* (恬惔无为), and to delight in the harmonious and plain (咛哼之意), bending and folding ritual and music to rectify the forms of the world, sighing and breathing forth virtue and righteousness to soothe the hearts of the people—this was done to enlighten them.

Though called *governance* (治之), what greater chaos could there be?

Wang Bi said: *Enlightenment* (明) means excessive perception and craftiness, dispersing their simplicity.

*Simplicity* (愚) means having no knowledge to guard, following nature.

Wen Gong said: To remove ornamentation and pursue reality, to return to purity and simplicity.

Cao Dao Chong said: When the people are clever, deceit arises; when the people are simple, they return to purity.

The enlightened one enlights the people with his cleverness, but not to enlighten them—that is what is meant by *not governing the state with cleverness*.

*Simplicity* is the opposite of cleverness; one seeks to keep the people simple, making them abandon cleverness and return to purity, forgetting distinctions, severing good and evil, and aligning with the state of *no knowledge, no desire*.

The difficulty of governing the people lies in their cleverness being too great.

Shu Wang said: The Sage’s cleverness is a state’s beneficial tool.

The ancient masters who excelled in the Way did not display their Sage’s cleverness to the people, but rather sought to keep them simple, without knowledge or desire.

Thus, Zhuangzi said: If the ruler truly loves cleverness yet lacks the Way, then the world will fall into great disorder.

How do we know this to be true? The more cunning the bows, crossbows, and snares are, the more the birds are disordered above. The more intricate the hooks, baits, nets, and traps, the more the fish are disordered in the water. The more refined the fences, enclosures, and traps, the more the beasts are disordered in the marshes. The more varied the clever distinctions of *allowing, gradual, poisonous, slippery, firm, white, resolving filth, and harmonizing differences*, the more the people are bewildered by sophistry.

Thus, the world often falls into great disorder, and the blame lies with the love of cleverness. Cleverness leads to deceit; simplicity leads to no knowledge. When the people are filled with clever deceit, cunning and falsehood flourish, making governance difficult.

Thus, governing the state with cleverness is the state’s ruin.

Lu Yu said: To uphold the Way and employ cleverness is to give rise to great falsehood.

Falsehood arises from below, cleverness from above; when the two deceive each other, the harm is great.

Tang Ming Huang said: *With* (以) means *to use*; *ruin* (贼) means *harm*. When a ruler employs clever ministers to govern the state, cleverness must inevitably produce laws. Laws bring forth deceit, and orders give rise to cunning, which is why the state grows increasingly corrupt and cleverness becomes the state’s ruin.

Cao Can guarded the law, yet the Han prospered through him; Shang Jun altered the law, yet Qin declined through him. Thus, the *Classic* above says: *When cleverness and wisdom arise, great falsehood follows*—this is the case.

Su Zi You said: I govern others with my cleverness; they also respond with their cleverness. Thus, the ruler and people deceive each other, and the harm is great.

Not governing the state with cleverness is the state’s blessing.

Tang Ming Huang said: If one does not employ clever ministers but instead takes pure-virtued officials, who can rest and flourish, manage affairs and resolve difficulties naturally, then clever deceit will grow thin and simplicity will flourish. When the people are harmonious, the years will be bountiful—this is why it is the state’s blessing.

Liu Zhong Ping said: Not governing the state with cleverness is to *open the heavens* (开天); governing the state with cleverness is to *open the people* (开人). To open the heavens is to follow, and following means to act in what requires no effort—this is why their governance is not strict yet effective. To open the people is to pierce, and piercing means to lose oneself in excessive scrutiny—this is why their people are not simple but deficient.

Thus it is said: Governing the state with cleverness is the state’s ruin; not governing the state with cleverness is the state’s blessing.

The *New Sayings* say: Yao and Shun’s cleverness lay in not restricting things; Yu’s cleverness lay in acting in what required no effort. Not restricting things means not scrutinizing them to claim enlightenment; acting in what requires no effort means not piercing them to claim utility—this is truly to excel in the use of cleverness.

If we speak of the Laozi’s *not governing the state with cleverness*, it lies in preserving it, and then the people will benefit a hundredfold.

Understanding these two principles is also the *standard model* (楷式).

He Shang Gong said: The two principles refer to cleverness and the lack of cleverness.

Those who always know cleverness are the ruin; those who lack cleverness are the blessing.

This is the standard model for governing oneself and the state.

Tang Ming Huang said: To employ clever deceit harms the people; to employ pure virtue blesses the state. When a ruler understands these two principles, he entrusts pure-virtued officials—this is the standard model for being a ruler.

Lu Ji Fu said: Understanding these two principles is not just for governing the state, but also serves as a standard model for oneself, and it is never violated.

Always knowing the standard model is called *Mysterious Virtue* (玄德).

He Shang Gong said: *Mysterious* (玄) is the *heavenly* (天). To always know the standard model for governing oneself and the state is to align with the virtue of heaven.

Tang Ming Huang said: *Mysterious* (玄) means *deep* (深) and *wondrous* (妙). When a ruler always knows whom to entrust, this is called the deep, far-reaching, wondrous, and supreme virtue.

Mysterious Virtue is deep and far-reaching, opposing all things—

*Commentary*: *Mysterious* (玄) is the color of heaven. To always know the standard model yet not use cleverness is to align with heaven’s virtue, deep beyond measure, far beyond reach, standing alone above all things, so that no thing can couple with it. Thus it is said: *It opposes all things.*»

Liu Renhui said: *Mysterious Virtue* is unfathomable, hence it is called *deep*. It is exhaustive in all directions, hence it is called *far-reaching*. Abandoning cleverness and entrusting to simplicity, hence it is said: *It opposes all things*.

*Mysterious Virtue* ascends and enters the realm of the *Small* (小), distinguishing itself from all things, hence it opposes all things. Only then does it reach the *Great Harmony* (大顺).

*Commentary*: *Harmony* (顺) is the *principle of heaven* (天之理). To reach the *Great Harmony* is to discard cleverness and return to simplicity, following only the principle of heaven.

Zhuangzi said: *"To align with Heaven and Earth, its alignment is seamless—
as if simple, as if confused—
this is called *Mysterious Virtue*,
in harmony with the *Great Harmony*."*

Only through such simplicity and confusion does one remove cleverness. Gu Huan said: *"Though it may seem contrary to worldly ways, it is in harmony with the Dao."*

This chapter speaks of the *Dao* being employed secretly, guiding the people without their awareness. Without cleverness or deceit, it returns to the purity of simplicity. If one knows this as the standard model, it is the profound, far-reaching, wondrous, and supreme virtue—discarding cleverness and following the principle of heaven.

*The Daoist Classic of Authentic Virtue and Goodness, Volume 10, Chapter 10* / *The Daoist Classic of Authentic Virtue and Goodness, Volume 11, Chapter 11*

---

**Chapter 66: The Jianghai (River and Sea) as Sovereign of All the Valleys**
*Collected by Master Li Lin of Song’s Rao County*

The Jianghai (River and Sea) is able to be the sovereign of all the valleys
because it excels in yielding.

Gu Huan said: *"The Jianghai dwells in lowliness,
thus all the valleys reverence it as their sovereign.
A ruler abides in humility,
and thus all things return to him.
Hence, he is able to be the sovereign of all the valleys."*

The Tang Emperor Minghuang said: *"The Jianghai’s ability to command all the rivers
to pay homage and make it their sovereign
arises from its excellence in occupying the lower reaches.
Thus, the *Yijing* says: ‘The Way of Earth transforms fullness into yielding flow.’
This is a metaphorical illustration.
When the Way of Earth employs yielding,
all the rivers converge and flow toward it.
When the Sage employs yielding,
the common people come as his children
and do not grow weary of him.
It is through lowliness and yielding
that one is able to be the sovereign of all the valleys.
The sovereign is the one to whom all things return.
If one yields, the people will return to him
like water flowing downward.
Thus, the Sage who seeks to elevate others
does so through his words of yielding.
Whoever seeks to precede others
places himself behind in deed.
Hence, though he dwells above,
the people do not feel burdened;
though he stands before,
the people do not feel harmed.
Master Shàngshǔ Gōng said: ‘To be above others,
follow the Jianghai’s example of humility.
To precede others,
act first for others, then for oneself.’
The Sage, though above the people as their ruler,
does not oppress them with his exalted position,
so they revere him without feeling the weight.
The Sage, though before the people,
does not obscure those behind with his brilliance,
so the people love him as they would a parent,
and have no thought of harming him.
Thus, all under heaven willingly promote him
and never tire of doing so."*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"The Sage’s possession of the world—
in terms of his station, he is indeed the one above others.
Yet, when he is called ‘孤’ (*gū*, solitary),
‘寡’ (*guǎ*, few), or ‘不谷’ (*bùgǔ*, unblessed),
and bears the shame and ill fortune of the state,
it is because of his words of yielding.
In terms of his order—
he is indeed the one who precedes others,
yet he is pressed to act only after being compelled,
moved to respond only after being touched,
and rises only when there is no alternative—
this is because he places himself behind in deed.
If he dwells above but the people do not feel burdened,
then the burden of reverence becomes light.
If he places himself behind in deed,
then though he stands before, the people do not feel harmed,
and thus they follow him as a benefit.
No burden, no harm—
this is why all under heaven willingly promote him
and never tire of doing so.
Because he does not contend,
all under heaven cannot contend with him."*

Luo Shi said: *"The heart and form being empty,
who could contend with one who possesses nothing?"*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"Because he yields in words
and places himself behind in deed,
he does not contend.
All under heaven willingly promote him
and never tire of doing so,
so none can contend with him.
Those who do not embody *Mysterious Virtue*—
could they ever be like this?
Thus it is said: ‘Because he does not contend,
all under heaven cannot contend with him.’
This chapter takes yielding as its core:
The Jianghai excels in yielding,
so it is the sovereign of all the valleys.
The Sage yields in words and places himself behind in deed,
so all under heaven willingly promote him
and never tire of doing so.
Who could contend with one who yields?"*

---

**All Under Heaven Say: My Dao**
*All Under Heaven Say: My Dao Seems Like the Unresembling*

All under heaven say: *"My Dao seems like the unresembling."*

Du Bì said: *"Unresembling means ‘not conforming to things.’
Since it does not conform to things,
it is called unresembling.
Only because it is vast,
it seems like the unresembling."*

Sū Zǐyóu said: *"The Dao is spacious and formless,
decayed and nameless.
It pervades all things,
yet does not resemble any one thing—
this is why it is vast.
If it resembles things,
then it is merely one thing,
and how could it be vast?
If it resembles, then it has long been small."*

The *Yizhù* (Commentary) says: *"Resembling things is small;
being resembled by things is vast.
The Dao encompasses all things—
how vast it is!
Thus, if it seems like the unresembling or resembling,
then the Dao has something outside of it—
how could it still be vast?"*

Wáng Bì said: *"‘It has long been small’—
this is also to say ‘its smallness has long endured.’
If it resembles, it loses its basis for being vast.
Thus it is said: ‘If it resembles,
then it has long been small.’"*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"All under heaven merely see my Dao’s vastness
and say it seems like the unresembling,
yet they do not know why it is vast—
indeed, it seems like the unresembling.
How so? The vast Dao is boundless,
its influence cannot be confined to left or right,
it is everywhere without exception.
If it is everywhere without exception,
there is nothing to doubt—
yet they call it unresembling without knowing
that its vastness lies in its unresembling.
If it resembles anything,
then there would be something outside the Dao,
and if the Dao has something outside of it,
then it is not everywhere—
could it still be vast?
Wáng Yuànzé said: ‘Resembling means
that something resembles the Dao,
and the Sage is the progenitor of all things.
Thus, those who embody the Dao,
things should resemble him—
how could he resemble things?
If there is resemblance,
then he is an image of that thing.
If he is an image of that thing,
then that thing must be vast,
and he would be small.
I have the Three Treasures,
to cherish and uphold them.’"*

The Tang Emperor Minghuang said: *"Though my Dao is vast,
it does not resemble anything,
yet there are these three practices—
most precious indeed!
If one can always cherish, lean upon, and uphold them,
they can govern oneself and govern the state."*

Wáng Yuànzé said: *"All these Three Treasures—
though worldly sentiment calls them small,
they are the basis by which the highest person is vast."*

The first is *cì* (compassion).
Wáng Yuànzé said: *"Compassion is rooted in love;
love is the virtue of benevolence toward things,
yet it is uniquely called compassion—
for when it transforms, it spreads vast virtue
to cover all below, reaching its peak at the end.
The *Laozi* speaks of its origin,
so it does not say ‘transform,’
but says ‘compassion.’
Compassion is the fundamental Way of the fatherly Dao,
and it does not rely on artificial means."*

Táng Zhì said: *"Compassion is love.
The *Liji* says: ‘For the man who is a father,
his virtue stops at compassion—
this is beneficial.’"*

**Compassion** is the foundation of humanity.
The Dao of father and child is innate to nature.
A person’s compassion and filial piety arise from their inherent nature,
requiring no external reliance.

The **virtuous** is the essence of humanity,
taking universal love as their heart—
this is far removed from innate nature.
The *Laozi* elucidates the essentials of virtue and power,
thus treasuring compassion as the jewel.

The **Prophet**’s Five Constants place benevolence first.
The **Buddha’s** Ten Precepts forbid killing above all.
Though the Three Sages differ,
they all ground themselves in compassion—
thus they are one.

**The second is frugality.**
Gu Wén said: *"Treasure vital essence, cherish qi—
do not indulge in extravagance."*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"In conduct, he moves with measured restraint,
taking moderation as his standard—
is this not frugality?"*
Frugality is the shared virtue of power.
Great Yú cultivated himself,
considering extreme frugality as expenditure.
King Wén governed by promoting restraint as transformation.
Great indeed is the virtue of frugality!
The *Shū* says: *"With reverence and frugality, virtue is achieved."*

**The third is not daring to lead the world.**
Gu Wén said: *"Walk in humility, dwell in the rear—
do not take precedence over others."*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"Never taking the lead,
yet always following others,
while all take precedence,
he alone takes the rear—
is this not not daring to lead the world?"*

Wáng Yuànzé said: *"In the *Yì*, this is ‘humility.’
Heaven, Earth, humanity, and spirits—
all hold humility as noble,
thus the Sage treasures it.
The Sage of Confucius grounds himself in the Dao of benevolence,
takes frugality and yielding as virtue,
and treasures these three as well."*

**Compassion thus enables courage.**
Wáng Bì said: *"Compassion, when deployed, conquers;
when held firm, it endures—
thus it enables courage."*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"Compassion is soft and weak,
yet it overcomes rigidity—
this is courage."*

Mèngzǐ said: *"The benevolent have no rival."*
Shùn’s nature was benevolent,
yet his form was punished with four crimes.
Tāng’s benevolence conquered,
overcoming Xià Jié’s tyranny.
King Wén treated the people as wounded—
truly compassionate—
yet his single anger settled the world’s people.
Is this not courage?

**Frugality thus enables breadth.**
Gu Wén said: *"Cultivate the body, cherish qi—
then life and nature prolong themselves.
Govern the state, cherish the people—
then virtue and transformation spread naturally.
Frugality in self-cultivation preserves spirit;
restraint in governance ensures surplus resources.
The *Shū* says: ‘Be cautious with your frugal virtue,
and hold fast to enduring plans.’"*

**Not daring to lead the world thus enables completion as an instrument.**
*Commentary*: *"The Way of Heaven is to triumph without contending.
The Way’s exaltation makes one the foremost instrument."*

Hé Shànggōng said: *"Not daring to lead the world,
yet completing as an instrument—
this is the Dao-person.
I can be the foremost among Dao-persons."*

Shū Wáng said: *"If one does not dare to lead the world,
then none will dare to lead them—
thus they complete as an instrument."*

Now they abandon compassion for courage,
frugality for breadth,
and humility for precedence—
they are doomed.

*Commentary*: *"The world knows that courage suffices to conquer others,
yet they do not know that compassion alone enables courage.
They know that breadth suffices to boast before the masses,
yet they do not know that frugality alone enables breadth.
They know that completion as an instrument is worthy of reverence,
yet they do not know that taking the rear is essential—
thus they are merely rigid and strong,
and their path leads to death."*

Gu Wén said: *"Discarding compassion and mercy,
they instead seek daring—
this is reckless abandon, valuing fearlessness as courage.
Not treasuring their qi, they dissipate their essence;
not cherishing others, they expend their strength;
abandoning humility, they only seek precedence—
such conduct leads only to death."*

**Compassion, when wielded in war, conquers;
when held in defense, it endures.**
*Commentary*: *"The benevolent have no rival in the world,
thus they conquer in war.
The people love their ruler as limbs protect the head,
as children defend their parents—
they die in defense without retreat,
thus they endure in defense."*

Táng Mínghuáng said: *"Using compassion in war benefits in preserving the multitude.
Using compassion in defense benefits in securing the people.
Each safeguarding their own security,
thus they conquer and endure."*

Heaven, intending to rescue them, shields them with compassion.
*Commentary*: *"Those who aspire to benevolence are drawn by Heaven’s sincerity.
Those who aspire to non-benevolence lose Heaven’s mirror.
Thus Heaven rescues and shields them—
it does so through compassion alone."*

This is why compassion is first among the Three Treasures.
Hé Shànggōng said: *"When Heaven intends to aid the virtuous,
it endows them with the nature of compassion and benevolence,
so they may aid themselves."*

Wáng Yuànzé said: *"The Three Treasures all take compassion as their heart.
Speaking of Heaven’s rescue and protection,
it is because of their compassion.
This chapter speaks of the three great Treasures of the Way—
among them, compassion is the root,
thus the passage ends with Heaven rescuing and shielding them through compassion."*

---
**Chapter 68: The Virtuous Warrior Does Not Rely on Force**
Lú Yù said: *"A warrior is a general who does not rely on force—
meaning he does not oppress others with force."*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"A warrior’s name signifies ‘affairs of the Dao,’
and his task is to lead others.
In affairs of the Dao, he does not contend for victory;
in leading others, he uses their strength—
where is the need for force?
Like Shì Nán Yíliáo resolving two households’ dispute by juggling,
or Sūn Shū’áo lying down with a feather fan while the Yǐng people laid down arms."*

**The skilled warrior does not grow angry.**
*Commentary*: *"The highest warfare subverts strategy,
yet anger truly overcomes deliberation."*

Hé Shànggōng said: *"The skilled warrior, wielding the Dao,
restrains evil in the heart,
cuts off calamity before it arises—
there is no need to punish with anger."*

Shū Wáng said: *"Not growing angry is to be a skilled warrior."*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"For a warrior to have no need of force,
the skilled warrior does not grow angry,
the skilled victor does not contend,
the skilled leader places himself below others—
this is fitting indeed."*

War is a perilous affair.
The skilled warrior laments the people and punishes crime—
how could there be private anger to employ peril and harm the people?

If the Shang King Wu overthrew Jie, as did King Tang in deposing Jie—this is how one conquers an enemy without contention.

*The skilled victor does not contend.*
*Commentary*: Contention is contrary to virtue.

To fight for land, the dead fill the fields;
to fight for cities, the dead fill the streets—
victory and defeat are yet undetermined.

It is through non-force that force is perfected,
through non-anger that anger is fulfilled,
through non-contention that contention is transcended—
these three arise from virtue, hence they are called *skilled*.

Hé Shànggōng said: *"The skilled warrior who conquers by the Dao
attracts the near with benevolence,
draws the distant with virtue,
and does not contend with the enemy—
yet the enemy surrenders of its own accord."*

Liú Rénhuì said: *"Virtue moves things to submission—
thus it is called conquering the enemy.
Weapons do not clash—
thus it is called not contending."*

Contention is contrary to virtue.
Not to contend yet to conquer—
this is what is called *skilled*.

Warfare is most noble when strategy is struck down;
repeatedly exchanging blows is not the highest use of arms.
A hundred battles, a hundred victories—
this is not the skillful use of warfare.
The skillful user of arms conquers without battle—
this is the supreme skill.

The skillful leader places himself below others.

*Commentary*: Though wisdom encompasses heaven and earth, it does not presume—
thus the wise are employed for counsel.
Though ability exhausts the seas within, it does not act alone—
thus the capable are employed for service.
Though eloquence carves through all things, it does not boast—
thus the eloquent are employed as messengers.

Hé Shànggōng said: *"The skillful leader who relies on assistants
must practice humility and deference."*

Zhuǎn Wēi said: *"The skillful leader employs virtue, not force.
Thus, using ministers of support and counsel,
they are compared to limbs and sinews of the body.
Using generals,
they kneel to receive their commands,
and march while pushing their chariots forward—
this is the virtue of non-contention."*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"Those who embody the Dao do not contend.
If they do not contend, none in the world can contend with them.
Thus, the skilled warrior does not wield force,
the skilled victor does not grow angry,
the skilled conqueror does not contend—
this is called the virtue of non-contention.
This is called employing the strength of others."*

*Commentary*: The clever exhaust their sight and hearing,
the capable exhaust their schemes and abilities—
yet those who place them in position have no such need.

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"Those who embody the Dao can humble themselves.
Those who humble themselves have others gladly serve them,
yet do not rely on themselves alone.
Thus, the skillful leader places himself below others—
this is called employing the strength of others.
This is called aligning with the Dao,
the ancient pinnacle."*

*Commentary*: Non-action as action is called the Dao.
Not contending yet employing others—
thus one can align with the Dao,
reaching the utmost that cannot be surpassed.
Hence it is said: *The ancient pinnacle is the utmost pinnacle.*
The most perfect wood is the ridgepole—
this chapter speaks of the skilled warrior not wielding force.

Not to grow angry, not to contend,
to employ the strength of others—
this is not wielding force.
Virtue does not contend;
strength employs others—
thus one can align with the Dao,
and non-action cannot be exceeded.

**Chapter Sixty-Nine: The Use of Arms**

When using arms, I dare not be the aggressor,
but only the responder.

*Commentary*: The one who provokes is the aggressor,
the one who responds is the defender.
To act only when pressed,
to rise only when compelled—
this is called responding with arms.
Responding with arms is to be the defender.

Gù Huān said: *"To act first in the state or move first in battle is to be the aggressor.
To harmonize yet not provoke,
to act only when pressed—
this is to be the defender.
To act first or move first is to invite danger through arrogance.
Now, desiring to follow the Dao and respond to others
to preserve oneself—
since one does not act first,
this is not being the aggressor.
Since one acts only when compelled,
this is being the defender."*

I dare not advance an inch,
yet retreat a foot.

*Commentary*: Not delighting in killing,
advancing is difficult, retreating is easy.

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"The aggressor is contrary, the defender is compliant;
the aggressor labors, the defender rests;
advancing is arrogant, retreating is humble;
advancing is hasty, retreating is composed—
to meet contrariness with compliance,
to meet labor with rest,
to meet arrogance with humility,
to meet haste with composure—
these are not to be opposed."*

Wáng Yuànzé said: *"Not eager for slaughter."*

This is called *action without action*.

*Commentary*: The skilled warrior does not wield force,
acting yet leaving no trace.

Emperor Xuánzōng of Tang said: *"To retreat as the defender,
not to contend with others—
though engaging the enemy,
it is as though there were no action."*

Zhāng Jūnxiàng said: *"Arms arise from others;
I truly do not act.
Responding to things and moving—
though acting, it is as though there were no action."*

Wáng Yuànzé said: *"The most benevolent warfare
has campaigns without battles,
and is as though there were no action."*

*Without arms to raise,*
Emperor Xuánzōng of Tang said: *"Raising the arm is to display anger;
the skilled warrior does not grow angry—
if there are no arms to raise."*

*Yet without an enemy,*
*Commentary*: The skilled conqueror does not contend.

Gù Huān said: *"‘Yet’ means to draw out.
Once people submit to virtue,
the troops are disbanded and returned—
though one might wish to provoke an enemy,
there is none left to provoke."*

*Without weapons to grasp,*
*Commentary*: Employing the strength of others,
there is no need to grasp weapons.

Emperor Xuánzōng of Tang said: *"To grasp weapons is to display killing;
now, with benevolence and harmony as the principle,
though one grasps weapons,
it is as though there were none."*

Wáng Yuànzé said: *"Though there is the name of battle,
there are none before to oppose—
thus it is as though there were no battle."*

No greater calamity than to treat the enemy lightly.

Chéng Xuányīng said: *"To perceive the enemy’s terrain yet be reckless,
stained by worldly dust,
leads to the three woeful paths."*

To treat the enemy lightly risks losing one’s treasure.

Chéng Xuányīng said: *"‘Risks’ means to exhaust.
To treat the enemy lightly is to love courage yet lack benevolence,
to expand greed yet lose frugality,
to love advancing yet forget retreat—
all three treasures are exhausted."*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"If one cannot emerge from non-action,
knowing the aggressor yet not the defender,
knowing advance yet not retreat—
this is called treating the enemy lightly.
To treat the enemy lightly is to lose one’s benevolence
and forfeit the hearts of the seven peoples and all things."*

Thus, when armies clash,
the one who first displays mercy will win.

「Wang Yuanzé said: *"The heart of compassion—this is virtue.
Virtue is why one can be courageous, and thus prevail.*
Another reading has ‘相若’ (mutually comparable), which also makes sense.
‘若’ here refers to armies clashing in arms.
This chapter speaks of wielding arms when there is no alternative,
thus being the guest in conflict,
and yielding by a foot—
not daring to treat the enemy lightly,
for fear of losing one’s treasure.
Hence, when armies clash,
the one who shows compassion and mercy will prevail.

My words are easy to understand, easy to follow.
Yet none in the world can understand, none can follow.
The imperial commentary states:
*The Dao is bright and easily seen,
so when spoken of, it is easy to understand.*
It is simple yet easy to uphold,
so when encountered, it is easy to follow.
Mencius said:
*The Dao is like a broad road—
yet how could it be difficult to know?*
Thus, the Dao has no difficulty,
yet the world has none who cannot find it difficult—
those who cannot,
simply do not seek it within themselves.
Su Ziyou said:
*The greatness of the Dao lies in restoring one’s nature—
and this is enough.*
The wondrous applications of nature
are seen in one’s daily actions and meals.
The sage points to this and shows it to others—
is it not easy to understand?
If one can embody this to respond to things—
is it not easy to follow?
Yet the world often suffers from knowing yet not recognizing,
and even knowing, cannot follow—
how much less can it follow?

Easy to understand, easy to follow—
because of its utmost simplicity.
It cannot be grasped by mere thought or reasoning,
so it is called *‘not thinking yet knowing.’*
Because of its utmost proximity,
it cannot be reached by slow or hasty steps,
so it is called *‘not walking yet arriving.’*
Using intelligence to know it,
one’s understanding will be shallow—
thus, none in the world can truly know it.
Using force to follow it,
one cannot attain—
thus, none in the world can truly follow it.
Words have a root, affairs have a lord.
The imperial commentary states:
*Words cannot be exhausted,
yet principle is their foundation.*
*Affairs cannot be fully addressed,
yet the Dao is their sovereign.*
Following principle to seek understanding,
and adhering to the Dao to act—
then the world has no difficulty.
Words take *non-speech* as their root;
affairs take *non-action* as their lord.
Obtaining the root makes understanding easy,
recognizing the lord makes action easy.
Those in the world who cannot understand or cannot follow,
simply do not know their root or their lord.
Only those without knowledge,
are why they do not know me.

Wang Bi said:
*‘Being clothed in coarse sackcloth yet holding jade within’*—
clothing in coarse sackcloth means blending with the common,
holding jade within means treasuring one’s true essence.
The sage is difficult to know,
because they blend with the common yet do not stand out,
hold jade within yet do not display it—
thus, they are difficult to know and yet noble.
Coarse sackcloth is the garment of the vulgar,
jade is the precious treasure.
Clothing in coarse sackcloth means appearing crude externally,
harmonizing one’s light with the dust,
acting as if foolish or dim-witted,
not differing from the common.
Holding jade within means concealing treasure inwardly,
embracing the Dao and nurturing virtue,
hiding one’s name and reputation,
not revealing oneself to others.
This differs from the vulgar crowd who flaunt jade on their sleeves.
Zhuangzi said:
*The sage holds it within themselves,
while the common people debate to show it to others.*
This chapter states that the Dao is easy to know and easy to follow,
yet none in the world can know or follow it—
because words have a root and affairs have a lord.
Only those who have the root and lord,
are why the knowing ones are few.
The sage is difficult to know,
because they treasure this Dao and wear coarse sackcloth.

**Chapter Seventy-One: Knowing the Unknown**
Knowing the unknown is supreme.
Not knowing yet claiming to know is an affliction.

Lǚ Jìfǔ said:
*The essence of the Dao is
to know without knowing yet be able to know.*
To recognize one’s own ignorance,
and yet know through that ignorance—
this is the highest attainment.
Thus it is said: *‘Knowing the unknown is supreme.’*
Though one recognizes their ignorance,
yet insists on knowing through knowledge—
their mind is mediocre and unpeaceful.
Thus it is said: *‘Not knowing yet claiming to know is an affliction.’*

Wen Gong said:
*‘Knowing yet acting as if one does not know,
keeps one distant from blame.’*
*‘Not knowing yet insisting on knowing,
invites disaster.’*
Only by recognizing the affliction of affliction,
does one avoid affliction.
Tang Minghuang said:
*‘Only by recognizing the affliction of insisting on knowing,
does one not fall prey to that insistence.’*
The sage is not afflicted,
because they recognize affliction—
thus they are not afflicted.

Lǚ Jìfǔ said:
*‘Only by recognizing the affliction of knowing as an affliction,
and yet overcoming that affliction,
does one return to non-knowing—
and then knowledge is no longer an affliction.’*
Thus, the sage is not afflicted—
this is why.

Nanbo Ziqí said:
*‘I pity those who destroy themselves.
I also pity those who pity others.
I also pity those who pity those who pity others.’*
Later, he said: *‘Far indeed.’*
If one like Ziqí’s tea,
can be called *‘recognizing the affliction of affliction’*?

Wang Yuanzé said:
*‘If one is afflicted yet does not recognize it,
the afflicted will never awaken.’*

This chapter speaks of the **Dao/Way** having no inherent knowing—
knowing that one does not know is superior,
but if one does not know yet acts,
and knows through knowing,
then this becomes an affliction.

The sage’s non-affliction lies in
knowing knowing as an affliction,
and returning to non-knowing—
thus they are not afflicted.

**Chapter 72: The People Do Not Fear Threat**

If the people do not fear the **threat/menace**,
then great threat will come.

**He Shang Gong** said:
*"Threat is harm.
If people do not fear small harm,
then great harm will come—
speaking of death.
Those who fear it should cherish their vital essence and nurture their spirit,
harmonizing with Heaven and following Earth."*

**Cheng Xuan Ying** said:
*"Within, they understand the threat of penalties—
the scriptures, precepts, and laws.
The great threat refers to the punishments of the Three Hells (Skt. *naraka*), the Northern Cities of Retribution (*Douluo Yanzheng*), and the *Tianluo* realm.
All beings, not yet comprehending true **dharma/principle**,
indulge in worldly **laws** and act lightly,
so the true **dharma/principle** brings great punishment,
which must surely come."*

**Liu Zhong Ping** said:
*"The **Book of Poetry** says:
‘Fear the awe of Heaven,
and in time, you will be preserved.’
Indeed, the multitude does not know to fear Heaven’s awe,
so great threat comes.
That which has threat to be feared is called threat.
When the multitude does not fear Heaven’s awe,
they do nothing they should not.
This is why Heaven descends its great calamity and retribution,
and the disasters and punishments are applied.
Thus it is said:
‘Great threat will come.’"*

*"The **Book of Documents** says:
‘None dare act without fear,
but those who do not fear will be made to fear.’
Do not constrain their dwelling,
**Commentary:** ‘Dwelling’ refers to the abode of nature.
Human nature is vast and unrestricted—
it cannot be enclosed or bent.
The scholar does not speak of the **Dao/Way**,
because their dwelling is constrained.
Expand and fulfill it,
and it will fill Heaven and Earth,
embrace the Six Extremes (*liuji*—the four cardinal directions and heaven and earth),
and nothing will be beyond its reach.
Mencius said:
‘Dwell in the broadest place of the world.’
The Tang Emperor Ming Huang said:
‘The abode of the spirit is the heart.’
Do not constrain their dwelling—
this means removing emotions and desires,
making them pure and void,
so that the spirit may dwell within without constraint.
Do not satiate their birth—
**Commentary:** ‘Birth’ refers to the gathering of **qi**.
Human birth is connected to the transformations of all things,
but those who satiate their birth
are bound by the actions of day and night,
and their **qi** is diminished.
Those who preserve the Great Harmony (*Dahua*—the perfect balance of Heaven and Earth)
and do not perish by the **middle path** (*Zhongdao*—the path of moderation),
do not satiate their birth."*

**He Shang Gong** said:
*"Why people are born is because they have spirit.
The spirit depends on emptiness and purity,
loves tranquility and stillness,
but if eating and drinking are unregulated,
and one neglects the **Dao/Way** while indulging in sensuality,
then perverse and disruptive **qi** fills the body,
and this is how life is cut short and the spirit scattered."*

**Liu Zhong Ping** said:
*"‘Do not constrain their dwelling’—
this is the foundation of **virtue/power**.
‘Do not satiate their birth’—
this is the root of **virtue/power**.
The foundation of **virtue/power** is to fulfill nature,
and the root of **virtue/power** is to reach the vital essence of life.
Thus, the sage does not use human means to extinguish Heaven’s **virtue/power**,
nor does they use worldly ways to diminish life’s essence.
**Wang Yuanze** said:
‘Human nature is originally broad and flowing,
but as worldly teachings decline,
they cannot restore it to its pristine state,
so they narrow their dwelling and make it constrained,
and they block their birth and make it stifled.
Only by constraining their dwelling
do the people become impure and false.
Only by satiating their birth
do the people become thin and shallow.
But the sage is not so—
they let the people roam freely in the broadest place of the world,
and each fulfills their vast and unrestrained nature,
so who among them would dare to provoke Heaven’s threat?’"*

**Zhuangzi** said:
*"The garden of the **Wei** family,
the park of the Yellow Emperor,
the domain of Yao and Shun,
the palace of Tang and Wu—
this is the world of the sage.
The world declines and grows narrow in its dwellings.
But the sage has no superiority or inferiority—
their dwelling grows narrow only because the times they encounter are so."*

*"Only by not satiating
is one not satiated."*

**He Shang Gong** said:
*"Only those who do not satiate their spirit
wash away the filth of the heart,
being serene and content without desire,
can have the spirit dwell within them without satiation."*

**Gu Xuan** said:
*"People do not satiate their birth,
and their birth does not satiate them.
People do not abandon the **Dao/Way**,
and the **Dao/Way** does not abandon them.
Thus it is said:
‘Birth and people mutually preserve,
people and the **Dao/Way** mutually benefit.’
The **Western Ascension Scripture** says:
‘The spirit always loves people,
but people do not love the spirit.
If people do not satiate their spirit,
then the spirit will dwell within them and not depart.
Thus, the sage knows themselves but does not display themselves,
loves themselves but does not exalt themselves.’"*

**He Shang Gong** said:
*"They know their own gains and losses,
yet do not reveal themselves outwardly.
**Virtue/power** and beauty exist externally but are stored internally.
They love their own body to preserve their vital **qi**,
yet do not elevate themselves in status or honor.
They cherish their name in the world."*

**Lü Jìfǔ** said:
*"The sage knows themselves in constancy yet does not display themselves outwardly.
Their body knows constancy while their outward actions transcend it—
this is how they do not constrain their dwelling.
They love themselves through moderation yet do not exalt themselves through excess—
their birth is preserved while their life is not squandered—
this is how they do not satiate their birth."*

**Liu Jin Xi** said:
*"Preserving and nurturing true nature,
not lightly indulging in desires—
this is self-love.
Modesty, humility, stillness, and retreat,
placing others before themselves—
this is not exalting themselves.
If they know themselves through clarity yet do not display themselves through boasting,
then their nature is broad, and nothing can obscure it—
this is ‘not constraining their dwelling.’
If they love themselves through virtue yet do not exalt themselves through belittling others,
then their birth is complete, and nothing can harm it—
this is ‘not satiating their birth.’
If this is so,
how could they ever provoke Heaven’s threat?"*

Thus, one discards that and takes this.
**Gu Xuan** said:
*"Discarding that and taking this—
if one sees the exalted as noble, then external punishments will fade.
If one knows and loves, then inner sufficiency of life and **Dao/Way** will be fulfilled."*

This chapter speaks of
the upper losing its great **Dao/Way**’s transformation,
and the people losing their true nature of life.
Thus, though there are threats to be feared,
they do not fear them.
Only by not constraining their dwelling to fulfill nature,
and not satiating their birth to reach life’s essence,
could they ever provoke Heaven’s threat?
Thus, one discards that which exalts and displays,
and takes this which knows and loves—
also making the people broaden their dwelling and not constrain it,
preserving their birth and not satiating it.

The sage’s discernment in discarding and taking
is clearly visible.

**Chapter 73: On Bold Courage**

*"Bold in daring leads to death.
Metuan said: ‘Fearlessness is courage; resoluteness is daring. To see threats and fear them not, yet act without hesitation—this is the art of forcing *qi* to destroy the body.

Chen Xuanying said: ‘Courage is fierce advancement. Daring is resolute resolve. Death is annihilation. If one advances yet lacks resolve, clinging to worldly affairs, then annihilation follows.

Luó Shì said: When the mind is steadfast in action, resolute in seeking, truly abandoning evil and inviting virtue, one meets one’s fate—thus it is called death.

Bold without daring brings life.
Luó Shì said: If one acts with gentleness and goodness alone, one gains benefit—this is life, the way of long endurance. If the heart is empty and detached, not clinging to defilements, then there is long life.

Metuan said: To stand alone, fearless, yet not daring to act—guarding softness and yielding fully—this is the way to preserve life.

Shū Wáng said: Zhuangzi said: ‘The Sage treats the necessary as unnecessary, thus wields no arms. The common man treats the unnecessary as necessary, thus wields many arms. Bold in daring treats the unnecessary as necessary, thus wields many arms and meets death. Bold without daring treats the necessary as unnecessary, thus wields no arms and lives.

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: Those who wield their strength and fixate on externals are bold in daring—these are the doomed. Thus it is said: *Bold in daring leads to death.*

Those who govern with gentleness and fixate on nothing are bold without daring—these are the living. Thus it is said: *Bold without daring brings life.*

These two paths—one brings benefit, one harm.
Hé Shànggōng said: The two refer to daring and not daring. Preserving life is benefit; destroying life is harm.

Wang Bì said: Both are forms of courage, yet their applications differ—thus benefit and harm arise. Hence it is said: *One brings benefit, one harm.*

Lǚ Jìfǔ said: Bold in daring may seem beneficial, yet harm lies within. Bold without daring may seem harmful, yet benefit lies within. What Heaven abhors, who knows its reason?

Official Commentary: Those who deviate from humanity align with Heaven. What benefits humanity, Heaven abhors—who can know its source? Thus even the Sage finds it difficult.

Official Commentary: Those who follow Heaven endure; those who defy it perish—even the Sage dares not alter this.

The Way of Heaven does not contend yet triumphs well.
Official Commentary: All things emerge, and Heaven emerges with them without resistance. All things return, and Heaven receives them without obstruction—this is called not contending. Ebb and flow, fullness and emptiness, accompany all things, yet all their abundance comes from received fate—this is called not contending yet triumphing well.

Táng Mínghuáng said: Below, it speaks of Heaven’s humility and emptiness, admonishing human boldness. Heaven does not contend with things; the four seasons wax and wane without resistance—thus it triumphs well.

It does not speak yet responds well.
Wáng Yuānzé said: How does Heaven speak? The seasons move, myriad things flourish, rewarding virtue and punishing licentiousness—all changes respond without a word.

Liú Rénhuì said: Silent and still, thus it is called ‘not speaking.’ Yet when moved, it responds—thus it is called ‘responds well.’

It is not summoned yet comes.
Official Commentary: Those who receive fate can summon it, yet Heaven governs all things’ fates—who can summon them? It simply follows the natural course of *qi*.

It moves with effortless ease, yet plans with consummate skill.
