Cài Zǐhuǎng said: Though vast and unconstrained, it plans with precision—good and evil differ not by a hair.

Wáng Yuānzé said: Ever easy, thus unconstrained; knowing peril, thus plans well.

Heaven’s net is vast, sparse yet nothing escapes.
Official Commentary: Dense yet with gaps—this is human artifice. Heaven, though sparse, has no gaps. Accumulated virtue and vice bring retribution in kind—thus nothing is lost. Yet only through contention does one triumph, only through speech does one respond—this is humanity. Heaven does not contend yet triumphs well, does not speak yet responds well. Summoned, it arrives; yet to know Heaven is difficult for humanity. Heaven arrives unsummoned, unconstrained yet plans well.

Only the Sage can embody this, thus does not seek benefit nor shun harm—always benefiting, never harming—thus aligning with Heaven’s virtue, differing from those bold in daring.

Cáo Dàochōng said: Nothing escapes Heaven’s net—vast yet encompassing all. Though sparse, nothing slips through. Encompassing all six directions, no thing escapes within—this is Heaven’s net. *Héihéi*—vast indeed. Though seemingly sparse, rewards and punishments differ not by a hair.

This chapter speaks of bold daring and not daring. Bold daring is what Heaven abhors—even the Sage finds it difficult. Below, it speaks of Heaven’s humility and emptiness, urging it as a model. The people do not commonly fear death—

**Chapter 74: The People Do Not Commonly Fear Death**

*The people do not commonly fear death—how then can you frighten them with death?*

Metuan said: *Nàihé*—how, as in ‘how can this be?’ When Dao and virtue decay, punishments grow harsh and cruel, then life is not cherished, death not feared. People do not fear death because governance is oppressive. If they do not revere great virtue in life, yet you impose severe punishments to instill fear, the people will defy laws and never reform.

Luxury and licentiousness drain the vital essence and *qi*, causing the spirits to scatter—driven to perilous acts that shock the soul. When the ruler loses the Way, the people lack constancy, defying laws—what is there to fear in death? Yet the ruler still threatens death, using saws and axes, ropes and measuring lines, yet chaos never ceases—this is the case of Qin and after.

If the people commonly feared death, and acted strangely, I would seize and kill them—who would dare?"*

The Imperial Commentary states: *"The world delights in life yet often violates the law. Only then do those with strange words face punishment, and those with deviant conduct face prohibition. As Coeficial Master said, the crime of defying governance is indeed grave."*

River Master declared: *"If one governs through the Dao/Way and the people still do not comply, instead engaging in strange artifices, then the king’s law should be applied—seizing and executing them. Who would dare transgress?"*

Laozi lamented the times: *"The ruler does not first cultivate the people with virtue and the Dao/Way, but instead begins with punishments and penalties."*

Wang Bi remarked: *"Deviance disrupts the true—this is called ‘strange.’ The constant agents of execution carry out the killings."*

Emperor Xuanzong of Tang said: *"Such deceitful and strange individuals—none escape the Heavenly Net. It is the constant agents of execution who carry out the killings."*

Du Guangting explained: *"司 (sī) means ‘master.’ Heaven’s nurturing of humanity is profound, its love for them is supreme. The Southern Palace’s Golden Register rewards virtue and oversees life, while the Northern Palace’s Black Ledger records offenses and governs death. Heaven and Earth, along with the myriad spirits, oversee and judge good and evil, using them to admonish and encourage, so that evil may be abandoned and virtue cultivated. Thus, those with merit extend their years, while the guilty have their fate diminished—no fraction is lost, as in the meticulous reckoning of the Yang Palace. Heaven has the Four Official Stars of Destiny, located between Xu and Wei, beside the Human Star, overseeing humanity. These Four Official Stars are what the *Three Elements Scripture* calls ‘Heaven’s agents of execution.’*

*"To substitute as the agent of execution is to usurp the Great Carpenter’s axe. River Master said: ‘If a ruler seeks to execute in place of Heaven, it is like a clumsy man substituting for the Great Carpenter in hewing wood—laboring without success.’ To substitute for the Great Carpenter in hewing wood is to rarely avoid injuring one’s own hands. River Master said: ‘When a ruler enforces punishments, it is like a clumsy man substituting for the Great Carpenter in hewing wood—squares and circles will never align, and he will inevitably harm himself. To usurp Heaven’s execution is to disrupt its order. Without proper order, one will instead invite calamity.’*

Lu Jifu asked: *"How should the Sage govern the world through the Dao/Way?"*
*"Simply let the constant agents of execution carry out the killings—do not usurp their role."*

What are the ‘constant agents of execution’? The Heavenly Net is vast yet never fails to ensnare. Heaven’s retribution for crimes employs the Five Punishments and Five Executions—this is the role of the agents of execution. If executions arise from personal bias rather than Heaven’s retribution, then it is usurping the role of the agents of execution. To usurp the agents of execution is to usurp the Great Carpenter’s axe—rarely does one avoid injuring their own hands. Thus, the harm of usurping the agents of execution is clear.

This chapter states that when the people lack constancy and do not fear death, one must first cultivate them through the Dao/Way, not intimidate them with punishments. If the people fear death and those who act strangely are executed, who would dare transgress? Yet one must not execute oneself—there are Heaven’s agents of execution. If one usurps their role, it is like substituting for the Great Carpenter, and none escape harming themselves.

*Conclusion of Volume Eleven of the *Collected Virtues of the Dao/Way and Its Power Scripture***
*Beginning of Volume Twelve of the *Collected Virtues of the Dao/Way and Its Power Scripture***

「Thus, the people’s hunger arises from the excessive taxes and levies imposed by their rulers—hence their hunger. *The Imperial Commentary* states: Heavy taxation leads to widespread barrenness of fields and crops, and the people lack sufficient food.

 Lü Jiufu remarks: If one man’s labor can feed several households, why should there ever be hunger? Yet when hunger does arise, is it not precisely because of the excessive taxes and levies demanded by their rulers?

 Wang Yuanzhe asserts: If one establishes official salaries and implements the rites of taxation properly, it would be to enrich the people. But when expenditures grow lavish while the foundations of governance weaken, and taxes multiply while essentials decline, the result is merely that the people starve.

 The Duke of Zhou imposed heavy taxes, leading to the rise of Chu. *The Book of Songs* composed the *Huge Rat* ode to censure the Duke of Wei for his oppressive taxation.

 The difficulty of governing the people stems from the rulers’ excessive interventions—hence their difficulty in being governed. *The Imperial Commentary* states: When governance is intricate, deceit and falsehood flourish, and the people lose their simplicity.

 Liu Jinxi notes: Excessive intervention makes governance cumbersome; non-intervention simplifies affairs. Simplicity is easy to follow, while intricacy is hard to govern.

 The six passions are hard to control because of the rulers’ excessive interventions. Lü Jiufu says: Weaving yields clothing, tilling yields food—this is called harmonizing virtue. Why should governing be difficult? Yet when governance becomes difficult, is it not because of the rulers’ excessive interventions?

 Wang Yuanzhe states: If one entrusts discerning officials to manage affairs, it would be to govern the people. But when men are burdened by customs and refined by worldly influence, craftiness and deceit proliferate, making governance even harder. Observing the ancient past and later ages confirms this truth.

 Shun governed through non-intervention, enabling his rule as Emperor. Wu governed with relaxed oversight, securing his reign as King. The rise and flourishing of emperors and kings all originate from this principle.

 *The Scripture* says: To govern through non-action is to ensure nothing remains ungoverned.

 The people’s disregard for death stems from their excessive pursuit of life—hence their lightness toward death. *Zuan Wei* explains: Heavy taxation and cumbersome governance strip the people of their livelihoods, driving them to compete for treasures and risk laws and prohibitions. They court death recklessly because their pursuit of means to sustain life is too fervent, leading to those who fear neither drowning nor burning, nor wielding blades.

 Lü Jiufu says: If their food is sweet, their clothing fine, their dwellings secure, and their customs joyful, why should they ever regard death lightly? Yet when they do regard death lightly, is it not because their pursuit of life’s sustenance is too fervent?

 Wen Gong observes: The pursuit of profit is meant to nourish life, yet the people often lose their lives through profit. Overvaluing life while greedily seeking profit inevitably leads to courting danger, and thus they view death lightly.

 Only those who do not cling to life for its own sake are wiser than those who prize it. *The Imperial Commentary* cites *Zhuangzi*: "Those who understand the nature of life do not strive for what life cannot provide." Not clinging to life means abandoning what life cannot provide. To forsake worldly affairs and relinquish life’s burdens is the way. When one’s form is unlabored and essence unexhausted, form preserved and essence restored, one aligns with Heaven—this is wiser than merely prizing life.

 Prizing life differs from regarding death lightly; relinquishing life’s burdens is wiser than merely prizing it. Extending the principles that benefit one’s own life to the people would result in light taxation and few punishments, ensuring households are well-provided and people are content, while distinct clothing and customs would prevent transgressions. This is the ultimate achievement of emperors and kings.

 This chapter speaks of heavy taxation and cumbersome governance, which strip the people of their livelihoods, leading them to regard death lightly. These three issues all harm the essentials while neglecting the foundations. Prizing life differs from regarding death lightly, while not clinging to life’s burdens is wiser than merely prizing it.

---
*Chapter Seventy-Six: On the Nature of Life and Death*

The nature of human life is soft and weak, while death brings rigidity and strength. *He Shang Gong* says: In life, humans contain harmonious *qi* and embrace spirit, hence their softness and weakness. In death, harmonious *qi* is exhausted and spirit departs, hence their rigidity and strength.

 All things, grasses, and trees are soft and pliable in life—*He Shang Gong* says: Because harmonious *qi* remains. In death, they wither and decay—*He Shang Gong* says: Because harmonious *qi* disperses.

 Yan Zun explains: Where *yang qi* resides, trees can be bent and grasses can be tied. Where *yang qi* departs, water can solidify and ice can break. Thus, rigidity and strength belong to death, while softness and weakness belong to life.

 *He Shang Gong* says: By observing these two phenomena, one knows that rigidity and strength signify death, while softness and weakness signify life.

 Emperor Ming of the Tang dynasty remarked: It is clear that those who preserve their softness and weakness remain alive and long-lived, while those who grow rigid and overbearing perish and lose their nature.

 Lü Jiufu says: *The Dao* is a thing without form yet not contentious. Thus, it is the most soft and weak in the world, yet none comprehend it. Hence, to illustrate it with things of form: The nature of human life is soft and weak, while death brings rigidity and strength. Grasses and trees are soft and pliable in life, but wither and decay in death. Even though these things have form, they die through rigidity and strength, and live through softness and weakness—how much more so the Dao, which has no form, yet does not lead to softness and weakness! It is impossible to attain.

 Therefore, when armies grow strong, they cannot prevail—*The Imperial Commentary* states: When opposing armies clash, the sorrowful will triumph.

 Wang Zhen notes: The remnants of Xia and Shang, with armies numbering in the hundreds of thousands, overthrew the four seas. Yet at the end of Qin Shi Huang’s reign, despite his unified empire, he lost all nine regions. Xiang Yu rose to power swiftly and perished just as quickly. Wang Mang usurped the throne and was destroyed soon after. Fu Jian was defeated in humiliation at the Huai River; Emperor Yang of the Sui dynasty collapsed in splendor at the Chu Palace. These numerous dynasties all fielded armies of tens of millions, or even hundreds of millions, yet none failed to rely on their strength, only to meet defeat—this is all evidence that when armies grow strong, they cannot prevail.

 Armies that rely on their strength inevitably become arrogant and haughty. Arrogance and haughtiness are the paths to ruin and annihilation. Qin Shi Huang was at his strongest, yet his dynasty did not last two generations before collapsing. The Xiang family’s sudden rise to power was swiftly followed by extinction. When wood grows rigid, it splits—*The Imperial Commentary* says: Even the sturdy *tong* or *zhi* trees, when bent, are known to crack.」

「When strong, they are felled and split. Lush forests invite the axe—when wood is strong, it is felled and split. The mighty are brought low, while the yielding rise.

*The Imperial Commentary* notes: The yielding overcomes the rigid, the weak overcomes the strong—this is the Dao/Way and skill of the Master Laozi, as Lao Dan [Zhuangzi] demonstrates with his emphasis on moist-weakness and humble yielding.

Gazing upward at Heaven, Heaven accumulates *qi* and thus stands above; Earth accumulates form and thus lies below. Taking examples from the natural world, the flexible branches yield while above, the sturdy trunk stands firm below. By this analogy, it is clear that the rigid and strong reside below, while the yielding and weak stand above.

This passage broadly discusses how all beings—whether human or otherwise—thrive through yielding and weakness, perish through rigidity and strength. Those who seek to preserve the Way of life must surely take heed of this.

**Chapter Seventy-Seven of *The Dao of Heaven***:
The Dao/Way of Heaven is like drawing a bow. The lofty are pressed down, the lowly are raised up, the overabundant are diminished, the insufficient are supplemented. *The Imperial Commentary* notes: The Dao/Way of Heaven holds the mean as supreme, thus pressing down the lofty so they do not become excessive, raising up the lowly so they do not fall into deficiency. It advances those who are about to come, withdraws those who have succeeded. The Four Seasons cycle in turn, each attaining its proper *chǔ* [seasonal order/position].」

**严遵** said: The artisan who crafts a bow neither overbends nor underbends it, neither slackens nor over-tightens it. He shapes it by the compass and plumb-line, ensuring strict alignment. If the string is too taut and high, he relaxes and lowers it; if the string is too slack and low, he tightens and raises it. Where there is excess, he carves away to diminish it; where there is deficiency, he fills and supplements it. The string and the bow’s body balance each other—upper and lower in harmony—with evenness as the principle and attunement as the norm. Thus, the bow may be struck, and the arrow may fly true.

The principle of pressing down the high and raising the low, diminishing the excessive and supplementing the deficient—this is the Dao/Way of Heaven and Earth.

**Zhang Junxiang** said: The art of using a bow lies in this: the high must be feared for excess, so it is pressed down lest it rise too far. The low must be feared for insufficiency, so it is raised lest it fall too low. Neither too high nor too low—this is the mean, the measure of perfection. In governance, too, one must seek this equilibrium: excessive height invites rejection, while harming the low invites censure. Pressing down the high and raising the low—this aligns with the natural order.

**Emperor Xuanzong of Tang** said: Only when the bow is thus attuned can one strike true. This is like the Dao/Way of Heaven, which diminishes excess and supplements deficiency, teaching rulers to emulate Heaven’s *de* [virtue/power] and govern humanity accordingly—hence the principle of pressing down the high and raising the low.

The Dao/Way of Heaven diminishes excess and supplements deficiency.
*The Imperial Commentary* notes: *"Fullness invites loss; humility invites benefit—this is the Dao/Way of time."* But the Dao/Way of humanity is reversed: the deficient are sacrificed to serve the excessive.
*The Imperial Commentary* notes: Human nature elevates the low to oppress the high, favors the few while fearing the eminent.

**Wang Yuanzai** said: Human governance follows inclination, thus it is unequal.

**He Shangong** said: The Dao/Way of humanity is contrary to that of Heaven. Common folk diminish the poor to enrich the rich, plunder the weak to empower the strong. Who, then, can use their excess to nourish the world, but the sage alone?
*The Imperial Commentary* notes: Only the sage does not oppress the few while exhausting the many, nor does he fear the eminent while depriving the generous. Only the sage can do this.

**He Shangong** said: Who can occupy a position of excess, yet voluntarily reduce their rank and wealth to nourish those in want? Only the sage-ruler can act thus.

**Wang Yuanzai** said: The sage harmonizes with the Dao/Way of Heaven. Therefore, the sage acts without claiming merit, achieves without taking credit, and does not seek to display virtue.

**Luóshī** said: Having attained this emptiness and openness, free from thought and deliberation, how could one’s mind and intellect desire to exalt their own wisdom and ability? The sage acts without arrogance, achieves without self-satisfaction.

「If one relies on their actions to claim merit and achievement, then they will be seen as virtuous, and such virtue will inevitably lead to the self-serving path of using excess to invite loss.

This chapter speaks of the Dao/Way of Heaven, where moderation is supreme. Thus, it suppresses the high to elevate the low, reduces the excessive to supplement the insufficient. The sage follows Heaven’s Dao, not relying on actions or achievements to display their virtue, so they receive benefits without suffering loss. Only those who understand Heaven’s Dao can do this.

**Chapter 78: The Soft and Weak of the World**

Nothing in the world is as soft and weak as water, yet nothing can surpass it in overcoming hardness and strength. There is no substitute for it.

**Lǚ Jífǔ** said: Among all things in the world, only water can conform to the curves and straightness, angles and roundness of objects, flowing with them. Thus, nothing is as soft and weak as water. Yet it can carry great vessels, turn massive stones, pierce steep cliffs and valleys, and float and bear all things under Heaven—only water can do this. Hence, nothing can surpass it in overcoming hardness and strength. This is so because, though it twists and changes in myriad ways, it never loses its essence as water. This is why there is no substitute for it. Water adapts to all things yet never alters itself, for its softness and weakness. If people can embody this, though they face countless transformations, their minds remain steadfast and unchanging. Thus, they can overcome all things without harm.

Therefore, the soft overcomes the hard, the weak overcomes the strong. All under Heaven know this, yet none can practice it.

**Hé Shànggōng** said: The tongue is soft, the teeth are hard, yet the teeth perish before the tongue. Water can extinguish fire, yin can consume yang. Those who know the enduring nature of softness and weakness, and the harm that comes to hardness and strength, should be ashamed of their arrogance and fondness for aggression. Hence, the sage speaks of this.

This refers to the following text.

To bear a nation’s impurities is called the **Duo Ji** sovereign.
To bear a nation’s misfortunes is called the **King of All Under Heaven**.

**Wang Yuanzhe** said: The Sage’s ability to embody softness and weakness lies in taking water as his virtue.

To bear a nation’s misfortunes—does this not mean embracing filth and enduring humiliation?

To draw all the world’s crimes upon oneself is to receive the nation’s impurities and filth.
Thus, as a ruler of a single state, he calls himself *"lonely," "widowed," "unworthy"*—this is to bear the nation’s misfortunes.
Hence, all under Heaven turn toward him.

**Right speech as if reversed.**

**Wang Yuanzhe** said: Reversed for the petty wisdom of near-emotion, yet aligned with the utmost rectitude of the Dao.

**Su Ziyou** said: Right speech aligns with the Dao yet opposes convention. The common folk see bearing impurities as shame, bearing misfortunes as calamity—thus it is.

This chapter speaks of softness and weakness overcoming hardness and strength.
Hence, it first uses water as an analogy, then cites the Sage’s words to confirm it.

---
**Chapter 79: Harmonizing Great Grievances**

To harmonize great grievances will inevitably leave lingering grievances—how then can it be called good?

**Lü Jifu** said: Those who seek vengeance do not break their spears; though they harbor resentment, they do not blame the wind and tiles. Thus, the world attains balance. If one does not follow this path, grievances will only multiply. Yet people still strive to harmonize them endlessly.

Thus it is said: To harmonize great grievances will inevitably leave lingering grievances.

*"The unvirtuous, I too treat with virtue"*—this is how one cultivates true virtue.

To harmonize great grievances yet still leave lingering grievances—how then can it be called good?

A ruler cannot avoid action, yet if he is humble, yielding, and soft, the people will grow greedy and quarrelsome, and grievances and hatreds will flourish.

If the ruler enacts laws to restrain them—those who kill are put to death, those who injure are punished—yet if he harmonizes and repays their grievances, how can deceit grow worse and hatreds multiply? How then can it be called good?

Thus, the Sage holds the **left contract**.

**Hé Shànggōng** said: In ancient times, the Sage held the left contract, using inscribed tablets and matching seals as proof.

Without written laws or statutes, they carved contracts and matching seals to establish trust.

**Lü Xisheng** said: In ancient times, they tied knots to make agreements, and the people did not deceive. They split wood into contracts, and the people did not violate them. Why? Because the Sage had no fixed intentions—he took the people’s hearts as his own.

Thus, the Sage’s heart and the people’s hearts were like left and right contracts. When the contract came, they aligned without blame, so ruler and subjects grew close, and grievances did not arise.

**Li Rong** said: In ancient times, the Sage carved wooden contracts—the ruler held the left, the subject the right—as proof, sealing them with matching seals. They did not rely on laws or statutes, imposing no penalties on things, hence *"without blaming others."*

Thus, without punishment or blame, what grievances could there be to harmonize?

**Wang Bi** said: The left contract prevents the origin of grievances, yet does not blame others.

**Lü Jifu** said: Thus, the Sage holds the left contract without blaming others—this is how those who seek vengeance do not break their spears, though they harbor resentment, they do not blame the wind and tiles, and the world attains balance through this path.

Indeed, in ancient times, those who offered oxen and horses held the right contract—the right contract was for taking, the left for giving. Thus, the left contract was always given to others, never held as possession.

The Sage acts yet does not rely on it; when his work is done, he does not claim it. He always gives in abundance to the world. Even when killing, he lets officials execute, yet never takes personal responsibility—this is holding the left contract, giving to others without possession, the way of having no grievances and not blaming others.

Is this not because harmony is not a source of joy?

**Wang Yuanzhe** said: The left contract takes from others; the right contract takes from people. The left is without action, the right holds authority—thus the ancient division of contracts was so.

The Sage holds the left contract, not interfering in things, yet things come to align with him. He responds to their alignment—this is called *"responding and thus communicating with all under Heaven."*

Then, the Sage always bears the world’s blame yet harbors no blame toward others—thus, he remains without grievance.

**Zhuangzi** said: *"To gain is attributed to others; to lose is attributed to oneself."* **Tang** said: *"All under Heaven have faults; the fault lies with me."* This is what is meant.

*The Records* say: Those who offered oxen and horses held the right token—clearly, the offering party presented both tokens together, so the left token was held by the one bearing responsibility.

*The Historical Records* say: The right contract was used to demand accountability.

Thus, the virtuous hold the contracts;

**Wang Yuanzhe** said: Not interfering in things, yet responding to their alignment.

**Yan Xian** said: Seeking within oneself.

A virtuous ruler oversees the contracts and trust, preventing grievances from arising, and only then does he blame others.

The unvirtuous oversee **through** (彻).

**Hé Shànggōng** said: The unvirtuous ruler betrays his contracts and trust, overseeing others’ failures.

**Yan Xian** said: Seeking without.

**Shu Wang** said: If one oversees *through* (彻) to the end of matters, he cannot avoid blaming others. If he cannot avoid blaming others, he cannot make others without grievance—this is why he is unvirtuous.

*彻*, to penetrate.

The unvirtuous seek to penetrate all things, yet harbor the heart of harmony and grievance.

Mercy is penetration.

Is this not the source of obstruction?

Thus it is said: *"Joy in penetrating things is not the Sage’s way."*

The Dao of Heaven has no favorites; it always aligns with the virtuous.

**Commentary:** If virtuous, it aligns with them—what favoritism is there?

**Lü Jifu** said: Without favoritism, yet aligning only with the virtuous—this too is the way of holding the left contract without blaming others.

**Wang Yuanzhe** said: Only Heaven is without intention, yet it clearly aligns with virtue—Heaven and the Sage share the same path.

This chapter speaks of how having the heart to harmonize grievances is not as good as having no heart and merely overseeing contracts. To harmonize grievances will inevitably leave lingering grievances; to oversee contracts is to not blame others.

「The Dao of Heaven has no favorites—only the virtuous does it favor, and this is also the way of holding the left contract without blaming others.

**Chapter Eighty of *The Dao of the Small State and Few People* (*Xiǎoguó guǎmín*):**
The Small State and Few People.
**River Master** said: Though the Sage governs a great state, he still treats it as small, demonstrating frugality and restraint, not indulging in extravagance. Though the people are many, they are treated as if few—so no one dares to burden them.

**Zang Xuanjing** said: A state with little land is a small state. A people with few soldiers is few people.

**Wang Yuanzhe** said: In a small state with few people, the populace is pure and honest. But in a great state with many people, interests and harms clash, and crafty deceits arise. Observe the people in the capital and those in the rural settlements—their manners differ starkly between guile and sincerity. Thus, a small state means simple governance, and few people mean honest folk. Simple governance and honest folk can restore the administration of the Great Ancients.

Though there are implements for every ten or hundred households, they are not used.
**Duke Shou of Shu** said: If the people are few, then my needs are also narrow. Thus, in a small state with few people, even if there are implements for every ten or hundred households, they are not used.

**Wang Yuanzhe** said: Implements shared by ten households are called *shí* instruments. Implements shared by a hundred households are called *bǎi* instruments. Under the governance of simplicity and stillness, the aim is to let the people fulfill their natural inclinations without needless labor or contrivance, so there will never be matters of collective migration or warfare. Hence, though implements exist for every ten or hundred households, they are not used.

Let the people value death and not seek distant resettlement.
**River Master** said: If a ruler can create benefits and remove harms for the people, ensuring each finds their proper place, then the people will value death over life and cherish their existence. If governance and commands are not burdensome, the people will be content with their occupations and thus will not seek distant resettlement, abandoning their familiar dwellings.

**Wang Yuanzhe** said: Those who find joy in life and fulfill their nature will value death. Those who are at peace with their land and have no desires will not seek distant resettlement. Those who value death do not lightly regard life. Those who share life with others but do not value their own existence are different. Those who do not seek distant resettlement are at peace in their homes. Those who scatter among the myriad people and are not at peace in their homes are different.

Though there are boats and carriages, they have no need to ride them.
Though there are armor and weapons, they have no need to display them.
**Commentary:** There are no urgent journeys across perilous terrain, so though boats and carriages exist, they are not used. There are no battles to be fought, so though armor and weapons exist, they are not displayed.

**Zanwei** said: Hollowing wood makes boats, to ferry across water; carving wheels makes carriages, to traverse land. These are for distant travel. In an age of non-action, mountains have no paths or tunnels, marshes have no boats or bridges—since travel is already severed, what need for riding or display?

**Zhang Junxiang** said: Weapons are used to punish rebels, martial might to quell disorder. Once the ruler upholds the Dao, the people naturally turn to virtue and follow transformation—why would there be need to display weapons?

Boats and carriages are metaphors for the *Small Vehicle* (小乘), armor and weapons for the *Small Wisdom* (小智). When the people’s hearts are at ease, these are truly abandoned and unused.

**Wang Yuanzhe** said: If the people suffice within their natural endowments, then there will be no calamities of distant travel or warfare.

**Cao Daochong** said: Great states do not encroach; small states defend their lands. Armor, helmets, spears, and halberds—why use them if there is no war?

Let the people return to using knotted cords to record matters.
**Wang Yuanzhe** said: Governance is simple, the people are honest—so there is no need for written contracts.

**Emperor Xuanzong of Tang** said: Boats and carriages are for the convenience of migration; armor and weapons are for waging war. Since both are not desired, they are not used, and the people revert to simplicity, returning to the use of knotted cords.

**Explication:** In ancient times, before writing was invented, knotted cords were used to record events. Thus, the *Systematic Discourse* says: "In the Upper Antiquity, knotted cords were used for governance; later, the Sages replaced them with written contracts, desiring to show that in the age of knotted cords, people were all pure and simple." Once writing emerged, deceit and artifice grew daily. Now, to make people forget their emotions and desires and return to purity and simplicity, it is said: "Let the people return to using knotted cords."

Let their food be simple, their clothing fine, their dwellings secure, and their customs joyous.
**Commentary:** They till the soil and eat; they weave and wear. With mouths full, they play; with bellies satisfied, they wander. If the people can attain this, then:
- Stopping at what is proper makes food sweet.
- Removing ornamentation makes clothing fine.
- Not being disturbed makes dwellings secure.
- Preserving life makes customs joyous.

**Emperor Xuanzong of Tang** said: Not coveting rich flavors makes their food always sweet. Not laboring over embroidered patterns makes their clothing always fine. Not adorning their homes makes their dwellings secure. Not corrupting simplicity and purity makes their customs joyous.

Neighboring states are visible to one another, and the sounds of roosters and dogs can be heard. Yet the people die of old age without ever visiting each other.

**Wang Yuanzhe** said: This is the governance of fulfilling nature, and the people also fulfill their nature. I have often examined the closing passages of *The Analects* and *Mencius*—both end by praising the virtuous deeds of Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang. It seems that if one applies this book to governance, its effects will match those of the Sages of those ages.

Laozi was the greatest Sage. Yet the changes he encountered were precisely when the world reverted to its original simplicity and fulfillment of nature. Thus, he alone illuminated the meaning of *virtue* and *power* (德道), gathering scattered matters and uniting them in simplicity and sincerity. If one applies his book to governance, the people will transform and customs will flourish—this chapter’s effect is proof of it. Hence, the *Text’s* intent concludes here.

**Lü Jifu** said: Since the Three Dynasties, until the decline of Zhou, its culture became greatly corrupted. The people lost their natural and vital essence, so Laozi’s words saved them by emphasizing simplicity and returning to the governance of the Great Ancients. Yet the words of *The Book of Poetry* and *The Book of Documents* only reach as far as Yao, Shun, and the Three Dynasties—why does Laozi seek to return to the governance of the Great Ancients?

He replied: The Dao and the world have long corrupted each other. Only the Great Dao can make people return to their natural and vital essence. If one speaks of the Dao without addressing its worldly manifestations, one cannot truly know that the Great Dao has already been tested. This is why it must necessarily return to the Great Ancient Dao.»

The world has long departed from the Great Ancient Dao—can it all be restored?
He said: Not yet.

Then what is the purpose of his words?
He said: Ritual reached its fullness in the combined practices of the Three Kings, and music in the complete forms of the Six Dynasties. Yet even then, ritual did not place the *xuanshui* (玄水) and *dajīng* (大羹) beneath the *lìjiǔ* (醴酒) and harmonized soups, nor did music add the *huǐguǎn* (嘒管) and clear *qìngqìng* (清磬) above the *zhūxián* (朱弦) and sparse *shūyuè* (疏越)—this was to make people understand the true intent of ritual and music. What was unavoidable was to refine and return to the origins in this way.

At that time, Confucius was seeking to restore the lost traditions of Wen, Wu, and the Duke of Zhou, while Laozi expounded on the Dao to match the times—his intent was no different.

It is like the month of *sì* (巳) when fire reigns, yet the *jīn* (金) qi has already emerged within it. This is how the Five Phases succeed one another without interruption.

Thus, though the ancients had tenfold or hundredfold tools yet did not use them, had boats and carriages yet did not ride them, had armor and weapons yet did not display them, when great matters arose, they gathered the people—this was unavoidable. Though their people used knotted cords for records, neighboring states were within sight and hearing, yet they grew old and died without visiting one another, it was only when governance grew burdensome, when officials were pressed to enforce laws and supervise travel, that footsteps reached the borders of neighboring states and carriage tracks stretched beyond a thousand *lǐ*—this too was unavoidable.

What was unavoidable was always here, yet what they sought to restore was always there.

Then would the Sage not weary of elaborate governance to correct the people’s excesses?

Yet if one seeks a world that is not at peace and prosperity, it cannot be attained. Even if he spoke no more, still he would have set aside the *xuanshui* and removed the *shūyuè*—who would know the ritual’s true simplicity and the music’s moderation, or the intent to return to the origins?

The words of the Sage—how could they be mere trifles?

When neighbors live close yet do not interact, when voices are heard but no demands are made, this is the utmost of *wuqiú* (无求). This chapter speaks of small states and few people, where governance is simple and the people pure—thus the Great Ancient Dao could be restored. From the time of knotted cords onward, all was the Great Ancient Dao.

If one truly upholds this book to transform the people, such restoration could be achieved.

*"Sincere words are not ornate"* (信言不美), Chapter Eighty-One.

Shū Wáng said: *"Sincere"* is nature. When words approach nature, they are perfect, and this is the utmost harmony of the world. Hence, sincere words are not ornate.

To govern the world, when the food is sweet, the clothing is fine, the dwelling is secure, the customs are joyful, and the aged die without mutual visits, then governance has reached its peak. If one then gathers and returns to the Dao, this is why it follows with *"sincere words are not ornate."*

Wáng Yuànzé said: *"Sincere words"* are essential to the Dao. The Dao expressed through speech is plain and without flavor—how could there be ornamentation?

*"Theft"* speaks of excessive sweetness; sweetness is the beauty of flavor.
*"Prostitute"* speaks of extravagance; extravagance is the beauty of color.
*"Craftsman"* speaks like a bellows; bellows are the beauty of sound.
*Sincere words* are those spoken in accordance with the Dao, plain and without flavor, yet not departing from the Dao. Speech without ornate words is not mere appearance. Speech without melodious charm is not mere empty rhetoric. This is why they are not ornate.

Ornate words are not sincere.

Héshàng Gōng said: Ornate words are embellished, flowery language. Insincerity is the adornment of falsehood and the proliferation of emptiness.

Jì Wáng said: If words are ornate, they cannot approach nature. Hence, ornate words are not sincere.

Wáng Yuànzé said: To speak of affairs without reference to the Dao, though it may please the shallow ears of the unenlightened, it is not profound discourse. Hence, it cannot be fully sincere.

The virtuous do not argue. Those who truly practice the Dao do not engage in debate or persuasion. Those who argue are not virtuous.

Táng Mínghuáng said: Empty, hollow argumentation is why they are not virtuous.

Wáng Yuànzé said: Speech is only for illuminating the Dao. The Dao is concise and unburdened—why use excessive words?

Kǒngzǐ said: *"The purpose of speech is to convey meaning."*

The wise are not all-knowing. *御注*: The wise grasp the subtleties of the Dao, returning to its essence. The *经* says: *"Few words suffice."* *Zhuānwēi* says: The wise understand their Dao. They are clear in principle and know its roots, obtaining its essence alone—why need they be all-knowing? This is what is meant by *"few words suffice."* Those who are all-knowing are not truly wise. *御注*: The more one hears and sees, the more one should simplify.

Zhuāngzǐ said: *"Extensive knowledge is not necessarily profound; eloquence and debate are not necessarily wise."*

Héshàng Gōng said: Those who are all-knowing have many experiences but lose the essence and truth.

Sū Zǐyóu said: *"If one has a single thread that unifies all things, then there is no need for extensive knowledge—yet daily growth does not necessarily mean understanding the Dao."*

The Sage does not accumulate.

Wáng Bì said: Without private possession, one naturally has abundance; only goodness is given, and things are fulfilled as they come. When one gives to others, one’s own abundance increases. When one gives to others, one’s own abundance grows ever more.

Wáng Yuànzé said: *"To act for others"* means to apply oneself to affairs and governance of the world. To follow their momentum and benefit them, then one’s Dao is not diminished, and one’s affairs become ever more expansive. *"To give to others"* means to impart the Dao. To impart the Dao is like the heavens giving birth to things—I have never expended anything, yet things multiply daily. Since it is said *"the Sage does not accumulate,"* it is also clarified that one can satisfy all things. Only by not accumulating can one truly suffice.

Cáo Dàochōng said: *"The richness of boundless generosity is inexhaustible; the response of non-accumulation is limitless. Marvelous use arises daily, and the more one seeks, the more one finds—great generosity gives daily, and the more one takes, the more one prospers."* If one accumulates, then there is insufficiency. If one has no hoarding, then there is abundance.

The Sage’s non-accumulation is also mere emptiness. Emptiness is why one can respond to all demands, while fullness cannot be diminished. This is how one gives to others—one’s own abundance increases, and the more one gives to others, the more one’s own abundance grows.

The Dao of Heaven benefits without harming; *御注*: Though the *乾* begins by benefiting and sweetly nourishing the world, it does not speak of what it benefits, yet all things are truly benefited by it—never does it cause harm.

Lú Yù said: Human affairs benefit one thing while harming another. The Dao of Heaven is balanced, so there is benefit without harm.

Gù Huān said: The virtuous obtain the Dao to fulfill their nature; the wicked obtain the Dao to transform their nature. All return to the One, benefiting without harming.

Heaven follows the Dao, hence in spring and summer it nurtures and grows, while in autumn and winter it matures and ripens. This is benefiting without harming.

The Sage’s Dao acts without striving. Lǚ Jìfǔ said: *"The words of Lǎozǐ—when observing inwardly the hearts of all, outwardly all things, upward to Heaven, and downward to Earth—there is nothing that does not accord with them. This is sincerity. Yet the common scholar hears them and laughs, the world thinks them like the unworthy. This is not ornate. When speech is most natural, its meaning is most profound—this is virtue. Yet they do not delight in speech—this is not argumentative. Their wisdom reaches to no-wisdom—this is wisdom. Yet their simplicity never departs from my heart—this is not all-knowing. Yet scholars seek ornamentation, argumentation, and extensive knowledge, and thus they are distant. The Dao of Lǎozǐ—though the Sage’s wisdom, it still rejects and abandons accumulation. Hence, reaching to no-accumulation, it satisfies all demands of things, and this is why one’s own abundance increases and grows ever more. Yet scholars cannot carve their hearts here, and thus they cannot attain this Dao. All things have what benefits them and what does not benefit them. What does not benefit them cannot avoid harming them. Only Heaven’s Dao—having no benefit, there is no non-benefit; having no non-benefit, it benefits without harming. All things that act—none do not have ‘self.’ Having ‘self’ means having strife. Only the Sage’s Dao—though acting, it is without action. Being without action means without ‘self,’ and without ‘self’ means without strife. This is the Dao of Heaven alone. The Sage follows Heaven’s Dao—though acting, it arises from non-action. Transformation and fulfillment occur in affairs, yet there is no strife with things. Thus, one’s sagely accomplished deeds are preserved."*

This chapter summarizes the essence of the *经*, speaking in accordance with the Dao. The Dao is plain and without flavor, hence it is not ornate.

The Way is spoken of to illuminate the Dao, hence it is not embroidered.
The Dao is essential and not burdensome, hence it is not extensive.
The Sage embodies the Dao, hence does not accumulate.
Thus, when one attains the utmost of having no need to fulfill the demands of all things, the more one has, the more one possesses.
Heaven follows the Dao, hence benefits without harming.
The Sage follows Heaven, hence acts without strife.

If one truly seeks to embody the teachings of Dao and virtue, there is none more exalted than benefiting things while not engaging in strife, hence this is where it concludes.

Dao and virtue, when unified, are discussed before form takes shape. Before form, Dao and virtue are both hidden.
After form appears, Dao and virtue are both revealed.
What is Dao? That which all things follow is Dao.
What is virtue? That which is inherent within Dao is virtue.

When viewed from their differences, Dao and virtue, though bearing two names, are distinct.
When viewed from their unity, Dao and virtue, though named separately, remain inseparable in essence.
Dao descends as virtue, yet virtue has never been separate from Dao.
Virtue arises from Dao, yet Dao has never been separate from virtue.

The *Nanhua Jing* states:
"From the One, there arises a One yet unformed. That all things obtain from this to be born is called virtue.
When spoken of as the One yet unformed, it is called Dao.
When spoken of as that from which all things obtain to be born, it is called virtue."

Again it says:
"Virtue encompasses the unity of Dao.
When Dao and virtue are united, they blend as one.
When separated, they scatter as two.
Now, those who speak of the *Daode Jing* are correct.
Those who speak of the *Dao Jing* and the *De Jing* separately are not.

Later scholars, seeing the first chapter of the upper text and taking its words ‘Dao that can be spoken of,’ named it the *Dao Jing*.
Seeing the first chapter of the lower text and taking its words ‘the utmost virtue is not virtue,’ named it the *De Jing*.
Thus arises the division of Dao and virtue.

If the upper text speaks only of Dao, how then does it mention the fullness of virtue’s manifestation?
It follows Dao alone, yet Dao does not depart from virtue.
If the lower text speaks only of virtue, how then does it say ‘Dao gives birth, virtue nourishes’?
It is virtue that does not depart from Dao.

Examined through the text, Dao and virtue mutually depend upon each other and cannot be considered in isolation.
When one attempts to discuss this, Dao without virtue cannot be revealed, and virtue without Dao cannot be illuminated.
Dao is without action and without form, hence it precedes the transformation of all things.
Virtue has application and action, hence it follows after the generation and transformation of things.
Dao precedes, hence it is placed above.
Virtue follows, hence it is placed below.
Dao and virtue, when united, are one; they cannot be divided into two.

The *Xisheng Jing* states:
"Dao and virtue united is to be in harmony with the Dao."

The *Nanhua Jing* states:
"Form does not arise from Dao without generation.
Generation does not proceed from virtue without illumination.
Exhausting form and probing generation,
Establishing virtue and illuminating Dao."

From this, it is clear that Dao and virtue mutually depend upon each other and are not separate.

Master Sima Wen Gong summarized the essence, and the Grand Historian said:
"The Master Laozi wrote the upper and lower chapters, speaking of the meaning of Dao and virtue.
Later scholars, following the titles from the opening words of each chapter,
Named the upper chapter the *Dao Jing* and the lower chapter the *De Jing*.
Dao and virtue are inherently connected and cannot be considered in isolation.
Now we adhere to the original name.
The *Daode Jing*—the True Classic of Dao and Virtue—takes its entirety from the *Shan Ji* (卷十二)."
